Law, Rights and Ideology in Russia


Law, Rights and Ideology in Russia: Landmarks in the destiny of a great power brings into sharp focus several key episodes in Russia’s vividly ideological engagement with law and rights. Drawing on 30 years of experience of consultancy and teaching in many regions of Russia and on library research in Russian-language texts, Bill Bowring provides unique insights into people, events and ideas.

The book starts with the surprising role of the Scottish Enlightenment in the origins of law as an academic discipline in Russia in the eighteenth century. The Great Reforms of Tsar Aleksandr II, abolishing serfdom in 1861 and introducing jury trial in 1864, are then examined and debated as genuine reforms or the response to a revolutionary situation. A new interpretation of the life and work of the Soviet legal theorist Yevgeniy Pashukanis leads to an analysis of the conflicted attitude of the USSR to international law and human rights, especially the right of peoples to self-determination. The complex history of autonomy in Tsarist and Soviet Russia is considered, alongside the collapse of the USSR in 1991. An examination of Russia’s plunge into the European human rights system under Yeltsin is followed by the history of the death penalty in Russia. Finally, the secrets of the ideology of ‘sovereignty’ in the Putin era and their impact on law and rights are revealed. Throughout, the constant theme is the centuries long hegemonic struggle between Westernisers and Slavophiles, against the backdrop of the Messianism that proclaimed Russia to be the Third Rome, was revived in the mission of Soviet Russia to change the world and which has echoes in contemporary Eurasianism and the ideology of sovereignty.

Bill Bowring is Professor of Law at Birkbeck College, University of London, where he teaches human rights, public international law and minority rights. He is also a practising barrister specialising in human rights, since 2000 representing applicants against Russia and other post-Soviet states at the European Court of Human Rights. He has taught at a number of Russian universities and has worked as a consultant with DFID, the EU, Council of Europe, and the OSCE’s High Commissioner on National Minorities.

Introduction


DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-1

This book is not a general history of law in Russia; neither is it one of politics or ideas. Andrzej Walicki has written an excellent history of Russian thought up to the late Soviet period1 and a definitive exploration of legal philosophies of Russian liberalism.2 Instead, a number of episodes and themes are brought into sharp focus. The word ‘landmarks’ has been chosen deliberately. In 1909, following the defeat of the First Russian Revolution in 1905, a group of leading liberal and religious theorists published the collection Vekhi (Landmarks), in which Struve, Berdyaev, Kistyakovsky and others attacked the Russian intelligentsia who, in the tradition of Herzen, Belinsky and Chernyshevsky, all of them later given hero status in Soviet mythology, had supported the revolutionary movement. The collection has recently been republished in Russia in paperback in a widely distributed series.3 So the word ‘landmarks’ resonates in Russia, where history has great symbolic and mythological force.

1 Walicki (1979). 2 Walicki (1992). 3 Gershenzon (1909/2011). Thus, the imprisoned oligarch Mikhail Khodorkovskiy has presented himself to the educated public as a ‘Decembrist’, inheriting the tradition of the revolutionary democrats of 1825. The authorities were stupid enough to send him to serve his sentence in Chita in Siberia, where those Decembrists who were not executed after 1825 were sent into exile. And on 21 March 2011 a large number of Russian human rights activists and cultural figures, especially writers, signed an open letter in support of Khodorkovskiy to the former President Medvedev headed ‘Free the Decembrists, Mr President!’4 The web page on the khodorkovskiy.ru website had at its head a joint portrait of Medvedev and Tsar Aleksandr II, the initiator of the Liberation of the Serfs in 1861 and Great Reforms of 1864.

4 Website:http://khodorkovsky.ru/publicsupport/news/16020.html (accessed 27 October 2012). The open letter started:

The intelligentsia demand from Medevedev political amnesty and liberalisation.

On 3 March (2011) in your speech commemorating 150 years since the abolition of serfdom in Russia, you said that the model for the implementation of liberal reforms for you was the policy implemented by the emperor Aleksandr II, upon whom fell the heavy historical task of liberating the country from the despotic ‘military-bureaucratic’ inheritance of his predecessor Nikolai I.

As you know, the liberation of the serfs was one of the most important measures of Aleksandr ‘The Liberator’, but this was preceded by other actions without which it would have been impossible to implement his reforming course.

Already, within a year of occupying the throne, the emperor in his Manifesto ‘On ending the war’ of 13 March 1856 made clear his desire to reform and to bring about equality before the courts.

In Russia the famous ‘spring’ began – and a wide and rather free public discussion unfolded on the situation in the country and on the roads to reform, and a mass of liberal publications appeared. On the commemoration of his coronation on 26 August 1856 the emperor amnestied – without seeking confessions or repentance – the Decembrists, and the participants in dissident circles (the Petrashevtsy including Dostoevsky) and the participants in the Polish uprising in 1830–31.



Several significant points should be noted here. First, the term ‘intelligentsia’ is used in today’s Russia without irony. Second, there is a strong sense of history and of significant historical landmarks in Russia. Third, the Great Reforms of the 1860s are fresh in the minds of the educated public in Russia. One source from which I draw in this book is a new collection Aleksandr II: the tragedy of a reformer. People in the destiny of reforms, reforms in the destiny of people.5

5 Lapin (2012). I also use the word ‘ideology’ in my title purposefully.

The usual response, if the words ‘Russia’ and ‘ideology’ are placed together, is to think of Marxism–Leninism or scientific communism. But in fact, ideology, as the systems of ideas legitimating Russia’s statehood and development, has much deeper roots and has taken a wide variety of forms. The ideology of the Putin regime, the ideas of ‘sovereignty’ and ‘sovereign democracy’, developed by the regime’s chief ideologist, Vladimir Surkov, have, as I explore in Chapter 10, surprising foundations.

Moreover, a constant thread in Russian ideology is that of Messianism, the idea that Russia has a special or sacred task, of saving the world. With the fall of Constantinople in 1453, there was a growing tendency to refer to Moscow as the ‘Third Rome’. In 1510 (during the reign of Henry VIII of England) the Russian Orthodox monk Filofei composed a panegyric letter to Tsar Vasiliy III (1505–1533) in which he warned: ‘And now I say unto Thee, take care and take heed, pious Tsar: all the empires of Christendom are united in thine. For two Romes have fallen, and the Third exists and there will not be a fourth. Thy Christian Empire, according to the great theologian, will not pass away.’ Moscow thus became, symbolically, the ‘Third Rome’ and the ‘Second Jerusalem’, inheritor of both the Roman Empire and the Christian Church.

The double-headed eagle was the symbol of the late Byzantine Empire and symbolised the unity between the Orthodox Church and the Empire. It was adopted by Ivan III when he married the Byzantine princess Sophia Paleologo, whose uncle Constantine was the last Byzantine Emperor. It is the state symbol of Russia today and extremely controversial in a country whose Constitution proclaims it to be a secular state and the state of all its various peoples, more than 150 of them.

‘Destiny’ is another word that, in Russia, comes with a great deal of baggage, as I show especially in Chapter 1.

Is Russia therefore simply a land of mysticism, obscurantism and Messianism?

In Chapter 1, I tackle the question of how Russian is to be theorised. In 1939 in a radio broadcast Winston Churchill famously said: ‘I cannot forecast to you the action of Russia. It is a riddle, wrapped in a mystery, inside an enigma; but perhaps there is a key. That key is Russian national interest.’6 As a theory of Russia, this is plainly wholly inadequate.

6 Website:http://www.churchill-society-london.org.uk/RusnEnig.html (accessed 11 July 2012). Marx and Engels both had a fascination with Russia and exerted a certain influence over developments. There is no ‘Marxist’ theory of Russia, just as Marx himself insisted that he was not a Marxist. For most of his life Marx was an uncompromising enemy of the Russian Empire, but mastered the Russian language in his later years and entered into correspondence with the Russian Narodniks, including Vera Zasulich (1849–1919). In his letter to her of March 1881, he took very seriously the role of the Russian peasant and the ‘rural commune’. Marx wrote:

Theoretically speaking, then, the Russian ‘rural commune’ can preserve itself by developing its basis, the common ownership of land, and by eliminating the principle of private property which it also implies … it can gain possession of the fruits with which capitalist production has enriched mankind, without passing through the capitalist regime.



That is not, of course, how things turned out in Russia.

Next, I turn to a Gramscian analysis of Russian ideology and politics, written in the early years after the collapse of the Soviet Union. This persuasive account continues the story, familiar to some of my readers, of the dispute between the Westernisers and the Slavophiles, which began in the nineteenth century. I refer in this regard to some of the most recent Russian scholarly literature. Gramsci was, of course, a Marxian thinker. Soviet ideology, too, had more than a trace of messianism: Soviet leadership of the 3rd International was at least at first intended to save the world from capitalism. Caterina Clark has recently written of Moscow: the fourth Rome.7 She wrote:

7 Clark (2011). Arguably Stalin, and many Soviet cultural bureaucrats and intellectuals – themselves inheritors of a messianic tradition – aspired to generate a superior civilisation, to make their country a primus inter pares in a cultural confederation within their world, continental Europe.8



8 Clark (2011), 10. In the twentieth century the project of ‘Eurasianism’, first mooted by Count Trubetskoy in the White emigration after 1917, has become an important source of the ideology of the Putin regime. Aleksandr Dugin (born 1962), who is now professor of sociology at Moscow State University, started as a propagandist of ‘Russian fascism’ and was deeply engaged in occultism. He summed up his ‘Eurasianism’ in 1997 as follows:

In principle, Eurasia and our space, the heartland Russia, remain the staging area of a new anti-bourgeois, anti-American revolution … The new Eurasian empire will be constructed on the fundamental principle of the common enemy: the rejection of Atlanticism … and the refusal to allow liberal values to dominate us.9



9 Dugin (1997). Eurasianism is intended to unite the traditional religions of Russia, Orthodoxy, Islam, Judaism and Buddhism, in a common opposition to Western materialism and consumerism. Dugin speaks and publishes very regularly in the Russian media and Putin and others in the elite use his rhetoric. I explore Messianism in Chapter 1 and return to it in Chapter 10, where I show that, in contemporary Russia, there is an unlikely marriage between Eurasianism and a decisionism, which has its roots in Carl Schmitt’s ideas.

Next, it may come as a surprise for my readers to learn that the first Russian professor of law in Russia was Semyon Desnitskiy (1740–1789), who was sent to study at the University of Glasgow from 1760 to 1767, at the time of the Scottish Enlightenment, attended lectures by Adam Smith, successfully defended his doctorate on civil and church law and was Professor of Law at Moscow University from 1767 to 1787. He was the first to teach in Russian – his colleagues all taught in German – and brought with him from Scotland a passion for Roman law. He translated William Blackstone’s ‘Commentaries on the Laws of England’ into Russian. As a result of his inspiration, the academic discipline of law in Russia has remained strong to the present day. In Chapter 2, I not only tell his own story and that of his influence on the teaching of law in Russia, but place him in the context of the short-lived Russian Enlightenment, which survived the Pugachev Rebellion, the mass uprising of the peasants which threatened the very existence of the Empire, but did not survive the French Revolution.

In 1812 Napoleon arrived at Moscow, only to witness from the Sparrow Hills the city in flames, its population having set the fires departing eastwards. In 1814 the Russian army, led by Aleksandr I and his generals, whose names, Wittgenstein, Barclay de Tollay and others, announced their varied origins, pursued Napoleon from the burnt-out ruins of Moscow, all the way across Europe and triumphantly marched through Paris before departing home, their work done.10 The Russian Empire appeared to be invincible. The disaster of defeat in the Crimean War changed all that and plunged the Empire into deep crisis.

10 Lieven (2010). There is a strong ideological factor at play here too. One of the factors that precipitated the Crimean War was the Russian Empire’s claim to lead and protect the Christians of the Ottoman Empire, to reclaim Hagia Sophia in Istanbul as the ‘Mother Church’ and to re-establish Constantinople as the capital of Orthodoxy connecting Moscow to Jerusalem. Part of the reason for the downfall of Nikolai II in World War I was his ambition, egged on by Rasputin, to reclaim Constantinople for Orthodox Christendom.

I do not explore 1825 and the Decembrists’ uprising in any detail, but, in Chapter 3, move on to the 1850s and 1860s, in my view a true landmark in the history of law in Russia. Aleksandr II, his liberation of the serfs, and the Great Reforms he initiated are a constant point of reference in contemporary Russia as I have shown earlier. With the help of newly published work by the Russian scholar Anna Popova, I analyse the legal and judicial reforms of 1864, which saw trial by jury established in the Russian Empire, along with a much reduced role for the prokuratura and truly independent judges, chief among them the venerated figure of judicial reform, A. F. Koni, cited to the present day. In the Soviet period the role of Aleksandr II was minimised and, instead, historians depicted a revolutionary situation in the 1860s. It was, according to the Soviet historians, the threat of a peasant uprising led by revolutionary democrats, Belinsky, Chernyshevsky and Herzen, which forced Aleksandr’s hand.Recent historiographical research shows the Soviet account to have been deeply flawed.

Friedrich Engels was the primary influence on Russia in relation to law. The article he wrote with Karl Kautsky in 1887, ‘Juridical socialism,11 was the touchstone for the Russian Social-Democrats. Engels emphasised that the worldview of the bourgeoisie was the ‘juridical worldview’. He endorsed the dominant theory of legal positivism and saw law as an instrument of class domination. This view was taken up by Georgy Plekhanov and other social-democrats, and, after the 1905 Revolution, provoked a spirited response by the Russian ex-Marxist, liberal and religious legal theorists, in the famous collection Vekhi, to which I referred earlier. Lenin denounced Vekhi as ‘an encyclopedia of liberal renegacy’.

11 Engels and Kautsky (1977). In Chapter 4, I tell the story of the most well known (in the West) and accomplished of the generation of Marxist legal scholars who wrote and disputed in the years after the 1917 Revolution, Yevgeniy Pashukanis. Research in Moscow libraries has given me access to the journals published in this period; and this is the first time, as far as I know, that Pashukanis’ intellectual journey has been analysed in depth, from a precocious early blossoming to deep conformity. In particular, I have ascertained the place and circumstances in which he wrote his famous A general theory of law and Marxism. An attempt at a critique of fundamental juridical concepts and can offer some explanation as to its content, references and its unique place in his theoretical output. I also analyse some of the crucial debates of the period; and provide, in a number of annexes, the tables of contents of some of the key journals.

Chapter 5 continues with the thoroughly paradoxical and even schizophrenic history of Soviet public international law, which managed to combine a strict positivism with a revolutionary principle, the right of peoples to self-determination, first adumbrated by Lenin before World War I. At first fiercely resisted by the West, this principle achieved, through a combination of Soviet diplomacy and the role of national liberation movements in the struggle for decolonisation, the status not only of a right, but of a right in which all states have an interest in compliance, erga omnes. By the late Soviet period this focus had entirely disappeared.

Sovietologists and indeed almost all scholars of Russia and the USSR were confounded when, at the end of 1991, the USSR collapsed, ignominiously and without protest. A key landmark in the history of law in Russia is what preceded and followed the collapse: an unprecedented series of proclamations of sovereignty by many components of the Russian Socialist Federation of Soviet States, the RSFSR. The extraordinarily complex and sometimes violent consequences of these events continue to haunt Russia’s leaders. Chapter 6 provides a close analysis, drawing on the work of Valentin Mikhailov.

Chapter 7 turns to another utterly unexpected moment, in 1996, when Russia enacted a law ‘on national-cultural autonomy’. My abiding interest in this topic was sparked when I was asked by the Council of Europe to provide an expert comment on Russia’s first periodical report having ratified the Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities. National (or personal) non-territorial cultural autonomy was anathema to Lenin and the Bolsheviks, a matter that united Lenin, Stalin and Trotsky.

I have a special interest, for reasons I outline in what follows, in the extraordinary history of Russia’s accession to the Council of Europe in 1996 and ratification of the European Convention on Human Rights in 1998. Why did the Western European member states permit Russia to join, at a time when the First Chechen War (1994–1997) was raging? Why did the Communists and nationalists in the state Duma vote overwhelmingly for Russian accession and ratification, given the very high level of interference in its internal affairs to which Russia was subjecting itself? I explore and seek to answer these questions in Chapter 8.

Russia is the only Council of Europe member state not to have definitively abolished the death penalty and it has signed but not yet ratified Protocol 6 to the European Convention on Human Rights, which would require it to do so. In Chapter 9, I trace the history of the death penalty in Russia; in earlier centuries Russia was, very much ahead of its time, the only state in Europe to abolish the ultimate penalty. I show how the Russian Orthodox Church played as surprising role. The dues ex machine that brought about de facto abolition turned out to be the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation.

In the present day, Vladislav Surkov (b.1964) emerged as the preeminent ideologist of the first Putin regime and then Medvedev’s term as President (although he has recently been moved from the President’s Administration to become a deputy prime minister). He is responsible for the doctrines of ‘sovereignty’ and ‘sovereign democracy’. In 2006 a collection appeared entitled Sovereignty, edited by the ‘young conservative’ Nikita Garadzha, with essays by Putin, Medevedev, Surkov and others. The key essay is entitled ‘Sovereignty as a political choice’, by Aleksandr Filippov, who is the chief Russian translator and exponent of the Nazi legal theorist Carl Schmitt. Indeed, the whole collection is infused with Schmitt’s decisionistic ideas. Surkov and his circle have strongly influenced senior figures in the judiciary, especially Valery Zorkin, the Chairman of the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation. His speeches and articles make frequent reference to ‘sovereignty’ in the special sense given to it by the Putin regime.





Chapter 1

Theorising Russia’s ideological history




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-2

In this chapter, I delve more deeply into two significant approaches to understanding Russia’s ideology and history, by Karl Marx and by Jeremy Lester and, from this basis, reflect on the perennial Russian conflict between Westernisers and Slovophiles and on Russia’s Messianism and, since the early twentieth century, Eurasianism.

Paradoxically, Karl Marx, who began with views consistent with those of the Westernisers, late in life taught himself Russian and corresponded with the Russian revolutionaries and formed views that are in some ways close to the Slavophiles. Early in his writing career, in 1851–52, Karl Marx (5 May 1818–14 March 1883) announced his deep hostility to the Russia Empire. In Revolution and counter-revolution or Germany in 1848, published as a book edited by his daughter Eleanor Aveling (Marx) in 1912, he referred to:

[T]he terrible reality of the Russian Empire; that empire which by every movement proclaims the pretension of considering all Europe as the domain of the Slavonic race, and especially of the only energetic part of this race, of the Russians; that empire which, with two capitals such as St. Petersburg and Moscow, has not yet found its centre of gravity, as long as the ‘City of the Czar’ [Constantinople, called in Russian Tzarigrad, the Czar’s city], considered by every Russian peasant as the true metropolis of his religion and his nation, is not actually the residence of its Emperor; that empire which, for the last one hundred and fifty years, has never lost, but always gained territory by every war it has commenced. And well known in Central Europe are the intrigues by which Russian policy supported the new-fangled system of Panslavism, a system than which none better could be invented to suit its purposes.1



1 Marxh (1852/1912), 92. In 1856–57, when in his 30s, and following the end of the Crimean War (October 1853–February 1856), he wrote Revelations of the diplomatic history of the 18th Century, published in instalments in The Free Press from August 1856 to April 18572 These articles were edited by his daughter Eleanor and published after his death, in 1899, by Swan Sonnenschein in London. The title page of this edition carries ‘Opinions of the press’. The opinion of the Westminster Review (founded by Jeremy Bentham as the official organ of the Philosophical Radicals and published from 1824 to 1914) was:

2 Marx (1856/1899/1986). With all Marx’s faults and his extravagant abuse of high political personages, one cannot but admire the man’s strength of mind, the courage of his opinions, and the scorn and contempt for everything small, petty and mean. Although many and great changes have taken place since these papers appeared, they are still valuable not only for the elucidation of the past, but also for throwing a clearer light upon the present as also upon the future.



Marx’s target in the book was British connivance with Russia:

[A]t the time of the Empress Anna [ruled 1730 to 1740], England already betrayed her own allies to Russia, it will be seen from the pamphlets we are now about to reprint that, even before the epoch of Anna, at the very epoch of Russian ascendency in Europe, springing up at the time of Peter I, the plans of Russia were understood, and the connivance of British statesmen at these plans was denounced by English writers.3



3 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 40. Chapter IV presented a ‘general history of Russian politics’.4 For Marx, the ‘cradle of Muscovy’ was the ‘bloody mire of Mongolian slavery’, the ‘Tatar yoke’ which lasted from 1237 to 1462 – ‘a yoke not only crushing but dishonouring and withering the very soul of the people that fell its prey’.5 He identified the state policy of Muscovy, from Ivan Kalita (the moneybag, 1288–1340) who used tax money collected ostensibly for the Tatars to build his own possessions, to Ivan III (the Great, 1440–1505), who gathered the Russian lands and to Peter the Great, who gave Russia access to the sea, and nineteenth century Russia as ‘to encroach by the fraudulent use of a hostile power, to weaken that power by the very act of using it, and to overthrow it at last by the efforts produced through its own instrumentality’.6 He summed up his theory of Russia as follows:

4 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 74. 5 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 77. 6 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 87. It is in the terrible and abject school of Mongolian slavery that Muscovy was nursed and grew up. It gathered strength only by becoming a virtuoso in the craft of serfdom. Even when emancipated, Muscovy continued to perform its traditional part of the slave as master. At length Peter the Great coupled the political craft of the Mongol slave with the proud aspiration of the Mongol master, to whom Genghis Khan had, by will, bequeathed his conquest of the earth.7



7 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 87. Peter the Great’s achievement was as follows:

It was but by the conversion of Muscovy from a country wholly of land into a sea-bordering empire, that the traditional limits of the Muscovite policy could be superseded and merged into that bold synthesis which, blending the encroaching method of the Mongol slave with the world-conquering tendencies of the Mongol master, forms the life-spring of modern Russian diplomacy.8



8 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 89. The conquest of the Baltic provinces, from Sweden, with their German aristocracy, gave him diplomats and generals, and a ‘crop of bureaucrats, schoolmasters and drill-sergeants, who were to drill Russians into that varnish of civilisation that adapts them to the technical appliances of the Western peoples without imbuing them with their ideas.’9 Avraham Yassour contends that:

9 Marx (1856/1899/1986), 91. In the 1850s Marx’ vision of Russian realities was that it was a ‘semi-Eastern’ society. He observed the following characteristic traits: the reign of a despotic potentate: scattered village communities; the absence of predominantly privately owned land; exploitation of the peasants’ communities by a bureaucratic, hierarchic apparatus, etc. His denomination of Russia as ‘semi-Asiatic’ was not a journalistic geographical metaphor: Russia seemed to be isolated from the socioeconomic dynamics of modernization exemplified in Western Europe. Russian autocrats – the Ivans and Peters – followed the Mongol pattern, structuring the evolving Russian Imperial-State into a pyramidal framework of centralized and militarized despotism.10



10 Yassour (1987), 8–9. In his Late Marx and the Russian road: Marx and ‘the peripheries of capitalism’,11 Teodor Shanin explored Marx’s later fascination with Russia, teaching himself Russian from 1870–71 and building a substantial library of Russian texts, including Flerovskiy and Chernishevskiy. The attraction of Russia to Marx was, as Shanin points out, ‘rich evidence concerning rural communes (“archaic” yet evidently alive in a world of capitalist triumphs) and of direct revolutionary experience, all encompassed by the theory and practice of Russian populism’.12 Marx was taken to support the Populists, and his letters were suppressed by the Russian Marxists, Plekhanov and others, and the draft manuscripts were discovered in 1913 by D.B. Ryazanov and deciphered with the help of Nikolay Bukharin. However, they were not published until 1923.13

11 Shanin (1983). 12 Ibid. 13 Wada (1981), 130. Marx himself initially denounced Russian Populism in a footnote to the first edition of Capital, but erased the footnote and added, in the postscript, a glowing tribute to Chernyshevskiy, author of What is to be done?, as the ‘great Russian scholar and critic’.14 In the French edition of Capital, he changed a passage in Chapter 26, ‘The secret of primitive accumulation,’ so as to imply that the English form of the expropriation of the peasants is applicable only to Western Europe or, to put it differently, Eastern Europe and Russia may follow a completely different path of evolution.15

14 Wada (1981), 133–34. 15 Wada (1981), 135. In his famous reply to Vera Zasulich (1849–1919) of 8 March 1881,16 Marx emphasised that the expropriation of the agricultural producer, carried out in such a radical way in England, was ‘historically inevitable’ only in the countries of Western Europe. He continued:

16 Text at Shanin, (1983), 123; Marx’s four earlier drafts of the letter are also set out in Shanin (1983), Part II. In the Western case, then, one form of private property is transformed into another form of private property. In the case of the Russian peasants, however, their communal property would have to be transformed into private property.

The analysis in Capital therefore provides no reasons either for or against the vitality of the Russian commune. But the special study I have made of it, including a search for original source-material, has convinced me that the commune is the fulcrum for social regeneration in Russia. But in order that it might function as such, the harmful influences assailing it from all sides must first be eliminated, and it must then be assured the normal conditions for spontaneous development.17



17 Shanin (1983), 124. In other words, Marx was open to the idea that Russia might have its own path of non-capitalist development, through the elimination of the inroads already made by capital.

Marx’s four drafts of his letter to Vera Zasulich have been of great interest to Japanese scholars of Marx. Shizumu Hinada18 argued that:

18 Hinada (1977). Marx was able to and had to theorize a general problem of ‘the village community in more belatedly started capitalistic countries under the capitalistic world system’, through the analysis of ‘commune russe’ and its environment as a whole from the angle not only of her happy contemporaneity but of her unhappiness which was made by the Western capitalist invasion.19



19 Hinada (1977), 74. For Hinada, Marx achieved a considerable degree of prescience:

Marx’s theory that ‘the landlord’s property vis-à-vis d’elle (de la commune rurale (= mir))’ promotes ‘le mouvement general ‘should be regarded as a merit in ‘the first draft’, and it grasps intuitively the necessity of the movement for the ‘Chornyi peredel’,20 recognizing in fact the actual situation of ‘mir’, which was provided by Russian ‘primitive accumulation of capital’ in the second half of the nineteenth century.

20 Chornyi Peredel (Black Partition) – also Party of Socialists and Federalists, a revolutionary populist organisation in Russia in the early 1880s. The Black Partition was formed in August and September 1879 after a split in the secret society Land and Liberty. Its name is connected with the rumor, widespread among the peasants, of an imminent partition of the land among the common people (chern). They favoured large-scale agitation and propaganda among the masses. The organisers of the Central Circle of the Black Partition in St Petersburg included G.V. Plekhanov, B. Aksel’rod, and V.I. Zasulich. The circle set up a press, which published the journal Chornyi peredel and the newspaper Zerno and established ties with students and workers; Great Soviet Encylopedia, http://encyclopedia2.thefreedictionary.com/Chernyi+Peredel (accessed 14 October 2012). In real Russian history, such a ‘le mouvement general’ as had been expected by Marx was realized in ‘the whole village agreement movement (prigovor)’, which was initiated by peasant-workers in the revolutionary summer–autumn of 1905, and in ‘the acquisition movement for the authority of the county committee’ in the revolutionary autumn of 1917.



There is another attempt to theorise Russia that is worth serious attention.

In a splendid Gramscian analysis of Russian politics in 1995, following the August 1991 putsch and the collapse of the USSR in November 1991, Jeremy Lester explained the ‘hegemonic combatants’ struggling for the future of Russia.21

21 Lester (1995). It would be a mistake to think that Gramsci originated the concept of hegemony. Lester showed, as had Perry Anderson,22 that first Georgiy Plekhanov and then Vladimir Lenin had found the concept to be of ‘great strategic significance’.23 But whereas for Lenin hegemony must be defined only in class terms, for Gramsci the ‘terrain of hegemonic struggle was considerably broader’.24

22 Anderson (1977). 23 Lester (1995), 3. 24 Lester (1995), 4. The two main combatants, according to Lester, were the Westerners and Russophiles. For the Westerners, the West personified progress and development and was the one means of genuinely overcoming the more reactionary elements of Russia’s past.25 According to Lester, this was the heart of the Westerners’ collective will, ‘that common mode of understanding which for Gramsci was the essential prerequisite to the attainment of a cultural-social unity through which a multiplicity of heterogeneous aims could be welded together’.26 He added that the dominant theme at the core of the Westerners’ hegemonic strategy was the emphasis on individualism or rather possessive individualism.27

25 Lester (1995), 86. 26 Lester (1995), 86. 27 Lester (1995), 92. This was not a current of thought emerging at the end of the twentieth century; its roots were to be found in the middle of the nineteenth century. Lester referred to Andrzej Walicki’s classic A history of Russian thought: from the enlightenment to Marxism,28 Chapter 8 of which, ‘Belinsky and different variants of Westernism’, explained how, in contrast to the Slavophiles, the ‘Westernisers’ ‘did not form a homogeneous movement with a single cohesive ideology and social philosophy … Westernism was only a loose alliance of potentially divergent trends, a platform where democrats and liberals in the 1840s found common ground in their opposition to Slavophilism.’29

28 Walicki (1979). 29 Walicki (1979), 135. According to Lester, ‘the contemporary Russophiles owe their revived intellectual prominence largely to the anti-liberal, anti-Western backlash that set in after 1968.’30 He described a set of fragmented and very general self-conceptions, trying to give concrete and specific meanings to these conceptions: Russophilia as a counterhegemonic project, identified even before the collapse of the USSR, as ‘a rallying point of counter-reform’.31 The collective will of the Russophiles combines the theme of Russia’s distinctiveness (samobytnost), combined with a virulent anti-Westernism. It brings together the concepts of the unique Russian narod or people, of pochvennost, the intimate bond with the soil of the Russian people and the old Orthodox insistence on sobornost, as Lester explained it ‘the organic synthesis of multiplicity and unity, the yearning for a unified collective instead of the atomised individual’. The roots of the word sobornost are to be found in the noun sobor (council) and the verb sobirat (to collect, connect, unite).

30 Lester (1995), 128. 31 Yanov (1988). The progenitors of these Russophiles were also to be found in the nineteenth century, when they were known as ‘Slavophiles’, described by Walicki as ‘a group of ideologists belonging to the conservative nobility, whose outlook became formed in the late 1830s in opposition to the trend known as Westernism’.32 This was not so much solidarity with brother Slavs (Serbs etc.), as ‘a cultivation of the native and primarily Slavic elements in the social life and culture of ancient Russia’.33 They also focused on Orthodox Christianity (which early Russia had borrowed as one piece from Byzantium) and its principle of sobornost or conciliarism, a free unity of believers. They were profoundly opposed to private property and possessive individualism and found, in parallel with Karl Marx as I showed earlier, inspiration in the ancient Russian village commune or obshchina, founded on the common use of land, mutual agreement and community of custom, governed by the mir, the council of elders that made decisions on the basis of unanimity and settled disputes in accordance with hallowed custom.34

32 Walicki (1979), 92. 33 Walicki (1979), 92. 34 Walicki (1979), 96. And it is no surprise that many of the leading legal philosophers of Russia have been motivated by religious concerns. One of the most influential, to this day, was Vladimir Solovyov (1853–1900), who worked out a complete philosophical system, based on the German idealist philosophers. His objective was the unification of all Christians, to be followed by a Messianic Kingdom of God on earth, with political motivation under the Russian Tsar. The young intellectual historian Konstantin Alekseev, who has specialised in the thought of Solovyov,35 concluded that Sovolyov, synthesised ideas of personal freedom, especially freedom of conscience, with principles of social justice, under the positive guidance of Christian religion.36 This led him, for Alekseev, to a European type of conservatism, which while conserving tradition did not contradict democracy or socialism.

35 Alekseev (2007). 36 Alekseev (2007), 131. He was a strong opponent of the leading conservative Pobedonostsev, whose views he considered to be dangerous.37 And Andrzej Walicki pointed out that Solovyov ‘attacked all forms of nationalism, rejected the Slavophile idealisation of Orthodox Christianity, and condemned the persecution of national and religious minorities’.38 These ‘hegemonic combatants’, infused on both sides with religion, thus had their roots in the nineteenth century. But much older in Russia is the tradition of Messianism, the Russians as the chosen people.

37 Alekseev (2007), 132. 38 Walicki (1979), 373. In 2000 Peter Duncan provided a definitive account of Messianism covering the whole of Russian history from 1511.39 According to Duncan:

39 Duncan (2000). Analytically, Russian messianism can be given the status of a ‘national myth’; in the taxonomy of George Schöpflin, it is both a myth of redemption and suffering and a myth of election.40 Paradoxically, the strength of this national myth has militated against the development of the nation … In contrast to the empires centred in Western Europe, neither in the Russian Empire nor in the Soviet Union did the ethnic Russians gain from being the principal building block of the state.41



40 Schöpflin (1997). 41 Duncan (2000), 3. Therefore, we must go back to the very beginning of Russia.

The first green shoots of Russia emerged in the ninth century AD, in a mix of Vikings (Varangians) and Slavs, protected by a Turkic semi-nomadic aristocracy, living on the river Dnieper and centred on Kiev.42 In 980 AD Prince Vladimir (later Saint Vladimir) inherited the crown of Rus, thanks to the help of an army recruited in Sweden. Vladimir embraced Christianity and allied himself with Byzantium, marrying the sister of the Byzantine emperor, Anna, in 988 AD. He expanded Kievan Rus with successful campaigns against the Poles, Bulgars and Pechenegs and decreed the conversion of Novgorod and Kiev to Orthodox Christianity (the ‘Baptism of Russia’). According to the chronicles, ‘Vladimir sent emissaries to inquire into the teaching and rituals of Islam, Judaism, Catholicism and Orthodoxy. They reported that the Catholic ritual was without beauty and that Islam did not permit the consumption of alcohol … Judaism they passed over in silence, but the Orthodox divine service they described as being so beautiful that “we knew not whether we were in heaven or on earth”.’43

42 Hosking (2001), 29–33. 43 Hosking (2001), 38. On 31 May 1223 (in the reign of Henry III, the Plantagenet king of England), the army of Kievan Rus was defeated by the Mongol armies of Chingiz Khan at the battle of the Kalka river, in present-day eastern Ukraine. In 1240 the Mongols sacked Kiev and established control over the whole of the territory of Russia for more than 200 years, not so much occupying the territory, as receiving tribute and relying on divisions between the Russian principalities. The hitherto insignificant settlement of Moscow grew in power and became a separate principality in 1263.

Moscow became the spiritual centre of Russian Orthodoxy. In 1299 Metropolitan Petr chose Moscow as the location of the see of all Rus.44 Petr was canonised a year after 1326, the year of his death, ‘in a ceremony designed to make Moscow the lasting centre of the Orthodox Church in Rus. His tomb became a shrine for all Orthodox believers and greatly enhanced the standing of the city.’45 From this symbolically significant moment, the principality of Moscow began to expand in all directions.

44 Hosking (2001), 72. 45 See Ostrowski (1993), 83–101; see also Fennell (1995), 134–36. Soon Moscow could challenge the Mongols. On 8 September 1380 an army commanded by the Prince of Moscow, Dmitriy I (1359–1389), defeated the Golden Horde at the battle of Kulikovo (near Tula, south of Moscow). However, Mongol rule in Russia did not end until 1480, at the ‘Great Standing on the Ugra River’, a standoff between Akhmat Khan of the Golden Horde and Grand Duke Ivan III of Russia.

Ivan III (1440–1505 – the reign of Henry VI in England) was the first ruler of Moscow to proclaim himself first ‘grand duke of all the Russias’ and, increasingly, Tsar of Russia. With the fall of Constantinople in 1453, there was a growing tendency to refer to Moscow as the ‘Third Rome’.

In 1510 (the reign of Henry VIII of England) the Russian Orthodox monk Filofei composed the panegyric letter to Tsar Vasiliy III (1505–1533) in which he warned:

And now I say unto Thee, take care and take heed, pious Tsar: all the empires of Christendom are united in thine. For two Romes have fallen, and the Third exists and there will not be a fourth. Thy Christian Empire, according to the great theologian, will not pass away.46



46 Andreyev (1959). Hosking writes: ‘Moscow thus became symbolically both the “Third Rome” and the “Second Jerusalem”, inheritor of both the Roman Empire and the Christian Church.’47

47 Hosking (2001), 107. The strong bedrock of Messianism in Russian identity and ideology thus provided support for the Slavophiles of the nineteenth century. Olga Malinova has written extensively on the question of Russian national identity.48 She has in mind ‘the West’ as a ‘constructed other’ of Russia. In her view, for most of the nineteenth century, a central factor in the complex of problems whose working out formed various ‘ideas of the nation’ was the question of preserving national ‘originality’ in the situation of ‘catching up modernisation’.49

48 Malinova (2005); (2005–06); (2007); (2009). 49 Malinova (2005), 39. As Duncan notes, the classic statement of Russian universalist Messianism was made by Dostoevsky in his ‘Pushkin speech’ of June 1880, which was acclaimed by Westernisers and Slavophiles. Here is Duncan’s own translation:50

50 Duncan (2000), 40, Dnevnik pisatelia za 1877 god (Paris: YMCA Press), 526; there is a recent English translation by Kenneth Lange, F.M. Dostoevsky Writer’s diary: 1873–1876 (Evanston, IL: Northwestern University Press, 2008) Yes, the Russian’s destiny is incontestably pan-European and universal. To become a true Russian, to become completely Russian, perhaps means only (in the last analysis, underline this) to become a brother to all people, a universal person if you like. Oh, all this Slavophilism and Westernism of ours is a great misunderstanding with us, although historically inevitable. For a true Russian, Europe and the fate of the whole great Aryan race are as dear as even Russia herself, as her native land, because her fate is universality, acquired not by the sword but by the force of brotherhood and our brotherly striving for the reunification of people … Oh, the peoples of Europe have no idea how dear they are to us! And later, I believe that we, that is of course not we but the future Russians, will understand to a person that to become a true Russian will mean precisely this: to strive finally to bring reconciliation to the European contradictions, to show the way out of European despair in our own Russian soul, pan-human and all-uniting, to embrace in it with brotherly love all our brothers, and finally, perhaps, to utter the final word of great, general harmony, of the final brotherly accord of all races according to Christ’s Gospel law.



The human geographer Alastair Bonnett has described the mindset of the Slavophiles thus:

To a degree unique among ‘other major European’ nations, the assumption that ‘European civilisation’ was inherently superior to all others, or even a meaningful category, was actively contested. Russia’s defeat by Britain, France and Turkey during the Crimean War (1853–6), combined with a persistent unease at the prospect of Russia ever really being accepted as fully European, encouraged those voices that condemned Westernisation as the spirit of alienation, materialism and superficiality. Throughout the mid and late 19th century, Slavophile and pan-Slavic critics poured scorn on the empty and instrumental world of the Occident.51



51 Bonnett (2002), 444. This was the fertile ground on which the notion of ‘Eurasianism’ emerged in the post-1917 context of the White emigration.

The philologist and ethnographer Count Nikolai Trubetskoy (1890–1938) wrote his key work Yevropa i chelovechestvo (Europe and humanity)52 in 1920 (it was published in Sofia) and inspired a group of authors to publish Iskhod k Vostoku (Exodus to the East) in 1921.53 He argued that no European state could be compared with Russia, since Russia is not a nation in the ordinary sense of the word, but a whole continent – Eurasia. ‘Turkic blood mingles in Russian veins with that of the Ugro-Finns and the Slavs,’ he wrote, and referred to Russia’s ‘non-European, half-Asiatic face’. As Mark Bassin points out, he insisted that Russia’s ‘existence as an empire was a thing of the past; Russians now represented just another of the constituent “ethnographic” groups which collectively comprised Eurasia’s multi-cultural complexion’.54

52 Trubetskoy (1995), 55–104. 53 Luks (1996), 58–9. 54 Bassin (2001), 3. ‘Eurasia’ is not only an anti-Western discourse; it also provides the space for an accommodation between Russian Orthodox and Muslim traditions. A. Agadjanian notes that:

Still another cultural framework is Eurasia, a notion of the old continental cultural space of tradition opposed to the ‘Atlantic civilisation’ of modernity. Also, Eurasia is the place of Christianity’s encounter with Islam. While the usual stereotyped attitude towards Islam is unfavourable, in the public discourse Christianity and Islam are sometimes drawn together. Russia is a unique combined Eurasian civilisation of Orthodox Slavs and Muslim Turks and, therefore, the ‘Western democratic standards cannot be fully transferred into Russian reality.’ Thus, V. Polosin, a former Orthodox priest converted to Islam, has defended the idea of a ‘social alliance’ of the two religions to create a Eurasian identity.55



55 Agadjanian (2001), 360. Ilan Berman identifies a change of course by President Putin, in the direction of Eurasianism. He argues that: ‘Indeed, by all indications, the growing emphasis on geopolitics from all corners of the Russian political spectrum is rapidly elevating Eurasianism to the level of a mainstream ideology.’56 Berman catalogues the growing influence of the doctrine and its controversial guru, Aleksandr Dugin,57 on Russian official and policymakers, and points out that on 13 November 2000, Putin himself affirmed that ‘Russia has always conceived of itself as a Euro-Asian nation.’ This speech is to be found on the website of Dugin’s Eurasia Party.58 Putin’s creation of the Collective Rapid Deployment Force as a regional ‘rapid reaction force’ in May 2001 and of the Eurasian Economic Community on 1 June 2001 can be seen as a concretisation of Eurasianist concepts.

56 Berman (2001); see also Berman(2002). 57 Dugin is a former member of the radical anti-Semitic Pamyatmovement and later of the racist Conservative Revolution, and has close links with the ‘national-Bolshevik’ Eduard Limonov. See also Kullberg (2001) and Yasmann (2001). 58 Putin (2000). On 21 June 2002, Dugin’s Eurasian Party was registered.59 The co-founders were significant: the Supreme Mufti of Russia and the Chairman of the Central Muslim Religious Board, Talgat Tadjuddin. The ideology of the new party was encapsulated by Aleksandr Panarin’s ‘History’s revenge’, which, in Gordon Hahn’s words ‘incorporates strains of Russia’s political culture: anti-Westernism, Messianism, Prometheanism, and anti-rationalism’.60 Thus, in the view of its founders: ‘It is uniquely poised to organise an alternative to the techno-economic globalisation threatening global ecology. Since Russia’s Orthodox civilisation is the only one with affinity to the Buddhist, Confucian and Islamic civilisations, only it can gather them together to counter the expansion of environmental and cultural pollution and poverty to countries left behind in globalisation.’

59 See http://www.eurasia.com.ru/english.html. 60 Hahn (2002). Dmitry Shlapentokh has concluded that:

Eurasianism belongs to currents of thinking which took their roots in nineteenth century Russia: the ambiguousness of relation to the Byzantine Empire, conservative pan-Slavism, the ‘yellow peril’ myth at the time of progression towards the Far East, Literary pan-Mongolism and Asiatism at the beginning of the century, movements of the Conservative Revolution in the 1920s, etc. Eurasianism rephrases in its own point of view, often in ways disconcerting to western thought, the Russian intellectuals’ questioning of the foundations of the identity of their country and of their own identity.61



61 Shlapentokh (2007), 193–94. As I show in the final chapter of this book, the ideology of the Putin regime and its impact on law and on human rights have their roots in the ideological currents explored in this chapter. In the next chapter, I focus on the very beginnings of law as an academic discipline in Russia.





Chapter 2

The Scottish Enlightenment in the Russian Empire




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-3

This chapter starts at the beginning; that is, the beginning of law as an academic discipline in Russia, with the first Russian professor of law and the first to teach in Russian, Semyon Yefimovich Desnitskiy (1740–1789). Desnitskiy studied for six years at Glasgow University, under Adam Smith. His influence in Russia was first as the victor in a successful battle against German abstract natural law, which had until then dominated the theory and teaching of law in Russia, in favour of a characteristically materialist, historical, sociological method taken from the Scottish Enlightenment. Second, following the Pugachev Rebellion (1773–1775) and the Empress Catherine II’s disillusionment with the French encyclopedists, she turned with satisfaction to Desnitskiy’s translation and commentaries on the first volume of William Blackstone’s 1766 Commentaries on the laws of England, which Desnitskiy annotated.

A recent textbook on the history of legal thought in Russia1 identifies Desntiskiy as ‘one of the first representatives of moderate liberalism in Russia’.2

1 Antonov (2012). 2 Antonov (2012), 55. Russia had a long tradition of sending its brightest young people to study abroad. According to David Saunders, Boris Gudonov, in 1602, may have been the first to do this.3 Peter the Great was well aware of the importance of the scientific revolution of Western Europe in the seventeenth century and despatched students abroad as well as appointing foreign tutors to run training establishments in Russia. Thus, an imperial ukase in January 1701 appointed Henry Farquharson head of the newly established Moscow Mathematics and Navigation School and in 1716 he became the Professor of Mathematics at its successor, the Naval Academy, ‘Russia’s first serious scientific centre’.4 And the great Mikhail Lomonosov (1711–1765), who founded Moscow University in 1755, was sent in 1736 to study in Marburg under Christian Wolff, the German Pietist philosopher. He remained there until 1741 and on his return became, in 1745, Professor of Chemistry in the Academy of Sciences in St Petersburg.5

3 Saunders (2010), 32. 4 Jones (2010), 75. 5 Jones (2010), 79–80. But it was the German princess who became Catherine the Great (1729–1796) who from the start of her reign in 1762 is considered by many to have initiated the Russian Enlightenment and who was responsible for the origins of law as an academic discipline in Russia. In 1767 she convened the Legislative Commission to which she presented her Nakaz (Instruction), which was intended to replace the 1649 Sobornoye ulozheniye with a modern legal code. No fewer than 294 of 526 articles in Part I of her Nakaz were drawn from Montesquieu’s 1748 Spirit of the laws, which she read along with the work of Beccaria (On crimes and punishments), d’Alembert and François Quenay’s Natural law.6 For 15 years, until 1778, when he died, she corresponded with Voltaire; and from 1773 to 1775 she invited Denis Diderot, the great French materialist and determinist, to her court in St Petersburg.

6 Jones (2010), 90. The Legislative Commission failed to produce the hoped-for reform of Russia’s laws.

But the focus of this chapter is the first Russian Professor of Law, Semyon Yefimovich Desnitskiy (1740–1789), who received his Doctor of Laws degree following six years, from 1761 to 1767, studying at the University of Glasgow.7 Walicki points out that he was also the first theoretical jurist in Russia.8

7 This chapter draws especially from Korkunov (1905), Brown (1969, 1975, 1977) and Mernikova (2000). 8 Walicki (1979), 12. Sadly, Desnitskiy’s archive was entirely destroyed in the great fire of 1812, when Moscow was destroyed by its inhabitants in the face of Napoleon. However, the historian and political scientist Archie Brown, who taught from 1964 to 1971 at the University of Glasgow, spent the years 1967–1968 at Moscow State University as a British Council exchange student and, as he pointed out in an article published in 1969 in Russian in the Moscow University Bulletin, he was able to draw together: (1) the intellectual milieu in Glasgow and the character of the lectures which he and his fellow Russian student I.A. Tretyakov attended; (2) some aspects of the student life of the two Russian students; and (3) documents now available throwing light on their published works.9

9 Brown (1969), 75. Moscow University was the first university in Russia; for comparison, the University of Glasgow was founded in 1451, the University of Bologna in 1088 and teaching began in Oxford in 1096. Lomonosov believed that the new university should include a gymnasium, or preparatory school, to prepare qualified students for admission: ‘A university without a gymnasium is like a ploughed field without seeds.’ For the university, he proposed three fakultety (departments) with a total of 12 professors: six in a philosophical department (philosophy, physics, eloquence, poetry, history and antiquities), three in a medical department (chemistry, natural history and anatomy), and three in law (general jurisprudence, Russian jurisprudence and politics). Ivan Shuvalov, the philanthropist, who had the ear of the Empress Elizabeth, agreed to only 10, combining philosophy with physics and eloquence with poetry.

Shuvalov submitted a report to the Senate and, on 12 January 1755, Elizabeth signed the decree setting up Moscow University and naming Shuvalov and Blumentrost10 as the first curators. Aleksei Argamakov, the son of one of Peter the Great’s aides who had studied abroad, became the first director of the gymnasium, which formally opened on 26 April.

10 Lavrentiy Blumentrost, the Tsar’s court physician, who became the first President of the Imperial Russian Academy of Sciences in 1724. Finding 10 professors for the new university was difficult. The university had several options. A student could finish the university with a master’s degree, begin teaching in the gymnasium and work his way up to university level, as did Professor Khariton Chebotarev, who taught history. Another method was for students to study abroad, as Lomonosov had done. On Shuvalov’s orders, in 1758 nine of the first gymnasium students were sent abroad: Danil Iastrebov, Ivan Svishchov, Petr Veniaminov and Semen Zybelin went to Königsberg and Leiden to study medicine and the natural sciences; Matvei Afonin and Petr Karamyshev travelled to Uppsala for metallurgy, botany and chemistry; and (two years later) Semyon Desnitskiy and Ivan Tretiakov went to Glasgow to study law.

It is not known exactly why Scotland was chosen in addition to Germany, the Netherlands and Sweden, but Archie Brown points out that there were strong links between Scotland and Russia in the eighteenth century. A great many doctors, soldiers, engineers, architects and diplomats settled in Russia. Three physicians in succession to the Empress of Russia (Elizabeth then Catherine), James Mounsey, Matthew Halliday and John Rogerson, all came from Dumfriesshire. The Russian educated public knew the work of William Roberston, David Hume, Adam Ferguson and Adam Smith (in French translation); and Desnitskiy himself was especially responsible for introducing Russian readers to the Scottish Enlightenment.11

11 Brown (1975), 248. Shuvalov found two of the first 10 professors from the Academy of Sciences. For the rest, he turned to Europe and located Johann Fromann, Johann Shaden, and Philipp Dilthey. Fromann came from the University of Tübingen and taught logic until his return to Germany in 1765.12 Shaden, also from Tübingen, was the rector of the gymnasium and taught rhetoric and logic. Only in 1772 did he begin to teach natural law and politics. For the first 10 years of the university, Dilthey was the only law professor.13

12 Information in this and preceding paragraphs from http://novaonline.nvcc.edu/eli/evans/his241/Documents/Dissertation/Chapter1.pdf (accessed 17 October 2012). 13 Korkunov (1905), 283. Thus, the first teacher of law (and for five years the only teacher of law) at this new university was Philip Heinrich Dilthey, born in the Tirol, who was awarded his doctorate in law at Vienna University in 1753 and was invited to Moscow in September 1756. In October 1756 he began his lectures with a presentation on ‘On the need for and use of law’. He also taught private lectures, in French, on natural law. Students each paid 12 roubles to attend these lectures, which must have been successful, because in 1760 Dilthey bought a house in Moscow for 1500 roubles. However, Dilthey’s colleagues felt he was spending too much time on private lessons, in order to make money. According to the catalogue of lectures for 1757, Dilthey taught natural law, Roman law, feudal law, criminal law and state law, in four languages, according to the choice of his listeners. It is highly likely, therefore, that Dilthey was Desnitskiy’s teacher at Moscow University, before Desnitskiy departed for Glasgow.

But his main subject matter was natural law, taught through the works of the German professors Benedikt Winkler (1579–1648), Samuel Pufendorf (1632–1694) and Daniel Nettelbladt (1719–1791).

In late 1761 the university dealt with a dispute between Dilthey, who was the gymnasium inspector at the time, and Boyer de Roquet, a French teacher. Roquet complained about Dilthey’s ‘inaccurate’ reports on his teaching ability and added that Dilthey, ‘while drunk’, had assaulted him in a tavern. The conference listened to the complaint and allowed Dilthey a response in ‘which he, instead of refuting the accusations against him, spoke in gross and insulting terms against the accuser and accused the conference of partiality’. Eventually, the university removed Dilthey from his position as inspector.

Dilthey was dismissed in 1765, during Desnitskiy’s absence in Glasgow. He was accused of failure to attend his lectures and often appearing in an intoxicated state and behaving in a manner unbecoming. However, on 8 March 1766 he was reinstated by handwritten decree of Catherine herself.14 He continued to teach as a professor at Moscow University until his death in 1781.

14 Korkunov (1905), 283. Much of Desnitskiy’s writing and teaching were directed against the German natural law school represented by Dilthey and I turn to the strong influence of Adam Smith and the Scottish Enlightenment later in this chapter.

Desnitskiy’s own history was as follows.

He was not a member of the land-owning class in Russia. The year of his birth is not known, but his place of birth is known to have been Nezhin in what is now Ukraine.15 He was the second son of a meshchanin (petit-bourgeois – an artisan or similar). He was educated first at the Troitsko-Sergievsky monastery, founded about 1345, in Sergiev Posad, north of Moscow, the centre of Russian Orthodoxy, and then the ‘gymnasium’ (secondary school), attached to Moscow University, which educated children of the raznochintsyi (those of non-noble birth – noble children had their own gymnasium).16 In 1760 he entered the philosophical faculty of Moscow University itself, five years after its foundation (all students were obliged to study philosophy first). At that time many students came from poor families, as Mikhail Lomonosov had intended. As I have pointed out earlier, Dilthey began to teach in Moscow in 1756 and was then the only law lecturer.

15 The city, one of the oldest in Ukraine, is now called Nizhyn, with a population of some 70,000. In the nineteenth century it was a centre of Jewish settlement, with a 25 per cent Jewish population. 16 Brown (1975), 249. From there, Desnitskiy was sent to the Academy of Sciences in St Petersburg and, on the instruction of Shuvalov, he was then sent to study at the University of Glasgow.

Desnitskiy lived in Glasgow from 1761 to 1767. In 1765 he was awarded a Master of Arts and in 1767 he successfully defended his thesis and became a Doctor of Civil and Church Law. According to the entry by Vladislav Kalmykov in the Large biographical encyclopedia (repeated elsewhere), he also received the special award of the privileges of British citizenship, although he remained a Russian subject.17 He studied Roman law and took the course on moral philosophy with Adam Smith (1723–1790). He and his fellow student I.A. Tretyakov were the first foreigners to be awarded a doctorate by the University of Glasgow.

17 Website:http://dic.academic.ru/dic.nsf/enc_biography/1993/ Десницкий. The passage reads: ‘vmeste s chem, yemu byli pozhalovany privilegii anglisskovo grazhdanstvo, zvaniya, osobenno pochetnovo dlya inostrantsa (in addition to [the doctorate] he was awarded the privileges of English citizenship, a rank especially honourable for a foreigner)’. Alexander Broadie noted, in the context of the Scottish Enlightenment, that chairs of law were not founded in the Scottish universities until the eighteenth century.18 At Glasgow University the chair of civil law was founded in 1713 and filled in 1714. Broadie argued that:

18 Broadie (1997), 11. The intellectual and moral strength of Scottish law, as represented especially by the works of Stair and Mackenzie, was no doubt one explanation of the provision for the continued separate identity of Scots law that was written into the 1707 Acts of Union.19



19 Broadie (1997), 12. Adam Smith became a professor at Glasgow in 1751 and was in post until 1764, when he left to become travelling tutor to the young Duke of Buccleuch. Brown has found proof positive that Desnitskiy and Tretyakov attended Smith’s lectures: a letter written by Desnitskiy to the Dean and members of the Faculty of Glasgow University on 31 December 1765, with a request to for him and Tretyakov be accepted as candidates for the degree of Doctor of Law. The letter contained the following:

We humbly request … that the Faculty would take into Consideration, whether our attendance on Dr Smith’s class of Ethicks and Jurisprudence and our attendance for three years on Mr Millar’s classes of civil law may procure us a Liberty of offering ourselves candidates for a degree in Law.20



20 Brown (1975), 252. Broadie’s Anthology includes Adam Smith’s lectures of 21 and 22 March 1763, On jurisprudence, in which he considered the duties of the sovereign.21 In his lecture of 22 March 1763, Smith started with a thorough critique of Locke and of the ‘voluntary contract’ between the people and the sovereign power. He declared: ‘Besides, this doctrine of obedience as founded on contract is confined to Britain and has never been heard of in any other country … and even here it can have influence with a very small part of the people, such as have read Locke!’22 Furthermore, the present sovereign could not be bound by a promise made by a predecessor: ‘The contract of ones predecessors never binds one merely because it was his.’23 For Smith, the principle or duty of allegiance was founded on two principles: first, the principle of authority and, second, the principle of common or general interest. For the first, Smith asserted that:

21 Broadie (1997), 617–27; from Meek et al. (1978), 311–21. 22 At Broadie (1997), 622–23. 23 Broadie (1997), 623. [E]very one naturally has a disposition to respect an established authority and superiority in others, whatever they be. The young respect the old, children respect their parents, and in general the weak respect those who excel in power and strength.24



24 Broadie (1997), 624. As to the second principle, Smith declared that:

[E]veryone sees that the magistrates not only support the government in generall but the security and independency of each individual, and they see that this security can not be attained without a regular government.25



25 Broadie (1997), 625. For Smith, the principle of authority ‘is that which chiefly prevails in a monarchy’. People are motivated to obey by respect and deference to the monarchy, the idea that there is ‘a sort of sinfulness or impiety in disobedience’ and the duty they owe to the monarch.26 In a republic, the principle of utility is the basis for obedience.

26 Broadie (1997), 625. Smith’s thoughts on the British system must also have impressed Desnitskiy. According to Smith, ‘the sovereign power is partly entrusted to the king, partly to the people and partly to the nobles.’ Thus, the principle of authority is engaged with regard to king and nobles: ‘But as the government is in great part democratic, by the influence of the House of Commons, the principle of utility is also found in it.’

This led him to a fascinating distinction between Whigs and Tories. For the Tories, the principle of authority is paramount, albeit thoroughly confused by ideas of contract. The Whigs, too, are confused by the notion of a contract, although they insist that since the government was established not for the benefit of the ruler but the people, the government has no authority other than that derived from the people. Thus, when power is abused, the people have a right to resist. These principles affect ‘people of different cast’. Whigs are ‘bustling, spirited, active folk, who can’t brook oppression and are constantly endeavouring to advance themselves’, whereas Tories are ‘calm, contented folks of no great spirit and abundant fortunes which they want to enjoy at their own ease, and don’t want to be disturbed or disturb others’.27

27 Broadie (1997), 626. Finally, Smith insisted on the right of resistance. ‘Absurdity and impropriety of conduct and great perverseness destroy obedience, whether it be due from authority or the sense of the common good.’28

28 Broadie (1997), 627. Broadie comments that there is one point stressed by Smith in The wealth of nations but not present in the lectures, which is the need to keep the power of the judiciary out of the hands of the executive. The liberty of the individual depends on the impartial administration of justice. Smith held that:

The judge should not be liable to be removed from his office according to the caprice of that [executive] power. The regular payment of his salary should not depend upon the good-will, or even upon the good economy of that power.29



29 Smith (1976), 723; Broadie (1997), 616 On Desnitskiy return in 1767 to Moscow, the Curator of the University refused to recognise the validity of the Glasgow doctorate, and insisted that Desnitskiy sit an examination to give him the right to lecture. According to N. Mernikova, the results of the examination attracted the attention of Catherine the Great.30 It was clear that Desnitskiy understood more about Roman law than his examiners.

30 Mernikova (2000). Having passed the examination, Desnitskiy was given the right to teach a course on Roman law in relation to Russian law. At that time these lectures were given in Latin; this was especially convenient for foreign professors who could not lecture in Russian. Desnitskiy asked to be permitted to lecture in Russian, but this was opposed by the university. The issue was resolved by Catherine herself, who decreed that it would be more proper to teach in Russian, especially for jurisprudence. This was a decisive moment for Russian higher education.31 In 1768 Desnitskiy was granted the title of ‘extraordinary professor’ and six years later, in 1774, he became an ‘ordinary professor’.

31 Korkunov (1915), 288. The content of Desnitskiy’s lectures is known from only two sources: his official lectures (aktoviye rechi) and the notes he wrote for his translations of the first volume of Blackstone, which appeared in three instalments, in 1780, 1781 and 1782.32

32 Korkunov (1915), 289; I am indebted to Archie Brown for the result of his own research, that, contrary to the views of Russian commentators and also of Walicki, Desnitskiy translated only the first volume of Blackstone, albeit published in Russia in three instalments. On 30 June 1768 Desnitskiy delivered his first address to the law faculty’s academic council – and the general public. This was entitled ‘On the direct and immediate means of teaching jurisprudence’ (Slovo o pryamom i blizhaishem sposob k izucheniyu iurisprudentsiy) – Brown33 translates it as the ‘Best means of teaching jurisprudence’. This was a long lecture indeed, 51 pages in its original published version and was delivered in honour of the sixth anniversary of the accession of Catherine to the throne. Many passages in the lecture were drawn verbatim from Adam Smith’s lecture, which Desnitskiy had attended. For example, having paid tribute to the richness of the British philosophical tradition, Desnitskiy continued:

33 Desnitskiy (1768). From hence have appeared the renowned Hobbes, Cumberland, Mandeville, Locke, Berkeley, Bolingbroke, Sidney, Harrington, and the more recent Hutcheson, David Hume and Mr. Smith; and of the last two names one has published his metaphysics and the other his moral philosophy, to the great pleasure of the scholarly world. In my opinion, if I am not mistaken, the moral philosophy of Mr. Smith is more closely connected with natural jurisprudence than are all the other systems of this science.34



34 Brown (1975), 268. This text became the programme for the scientific study of law and of law teaching in the Russian universities. It is plain also that he was engaged in throwing down a challenge to the German scholasticism, which at that time, through Dilthey, dominated the teaching of law in Russia.35

35 Walicki (1979), 12. Indeed, he launched a direct attack on Pufendorf, saying that he wrote about:

[T]he duties which man must observe in a state of nature and in civil society … The work of Pufendorf was really superfluous, for to write about imaginary conditions of mankind without showing how property, ownership, inheritance, etc. originate and are limited among peoples, is something which does not quite correspond with his intention and aim.36



36 Brown (1977), 125. Desnitskiy also displayed his knowledge of English legal history, drawing from Blackstone as to the situation in the eleventh to twelfth centuries, when the judges were mostly drawn from ecclesiastics and the monks taught Roman jurisprudence, and King Stephen (1096–1154) forbade by decree the reading and studying of Roman law37 – the monks declared that King Stephen was an atheist and an apostate. In a footnote, Desnitskiy added that for those who read Blackstone: ‘The monks in England in those days acted in every way no differently from the way the present-day German scholars in Russia act’ (emphasis in original).38

37 See Re (1961), 453. 38 Brown (1977), 126–27. Furthermore, having outlined ‘natural jurisprudence’, ‘Roman jurisprudence’ and ‘Russian jurisprudence’, Desnitsky concluded:

These in my judgment are the sciences necessary for the successful study of law in Russia … However, the German Doctors of law may invent as many systems of jurisprudence as they like.39



39 Brown (1977), 133. Desnitskiy’s enemy was the monopoly over the teaching of law in Moscow University that the German professors Dilthey and Langer (from 1765 to 1771) enjoyed, until the return from Scotland of Desnitskiy and Tretyakov. As noted already, in 1768 the Germans were ‘ordinary’ (full) professors, when Desnitskiy and Tretyakov were ‘extraordinary professors’. It can be safely assumed the question of teaching in Russian or in Latin was not the only point of conflict between them.40

40 Brown (1977), 127. Desnitskiy was also appointed one of the experts for the Legislative Commission (Ulozhennoi kommissiy) that worked from 1767 to 1768. In 1768 he wrote a ‘Proposal for establishing legislative, judicial and executive power in the Russian Empire (Predstavleniye o uchrezhdeniy zakonodatelnoi, sudutelnoy i nakazatelnoi vlasti v rossiysskoi imperiy)’,41 containing suggestions for political reform that reflect English and Scottish ideas and British constitutional practice, adapted in a highly original manner to Russian conditions.42 Many of Desnitskiy’s ideas and references found their way into Catherine’s Nakaz.

41 Available electronically at Desnitskiy (1988). 42 Brown (1975), 263. Desnitskiy was the first in Russia to propose the separation of powers. Walicki states:

His plan proposed that a permanent representative body known as the Senate should be elected every five years; formally this body was to have only advisory capacity, but there is no doubt that Desnitsky wanted to ensure some form of control and restriction of absolutism.43



43 Walicki (1979), 13. His proposal also included the reorganisation of the judiciary, including dispersal of courts throughout Russia to render justice more accessible, and the introduction of open trials, including trial by jury. He argued for the independence and irremovability of judges and for strict limits on the right of the monarch to appoint judges. As to executive power, this must be placed under judicial control, not going beyond the limits of judgments. At the head of the executive was the monarch, who appointed governors. The functions of the executive were criminal investigation, the fight against crime, maintenance of peace and quiet, upholding order in the towns, collection of taxes and excise and guarantee of fire and prison services.

And to prevent arbitrariness and abuse by the governors of regions and towns he proposed the establishment of regional courts, to ensure legality through special departments for receiving complaints. This was thus the first proposal in Russian history to subordinate the executive power to the judicial power.44

44 Mernikova (2000). As Walicki noted, a turning point in Catherine’s life was her suppression of the Pugachev Rebellion (1773–1775), which started as a peasant uprising led by Pugachev, a Don Cossack who promised a return to an imagined Russia pre-dating Peter the Great, but was joined by Urals metalworkers and Bashkir tribesmen. This was the greatest uprising in Russian history and threatened the existence of the Empire. Catherine now rejected both the theories of natural rights and her earlier Francophile enthusiasm for Voltaire and Diderot. In a vindication of Desnitskiy’s own project, she turned instead to William Blackstone’s sober conservatism. She now referred to her Nakaz as ‘idle chatter’. Her legislation was now intended to strengthen the position of the gentry. She returned to Russian nationalism and a study of Russian history.45

45 Walicki (1997), 6–7. In 1775 Desnitskiy published Juridical discourses on the principles and origins of marriage, in which he followed his characteristic method of analysing social phenomena as part of a historical, evolutionary process, the method he learned from Adam Smith and John Millar. This was a materialist methodology. He argued, using the exact terminology of Smith and Millar with regard to the four stages,46 that sexual relationships had also evolved: there were no institutionalised form of marriage in hunting society; polygamy was characteristic of the pastoral stage; in the agricultural stage, patriarchal monogamy ruled; and in the commercial stage, although still marked by monogamy, conditions were ripe for equal rights for women, a cause Desnitskiy supported wholeheartedly.47

46 See Brown (1975) – I am indebted to Archie Brown for this observation. 47 Walicki (1979), 13. In 1778 he delivered his Discourse on the benefit of knowledge of the legal art of the fatherland (Rasuzhdeniye o polze znaniye otechestvennovo zakonoiskusstva) in which once more he closely followed Blackstone as to the need for legal education, for gentlemen as much as for judges.

He applied the same method in 1781 in his Juridical discourse on the views of various nations concerning property and various forms of social relationship. Returning to the four stages of social evolution just noted, Desnitskiy wrote that private property only emerged during the third, agricultural stage, and ownership in the full meaning of the word could not develop fully until the commercial stage, when it replaced other forms of property.

Desnitsky’s final published work was Legal discourse on the concepts held by various peoples concerning property of estates in various forms of social relationships (Iuridicheskoye passuzhdeniye o raznikh ponyatiyakh, kakiye imeyut narody o sobstvennosti imeniya v razlichnikh sostoyaniyakh obshchezhitalstva), a lecture delivered in 1781.48 Entirely consistently with his work to date, he criticised the abstract theory of natural law, beloved of the German professors, and argued that instead of a focus on ‘imaginary conditions of the human race’, the scholar should rather study the historical genesis and evolution of ownership, property and inheritance. He distinguished, as before, between four stages of social evolution, based on economic criteria: the hunting, pastoral, agricultural and commercial stages.
