In this materialist account, private property only emerged during the agricultural stage and ownership (as the right to dispose freely of a given thing and to secure its return from any person who has taken unlawful possession of it) came to fruition in the commercial stage.49

48 See http://www.rosreferat.ru/economy/628.htm (accessed 18 October 2012). 49 Walicki (1979), 12. For more than 20 years Desnitskiy continued his scholarly, teaching and public activity. In 1787 he retired from his chair at the university, and died on 15 June 1789. The French Revolution had just begun. In May 1789 the Estates-General were convoked, in June the Third Estate proclaimed the Tennis Court Oath, in July the Bastille was stormed, on 26 August 1789 the Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen was adopted by the National Constituent Assembly and in October the march on Versailles forced the royal court back to Paris.

It could be said that he died just in time. The French Revolution was the definitive end of Catherine’s Russian Enlightenment. According to Walicki, the execution of the King of France on 21 January 1793 left her ‘stunned as by a hammer-blow’ and she had the busts of the encyclopedists removed from the hermitage, the last being Voltaire.50 She dealt very firmly with the two leading progressive thinkers of the Russian Enlightenment. In 1790 Aleksandr Radishchev (1749–1802, 10 years younger than Desnitskiy), author in that year of A journey from St Petersburg to Moscow, was exiled to Siberia and in 1792 Nikolai Novikov (1744–1818, four years younger than Desnitskiy), freemason and editor of satirical journals, was imprisoned without trial in the Schlüsselburg Fortress.

50 Walicki (1979), 8. Desnitskiy left not only an abiding influence on law as an academic discipline in Russia and the teaching of law to generations of students, but also helped to form a distinctive school of moderate reformism.

In his Legal philosophies of Russian liberalism, Andrzey Walicki, while comparing Radishchev with Thomas Paine, as typifying the ‘radical current of European Enlightenment – a current which managed to combine revolutionary radicalism with deep commitment to liberal-democratic values’, identified ‘a much less politically radical form of the liberal variant of the juridical world-view’, whose outstanding members were Ivan Pnin (1773–1805), author in 1804 of An essay on Enlightenment concerning Russia, and Vasily Popugaev (1778–1816), author of On the firmness of constitution and laws. In Walicki’s view, it was they who continued and developed some of Desnitskiy’s ideas – Walicki here described Desnitskiy as ‘the first theoretical jurist in Russia’.51

51 Walicki (1992), 24. This chapter has endeavoured to show that law and rights in Russia grow from a rich and varied history, in which Messianic ideology intermingles with many other schools of thought, primarily from Germany, but also from Scotland. Most legal scholars in the West are almost entirely unaware of the strength and creativity of Russian scholarship. The main cause is the absence of translation. I hope that my own access to texts in Russian will assist even if in a very small way in revealing the wealth of interest to be found in the history of Russian legal thought. In the next chapter, I move forward some 50 years, to perhaps the most significant landmark of pre-revolutionary Russia.





Chapter 3

The 1850s and 1860s in Russia: Revolutionary situation or great reforms?




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-4

In this chapter, I explain and analyse the great legal reforms of Aleksandr II. As I show, many of the legal and judicial reforms since 1991, under President Yeltsin and in the first years of President Putin, have been in effect a restoration of Aleksandr’s reforms.

The Crimean War ended in 1856; Aleksandr II had become Tsar in February 1855. The Empire was in a terrible state. It had no railroads and was crushed by an immense national debt and the huge expense of maintaining its vast army, quite apart from the debilitating effect of serfdom. One economic expert, L.V. Tengoberskij estimated that about 84 per cent of the expenditures of the Russian state in 1856 were used to pay for the military and the interest on the national debt.1

1 Lincoln (1975), 167, citing L.V. Tengoberskiy Ktatkoe izlozheniye nashevo finansovovo polozheniya (maia 1856) (A brief account of our financial situation, May 1856), Tsentralniy Gosudarstvanniy Istoricheskiy Arkhiv v Leningrad,958 (1), 742. The focus of this chapter is on the Great Legal Reforms of Aleksandr II in 1864. As a result of these reforms, the courts were separated from the administration; open and adversarial justice was introduced; a bar was created, guaranteeing the right of the accused to a defence; the system of ‘estates’ (soslovniy) courts was abolished; the presumption of innocence was entrenched; in place of the theory of formal proof there was free evaluation of evidence; minor criminal and civil cases were transferred to justices of the peace; even before 1864 the criminal investigators were removed from the police and became judicial investigators, attached to the courts; and the irremovability of judges and investigators was put in place.2

2 Kazantsev (1991), 4. How did this come about?

In 1856 Aleksandr II invited an assembly of nobles to Moscow to discuss the abolition of serfdom and initiated an extraordinarily open and dynamic debate. Between 1856 and 1863 some 33 new journals were founded every year and became forums for discussing economic and social change.3

3 Starr (1972), 57; cited in Lincoln (1975), 167, 178. The decisive moment was the abolition of serfdom on 19 February 1861.

The abolition of serfdom was followed by financial reform in 1862. This brought about a complete centralisation of financial affairs, with all expenditures made through the Ministry of Finance, and not each government department as previously. The reform of education followed in 1863, with a new ‘Charter of universities’, increasing the number of subjects taught and doubling the number of professors and lecturers and, in 1864, with the ‘Order on primary people’s colleges’ and the ‘Charter of gymnasiums and progymnasiums’. These statutes opened the possibility of opening schools for all who wanted them.

Prior to 1864, courts were organised on the soslovniy model: each estate had its own courts. For the nobles, every uyezd (district) had a uyezdniy court whose judge and two assessors were elected by the nobles for three years. The appellate instance was the supreme zemskiy court, one in each gubernia, made up of criminal and civil chambers. The chairman and vice-chairman of this court were appointed by the Tsar and assessors were elected by the nobles.

For town-dwellers, the lowest judicial instance was the ‘town magistrate’ and the appellate instance was the ‘gubernia magistrate’. These were elected for three years by the town-dwellers.

State peasants were judged by the uyezdnoi lower rasprave, with appeal to a ‘supreme’ rasprave. ‘Conscience courts’ (sovestniy sud) also existed, which could hear civil cases. These courts were also organised according to the ‘estates principle’, with each estate electing its own chairman and assessors. The judgments of all the higher courts could be appealed to the ‘judicial chamber’ and ultimately to the Senat in St Petersburg.4

4 Popova (2009), 22. Justice was performed by people with no legal education, which was required only of the court secretaries. Judicial procedure was archaic. There were no oral proceedings. The courts only read papers, the pleadings of the parties in civil cases and the records of examination of witnesses, accused and victim in criminal cases. The theory of formal proof was equally archaic, with the evidence of men considered more trustworthy than that of women and educated more than uneducated. The judicial system was riddled with bureaucracy and with corruption. Cases could continue for years. For example, a case of theft from the Moscow uyezd treasury of 115,000 pennies started in 1844 and finished in 1865, that is, it went on for 21 years.5

5 E.P. Tomachev (1998) Aleksandr II and his time (Moscow) Vol. 1, 252, cited by Popova (2009), 22–3. The roots of reform were to be found in the two Decembrist constitutional documents, which survived the unsuccessful uprising of 14 December 1825, Muraviev’s ‘Constitution’ and Pestel’s ‘Russkaya pravda (Russian truth)’. Both documents proposed the abolition of serfdom, the liquidation of the estates, the democratisation of the systems of government and politics and the introduction of parliamentary democracy.





Active debates in Russia


Although Nikolai I did his best to stifle dissent, the 1830s and 1840s were the time of the most active debates between Westernisers and Slavophiles. Both sides contained some liberal thinkers, who agreed on abolition of serfdom and widescale reform. The former, including B.N. Chicherin, K.D. Kavelin, T.N. Granovskiy and A.M. Unkovskiy, considered Russia to be a Western country like others, requiring Western democratic institutions: a parliament, local government and trial by jury. The latter, including A.I. Koshelev, Yu. F. Samarin, K.A. Aksakov and A.S. Khomyakov, considered Russia to be a unique country, requiring from the West only technical progress. Although they were for the abolition of serfdom, their ideal of governance was the Zemskiye sobory of the period of Ivan IV (the Terrible).6

6 Popova (2009), 30–32. Aleksandr II was no liberal, but understood the need for reform. In his first years as Tsar he lifted the ban on travel abroad, relaxed censorship and amnestied the Decembrists and many others who had been exiled. The liberals directed a whole series of proposals to the new Tsar: these included B.N. Chicherin’s ‘The eastern question from the Russian point of view’, N.A. Mengulov’s ‘Thoughts of the last thirteen years’, K.S. Aksakov’s ‘On the internal condition of Russia’, A.I. Koshelev’s ‘On monetary means in the present circumstances’ and P.A. Valuev’s ‘Russian thought in the second half of 1855’. In 1855 to 1857 alone 63 projects for various reforms were prepared and published.7 These nurtured a groundswell of support for reform in public opinion, and also had their effect in government. Aleksandr’s circle included hide-bound conservatives such as the Minister of Justice V.N. Panin, but others, such as the Tsar’s brother the Grand Duke Constantine, the Grand Duchess Yelena Pavlovna and the Miliutin brothers, also understood the need for reform.

7 Tolmachev (1998) Vol. 1 154, cited at Popova (2009), 32. In 1857 the Russkiy vestnik newspaper published the following words:

The present time is rich in new thoughts and new desires. The Russian mind awoke … How many vitally important questions were considered or simply posed in the last two years … We have not at all stopped thinking that our times will go down in history as the epoch of the most important transformations in Russia.8



8 Russkiye vestnik1857 No. 9 152, at Popova (2009), 33. Many newspapers and journals of the late 1850s published articles on reform of the judicial system, arguing that Russian judicial procedure must become oral and open, as in most European countries.9 S.I. Barshev, professor of criminal law at St Petersburg University, became one of the most outspoken supporters of reform, arguing that in Russian courts as in English courts it was essential to restrict the powers of the prosecution while giving wide possibilities for the defence.10 It was necessary to create a Russian bar, as the basis for an adversarial system that did not yet exist in Russia.

9 See, e.g., ‘On oral judicial procedure in Russia’ Russkiye vestnik 1857 Vol. 11, Col.1; S.I. Barshev, ‘Criminal court procedure’ Russkiye vestnik 1857 Vol. 10, Col. 2; Kh. D., ‘Some thoughts on judicial procedure’ Russkiye vestnik 1858 Vol. 16, Col. 2; A.A. Nalyumbetskiy, ‘The question of oral proceedings and of openness in courts’ Russkiye vestnik (1859) Vol. 16, Col. 2. 10 S.I. Barshev (1863) ‘On necessary guarantees in the criminal court’ Russkiye vestnik 1859 Vol. 20 Col. 2; S.I. Barshev, ‘Tasks of the jurors in criminal cases’ Russkiye vestnik Vol. 45, cited in Popova (2009).





Trial by jury in the Russian Empire


The apotheosis of these publications was the proposal to introduce jury trials. Only trial by jury could guarantee real independence and protect the court from miscarriages of justice, Barshev argued.11 Indeed, as S.M. Kazantsev pointed out, writing in the last year of Soviet power, in the opinion of contemporaries as well as researchers into the judicial reforms of 1864, the introduction of trial by jury was the most democratic of all the reforms of Aleksandr II.12 Even the leader of the reaction, and outright fighter against jury trial in the 1860s, M.N. Katkov, wrote that this was ‘not so much reform, as the creation of judicial power’.13 The first to propose the introduction of jury trial in Russia at the end of the eighteenth century was Semeon Desnitskiy (see Chapter 2). Desnitskiy sent a nakaz (instruction) to Catherine’s Legislative Commission proposing the introduction of courts on the English model, but his bold nakaz was not even read at the Commission.14 The great legal reformer M.M. Speranskiy also planned the introduction of jury trials, but his plans were never realised. The debates of the 1860s were fiery: the newspaper Den wrote that ‘The Russian people is not capable of self-government.’15

11 Barshev (1863) Vol. 45, Col.116, cited in Popova (2009), 51. 12 Kazantsev (1991), 4. 13 G.A. Dzhanshiev (1914) Collection of articles Moscow, 5; cited at Kazantsev (1991), 4. 14 Popova (2009), 275. 15 Den1862, No. 45; Popova (2009), 275. And trial by jury was indeed brought to Russia from England. Kazantsev reminded his readers that while trial by jury was developed in the fourteenth to fifteenth centuries, the French Revolution provided the impetus for the spread of the jury system throughout Europe. Revolutionaries saw in the jury a weapon against despotism, but also emphasised its intrinsic value. Thus, Robespierre claimed that the main achievement of the new court was the separation of the judgments of fact and of law, and that judicial power was to composed of ‘ordinary citizens, chosen from the people, who immediately return to the masses, where they will themselves be subjected to the very same power which they themselves only recently represented’.16

16 M. Robespierre (1959) ‘On the introduction of jury trial’ in the book Revolutionary legality and justice (Moscow); at Kazantsev (1991), 6. The more the various reform projects were examined in detail in the Russian press, the more actively the experience of the various foreign models was drawn on. From 1860 to 1866 interest in foreign experience peaked in Russia. A whole series of articles appeared in the press, many referring to English and French experience.17 Russia adopted the system of 12 jurors, ordinary people, sitting with a professional judge, with all the rights of the judge, participating in the investigation of all the evidence and, through their foreman, posing questions to witnesses, experts and the accused and retiring without the judge (this was especially important) in order to decide questions of fact, that is, whether the guilt of the accused had been proved or not.

17 ‘A comparison of English and French criminal procedure’ Journal of the Ministry of Justice1862 Vol. 19 Ch. 2; N. Utin, ‘A study of the historical formation of the jury trial in England’ Russkiy vestnik 1860 Vol. 26; A. Kistyakovskiy, ‘A study of the English criminal trial’ Journal of the Ministry of Justice 1860 No. 6, 9, 11, 12; V. Bervi, ‘A study of judicial administration in England’ Journal of the Ministry of Justice 1859, No. 9; B. Utin, ‘On justices of the peace in England’ Sovremennik 1860 No. 4, 11; K.S.U., ‘On advocates and khodatai in France’ Russkiy vestnik 1858 Vol. 18, book 2; ‘On the special juries for special classes of case in England, France and Italy’ Journal of the Ministry of Justice 1862 Vol. 14; Ch. 2; N.V. Shcherbanya, ‘Judges, magistrates and advocates in France’ Russkiy vestnik 1858 Vol. 15, books 1–2; A.O. Arsenev, ‘The contemporary condition of the French bar’ Russkiy vestnik 1861 Vol. 32; all cited at Popova (2009), 52, n. 103 The French jury system, like the German and others, was not a strict copy of the English jury. All these models were attentively studied by the Editing Commission along with, among others, the criminal procedural codes of Geneva and the Kingdom of Sardinia. In 1864 the well-known German legal scholar K. Mittermaier commented in a Russian journal that the Russian draft criminal procedure ‘stands higher even than many of the latest legislative works’. And as concerns the introduction of juries, he noted that ‘in comparison with the French legislation … contrary to it, the Russian model keeps to the English model to a much greater extent, even though on that side it is far from being a blind reflection.’18

18 K. Mittermaier (1864) Noviy proyekt russkovo ugolovnovo sudoproizvodstva (The new draft of Russian criminal procedure) Journal of the Ministry of Justice 1864, Vol. 22, 16; at Kazantsev (1991), 7. However, the Russian jury did not have jurisdiction concerning crimes against the state or most crimes of excess of authority and the most famous jury trials were murder cases. Yet the pool of jurors was relatively very wide and included all civil chinovniks (civil servants) of classes V to XIV in the Table of Ranks, all elected officers of town and nobility assemblies and peasants who had served for not fewer than three years in elected office as village starost, starshin and others. Only men aged between 25 and 70 who were subjects of the Empire could serve as jurors. However, clergy and monks were excluded, as were persons of the level of general (classes I to IV in the Table of Ranks), court employees, the police, the military, teachers and some others.19

19 Kazentsev (1991), 7. All persons meeting these qualifications were included in a general list of jurors. The general list was one of the main achievements of the jury system, since these were not chosen people, but every person meeting the qualification. Opponents of the jury described it as the ‘court of the street’ and ‘court of the crowd’. This phrase was used with success by the famous advocate S.A. Andreevskiy in his splendid speech for the case of the Kronstadt Bank:

Recently a certain publicist dared to call the jury ‘the street’. But, despite the intentions of the author, I see in that word no denigration or slur on the jury: on the contrary it brings together the most valuable features of this court. And in truth: you should be – the street! We are glad of this. On the street there is fresh air; we are all there without difference, rich and poor; there we are all equal, because under the eyes of the people we feel our security; in the street no-one permits himself shamelessness – we recall the funeral about which the prokuror spoke yesterday: when you take part on the street in the cortege of your relative, people you do not know take off their hats and cross themselves … On the street they help those who have become ill, extend charity to beggars, stop persons who are offensive, detain a thief running away! When you have a disaster at home – burglary, murder, fire – where do you run to for help? Onto the street. Because there you always find people ready to serve as the source of common human justice. Let us bring to us, to our court, this source … come and judge as from the street, because it is precisely from there that the legislator wished to bring his own judges, and not from offices and salons.20



20 S.A. Andreevskiy (1916) Drama zhizni (The drama of life) St Petersburg 1916, 609; Kazanstev (1991), 8. In 1901 even V.I. Lenin wrote: ‘The court of the street is valued precisely because it brings a breath of fresh air into that spirit of office formalism, in which our government departments are soaked.’21

21 V.I. Lenin Complete Collected Works Vol. 4, 407; Kazantsev (1991), 8. On the basis of the decision of the jury, the ‘crown judge’ declared the corresponding guilty or not guilty verdict. This verdict could only be appealed by way of cassation (on a point of law) to the Senat. In non-jury cases, the appeal instance was the ‘judicial board’. The ‘judicial board’ also heard at first instance the most dangerous crimes, crimes against the state and crimes committed in public office (dolzhnostnostniy). These cases were heard by a ‘crown court’ with representatives of the estates, one from each estate: the head of the gubernia or uyezd nobility, the town head and the volost starshina. The ‘crown judge’ and the representatives of the estates had equal voting rights, both as examining judges and in considering the verdict. V.I. Lenin wrote more critically that the estates representatives ‘were voiceless state-ists, and play the sad role of witnesses, participants in whatever the bureaucrats of justice order’.22

22 V.I. Lenin Complete Collected Works Vol. 4, 407; Kazantsev (1991), 10. Kazantsev’s collection also contains an extensive memoir by one of the most distinguished judges of the period, A.F. Koni,23 writing in 1914, who was able to reflect on a career spanning 30 years, as tovarishch prokuror, prokuror, chairman of a regional court, ober-prokuror and senator of the Criminal Cassation Department of the Senat.24 Moreover, it also reproduces the advocates’ speeches and judicial summings-up in some of the most famous jury trials. Perhaps the most famous, and infamous for opponents of jury trial, was the trial in 1878 of Vera Zasulich, charged with the attempted murder of the governor of St Petersburg, Colonel Teodor Trepov, whom she had shot in broad daylight and before witnesses.25 Koni, who was presiding judge, refused to be pressured by the authorities, and Zasulich was acquitted, a verdict that was respected by the authorities. I have already noted her correspondence with Karl Marx. In fact, she became a Menshevik, returned to Russia after the 1905 Revolution and moved away from revolutionary politics. She supported the Russian war effort during World War I and was an opponent of the October Revolution of 1917. She died in Petrograd on 8 May 1919.

23 A.F. Koni 1844–1927. 24 Koni (1991), 28–91. 25 In July 1877 a political prisoner, Alexei Bogolyubov, refused to remove his cap in the presence of Trepov, the governor of St Petersburg. Trepov, who was already infamous for his suppression of the Polish rebellions in 1830 and 1863, ordered that Bogolyubov be flogged. This outraged not only revolutionaries, but also sympathetic members of the intelligentsia. A group of six revolutionaries plotted to kill Trepov, but Zasulich was the first to act. However, in 1889 to 1891 the percentage of convictions in the ‘judicial boards’ was 68 per cent as against 65 per cent in jury trials – only a marginal difference26 and an indication of the independence of the ‘crown judges’ and a strong contrast to the 99 per cent conviction rate in non-jury trials in contemporary Russia.

26 Vysochaishe uchrezhdennaya komissiya dlya peresmotra zakonopolozheniy po sudebnoi chasti(Supreme departmental commission for oversight of legality in the courts): Trudy (works), Vol. 1, St Petersburg, 1895, 201; Kazantsev (1991), 201. Towards the end of the nineteenth century reactionary voices increasingly called for abolition of trial by jury. The conservative publicist M.N. Katkov called for a reform of the Sudebniye uchrezhdeniya so as to get rid of ‘slavish copying of alien models’ and demanded that ‘the Minister of Justice should be in truth a minister of his own state, and not as it were a diplomatic agent of the autocratic judicial republic.’27 In 1885 the leader of the conservatives, the notorious reactionary K.P. Pobedonostsev, sent to the Tsar a memorandum on ‘the necessity of judicial reform’ in which he proposed among other reforms ‘abolition of jury trial so as to restore the significance of the court in Russia’.28 And in the same year the Supreme Disciplinary Section of the Senat was created, abolishing the principle of irremovability of judges, with the power to move judges and in the final analysis dismiss them. In 1887 jurors were required to be able to read Russian and the property qualification was raised. In 1889 justices of the peace were abolished in most of the country. However, until the Revolution attempts to abolish jury trial failed.29 Juries and other features of the reformed judicial system were abolished by the Decree on the Courts No. 1, adopted by the Sovnarkom, the Council of People’s Commissars, on 22 November 1917.

27 N.A. Troitskiy (1975) Tsarism pod sudom progressivnoi obshchestvennost (Tsarism judged by progressive public opinion) (Moscow) 46: Kazantsev (1991), 17. 28 B.V. Vilenskiy Sudebnaya reforma i kontrreforma v Rossiy (Judicial reform and counter-reform in Russia) Saratov, 1969, 295–305; Kazantsev (1991), 17. 29 Jury trials abolished by the Decree on the Courts No. 1 adopted by the Sovnarkom (Council of People’s Commissars) on 22 November 1917.





Reform of the judicial system


From 1857 to 1858 in addition to the conservative views of the Minister of Justice V.N. Panin, there were two leading opinions. One was led by the head of the II Section of the Imperial Chancery30 D.N. Bludov, who considered that the judicial system of Russia could be improved without sharp changes and was against using ideas taken from Western countries. The other tendency was propagandised by Prince D.A. Obolenskiy, who, on the contrary, believed that it was essential to incorporate into the Russian judicial system the core aspects of Western procedure: oral and open procedure, jury trial, the bar. His opinions prevailed and in 1861 he founded the ‘Commission of the II Section for preparation of the project of judicial reform’.31

30 The Imperial Chancery was created by Tsar Nikolai I. The II Section was tasked with the codification of Russian legislation. 31 Tolmachev (1998) Vol. 1, 253; at Popova (2009), 53. In the autumn of 1863 work on the preparation of the new judicial charters was completed and on 20 November 1864 they were signed by Tsar Aleksandr II. The charters contained four normative legal acts: the Uchrezhdeniye of judicial institutions; the Statute of criminal procedure; the Statute of civil procedure; and the Statute of punishments, which may be imposed by justices of the peace.

These new statutes brought about cardinal changes in the judicial department. They were so far reaching that it was better to speak of the construction of a new judicial system and not of the reform of the old. They are in the view of many commentators, including Kazantsev, a true monument to the liberal thought of the nineteenth century.

The judicial charters created, in the words of Kazantsev, ‘an original and effective system of justice’.32 This new system had two branches, two subsystems, which were united in the highest judicial organ, the Senat. These were the general courts and justice of the peace courts. There were also courts with special jurisdiction: military, volost, commercial and others, as envisaged in various special laws. Judicial reform continued until the end of the nineteenth century. The first general courts were opened in 1866, but only in the 10 gubernia of central Russia. In the other regions, the previous courts continued to operate under the previous laws.

32 Kazantsev (1991), 5. What was especially important was that the administrative and judicial territories of the Empire did not coincide. Uyezdy and towns were divided into justice of the peace jurisdictions. Territories under the jurisdiction of okruzhniy (regional), general, courts of first instance covered several uyezds, while the jurisdiction of the ‘judicial boards’, courts of second instance covered several gubernia. At the beginning of the twentieth century there were 106 regional courts in the Empire and 14 ‘judicial boards’. Kazantsev commented, correctly, that the system created by the Great Reforms was far more effective at ensuring the absence of any executive or administrative interference with the judicial system than the reforms of the late twentieth and early twenty-first centuries.33

33 Kazantsev (1991), 5. The new principle of irremovability of judges, contained in Article 243 of the Uchrezhdeniye was perhaps of equal significance. This article provided that the chairmen and members of the regional (general) courts could not be removed or transferred from one responsibility to another except by order of the court. All permanent, professional members of the regional courts and judicial boards, the so-called ‘crown (koronniye) judges’, were appointed by the Tsar on the recommendation of the Minister of Justice. To become a member of a regional court a candidate must have higher legal education and not fewer than three years stage in the court or the prokuratura; and to become a prisyazhniy poverenniy, an advocate, permitted to defend in jury trials, five years’ experience was necessary.34

34 Kazantsev (1991), 5–6.





Justices of the peace


It should not be forgotten that justices of the peace were also an important democratic component of the Great Reforms of 1864. These had two instances: justice of the peace in the uchastky and the uyezd (town) congress of justices of the peace. Justices of the peace decided their cases unanimously and were elected by the zemskiy sobraniye (local assembly) for three years. They heard civil cases up to 300 roubles and criminal cases where the maximum penalty was one year of imprisonment. This form of justice was rapid and popular. The appeal verdict of the congress was final and subject only to cassation (appeal on point of law) to the Senat.





The bar


The Great Reforms created a bar in Russia and reformed the prokuratura. A large number of talented and eloquent advocates appeared from the stormy discussions of the 1860s and rapidly acquired prominence and popularity. For many of them, practice at the bar was a legal means of opposing the autocracy. There were two categories of advocate. The highest category of advocates was the prisyazhniy poverenniy, advocates permitted to defend in jury trials, ‘jury advocates’, who formed corporations in the regions of the ‘judicial boards’. The jury advocates elected a council, which supervised the admission of new members – higher legal education and a five-year stage (pupillage) must be spent with a practising jury advocate – and reviewed the activity of existing members. The second category was able to conduct less significant cases and was not required to have higher legal education, provided members passed an examinations set by the court.35

35 Kazantsev (1991), 11–12.





The prokuratura


The prokuratura no longer exercised the function of general supervisory review (nadzor) over all aspects of government – this was returned to them in Soviet Russia. Instead, the prosecutor was now responsible for oversight of investigation and for presenting the case for the state in the trial. The new prokuratura was attached to the courts. The new offices created were the prokuror of the judicial board and his tovarishchei (assistants) and the prokuror of the regional court and his tovarishchei. The Minister of Justice held the office of General Prokuror.

The prokuratura was therefore much more dependent on the government both because of their direct subordination to the Minister of Justice and because they had no principle of irremovability. However, the prokuratura generally rejected any attempt at influence by local authorities boldly and decisively. They were not only independent of police and gendarmerie, but saw themselves as in charge of them. Prokurors were often promoted to senior posts not only in the Ministry of Justice but also the Ministry of Internal Affairs. The prokuratura as an institution was relatively incorrupt, partly explained by the fact that a tovarishch prokuror at the start of the twentieth century received 2,400 roubles a month, that is, 10 times more than a middling worker.36

36 Kazantsev (1991), 13.





The historians’ debate – revolutionary situation or Great Reform?


There has been an intense debate since 1864 as to the significance of the 1860s, which can be summed up under the heading ‘Great Reform or revolutionary situation?’37 One of the first attempts to analyse what had happened was ‘Ten years of reform’ by the commentator A.A. Golobachev, published in 1872. In his opinion, there was not a complete break with the old laws and government and he was satisfied that the cause of the reforms was Russia’s disaster in the Crimean War. Twenty years later, in 1907, G.A. Dzhanshiev wrote in the context of political reaction, and assured his readers that there were no real threats of disturbance and the peasants patiently awaited their liberation.38

37 Vedernikov (2012). 38 Vedernikov (2012), 20. In 1912, soon after the first Russian Revolution in 1905, A.A. Kornilov published his course of lectures, which expressed scepticism as to the reform potential of the authorities. In his view, Aleksandr II was a moderate conservative, who remodelled himself as a reformer not because of his sympathy for the people who in the 1840s railed against serfdom, but because the Crimean War persuaded him of the need for moderate reform. N.A. Kotlyarevskiy in 1916 expressed greater scepticism and considered that the 1860s were in fact the epilogue of pre-reform Russia. The 50th anniversary of the reforms in 1911 inspired a flood of publications, among them works by the Marxists G.V. Plekahnov and V.I. Lenin. Plekhanov evaluated the 1864 reform highly critically, as a liberal apology for the liberation of the peasants. He saw the main cause of the reforms as to be found in the economic factor.39

39 Vedernikov (2012), 21. Lenin, on the one hand, saw great significance in the role of the revolutionaries of the 1860s, while, on the other, noting the extreme weakness of those social elements whose interests demanded reform.40 He saw the economic factor and the Crimean War disaster as the least of the factors causing the reform. Only in his works of 1915 did Lenin describe the period 1859 to 1861 as a ‘revolutionary situation’, but, according to Vedernikov without giving evidence for this.

40 Vedernikov (2012), 23. Plekhanov’s ideas were concretised in the work of the leading Marxist historian of the 1920s and 1930s, M N. Pokrovskiy, who was the only professional historian who was also a Marxist. He wrote that it was, in fact, the rise in the price of grain on the world market that no longer made it profitable for land-owners to own serfs. He also downplayed the significance of the revolutionary movement of that time; Chernyshevskiy, author of What is to be done?, had no real forces at hand. For Pokrovskiy, this was a revolution from above. Lenin’s views were not significant for him. Thus, for pre-revolutionary historiography, the decisive role in the 1860s was played by the government apparatus.

Pokrovskiy saw no need to change his assessment until the late 1920s, when Stalin intervened in the writing of history. In 1928 Pokrovskiy admitted his errors. He wrote that ‘in Russia at the time of Chernyshevskiy there was not and could not be a revolutionary bourgeoisie’, but Chernyshevskiy (and here Pokrovskiy cited Lenin) was a revolutionary democrat. The only revolutionary force at that time was the peasantry. The petit-bourgeoisie of the towns was exhausted and the proletariat had only begun to appear. The peasant revolution of 1850 to 1860 was a bourgeois revolution.

By the early 1930s the idea of a ‘revolutionary situation’ in 1861 was to be found in school textbooks, for example that written by Pokrovskiy’s student, S.A. Piontkovskiy. The ideologists of the revolutionary bourgeois nature of the peasantry were now the critic V.G. Belinskiy, A.I. Herzen and N.G. Chernyshevskiy. Belinskiy reflected the position of the enslaved serfs and the textbook author cited Lenin’s furious 1909 polemic against the collection Vekhi (Landmarks), in which the main enemies were Belinskiy and Chernyshevskiy. Herzen, the editor of Kolokol (The bell) from exile in London, stirred up the common people. And the author of What is to be Done?, revered by Lenin, was presented as the most uncompromising revolutionary democrat and supporter of a peasant revolution.

Vekhi 41 was published in 1909, edited by Mikhail Gershenzon and contained essays by the religious and idealist philosophers Nikolai Berdyaev, Sergei Bulgakov, Bogdan Kistyakovsky, Pyotr Struve, Semen Frank and Aleksandr Izgoyev. With invective, sarcasm and irony they attacked the Russian intelligentsia for its enthusiastic support of the 1905 Revolution. In the words of Adrzej Walicki42 it was ‘a manifesto of rightwing, anti-revolutionary liberalism’ whose authors agreed on the ‘need for a new intellectual canon focused on religious thought and efforts to define the Russian national identity’. Kistyakovskiy’s contribution was well summarised by Walicki:43

41 Gershenzon (1909/2011); full text, with an enthusiastic introduction by Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn, at http://www.vehi.net/vehi/index.html (accessed 26 October 2012). 42 Walicki (2010), 101. 43 Walicki (2010), 106. Kistjakovskij’s contribution to Milestones was entitled In Defense of Law.44 In it, he criticized the Russian intelligentsia for its absolute contempt of the law and legal culture, and its failure to realize that law is a social value in itself and not merely an instrument serving the attainment of this or that political end. To back his claims Kistjakovskij quoted philosophers from the right and the left—the slavophiles as well as Hercen, Leont’ev and Mikhailovskij; he also emphasized that even staunch ‘westerners’ like Kavelin and Plekhanov deeply mistrusted the law. He also saw evidence of the low level and deformed character of the intelligentsia’s legal awareness in the programs proposed by the country’s political parties, all of which in his opinion showed a strong preference for strict social control based on written lore, which was typical of bureaucracies in police states. This led Kistjakowskij to conclude that the Russian intelligentsia’s legal awareness in some way resembled that of a police state.



44 Text in Russian at http://www.vehi.net/vehi/kistyak.html (accessed 26 October 2012). Lenin argued that for the authors ‘the “intelligentsia” in fact appears as the spiritual leader, inspirer and mouthpiece of the whole Russian democracy and the whole Russian liberation movement … The attack is being pursued all along the line against democracy, against the democratic world outlook.’45

45 Lenin (1909/1963), 123–25. Lenin’s respect for Herzen was once again demonstrated in his ‘Memories of Herzen’, published on 8 May 1912 in the journal Sotsial-Demokrat, on the occasion of the 100th anniversary of Herzen’s birth.46 Herzen was celebrated by all liberal Russia, all those who sought to forget the revolutionary Herzen, who had played a great role in preparing the revolution in Russia. Herzen was born into the nobility, Lenin reminded his readers, but was not a gambler or drunkard like so many, but was one of the ‘men of December 14’ (1825), as Herzen himself wrote, ‘a phalanx of heroes reared, like Romulus and Remus, on the milk of a wild beast … They were veritable titans, hammered out of pure steel from head to foot, comrades-in-arms who deliberately went to certain death in order to awaken the young generation to a new life and to purify the children born in an environment of tyranny and servility.’47

46 Lenin (2010), 221–29; English translation (1968) ‘In memory of Herzen’ in Collected Works Vol. 18 (London: Lawrence & Wishart), 25–31. 47 Lenin quoted from Herzen: A. Herzen, ‘Ends and beginnings: letter to I.S. Turgenev’ (1862) in The memoirs of Aleksandr Herzen Vol. IV (London: Chatto & Windus, 1968), 1683. Piontkovskiy continued that the most powerful revolutionary organisations of the 1860s were Molodaya Rossiya (Young Russia) and Zemlya i volya (Land and Freedom), both of which, he said, were under the unmediated ideological influence of Chernyshevskiy.48

48 Vedernikov (2012), 30. The results of Stalin’s interventions were to be seen in the textbook History of the USSR for higher education institutions, published in 1940 and edited by M.V. Nechkina, who followed in the footsteps of Pokrovskiy.49 Chapter 21 of the volume ‘Russia in the XIX century’ bore the title ‘The revolutionary movement of the 1850s. The ripening of the revolutionary situation’.50 However, the immediate cause of social stirring was given not as the peasant movement, but as the disaster of the Crimean War, ‘which with shattering clarity unveiled the technical, economic and political backwardness of Russia’. According to the authors of the textbook, the government was obliged to undertake political reform ‘under the influence of the revolutionary situation’. The only evidence given for this, says Vedernikov, was the famous warning speech of Aleksandr II to the Moscow nobility. However, it is perfectly clear that the Tsar spoke not of an actual threat of a peasant uprising, but only of the possibility of unfortunate consequences in the future, if reform from above were not carried out. Aleksandr was presented as ‘weak and indecisive, deprived of serious interests’ and a disciple of his father’s political system.

49 Vedernikov (2012), 32–3. 50 Vedernikov (2012), 33; Nechkin (ed.) Vol. II, 384–428. Only by the mid-1950s were the ideas of a ‘revolutionary situation’ thrown into doubt. But the decisive role in undermining the thesis of a ‘revolutionary situation’ was played by English language scholars in the 1960s and 1970s.51

51 Adler, Ch. (1969) ‘The revolutionary situation 1859–1861: the uses of historical conception’, Canadian Slavic Studies 3(2); Emmons, T. (1968) The Russian landed gentry and the peasant emancipation of 1861 (Cambridge); Field, D. (1976) The end of serfdom: nobility and bureaucracy in Russia, 1855–1861 (London). Vedernikov’s own view, in 2012, is worthy of note, especially in view of his very thorough study of the preceding historiography. According to him:52

52 Lapin (2012), 290–91. [T]he most important period in the history of the Great Reform was the period 1856–1859, when the preparation of reform united in a single goal the Westernisers and the Slavophiles, and representatives of government circles and civil society. This was the time when M. N. Katkov and the Grand Duchess Yelena Pavlovna, M. P. Pogodin and N. G. Chernyshevskiy, Yu. F. Samarin and K. D. Kavelin formed one team. Not the mythical ‘revolutionary situation’ but its absence permitted the preparation and the implementation of the liberation of the serfs, and the working out of an interconnected complex of economic, social, political and cultural reforms.



Alekseev also confirms that the Great Reforms satisfied many of the demands of the Slavophiles, including Vladimir Solovyov: the abolition of serfdom, jury trial and institution of equality of rights.53 The situation, said Vedernikov, changed in 1859. The united camp of reformers broke up. The radical wing, represented in Russia by Chernyshevskiy, split away. In Vedernikov’s view, it was no accident that the action of I.S. Turgenev’s novel Fathers and sons, the book about the split in Russian society, took place precisely in 1859.

53 Alekseev (2007), 21. Chernyshevskiy came to be reviled by conservatives and liberals.





Chapter 4

The trajectory of Yevgeniy Pashukanis and the struggle for power in Soviet law




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-5

This chapter explores the brief flowering of a specifically Marxist understanding of law during the first decades of the Soviet Union, through the history of its most original exponent, Yevgeniy Pashukanis. Chris Arthur has described Pashukanis’ ‘important contribution to the materialist critique of legal forms’ as ‘to this day the most significant Marxist work on the subject’.1 I do not disagree.

1 Arthur (1983), 9. However, a recently published textbook, The history of legal thought in Russia,2 has no section on Pashukanis as such, although it has a very short section (1½ pages) on G.V. Plekhanov (the ‘father of Russian Marxism’, 1857–1918),3 as an example of ‘Russian radicalism of the XIX century’. Its author, Professor Mikhail Antonov, introduces Pashukanis in the context of the contest in Russia between the Slavanophiles and Westernisers, and then in their respective intellectual traditions, the conservatives (whose head was Konstantin Pobedonostsev) and liberals (led by Boris Chichern).4 According to Antonov, there was considerable continuity in legal theoretical debate following the 1917 Revolution. The first ideologists of the new legal system, Yevgeniy Pashukanis and Mikhail Reisner, continued the tradition of pre-revolutionary liberal legal discourse. The Slavophile, conservative tradition found its continuation in the Eurasianism developed by the White emigration (see Chapter 1). Thus Chapter 14, the final chapter, of Antonov’s textbook is entitled ‘Alternatives to Russian authoritarianism: Eurasianism and Bolshevism’.5

2 Antonov (2012). 3 Antonov (2012), 130–31. 4 Antonov (2012), 10. 5 Antonov (2012), 194–210. Eurasianism started with the thought of Ivan Ilyin (1883–1954), whose neo-idealism crystallised in his conceptions of the state as ‘organised spiritual solidarity’ and law as the ‘foundation for reciprocal spiritual recognition of the order (in the sense of “law and order”) of human relations’.6 Thus law for Ilyin has nothing to do with a contract, but was first and foremost a feeling of spiritual rightness, of justice. Ilyin is enormously popular in Russian law schools today and he is cited by Putin, among others.

6 Antonov (2012), 195. Antonov prefers the word ‘Bolshevism’ to ‘Marxism, since the latter is interpreted in various ways. In his view, Lenin’s weakness was his inability to find a scientific foundation for his position. The theory of law in the first years after 1917 was based on the ideas of the psychological school of law, finding support in Marx’s conception of class consciousness. Then, in the 1930s, in Antonov’s view, statist positivism dominated.7 Up to then, there was a wide variety of points of view, from the psychological (L.I. Petrazhitskiy), to Marxism as ‘god seeking’ (the Peoples’ Commissar for Enlightenment, A.V. Lunacharskiy).8 Pashukanis makes his appearance three pages before the end of the textbook, but as an example of the ‘most interesting and constructive attempt at an economic analysis of law from the position of Marxism’.9 Pashukanis apparently held the view that ‘law is always connected with economic relations and is unthinkable absent these relations.’ Public law as such did not exist.10

7 Antonov (1912), 201. 8 Antonov (2012), 202. 9 Antonov (2012), 208. 10 Antonov (2012), 209. In this chapter, I suggest that there is rather more of interest and substance in Pashukanis’ own work; and that the turbulent history of the ideological struggles over law in the early years of Soviet power are a further example of the close intermingling of law and ideology in Russia.





The life and times of Yevgeniy Pashukanis


Pashukanis’ life and career run as follows.11 Yevgeniy Bronislavovich Pashukanis was born on 23 February 1891 in the town of Staritsa in Tver Guberniya (north of Moscow), in a family with revolutionary traditions. His mother, Sofiya Pavlovna, was a member of the Russian Social Democratic Workers Party (RSDRP) from 1903. His father, Bronislav Frantsevich, worked as a medical doctor. In 1906 the Pashukanis family moved to Petersburg, and there Yevgeniy spent much time with his uncle, M.P. Lyadov, a Bolshevik from 1903, a leader of the December armed uprising in Moscow and, in the 1920s, Rector of the Sverdlov Communist University.

11 I draw here from the thesis of Protopopov (2007). In October 1917 Pashukanis worked actively in the Sushchevsko-Mariynskiy Military Revolutionary Committee in Moscow and was later elected a member of the Cassation (Appeal) Tribunal attached to the All-Russian Central Executive Committee (VTsIK),12 acting as a revolutionary judge.13 Having been up to then a Menshevik-Internationalist,14 he joined the Russian Communist Party (Bolshevik) – RKP(B) – in 1918.

12 These courts were established by the Decree of 22 November (5 December) 1917 ‘On the Court’: Decree of the Council of People’s Commissars SU 1917–1918, No. 4, item 50. English translation in Zile (1992), 95–6. 13 Pashukanis (1922), 15–16; see also Stuchka (1918), cited in Zile (1992). 14 The radical wing of the Menshevik Party, opposed to any coalition with the propertied classes, yet unwilling to accept the Bolshevik concept of the dictatorship of the working class. Trotsky was a member of this group, which also included Martov and Martinov. See http://www.marxists.org/glossary/orgs/m/e.htm (accessed 31 July 2012).





Pashukanis in Berlin – the writing of the General Theory


From 1920 to 1923 Pashukanis worked in the Peoples’ Commissariat for Foreign Affairs as the deputy head of the economic law department, and then as an adviser in the Soviet Embassy in Berlin, where he took part in the preparation of the Rapallo Treaty with Germany. This was the agreement signed at the Hotel Imperiale in the Italian town of Rapallo on 16 April 1922 between Germany and Soviet Russia, under which each renounced all territorial and financial claims against the other following the Treaty of Brest-Litovsk. This treaty enabled the young Soviet state to break the blockade of the capitalist states and to enter into full diplomatic relations with Germany.

Pashukanis prepared his General theory in 1920–21 while in Berlin, as he later related.15 This explains the large number of German authors cited; and the fact that Pashukanis’ text was in essence a response to ‘the extreme formalism of the normative school’ of Hans Kelsen.16

15 Pashukanis (1930), 26. 16 Pashukanis (1983), 70 – and see also 51, 58, 75 (where Kelsen is described as a ‘consistent neo-Kantian’) and 86. Pashukanis engaged with Karl Renner at 86, and the French positivist Duguit, 96–9. In 1922 Pashukanis entered the Institute of Law in the Communist Academy.17 The Institute’s Director was A.D. Goikhbarg. Pashukanis worked closely with the Latvian-born legal theorist Piotr I. Stuchka, his senior by 25 years – Pashukanis was 33 years old in 1917, Stuchka was 52. Stuchka lived from 1865 to 1932, when, unusually for those times, he died of natural causes; he was Chairman of the Supreme Court of the RSFSR from 1923.18

17 Isaev (1988), 76. 18 Stuchka (1988), x–xi. The Academy existed from 1918 to 1936 and it started life as the Socialist Academy of Social Sciences by way of a decree of 25 June 1918 of the VTsIK (All-Union Executive Committee) of the Russian Socialist Federation of Soviet Republics (RSFSR)19 and opened on 1 October 1918. On 15 April 1919 it was renamed the Socialist Academy and on 17 April 1924, the Communist Academy.

19 SU RSFSR 1918, First Section No. 49, 573. In 1922, Stuchka wrote the following about his first meetings with Pashukanis:

As for Comrade Pashukanis, in the legal field, we met for the first time in Moscow at a conference on the legal program of Sverdlov Communist University, and later at my lecture at the Communist Academy. Finding that our views coincided on basic questions, I invited Comrade Pashukanis to joint work on the theory of law at the Communist Academy, and he agreed. Even then I was aware of one disagreement between us: this was his underestimation of the role of the state for law, which later turned out not to be accidental. For me, in my very first work, the state played a central role for law.20



20 Stuchka (1931) 70; in English at Stuchka (1988), 223.





Pashukanis and the New Economic Policy


Pashukanis’ intellectual flourishing took place in the period of the New Economic Policy (NEP), which was Lenin’s response to the devastation caused by the civil war, from 1918 to 1921, and the interventions of Japan, Britain and the United States. According to Siegelbaum, in 1917 there were 3.5 million workers in factories employing more than 16 workers. In 1918 there were 2 million and just 1.5 million in 1921. In 1917 Petrograd had a population of 2.5 million and only 722,000 in 1920, its population in 1870. Moscow’s population fell from 2 million to just over 1 million. The Moscow death rate rose from 23.7 per thousand to 45.4 per thousand from 1917 to 1919, the result of waves of epidemics.21 By 1920 the area of cultivated land in Russia was 25 per cent less than in 1913. Many died of starvation.

21 Siegelbaum (1992), 26–9. In these dire circumstances, on 15 March 1921 at the 10th Congress of the Russian Communist Party (Bolsheviks),22 Lenin launched a programme of material incentives to peasants, cooperatives and private manufacturers. In November 1922 Lenin proposed a notion of ‘state capitalism’ in which capitalist production and exchange would be permitted subject to the supervision of the Soviet state.23 Very quickly small-scale production and retailing became dominated by capitalists, ‘NEP men’ and sophisticated legal regulation of their activities and of their disputes was required. In 1922 and 1923 the RSFSR enacted a full set of legal codes: judiciary code, civil and civil procedural codes, criminal and criminal procedural codes, a land code and a labour code, based, primarily, on German models. Lenin’s death in January 1924 did not mean the end of the NEP; but Stalin, increasingly powerful, began the process of systematic destruction of any opposition and ordered sharp turns to ‘socialism in one country’ in late 1924 and, from 1927 onwards, ostensibly ‘left’ policies of elimination of the market, industrialisation and forced collectivisation of agriculture.

22 V.I. Lenin (1921) Report to the 10th Congress, cited in Zile (1992), 144–52. 23 V. I. Lenin (1922/1964), 302. This was the context of Pashukanis’ rise to theoretical prominence.





Pashukanis’ early writings


The Selected Works of Pashukanis published in 198024 has a bibliography25 of the fundamental works of Pashukanis; but one is missing. Pashukanis’ first published article appeared in 1921 and was entitled Burzhuazniy yurist o prirode gosudarstvo (‘A bourgeois jurist on the nature of the state’).26 He wrote the article while working in the Soviet representation in Berlin. It appeared in the literary journal Krasnaya Nov (Red Virgin Soil), which was the first Soviet literary ‘thick’ journal, published between 1921 and 1941. From 1921 to 1927 it was edited by Aleksandr Voronkov (1884–1937), who was close to Trotsky, agreed with Trotsky’s 1923 Literature and revolution, did much to encourage new literary talent and in 1937 was executed for Trotskyism.

24 Pashukanis (1980). 25 Ibid, 261–67. 26 Pashukanis (1921). The subject of the article was the University of Toulouse professor Maurice Hauriou (1856–1929),27 whose textbook, Principles of public law, was published in 1910. Pashukanis did not refer in his 4,000-word article to any other literature. This contrasts strongly with the General theory, which has many references, including several to German authors. He referred to Marx, but without indicating his source. Most of the article consisted of often sarcastic references to Hauriou’s positivist doctrine. Pashukanis’ concluding paragraph was as follows:28

27 For biography see http://www.universalis.fr/encyclopedie/maurice-hauriou/ (accessed 27 July 2012). He was a positivist, influenced by Aquinas, Comte and Bergson. 28 Pashukanis (1921), 231–32. In one of his speeches Cde Lenin remarked that sincere defenders of capitalism may now be found only among our SRs [Social Revolutionaries] and Mensheviks. In the West they have become extinct. We can see the cause of this. Russia underwent an accelerated course of capitalism and therefore our intelligentsia may bona fide come out in defence of the sublime beginning of democracy and freedom, without sensing that it has been crucified by capitalism. But in order to be a sincere defender of capitalism in the West, where social relations have been able to mature and over-mature, one must sincerely, like Hauriou, assert that the principles of freedom, democracy, individual rights and so on, conceal behind themselves quite simply faire valoir de la propriété!29 And such courage is not given to everyone.



29 Enforceproperty!. In 1923 he published two articles in the Socialist Academy bulletin, ‘A survey of the literature on the general theory of law’30 and ‘Kunov, as an interpreter of the Marxist theory of society and state’.31

30 Pashukanis (1983). 31 Pashukanis (1923a). In 1924 Pashukanis published his classic A general theory of law and Marxism,32 which brought him to prominence and the start of a spectacular career. A further publication in 1924 was ‘Ruhr and reparations: the observations of a diplomat, notes from January to September 1923’.33 From 1925 to 1927 the Communist Academy began the work of publishing a three-volume Encyclopedia of state and law. Its authors were Pashukanis, P.I. Stuchka and V.V. Adoratskiy (see later). This was the first Marxist encyclopedia of law and it played a significant role in the development of Soviet legal scholarship. In 1927 Pashukanis became one of the editors of the newly published journal Revolution of Law, a full member (deistvitelniy chlen) of the Communist Academy and within a few years a member of its Presidium and then its vice-president. He also joined the editorial board of Soviet Law, as well as the Bulletin of the Communist Academy and World Economy and World Politics, later becoming editor of the journal Soviet State and Revolution of Law, which, in 1932, was renamed Soviet State. In June 1928 Pashukanis took part in the ‘Week of Russian historians’ in Berlin and presented a paper on ‘Cromwell’s soldier Soviets’. In 1929 at another conference in Berlin Pashukanis presented a paper on ‘Emergency legislation in capitalist countries’. In 1935 at the sixth International Conference on Unification of Criminal Law in Copenhagen, Pashukanis presented a paper on ‘The struggle against terrorism’.

32 Pashukanis (1983). 33 Pashukanis (1983).





The triumph of Pashukanis


From 1927 to 1936 Pashukanis was the leading theorist of law in the USSR, recognised as such by Stuchka, who wrote in 1924 shortly after its publication that the General theory was ‘to the highest degree a valuable contribution to our Marxist theoretical literature on law and directly supplements my work, which provides only an incomplete and greatly inadequate general doctrine of law’.34

34 Stuchka (1988), xvii; preface to the third edition of Stuchka’s Revolutionary role of law and state, 1924. In 1931, following a dramatic recantation of his previous views, Pashukanis became Director of the Institute of Soviet Construction and Law of the Communist Academy. He was effectively the USSR’s director of legal research and legal education. The American scholar John Hazard (1909–1955),35 who studied under Pashukanis from 1934 to 1937, summarised his effect on legal education as follows:36

35 Hazard was a founder of the field of Soviet legal studies in America who taught at Columbia for 48 years. On his graduation from Harvard Law School, he was sent by the Institute of Current World Affairs as the first American to study Soviet law at the Moscow Institute of Law. Only a handful of scholars were concerned with Russian diplomacy and business then and scholarship on Russia was limited principally to historical studies. He approached the field of Soviet law as a pioneer and received the certificate of the Institute of Law in 1937. He was the author of widely used textbooks and studies of Soviet law and public administration, and served the US government during World War II, helping to negotiate the Lend–Lease agreement with the Soviet Union. 36 Hazard (1938b); see also Hazard (1938a). His influence was so marked that the courses in civil law in the law school were abolished, and to replace them there appeared a course called economic-administrative law, concerning itself with regulation of the relations between state enterprises.37



37 Ibid, 13. Believing that the state was slowly withering away as socialism came nearer to achievement, Pashukanis advocated the cessation of courses in civil law. He understood civil law to be the regulation of the relations of men under the trading conditions of capitalism and, as such, no longer of importance, as the remnants of capitalism disappeared.

Following Pashukanis’ fall in 1937, courses on (Soviet) civil law were reintroduced to the syllabus.

Hazard also wrote that the situation in the Institute where: ‘[Pashukanis] projected a theory said to be infallible, and where those who strayed from Pashukanis’ line were castigated like Korovin38 or denied faculty appointments, promotions and salary raises was novel to me. I saw teachers compelled to conform not only to ideas of Marx but also to those of Pashukanis as his infallible interpreter.’39

38 Yevgeniy Aleksandrovich Korovin (1892–1964), one year younger than Pashukanis, but outliving him by many years, was indeed Pashukanis’ chief enemy, at least in Pashukanis’ eyes. Also in 1924, the same year as Pashukanis’ General theory, Korovin, already a professor at Moscow State University from 1923, published his first major work, International law of the transitional period. 39 Hazard (1938a). On 16 November 1936 Pashukanis reached the high point of his career: the Presidium of the Central Executive Committee of the USSR appointed him Deputy People’s Commissar for Justice of the USSR. In the same year he was deputy chairman of the Drafting Committee for the 1936 ‘Stalin Constitution’40 and the Institute of State and Law proposed him as a candidate for membership of the Academy of Science of the USSR. Also in 1936 he became the chairman of the Academic Council attached to the People’s Commissariat of Justice of the USSR.

40 Hazard (1938b). Pashukanis was a staunch loyalist in relation to the regime – in my opinion, by conviction rather than any sort of pressure. Thus, by 1932, Pashukanis, by then editor-in-chief of the official law journal Soviet State, was able to write a ‘hallelujah’ in response to Stalin’s letter ‘Some questions on the history of Bolshevism’.41 Pashukanis’ major work on international law, Essays in international law, appeared in 1935.42

41 Pashukanis (1932). 42 Pashukanis (1935). Most copies of the Essays were destroyed after he was denounced in 1937, but in this culminating work he declared that any attempt to define the ‘nature of international law’ was scholastic.43 In his view, such attempts were the result of the continuing influence of bourgeois legal methodology, which, he said, rested on the association of law with substance developing in accordance with its own internal principles. For him, in 1935, international law was a means of formulating and strengthening in custom and treaties various political and economic relationships between states; the USSR could use international law to further Soviet interests in the struggle with capitalist states. He saw no reason to believe that, in using these principles of international law for its own purposes, the USSR was compromising its principles, in a world in which most states were capitalist. For Pashukanis, there was no point in seeking to determine whether international law was ‘bourgeois’ or ‘socialist’; such a discussion would be ‘scholastic’.44

43 CitedinHazard (1957), 385–88. 44 SeeHazard (1957), 387. Within two years he was dead, following Pravda’s announcement on 20 January 1937 that he had been found to be an enemy of the people – just two months after he had been named by the regime to supervise the revision of the whole system of Soviet codes of law. On the same day he was arrested. On 4 September 1937 a military collegium sentenced him to death. He was condemned as a member of a ‘band of wreckers’ and ‘Trotsky-Bukharin fascist agents’. He was posthumously rehabilitated in 1956.





Legal journals of the 1920s


At this point I clarify the intellectual context into which Pashukanis exploded in 1924. In 1999 T.F. Yashuk published an important survey of law journals of the 1920s.45 He analysed the work of three journals, Pravo i zhizn (Law and Life), Sovetskoye pravo (Soviet Law), and Revolyutsiya prava (Revolution of Law). There were many other journals, central and regional, including bulletins of the work of the courts, administrative law and so on. Yashuk chose these three journals for their particular significance. He pointed out that the political situation of the early 1920s permitted a pluralism of scholarly publication, in which neither a precise class evaluation was required nor a formal recognition of Marxist method.

45 Yashuk (1999). In these conditions of relative intellectual freedom a privately published law journal, Law and Life, was published from 1922 to 1927, edited by the well-known professors A.M. Vinaver, M.N. Gernet and A.N. Trainin. The journal tackled issues of law and economic construction. As far as I know, Pashukanis was not involved in this journal.

Also in 1922, the Institute of Soviet Law, part of the Socialist (later Communist) Academy, began publication of Soviet Law. As with all the work of the Institute it was produced under the general editorship of the Institute’s Director, A.G. Goikhbarg (1883–1962), who drafted the 1922 Civil Code of the RSFSR, on the specific instruction of Lenin, who considered that such a code, based on the German model, was an essential component of the New Economic Policy.46 The Institute of Soviet Law was the first Soviet scholarly institution, created to bring together Marxist lawyers. The journal did not have an editorial board or regular contributors, but between 1922 and 1925 its most active authors were D.I. Kurskiy, P.I. Stuchka, D.A. Magerovskiy, A.I. Yelistratov, M.M. Isaev and P.I. Lyubinskiy. In 1928 its collaborators changed and I.S. Voitinskiy, D.A. Magerovskiy, N.N. Ovsyannikov, S.I. Raevich and E.G. Shirvind became its regular contributors. The journal continued publication until 1929, when it was renamed Zapiski instituta sovetskovo prava (Bulletin of the Institute of Soviet Law). It can be seen that Pashukanis was not one of the journal’s authors. In 1922 in his introductory article for Soviet Law, ‘The immediate tasks of Soviet law’, the People’s Commissar of Justice D.I. Kurskiy asserted that the journal must attract scholars for the study of law from a Marxist position, so as to build a system of Soviet law.47 As will be seen, Pashukanis strongly believed that there could be no such thing as Soviet law.

46 See V.P. Gribanov Osushchestvleniye i zashchita grazhdanskikh prav (The realisation and defence of civil law rights); http://civil.consultant.ru/elib/books/1/page_7.html (accessed 18 November 2012). 47 (1922) No. 1 Soviet Law, 3–4. In 1925 the Section of Law and State of the Communist Academy began publication of the provocatively entitled Revolution of Law. The publication of Soviet Law and Revolution of Law represented an important step in the consolidation of lawyers who took a Marxist position and also reflected the substantial shift in legal scholarship, as well as exposing significant differences. These were highlighted by the introductory article, ‘Nashi zadachi (Our tasks)’ of Revolution of Law,48 which evaluated the situation of legal scholarship quite differently from Soviet Law. The editorial board members, P.I. Stuchka, V.V. Adoratskiy, G.S. Gurvich, Ye.B. Pashukanis, I.P. Razumovskiy and A.Ya. Estrin, identified themselves as the Communist wing of lawyers and their articles ‘were not only theoretical weapons, but were intended for organisational intervention’.49 This required taking the line of a ‘materialist, class, revolutionary dialectical approach to questions of the state and law’.50 Such an approach was mandatory for contributors to the journal.

48 (1925) No. 1 Revolution of Law, 3–4. I made photocopies of key parts of this and later issues of Revolution of Law, at the SPARK Library in Moscow. Yashuk wrongly gives the year of publication as 1927. 49 Ibid, 4. 50 Ibid, 3. All three journals shared a common structure and were composed of three sections. The first contained significant scholarly articles. The second reflected on experience of implementing law. Soviet Law entitled this section ‘Soviet legal construction’, while in Law and Life it was entitled ‘Questions of contemporary legislation’ or ‘Legal chronicle’. The third section contained bibliographic surveys, abstracts of monographs, information on scholarly life. In the first years of Soviet Law, most of the articles continued the pre-revolutionary tradition of the posing of questions, argumentation and analysis. An example was Goikhbarg’s 1924 ‘Some reflections on law’.

Revolution of Law published programmatic articles, for example in No. 2 of 1927 Stuchka’s ‘State and law in the period of socialist construction’, in No. 3 Pashukanis’ article ‘The Marxist theory of law and the construction of socialism’ and in No. 4 the simultaneous publication of Stuchka’s ‘Three stages of Soviet law’ and Pashukanis’ ‘Ten years of Lenin’s “State and revolution”’. According to Yashuk’s analysis, by the middle of the 1920s there were clear disagreements between Pashukanis, Stuchka and Goikhbarg. Goikhbarg, who edited Soviet Law, set its general line: the interpretation of law as a form of ideology, calling for opposition to law by analogy with anti-religious propaganda, with a sceptical attitude to the very concept of ‘law’, which should be replaced.51

51 Goikhbarg (1925), 3–4. In the second half of the 1920s Pashukanis and Stuchka became the official interpreters of the Marxist understanding of law, their views found wide support among lawyers and they became the most cited scholars. There were still polemics in the journals, but these were less principled scholarly differences and more the result of competition for scholarly supremacy. Often Stuchka and Pashukanis exaggerated one another’s positions. For example, Stuchka alleged that Pashukanis’ disagreement on the relation of state and law, since there could be no law without the state, undermined the class character of any law etc.52

52 Stuchka (1927). At the end of the 1920s the content of the journals changed fundamentally, reflecting the evolution of the political regime. Scholarly references were, according to Yashuk, replaced by references to Party decisions. In 1930 Revolution of Law changed its name to Soviet State and Revolution of Law, in 1931 Pashukanis became its editor, in 1932 it changed its name to Soviet State, remaining under Pashukanis as editor until 1937 and, in 1939, changed yet again to Soviet State and Law. It became State and Law in 1992 and remains the journal of the Institute of State and Law of the Academy of Science of the Russian Federation, recently celebrating 85 years of publication.53

53 See ‘Eighty-five years of the journal State and Law’ http://www.igpran.ru/journal/85let/85letGiphp (accessed 1 August 2012).





Pashukanis’ 1924 General Theory


There is, a connection between Pashukanis’ sole publication before the General theory, his 1921 article and the 1924 General theory itself, although Pashukanis did not refer directly to his earlier work. In fact, Maurice Hauriou appears three times54 in the General theory, but this time with approbation – as ‘one of the most astute bourgeois theorists’55 and ‘an astute jurist like Hauriou’.56 And there are many references to the German legal scholars whom Pashukanis read for his doctoral dissertation in Munich and during his time in Berlin.

54 Pashukanis (1983), 122, 123, 134. 55 Ibid, 123. 56 Ibid, 134. However, Pashukanis’ paramount reason for writing this book was not to renew or explain his relationship with Hauriou, but to identify ‘law in its general definition, law as a form’.57 That is, to tackle the question of the nature of law as a materially grounded abstraction. In the next section, I follow a clear thread right through the General theory.

57 Ibid, 68. For Pashukanis, there had been two epochs in which ‘the general concepts of law reached their highest point of development: Rome, with its system of private law, and the seventeenth and eighteenth centuries in Europe’. But only bourgeois society was destined to embody ‘the universal significance of the legal form’.58 He posed his question in the following way: ‘Can law be conceived of as a social relation in the same sense in which Marx called capital a social relation? ‘[emphasis in original].59 Pashukanis insisted that only under capitalism does legal form appear: ‘the possibility of taking up a legal standpoint is linked with the fact that, under commodity production, the most diverse relations approximate the prototype of commercial relation and hence assume legal form’.60 Indeed, he asserted that ‘the commodity form produces the legal form’.61 In a further passage, Pashukanis left no room for doubt: ‘[W]e see how the economic relation in its actual workings is the source of the legal relation, which comes into being only at the moment of dispute. It is dispute, conflict of interest, which creates the legal form, the legal superstructure.’62 Furthermore: ‘It is readily evident that the logic of juridical concepts corresponds to the logic of the social relations of a commodity-producing society.’63

58 Ibid, 71. 59 Ibid, 74. 60 Pashukanis (1983): 82. 61 Ibid, 85. 62 Ibid, 93. 63 Ibid, 96. The legal form was to be found only in private, civil law, while public, constitutional law, was full of confusion:

Yet while civil law, which is concerned with the fundamental, primary level of law, makes use of the concept of subjective rights with complete assurance, application of this concept in public-law theory creates misunderstandings and contradictions at every step. For this reason, the system of civil law is distinguished by its simplicity, clarity and perfection, while theories of constitutional law teem with far-fetched constructs which are so one-sided as to become grotesque. The form of law with its aspect of subjective right is born in a society of isolated bearers of private egotistic interests.64



64 Ibid, 103. Pashukanis was clear that: ‘As a Marxist, I did not set myself the task of constructing a theory of pure jurisprudence, nor could I set myself such a task. From the very first I had a clear idea of the goal … My aim was this: to present a sociological interpretation of the legal form and of the specific categories which express it.’65

65 Ibid, 107. And a few pages later: ‘I merely maintain that property becomes the basis of the legal form only when it becomes something which can be freely disposed of in the market.’66 That is, under capitalism. This point is rammed home still further into the text: ‘Only in commodity production does the abstract legal form see the light: in other words, only there does the general capacity to possess a right become distinguished from concrete legal claims.’67 And ‘Only when bourgeois relations are fully developed does law become abstract in character … At the same time, the norm takes on the logically perfected form of abstract universal law.’68

66 Ibid, 110. 67 Ibid, 118. 68 Pashukanis (1983), 120–21. Pashukanis was conscious of writing in the context of the NEP. Commenting on Karl Marx’s comments in the Critique of the Gotha Programme on the ‘narrow horizon of bourgeois right’ by which, for a time, social relations will continue to be constrained in the transition period to socialism. Pashukanis argued that ‘Marx presupposes a social order in which the means of production are socially owned and in which the producers do not exchange their products. Consequently, he assumes a higher stage of development than the “new economic policy” which we are presently experiencing.’69 And, many pages later, Marx’s ‘horizon’ is once again to be seen: ‘Hence, in our transition period, the legal form as such does not contain within itself those unlimited possibilities which lay before it at the birth of bourgeois capitalist society. On the contrary, the legal form only encompasses us within its narrow horizon for the time being.’70

69 Ibid, 61. 70 Ibid, 133. Thus, Pashukanis risked becoming very repetitive indeed, but he made his point with absolute clarity. In his view, there was no law as such until capitalism, and law, as such, the legal form, can be found only in private law. Introduced into ‘public law’, the law of the constitution, it is the source of endless confusion. More importantly, if the legal form is necessarily to be found only in capitalism, then as a matter of logic there can be no such thing as ‘Soviet law’. Once capitalism has gone, law, like morality, will evaporate.

However, the question is posed straight away: can the form of law to be found in commodity exchange really serve the same role as the commodity, with its dual nature of use value and exchange value, played for Marx in Capital? That is to place law at the same level as the capitalist economy.





Criticisms of the General Theory


The 1983 English edition of the General theory ends with the 1930 evaluation of the volume by the German Marxist, Karl Korsch. Korsch accused Pashukanis of ‘deviating decisively, as he does in countless other instances which permeate his book as a homogenous thread, from the Marxist idea which regards the economic relation as the fundamental one, with the legal relation on the contrary, like the political relation, as derived from it’.71

71 Ibid, 195. From the standpoint of Marxist orthodoxy, Korsch was certainly right. And Pashukanis himself very soon abandoned his somewhat exotic stance.

In the first of several recantations, published in 1930, Pashukanis wrote the following about the genesis of the General Theory:

It is clear that much which was written in the first years of NEP deserves criticism and suffers from obvious anachronisms and now and then simply mistakes … But the question is not only that of particular formulations. The question concerns some defects of a general character. This was the overestimation of the role and significance of market relations which was without doubt characteristic of my first work. It is impermissible to hide from view the fact that this book was written at a time when the collective of Marxist legal scholars had not come together. It was written when I was alone, and it could not be exposed to the process of critical re-working. It was written finally, before the publication of Lenin’s notebooks on dialectics and on the works of Marx which were published in the ‘Archive’. It appeared before the discussions with the ‘mechanists’, before the discussions in the fields of political economy, literature etc. Therefore it was completely natural, that the book, which was written in 1923, and prepared still earlier in 1920–1921, displays defects, when we look at it from our higher present day theoretical and methodological point of view.72



72 Pashukanis (1930), 26. Bob Fine has criticised Pashukanis, pointing out that: ‘Whereas Marx derived law from relations of commodity production, Pashukanis derived it from commodity exchange.’73 This, according to Fine, leads Pashukanis to a conclusion that was plainly wrong:

73 Fine (2002), 157.
