Chapter 5

Soviet international law and self-determination




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-6

In this chapter, I trace the trajectory of Soviet international legal theory and practice after the demise of Pashukanis. As in previous chapters, I do this by highlighting the particular issues of interest to me within the scope of this book. So this chapter does not pretend to present a complete narrative or description of the topic. But the reader may well find a great deal more in the next pages than in most summaries of the nearly 70 years’ experience of Soviet public international law. I have written on this topic previously,1 but the material contained in this chapter is the deepest I have yet dug into the questions of Soviet public international law.

1 Bowring (2008a), Chapter 1; Bowring (2008b).





The career of Yevgeniy Korovin


First, however, I examine some features of Soviet international law during the period before World War II. In the previous chapter, I mentioned Pashukanis’ contemporary and rival, Yevgeniy Korovin. Korovin’s two major works were International law of the transition period (1924) and Contemporary public international law (1927), but he was very much in Pashukanis’ shadow, and in 1935 admitted the correctness of Pashukanis’s position and his own errors.2

2 Letter of 5 May 1935 No. 4 Sovetskoye gosudarstvo (Soviet State), 171. However, he became better known to the English-speaking scholarly audience through translations of his work in US journals.

In 1927 Korovin published an article in Soviet Law on ‘Soviet treaties and international law’. A translation into English was published in the American Journal of International Law in 1928.3 Korovin’s emphasis was on the large number of individual agreements entered into between the USSR and individual ‘capitalist states’. Korovin declared that:

3 Korovin (1928). All the attempts at Genoa, at The Hague, and at Geneva, all these endeav-ours to effect a collective and universal agreement between the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics and other states have remained vain. The tower of Babel of world-wide unity is left in ruins.4



4 Korovin (1928), 753. This is plainly a reference to the League of Nations, which the USSR finally joined in 1934.

Korovin’s article proceeded to address ‘Ratification of treaties’, noting that Soviet law repudiated secret diplomacy and also a separation of powers by checks and balances (for example the US signature by the executive and ratification by the legislature) and treated ratification solely as a technical function of the government. Next, he explained the Soviet position on ‘The most favoured nation clauses’, which were included in most economic and commercial treaties of the USSR. An example was the agreement with Britain of 16 March 1921 – albeit one that was denounced by Britain in 1926.5 Korovin noted that ‘the national economy of the USSR in passing from “war Communism” to the “new economic policy”’ is enlarging the field of application of the most favoured nation clause. Finally, he turned to ‘The rebus sic stantibus clause’ and the ‘unilateral repudiation of treaties looked upon as superannuated’ – in this case, the memorial presented by the Soviet delegation at Genoa on 20 April 1922:

5 Korovin (1928), 755. The revolution of 1917, having completely destroyed all the old relationships, economic, social and political, and having replaced the old social order (class divisions) by the new social order, the sovereignty of an insurgent people, turning over the power of the Russian State to a new social class, did by this fact break the succession of those civil obligations which were component elements of the economic relations of the social order now extinct.6



6 Korovin (1928), 763. In 1933–1934 an English translation of an article by Korovin, published in Russian in 1929, ‘The problems of the international recognition of the Union of Socialist Soviet Republics in practice’ appeared in the Iowa Law Review.7 This article started with discussion of the ‘constitutive’ and ‘declaratory’ theories of state recognition and an approving reference to the ‘well-known Soviet jurist, E. Pachoucanis [sic]’.8 Korovin cited Pashukanis’ entry on the topic in the final volume of the Encyclopedia of state and law.9 Pashukanis there defined recognition as a: ‘formal act or a conclusive action of one or several states, expressing on their part the readiness to consider any fact as having a juristic meaning’.

7 Korovin (1933–34). 8 Korovin (1933–1934), 259. 9 Pashukanis (1927). Pashukanis also considered that the construction of recognition, as a declarative act, was: ‘the reflection of the epoch, when the bourgeoisie struggled for the national liberty and the formation of national states’.

The theories of constitutive recognition, by the same token, reflected the practice of ‘the imperialist policy of the greater power’. Korovin cited the position of the British government through the speech of R. Cecil on 24 November 1917: ‘There can be no question of diplomatic relations with them; nobody intends recognising such a government.’10 At that time all the ‘greater powers’ drew a distinction between Russia as a state and the Russian government. In the famous 12 May 1921 judgment in Luther v Sagor,11 the English Court of Appeal decided the case on the basis of a note by the British Foreign Office:

10 Korovin (1933–1934), 260. 11 [1921] 3 K.B. 532 [1920. A. 1861]. His Majesty’s Government recognises the Soviet Government as the de facto Government of Russia, and that the Soviet Government had succeeded to the Provisional Government (which had been recognised by His Majesty’s Government on 13 December 1917).



Circumstances changed. In a note of 2 February 1924, the British government informed the Soviet government that ‘they recognise the Union of the Soviet-Socialist Republics as government de jure of these territories of the former Russian empire which are under their rule.’12

12 Korovin (1933–1934), 261–62. Korovin’s other works during this period included (according to Russian biographical sources) Razoruzheniye (Disarmament) in 1930, Katolitisim kak factor sovremennoi mirovoi politiki (Catholicism as a factor in contemporary world politics) in 1931 and Yaponiya i mezhdunarodnoye pravo (Japan and international law) in 1936.

Pashukanis was, as I noted in the last chapter, tried and executed in 1937. His work was immediately subjected to severe criticism as outlined by his former student (but also friend of Korovin) John Hazard in 1938.13 His new critics declared that Pashukanis had attempted to prove that Soviet legal forms were merely successors of bourgeois legal forms. This was, in fact, they argued, an attempt to subordinate Soviet policy to the practice of bourgeois international law. This was linked to the attempt of Trotsky and Zinoviev to bring the Soviet system down to the level of the capitalist system. It was necessary to recognise that the USSR had developed a new form of international law, which had already borne fruit in the increasing number of non-aggression pacts and the internationalising of conflicts.14

13 Hazard (1938). 14 Hazard (1938), 249–50. Korovin and other scholars of international law were now required to emphasise the struggle for peace waged by the USSR and, contrary to Pashukanis, show how this struggle rested on the sanctity of treaties and the observance of international obligations.15

15 Hazard (1938), 252. In 1946 the American Journal of International Law published another article by Korovin, ‘The Second World War and international law’.16 This article, written in the immediate aftermath of World War II, started by citing the pre-revolutionary legal scholar Nikolay Korkunov, cited by me in Chapter 2, on Desnitskiy,17 for his definition of international law as ‘the sum-total of legal norms guaranteeing international protection of the democratic minimum’.18 That is, an impeccably liberal definition.

16 Korovin (1946). 17 Korkunov (1915). 18 Korovin (1946), 743. However, Korovin mentioned two additional factors, of great importance for the remainder of this chapter. First, he addressed the need, following the war, for a new and more profound treatment of international sovereignty, which is ‘the expression in international law of the principle of national self-determination’.19 From this, he turned to the question of subjects of international law, especially international recognition of national resistance movements.20 He lauded the concern of the USSR for ‘the fate of the peoples of the colonial countries and satisfying their national demands, of the sovereignty and independence of small peoples and states’.21

19 Korovin (1946), 743. 20 Korovin (1946), 744. 21 Korovin (1946), 754. Today the Soviet theory and practice of international law, if it is the subject of any consideration at all, is usually dismissed as a purely historical example of an extreme species of positivism and of no contemporary interest. Most often it is ignored. For example, in his article ‘What should international lawyers learn from Karl Marx?’,22 Martti Koskenniemi does not mention Soviet international law at all. Even an avowed Marxist scholar of international law does little more. In his article23 ‘An outline of a Marxist course on public international law’,24 B.S. Chimni contrasts the definition of ‘treaties’ in what he terms ‘mainstream international law scholarship’:

22 Koskenniemi (2004). 23 In the same special issue of a progressive international law journal, the Leiden 24 Chimni (2004). [W]ith the definitions offered by the Soviet scholars Korovin and Pashukanis: ‘Every international agreement is the expression of an established social order, with a certain balance of collective interests’;25 ‘A treaty obligation is nothing other than a special form of the concretization of economic and political relationships’.26 These definitions, through drawing in extra-textual reality, offer greater insight into the meaning of a treaty than the formal definition offered by MILS. They refer us to both the fact of an established (capitalist) social order and to its concretization as economic and political rules embodying a certain balance of collective (class) interests.27



25 Korovin (1928), 763(emphasisadded). 26 Pashukanis (1980), 181 (emphasisadded). 27 Chimni (2004), n. 3, n. 12. However, these authors are not introduced, save as ‘Soviet scholars’; no context at all is given; neither is the fact that they were rivals. Soviet international law even in this Marxist account barely exists; in the standard genre of the history of international law, it is mentioned only to be dismissed.





The contradictions of Soviet international legal theory


I wish to take a very different position. I seek to argue in this chapter that the contradictions of Soviet international law have generated some of the most important propositions and principles of contemporary international law and are of continuing relevance.

Western scholars are familiar with what is generally termed the ‘Marxist– Leninist theory’ in international law and with its standard characterisation.28 In a recent comparison of Soviet and ‘New Haven’29 theories, Ian Scobbie refers to ‘the pre-perestroika Soviet theory of international law propounded by G.I. Tunkin’, which he sees, like New Haven, as a theory that sets out ‘to bolster and justify the external projection of the political values of the … Soviet Union’.30 For Scobbie, ‘Soviet theory has the aim of realizing proletarian internationalism, and thus the global triumph of socialism.’ Furthermore, on the basis of historical materialism, or as Scobbie puts it ‘objective grounds of societal development’, that triumph is historically inevitable.

28 Scobbie (2010) ‘Wicked heresies or legitimate perspectives? Theory and international law’ in Malcolm Evans (ed.) International law (3rd edn, 2010), 58–94. 29 ‘New Haven’ (based at Yale) was the dominant US theoretical school after World War II, led by McDougal and Laswell, who trained generations of international lawyers, as policy-makers ‘for the ever more complete achievement of the democratic values that constitute the professed ends of American Polity’. Laswell and McDougal (1943), 206. 30 Scobbie (2010), 70. There can be no surprise that Scobbie refers only to Tunkin. William Butler’s translation of Tunkin’s textbook31 made available to a Western audience the only substantial Soviet text in English on international law.32 Tunkin, born in 1906, died aged 87 in 1993,33 while completing the last edition of his Theory of international law and having just submitted an article – on customary international law – to the European Journal of International Law. Here he wrote of the attempt ‘to create a new world order based on the rule of law’.34

31 Scobbie (2010), 77. 32 Tunkin (1974). 33 I met him at Moscow State University a year before his death; he was very lively, but completely ignorant of the fact that in 1991 the USSR had ratified the First Optional Protocol to the UN’s International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights, giving those within USSR, and then Russian, jurisdiction the right to complain to the UN’s Human Rights Committee, in the event of a violation of their rights. 34 Tunkin (1993), 534. Scobbie comments that Soviet theory was structurally highly traditional and firmly rooted in Marxist–Leninist theory to the extent that ‘at time, it seems simply to amount to taking the dogma for a walk’.35 This was certainly true of Tunkin’s textbook. It was also strikingly conservative, recognising only rules and state consent to rules: as Damrosch and Müllerson explained it, Soviet theory treated ‘the existing corpus of international law as a system of sufficiently determinate principles and norms which all states are obliged to observe in their mutual relations’.36 As a direct consequence, Soviet theory rejected the ‘general principles of law recognised by the civilised nations’ referred to in the Statute of the International Court of Justice as one of the sources of international law.37

35 Scobbie (2010), 74. 36 Damrosch and Mullerson (1995), 9. 37 Article 38 (1)(c) of the Statute of the International Court of Justice. The existence of two opposed social systems meant that the only norms of ‘customary’ or ‘general’ international law could be those that were neither socialist nor capitalist: as Tunkin put it: ‘only those international legal norms which embrace the agreement of all states are norms of contemporary general international law.’38 Thus, Soviet theory recognised only treaties and custom – narrowly defined as above – as sources of international law.

38 Tunkin (1974), 250–51. The US scholar Alwyn Freeman (1910–1983),39 writing at the height of the Cold War, in 1968, also asserted that Soviet international law embraced:

39 Freeman was an editor of the American Journal on International Lawfrom 1955 to 1972, worked on international claims cases while in the US State Department and served in the Army Judge Advocate General’s Office in World War II, on the staff of the Senate Committee on Foreign Relations and as an official of the IAEA. [T]he most extreme form of positivism … The Soviet brand of positivism is much more restricted, much narrower, and is, in sum, a rejection of a great portion of international legal principles … Soviet positivism has been distinguished by the exclusion of customary practice as a source of international obligations. It views international law as embracing only those principles to which states have expressly consented through an international agreement or have otherwise manifested their acquiescence.40



40 Freeman (1968), 713. Indeed, writing in 1948, at the time of his frenetic activity in the United Nations as leader of the Soviet delegation, the notorious Andrey Vyshinsky41 wrote that the ‘Soviet theory of international law regards the treaty, resting on the principles of sovereign equality of peoples and the respect for mutual interests and rights as the basic source of international law. This secures for international law and its institutions full moral as well as juridical force since at their base will lie the obligations agreed to and voluntarily assumed by nations.’42

41 Andrey Vyshinsky was born in Odessa, Russia, on 28 November 1883. As a young man, he joined the Social Democratic Party. In the 1903 split, he sided with the Mensheviks. Vyshinsky became a lawyer and after the October Revolution he joined the Bolsheviks. He taught law at Moscow State University until becoming a state prosecutor. Between 1934 and 1938 Vyshinsky was the leading prosecutor in the ‘show trials’ of Stalin’s opponents. In 1940 he was given the responsibility of managing the (illegal) occupation of Latvia. He also helped establish communist rule in Romania before becoming Soviet Foreign Minister in March, 1949. He survived the purge that followed the death of Joseph Stalin in 1953 and continued as the Soviet representative in the United Nations. Vyshinsky died in New York on 22 November 1954. 42 Vyshinsky (1948a), 22, cited in Triska (1958), 713.





Sovereignty and self-determination


There is, however, a point at which this conservatism shows another, opposite side. Freeman did not fail to notice it, in his discussion of sovereignty. He explained that the Soviets:

[R]etain the classical, strict conception of states alone as the subjects of international law, with a rigid insistence on sovereignty in its most extreme form, a form which must deny the paramount nature of international law over national law. They do, however, recognise an exception in favour of peoples fighting for ‘national liberation’.43



43 Freeman (1968), 716. It is very odd, however, that Freeman did not notice the basis for such a claim: the right of peoples to self-determination. This ‘principle’ had become a ‘right’ as the common first article of the two international covenants of 1966 – the ‘International Bill of Rights’.

Scobbie notes in detail the so-called ‘Brezhnev doctrine’, that relations between socialist states are not based on ‘peaceful co-existence’, but on ‘proletarian internationalism’, which justified the invasions of Hungary in 1956, Czechoslovakia in 1968 and Afghanistan in 1980.44 But, curiously, he says nothing about the application of the ‘right of peoples to self-determination’ to Soviet support for the national liberation struggles of three decades from World War II.

44 Scobbie (2010), 77. In the third section of this chapter, therefore, I analyse the origins of the Soviet doctrine of the right of nations to self-determination. It should be noted that in Russian as in many other languages, ‘nation’ and ‘people’ are practically synonymous.

For Lenin, at least, self-determination was not a mere slogan, but a principle he put into practice with immediate effect within the former Russian Empire following the Bolshevik Revolution. I examine Lenin’s practice in Chapter 7. According to Igor Blishchenko (1930–2000), one of the best Soviet scholars of international law,45 in a text published, ironically, in 1968, the year that the USSR crushed the ‘Czech Spring’, Lenin’s Decree on Peace of 26 October 1917,46 for the first time extended the principle of the right to self-determination to all peoples, thereby discarding the imperialist distinction between ‘civilised’ and ‘uncivilised’ nations.47

45 I worked with Blishchenko for a number of years, in particular on the draft of the Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court; for a touching obituary by the International Committee of the Red Cross, see http://www.icrc.org/Web/eng/siteeng0.nsf/html/57JREV (accessed 22 October 2012). 46 Website:http://www.firstworldwar.com/source/decreeonpeace.htm (accessed 19 February 2013). 47 Blishchenko (1968), 69. In his article, Blishchenko moved next to answer a series of Western scholars who argued that the Decree was entirely hypocritical, first, having no application to peoples within the USSR and, second, having been applied only to Finland in the former Tsarist Empire. He pointed to the substantial autonomy, if short of secession, enjoyed by Union and autonomous republics in the USSR in accordance with Article 17 of its Constitution.

More importantly, he underlined the extent to which the principle was indeed put into practice by Lenin in the early years of the USSR. What he failed to point out, not surprisingly in 1968, is the fact that one of Lenin’s most bitter struggles with Stalin concerned independence for Georgia.48

48 See Lewin (2005). Writing in 1968,49 Blishchenko showed that the early Soviet government was entirely consistent in implementing self-determination. On 4 (17) December 1917 the Soviet government recognised the right to self-determination of Ukraine. In response to the request of the Finnish government, the Soviet of Peoples’ Commissars on 18 (31) December 1917 resolved to go to the Central Executive Committee with a proposal to recognise Finland’s independence. In fact, it was the Whites, seeking to restore the Empire, who opposed Finnish independence. By a Decree of 29 December 1917 (11 January 1918) the right of the people of ‘Turkish Armenia’ to self-determination was recognised. In answer to a request from the government of Soviet Estland, on 7 December 1918 Lenin signed a decree on recognition of the independence of Estonia, Latvia and Lithuania.

49 Blishchenko (1998), 71; see also, on national liberation movements; Baratashvili (1967). On 5 February 1919 the Presidium of the All-Union Central Executive of Soviet Russia insisted, in a principled manner, that in implementing the principle of self-determination, the issue was resolved by the self-determining nation itself, by the people itself. The dictatorship of the proletariat was not a condition for self-determination, which applied equally to bourgeois independence movements. Thus, the Soviet government recognised the republics of Bukhara and Khorezm, which were not socialist.

Blishchenko celebrated the break-up of the colonial system of imperialism and the broad national liberation movements in Asia, Africa and Latin America after World War II, which had posited the right of peoples to self-determination with new force. He asserted, with reason, that the USSR had done everything to ensure that the right became one of the fundamental principles of contemporary international law. This was due in part the work of the Soviet delegation at the 1945 San Francisco Conference,50 which drafted the Charter of the UN, as a result of which Article 2(1) of the Charter refers to ‘respect for the principle of equal rights and self-determination of peoples’.51

50 United Nations Conference on International Organisation, 1945, Vol. III, 622; see also Tunkin (1970), 67. The Conference brought together delegates from 50 Allied nations and took place from 25 April to 26 June 1945 in San Francisco. The delegates reviewed and rewrote the Dumbarton Oaks agreements. The convention resulted in the creation of the United Nations Charter, which was opened for signature on 26 June 1945. 51 Blishchenko (1968), 75. The UN’s Universal Declaration on Human Rights was drafted in 1947–1948 just as the European empires began to break up. As Morsink points out,52 in 1914 Lenin calculated that more than half of the world’s population lived in colonies, which covered three-quarters of the world’s territory, a calculation that was still roughly correct at the end of the 1940s. Two leading participants, Malik from Lebanon and Romulo from the Philippines, were from countries that became independent in 1946, together with Syria. India, Burma, Pakistan gained their independence in 1947, together with Ceylon in 1948. India and Pakistan were both active players in the drafting process.

52 Morsink (1999), 96. Andrei Zhdanov, Stalin’s favourite, delivered the key speech at the founding meeting in September 1947 of the Cominform (Communist Information Bureau) and announced that the world was divided into two camps, ‘the imperialist and anti-democratic camp’ led by the United States and the ‘democratic and anti-imperialist camp’ led by the USSR. He asserted that there was a ‘crisis of the colonial system’ and that ‘the peoples of the colonies no longer wish to live in the old way. The ruling classes of the metropolitan countries can no longer govern the colonies on the old lines.’53

53 Cited in Morsink (1999), 97. Cassese relates that the 1944 Dumbarton Oaks Proposals,54 the basis for the UN Charter, did not contain any reference to self-determination, but this was reconsidered at the end of April 1945, at the UN Conference on International Organisation in San Francisco – at the insistence of the USSR.55 Thus, a draft was presented referring to ‘respect for the principle of equal rights and self-determination of peoples’.

54 The Dumbarton Oaks Conference sat in Washington DC from 21 August 1944 to 7 October 1944. 55 Cassese (1995), 38. As Tunkin noted in 1970, at the 2nd session of the UN General Assembly in 1947 the Soviet delegation proposed an article for the Universal Declaration on Human Rights as follows:

Each people and each nation has the right to national self-determination. A state which has responsibility for the administration of self-determining territories, including colonies, must ensure the realisation of that right, guided by the principles and goals of the United Nations in relation to the peoples of such territories.



However, under pressure from the colonial powers this proposal was rejected, with the result that the principle of self-determination does not appear in the UDHR.56

56 Tunkin (1970), 69. Dmitriy Grushkin of the Russian human rights organisation ‘Memorial’ noted57 that one key factor at the end of World War II was the strengthened role of the USSR and the appearance of a whole bloc of states oriented towards it. Further, a bipolar system took shape in international relations in which the contradictory interests of the two sides could be clearly traced. Third, the role of the mass character of politics significantly grew during World War II: 110 million people from 72 states took part. It was a war of peoples, not of governments. Fourth, in place of the League of Nations a global international organisation appeared with real resources and much more effective instruments. The UN sought to create on the basis of new principles (human rights, self-determination, sovereign equality of states) a powerful and effective international legal system. In the documents adopted by the UN, the idea of self-determination received new support, but also aroused bitter disputes. However, the USSR, with the support of the socialist countries and the newly independent states of Asia campaigned for the establishment of a practically unlimited right to self-determination of colonial and dependent countries and peoples.

57 Grushkin (1997), 10. At the 10th session of the UNGA in 1955 the those who opposed of including the right to self-determination into the covenants argued that the UN Charter only refers to a ‘principle’ and not a ‘right’ of peoples to self-determination and that in various instruments the principle is interpreted in different ways. To the extent that the right to self-determination is a collective right, then it was inconsistent to include it in a document setting out the rights of individuals. Supporters, however, responded that despite the fact that the right to self-determination is collective, it affects each person and that to remove it would be the precondition for limiting human rights. Furthermore, a state accepting the UN Charter and recognising it, must respect the ‘principle of self-determination’ and the ‘right’ flowing from it. The latter point of view triumphed and the new ‘right’ found its way into the common Article 1 of both the International Covenants on Civil and Political Rights and Social, Economic and Cultural Rights, respectively.58

58 Grushkin (1997), 12. In this context, there is interest in a rather scornful article in 1955 in the American Journal of International Law by W.W. Kulski of Syracuse University, ‘The Soviet interpretation of international law’. Kulski reviewed five issues of Sovetskoye Gosudarstvo i Pravo (Soviet State and Law) from 1954 and 1955. The discussion in these issues had been initiated by Korovin in his article ‘Some fundamental questions of contemporary international law’.59 While finding all manner of contradiuctions in Korovin’s text, Kulski did note Korovin’s position on recognition – ‘he says that the socialist states immediately grant recognition to a new state because of their respect for self-determination, while the capitalist states delay recognition to safeguard their former financial and commercial privileges.’60 Kulski argued that, for Korovin, ‘Sovereignty thus becomes a slogan of self-determination and loses its legal meaning as an attribute of statehood.’61 Another of Kulski’s targets was Professor S.B. Krylov. Krylov was subjected to irony because he accepted the existence of two kinds of subject of international law: states and dependent nationalities, which are subjects of international law in their own right. ‘He seems to grant to the latter subjects of international law only one right, namely, that of self-determination.’62 Finally, Kulski subjected V.V. Evgenyev to mockery for the following:

59 Korovin (1954). 60 Kulski (1955), 519. 61 Kulski (1955), 521. 62 Kulski (1955), 522. State sovereignty is the independence of the state of any other state; this independence amounts to the right to decide freely and according to its own judgment all its domestic and foreign affairs without interference on the part of other states. State sovereignty embodies the right to self-determination, the latter being the right to decide freely all questions relating to the national fate without, however, infringing in any way upon the rights of other nations. A nation has the right to choose autonomy as a form of its statehood or enter with other nations into a federation or finally to secede completely, because each nation is sovereign and all nations are equal.63



63 Kulski (1955), 525;Evgenyev (1955), 77. Kulski could not foresee that the right he mocked would become in a few years a full-fledged right in international law, an extraordinary achievement of Soviet diplomacy.





The triumph of Soviet diplomacy


Heather Wilson wrote64 that the admission of 17 newly independent states at the opening of the 15th session of the General Assembly had a decisive effect on the UN. On 23 September 1960 the Soviet Union, grasping the opportunity presented by this dramatic development, requested the addition of a ‘declaration on the granting of independence to colonial peoples and countries and peoples’ to the agenda.65 This was a truly climactic moment in the development of contemporary international law.

64 Wilson (1988), 67–8. 65 UN Doc. A/4501, 23 September 1960. It was the USSR that submitted to the 15th Session of the UN General Assembly the draft of the historic Resolution 1514 (XV) of 14 December 1960, the ‘Declaration on the granting of independence to colonial countries and peoples’. This revolutionary resolution aroused a whole wave of reactions and protests, but was adopted nonetheless. This document noted the connection between the right of peoples to self-determination and individual freedoms.

Following on the heels of Resolution 1514 (XV) came a whole series of documents of a similar type: Resolution 1803 (XVII) of 14 December 1962 on ‘Inalienable sovereignty in relation to natural resources’; Resolution 2105 (XX) of 20 December 1965 ‘On the realisation of the Declaration on the granting of independence to colonial countries and peoples’ – the General Assembly recognised the legitimacy of the struggle of colonial peoples against colonial domination in the exercise of their right to self-determination and independence and it invited all states to provide material and moral support to national liberation movements in colonial territories.

Earlier I cited Alwyn Freeman’s contention in 1968 that ‘Soviet positivism has been distinguished by the exclusion of customary practice as a source of international obligations.’ But he seemed to have missed the article by G.I. Tunkin in the California Law Review in 1961, ‘Remarks on the juridical nature of customary norms of international law’.66 Tunkin’s thesis was, in part, that not one norm of international law has appeared as a result of international practice that had no interruption. Taking the principle of non-interference, he observed that this principle appeared for the first time in the eighteenth century in the context of the bourgeois revolutions. It was often violated in the nineteenth century, but nevertheless gradually became a generally recognised principle of international law.67 In a similar way, he continued, the Soviet state had advanced the principle of banning aggressive wars and treating such wars as crimes, the principle of self-determination of nations, the principle of peaceful coexistence, and others.

66 Tunkin (1961). 67 Tunkin (1961), 420. And, as we know now, the right of peoples to self-determination became a legal right in the ICCPR and ICESCR, achieved the status of customary law in the 1970 Declaration on Principles of International Law Concerning Friendly Relations and Co-operation among States in accordance with the Charter of the United Nations (General Assembly Resolution 2625). In its Judgment on the Case concerning East Timor (Portugal v Australia) of 30 June 1995 the UN’s International Court of Justice held at para. 29:

Portugal’s assertion that the right of peoples to self-determination, as it evolved from the Charter and from United Nations practice, has an erga omnes character, is irreproachable. The principle of self-determination of peoples has been recognized by the United Nations Charter and in the jurisprudence of the Court (see Legal Consequences for States of the Continued Presence of South Africa in Namibia (South West Africa) notwithstanding Security Council Resolution 276 (1970), Advisory Opinion, I. C. J. Reports 1971, pp. 31–32, paras. 52–53; Western Sahara, Advisory Opinion, I. C. J. Reports 1975, pp. 3–33, paras. 54–59); it is one of the essential principles of contemporary international law.68



68 At http://www.icj-cij.org/docket/files/84/6949.pdf (accessed 24 October 2012). Erga omnes means that all states without exception have a vital interest is seeing that the right is vindicated.

And the ICJ’s Advisory Opinion of 9 July 2004 on the Legal Consequences of the Construction of a Wall in the Occupied Palestinian Territory 69 repeated its view as to the status of the right.

69 At http://www.icj-cij.org/docket/files/131/1671.pdf (accessed 24 October 2012). In the 1966 covenants on human rights (the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights – ICCPR – and International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights – ICESCR), which to begin with were developed as a single document, it was decided that the provision on self-determination be included on the basis that:

it ‘is the source or essential foundation for other human rights, since there cannot be authentic realisation of individual rights without realisation of the right to self-determination’;

in drafting the Covenants the realisation and protection of the principles and goals of the UN Charter must be taken into account, including the principles of equal rights and self-determination of peoples;

a series of provisions of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights are directly connected to the right to self-determination;

if the right was not included in the Covenants, they would be incomplete and ineffective.70



70 Grushkin (1997), 12, citing A. Kristesky (1981) Pravo narodov na camoopredeleniye: istoricheskoye i sovremennoye pazvitiye (Right of peoples to self-determination: historical and contemporary development) (New York: ECOSOC). Writing in 1970, Tunkin also pointed out that if in 1919 as many as 64 per cent of the population of the planet lived in colonies and semi-colonies, then at the start of 1969 only 1 per cent of humanity remained in colonies. It was on this basis that both the international covenants have a common Article 1, on the right in international law of peoples to self-determination. This was a remarkable achievement by the USSR and its allies in the decolonised world.71

71 Tunkin (1970), 70. The success of the USSR and its allies in the 1960s had momentous consequences for the legal and political process of decolonisation. Later resolutions of the UNGA ensured that the so-called ‘national liberation movements’72 were recognised as the ‘sole legitimate representatives’ of the relevant peoples. In other words, ex-territorial social and political organisations were, in fact, made equal to sovereign subjects of international law. Examples were the Palestine Liberation Organisation (PLO), the South West African Peoples Organisation (SWAPO), the ANC (African National Congress) and PAC (Pan African Congress). In 1973 the UN declared that it recognised SWAPO as the ‘sole authentic representative of the people of Namibia’. And in 1974 the PLO was recognised by the majority of member states of the UN as the lawful representative of the Palestinians, with corresponding status at the UN.

72 SeeGolan (1988). There are writers, such as Christopher Quaye, who ignore the Soviet role in promoting the legal right to self-determination or supporting the national liberation movements.73 However, Galia Golan, although seemingly unaware of the international law dimension, wrote in the context of national liberation movements that: ‘The term preferred by the Soviets [to independence] as an overall, all-inclusive type of objective was self-determination.’74 Her book demonstrates the huge resources devoted by the USSR to support of all kinds for a very wide range of national liberation movements in the Third World. Tables she prepared list 43 movements in 26 countries, with 13 instruments of ‘Soviet behaviour’.75 Roger Kanet noted that ‘Soviet trade with the developing nations increased more than eleven times from 1955 to 1970’. In 1970 it increased an additional 15.7 per cent.76 Furthermore, Bhabani Sen Gupta pointed out that ‘in cultivating friendly, viable forces, the Soviet union has persistently tried to satisfy some of the felt needs of the power elites of Third World societies. In South Asia, they have come forward to provide aid for industrialisation programs in India, for which the Indians could not secure resources either domestically of from Western nations.’77

73 Quaye (1991). 74 Golan (1988), 136. 75 Golan (1988), 262–67. 76 Kanet (1974), 1. 77 Gupta (1974), 123. I would contend, contrary to these authors, that it was not as a result of Soviet propaganda, but through the logic of the new international law, developed through the efforts of the USSR and its allies, that a people with the right to self-determination faced with aggressive attempts to deny that right enjoyed the right of self-defence under Article 51 of the Charter and was in all respects be considered a subject of international law. Thus, Portugal was at that time waging war against the peoples of Angola and Mozambique; those peoples were therefore victims of aggression and enjoyed the right of self-defence and third-party states had the right and duty to come to their assistance.78 G.I. Tunkin, a year earlier, in a more formal article, defending the dubious concept of ‘proletarian internationalism’, also linked the ‘struggle for international peace and security’ with the ‘struggle for the freedom and independence of peoples’, with reference especially to Resolution 1514 (XV).79

78 Blishchenko (1968), 76–7. 79 Tunkin (1967), 144–46. 1968 was not only the year of the Soviet invasion of Czechoslovakia, but also a crucial moment in the US war in Vietnam. The invasion of Czechoslovakia took place against the background of the emergence of a new ‘socialist international law’, with a new approach to traditional concepts of sovereignty. G.I. Tunkin published a revised second edition of his textbook International law.80 According to him, it appeared to commentators in the United States that the new Soviet position could be dated back to Pashukanis’ conclusion in the 1920s that the Soviet Union could and did utilise generally accepted norms of domestic and international law both in the administration of the state affairs and in conducting relations with foreign states. Through this practice, it gave the bourgeois norms a new socialist content.81

80 Tunkin (1970). 81 Hazard (1971), 143. Dealing with the Czechoslovak events, Tunkin argued that these were a logical extension of the concept already well developed and applied in Hungary in 1956. This was the legal prevention of inroads by capitalist influences into a socialist state.82 The international law framework is provided through an analysis of the concept of sovereignty. Tunkin noted that both general and socialist international law respected the concept of ‘sovereignty’, but concluded that respect is not the same thing in the two systems.83 Socialist states would continue to insist on respect for the principle as developed in general international law when speaking of the relationships between themselves and capitalist states so as to prevent capitalist states from intervening in the internal affairs of socialist states, but the concept of sovereignty had evolved within the conceptual framework of ‘proletarian internationalism’ as regards the mutual relationships of socialist states. His translator, William Butler, commented: ‘The Soviet invasion of Czechoslovakia plainly was a difficult moment for his approach to international law, and his treatment of a “socialist international law” impressed, rightly or wrongly, as something less than enthusiastic.’84

82 Tunkin (1970), 493, cited in Hazard (1971), 145. 83 Tunkin (1970), 495. 84 Butler (2002), 394. Tunkin’s arguments should be contrasted with what, in the same year, the US scholar Alwyn Freeman was able to write:

In the years following World War II increasing interest has been evidenced in the extent to which Soviet theory and practice may have influenced the development of the law of nations. This is to be expected in view of the prominence and power which the USSR has come to enjoy in the world community.85



85 Freeman (1968), 710–11. Freeman denounced what he saw as a ‘political dogma dressed in treacherous legal trappings’, namely the official Soviet doctrine of ‘peaceful coexistence’. He referred, as do so many American scholars of the period, as well as President Kennedy in his post-inauguration speeches, to an alleged address by Khrushchev to a Soviet Communist Party audience on 6 January 1961.86 In one account:

86 Quoted in the American Bar Association (1964) Peaceful coexistence: a communist blueprint for victory, 14. Soviet Premier Nikita Khrushchev delivered a speech behind closed doors in which he asserted that ‘a mighty upsurge of anti-imperialist, national-liberation revolutions’ was sweeping through the ‘third world.’ He went on to say that ‘Communists fully and unreservedly support such just wars… of national liberation.’87



87 Speed (2005). The impact of Khrushchev’s words was felt in the USA itself and in its subsequent policy:

The speech, published in the Soviet press just two days before the newly elected President John F. Kennedy took his oath of office, had a profound effect on the new administration which regarded it as a portent of wars to come. Kennedy and his advisers concluded that the Cold War was entering a new phase which would take place in the ‘third world,’ and would be characterized by guerrilla wars. Accordingly, they sought to improve the nation’s ability to conduct counter insurgency warfare by dramatically expanding the Army’s Special Forces or ‘green berets.’ Before Kennedy’s assassination in Dallas in 1963, he had dispatched over 16,000 of them to South Viet Nam in order to engage in just such a conflict. The war for the ‘third world,’ and a new phase of the Cold War had gotten under way in earnest.88



88 Speed (2005). This address may well be apocryphal; it has proved impossible for me to track down a definite reference. But there is every reason to believe that its effect was as described.

It had its effect on the scholars too. For Freeman, while accommodations of mutually acceptable principles were possible in 1968, no progress in international law was possible until the ‘Soviet Union is prepared to abjure its messianic and compulsive espousal of the doctrine of world revolution’.89 Freeman was of course writing at the height of the Vietnam War: he expresses outrage that the public opinion barrage orchestrated by the USSR ‘actually inhibited the United States from using tear gas where such use was in the interest of humane treatment of the civilian population.’90

89 Freeman (1968), 722. 90 Freeman (1968), 720.





Abandoning self-determination


The leading Soviet scholars were, in the end, obliged to abandon both positivism and the revolutionary content of self-determination. In 1989 Rein Müllerson,91 then Head of the Department of International Law at the Institute of State and Law of the Academy of Sciences of the USSR, was one of the first Soviet scholars to write on human rights and after 1991 was appointed a professor of law first at the London School of Economics then at King’s College London, contributed an article on ‘Sources of international law: new tendencies in Soviet thinking’.92 In his view, it was still possible to speak of a Soviet doctrine of international law, with some reservations.93 He explained that ‘our legal doctrine has many features in common with positivist legal thought in the sense that positivists consider norms of international law to be the creation of the will of states or other subjects of international law’. Soviet scholars also considered it of paramount importance to investigate ‘extralegal factors’ and the ‘entire social environment’.94 He strongly criticised the New Haven school – Lasswell, McDougal and Michael Reisman.

91 Müllerson has had an extraordinary career. He is an ethnic Estonian, although he was born in Leningrad on 23 March 1944. In 1972 he became a member of the Communist Party of the USSR and from 1978 to 1987 taught at Moscow State University. Between 1987 and 1991 he was the Head of the International Law Department of the Institute of State and Law of the Academy of Sciences in Moscow and adviser to the Chairman of the Supreme Soviet of the USSR (Mikhail Gorbachev). From 1988 to 1992 he was a member of the Human Rights Committee of the United Nations, and from 1991 to 1992 was Deputy Foreign Minister of the newly independent Estonia, a post from which he resigned when Estonia violated its treaty obligations towards Russia. In 1992 to 1994 he was Visiting Centennial Professor of the London School of Economics and Political Science, a post that his colleague on the Human Rights Committee, Professor Rosalyn Higgins (later President of the International Court of Justice) helped him to achieve. He then taught at Kings College London until 2011, when he returned to Estonia. 92 Müllerson (1989). 93 Müllerson (1989), 494. 94 Müllerson (1989), 496. He turned to the question of Soviet doctrine on customary international law (it will be recalled that a common criticism was that there was no such doctrine). This doctrine ‘proceeds from the premise that the process of creating norms of international law is a process of coordinating wills (positions) of states and other subjects of international law’. This proceeded in two stages: the first in which rules of behaviour are worked out, the second in which these rules are recognised as rules of international law. Thus, Soviet doctrine recognised state practice and opinion juris.95 Indeed, on Müllerson’s account, it would be very hard to distinguish the ‘Soviet doctrine’ from what has become an absolutely orthodox account of international law. He made no mention at all of the right of peoples to self-determination; although shortly after coming to London he delivered lectures in which he explored self-determination, but only to argue that, while the three Baltic states, Estonia, Latvia and Lithuania, had a perfect right to sovereignty, Belarus and Ukraine did not.96

95 Müllerson (1989), 502. 96 There is no record to my knowledge of these lectures; I attended them and this is my recollection. And writing in 1991, just before the dissolution of the USSR and using the new language of ‘perestroika’, ‘common human values’ and ‘common European home’, Igor Blishchenko also argued for ‘re-thinking the periodisation of the contemporary history of international law, and for reading its formation not in the October Revolution of 1917 but the French bourgeois revolution, for the first time promoting such generally recognised norms and principles of international law as the right of peoples to self-determination.’97 Blishchenko, of course, had rather more of a scholarly history with regard to self-determination.

97 Blishchenko (1991), 135–36. However, the principle, then right, of self-determination, in my view, played a much more significant role, both in its practical effects in the international order and as the ‘obscene other’98 of Soviet positivism in international law.

98 In the Žižekian sense of a scandalous and unacceptable underride.





Chapter 6

The collapse of the USSR and the ‘parade of sovereignties’




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-7

In the last chapter, I examined the trajectory of Soviet public international law, in its contradictory and paradoxical way a true landmark, at the very least in establishing the right of peoples to self-determination as a legal right in international law. In this chapter, I show how this right, promoted abroad but suppressed at home, came to bite the USSR rather hard, contributing significantly to its collapse.

The Russian Federation (RF) is the largest and most complex in the world. When I wrote about this topic in 2000,1 it was composed of no fewer than 89 ‘subjects of the Federation’. It now has 83. The reasons for this surprising ‘shrinkage’ are explored later. It is plain that since 2004 there has been a premeditated assault on the foundations of Russian federalism, with profound consequences for potential for ethnic conflict.

1 Bowring (2000a).





History of the formation of Russian federalism


This can be divided into three phases.2 First, however, a brief mention of the Tsarist and Soviet history is in order.

2 See also Kossikov (1996), 1. The Russian empire was organised on administrative principles, although there is a long history of varying degrees of autonomy, explored in the next chapter, the Grand Duchy of Finland being the extreme example.3 Peter the Great created eight guberniya (provinces) in 1708, in the wake of his victories against Sweden in the Great Northern War and Catherine I increased their number to 14. In 1775 Catherine the Great, with Prince Potemkin’s immense conquests, established 44 provinces and two regions with provincial status. By 1914 the Empire consisted of 81 provinces and 20 regions.4

3 Kutafin (2006). 4 Teague (2008). Despite the fact that, in reality, the USSR functioned as a state with strongly centralised power, under the control of the Communist Party with its principle of ‘democratic centralism,’ the formal, constitutional position was different – and quite different from the Tsarist Empire. The USSR presented itself as a confederation, a union of sovereign republics with the right of secession; and the Russian Socialist Federation of Soviet Republics (RSFSR) as a unitary state with strong elements of territorial autonomy.5 Of course, the ethnic populations that did not receive their ‘own’ territory, especially the indigenous peoples of the north, lost out in this competition. The goal of leaders of the ‘titular’ nationality in a particular territory was to preserve as much as possible of its ethnic character and territorial integrity. Dowley observed as follows:

5 Khazanov (1997). Elites in the ethnic autonomous republics and national level republics were appointed to represent the ethnic group interests in the larger state, and thus, their natural political base of support was supposed to be the ethnic group. Other political appointments in these regions were made on the basis of ethnicity, a Soviet form of affirmative action for the formally, institutionally, recognised ethnic groups referred to in the early years of the Soviet Union as korenizatsiya or nativisation.6



6 Dowley (1998), 363. The chairmen of the Supreme Soviets of Tatarstan and Bashkortostan, both of which aspired to the status of ‘union republics’, were always members of the Presidium of the Supreme Soviet of the USSR, along with those of the Union Republics – the only two ‘autonomous republics’ so represented.7 By the end of the 1970s more than half of the professional cadre in half of the Union Republics and 11 of the 21 autonomous republics in the RSFSR were composed of the titular ethnic group. Social mobility of ethnic groups was higher than that of Russians.8 As the Soviet Union weakened and finally collapsed, in December 1991, it is hardly surprising that the same leaders sought to turn symbolic authority into real power and had a strong base for doing so.

7 Shaimiev (1996), 1. 8 Drobizheva (1996), 2. The first document of constitutional significance of the late Soviet period was the Declaration on State Sovereignty of the RSFSR of 12 June 1990,9 adopted by the Congress of People’s Deputies.10 The basic idea behind the Declaration was the establishment of Russia as a sovereign democratic rule of law state on the basis of people’s power, separation of powers and federalism. It also called for greater rights for the autonomous republics, autonomous oblast and autonomous okrug, as well as administrative krai and oblast. But at this stage Russia was only formally speaking a federation. According to Umnova, there was the parallel development of two contradictory developments: ensuring the statehood of Russia, on the one hand, and its disintegration, on the other.11

9 Although, according to Henderson (2011a), three months earlier the Lithuanian Socialist Republic had adopted a ‘Declaration of Independence’. 10 Vedomosti of the Congress of People’s Deputies RSFSR and Supreme Soviet RSFSR, 1990, No. 2, Art. 22. 11 Umnova (1998), 57. The process of ‘sovereignisation’ of the subjects of the RSFSR was also exemplified in laws that followed the declaration: the laws of the USSR ‘On the foundations of economic relations of the USSR and union and autonomous republics’ (10 April 1990) and ‘On delimitation of competences between the USSR and subjects of the federation’ (26 April 1990).12 These laws raised the autonomous republics in the RSFSR to a significant extent to the level of subjects of the USSR, equal to the union republics in their interconnections with the USSR.

12 Vedomosti of the Congress of People’s Deputies of the USSR and the Supreme Soviet of the USSR, 1990, No. 16, Art. 270; and No. 19, Art. 329.





First phase of Russian federalism


The first step was the period of the collapse of the USSR and the adoption of the Russian Declaration of State Sovereignty, the second was the signature of the Federative Treaty and the enactment of necessary amendments to the Russian Constitution of 1978 and the third was the adoption of the Russian Constitution of 1993.

The period between 1990 and March 1992 has been described as the ‘parade of sovereignties’. The abortive coup d’état (or putsch as it is known in Russia) of 18 August 1991 swiftly led to the demise of the USSR. The three Baltic republics were recognised as independent by the State Council set up after the putsch. The Minsk Agreement of 8 December 1991 and the Alma-Ata Declaration of 21 December 199113 laid the USSR to rest. During this period an extraordinary process began. From July to November 1990 the majority of the ‘ethnic’ autonomous republics attempted to throw off their autonomous status and to take on sovereign statehood. They did this by way of a series of declarations.14

13 Rossiysskaya Gazeta, 10 December 1991, 24 December 1991. 14 Deklaratsiy o suverenitete soyuznikh i avtonomnikh respublikh, Moscow, 1991. Mikhailov has shown graphically (see Table 6.1) how a large number of subjects of the Federation declared state sovereignty even before the collapse of the USSR in December 1991.15

These territories – two of them located in Georgia, soon to become an independent state – wanted to become full-fledged members of the USSR and to enter into treaty relations with the RSFSR. And some of the autonomous okrug unilaterally decided to become autonomous republics. Such decisions were taken in 1990 by the legislatures of the Chukotka and Yamalo-Nenets autonomous okrug. Chukotka successfully left the Magadan oblast to become a subject of the RF, according to the judgment of the Constitutional Court of the RF of 11 May 1993.16

16 Vestnik of the Constitutional Court of the RF 1994 No. 2–3, 53–9. Both Boris Yeltsin and Mintimer Shaimiev were democratically elected on 12 June 1991 – the former as the first president of the RSFSR, the latter as the first president of Tatarstan.17 One of the factors precipitating the abortive putsch of August 1991 was the real threat of ethnic separatism. The putsch leaders, who were the highest officials of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union, believed they were saving the Union.

17 Shaimiev (1996a), 2. The real threat of the transformation of Russia into a confederation provided the direct impetus for a draft Federative Treaty. On 31 March 1992 the RSFSR and most of the subjects signed the Federative Treaty, setting out the division of powers. The Treaty was incorporated into the 1978 Constitution of the RSFSR, coming into effect 10 December 1992. In the view of Umnova, Russia turned from a unitary state into a half-federation or quasi-federal state.18 She also considered that for the regions other than the ethnic republics, the Treaty ‘won’ a status of autonomy similar to the regions of unitary decentralised states, such as Italy and Spain (both since the 1980s). One of the most important guarantees of autonomy was the principle, to be found in Article 84 of the Treaty and Article 84(9) of amended 1978 Constitution, that the territories of these formations could not be changed without their agreement.

18 Umnova (1998), 63. It is notable that not all the subjects of the RSFSR agreed with the provisions of the Federative Treaty. Neither Tatarstan nor the Chechen-Ingush Republic signed it. On 21 March 1992 Tatarstan, despite the decision of the Russian Constitutional Court of 13 March 1992,19 held, referendum confirming the status of Tatarstan as an independent republic and subject of international law, with its own relations with the RF and other republics and also with foreign states on the basis of treaties and legal equality.

19 Vestnik of the Constitutional Court of the RF 1993 No. 1, 40–52. The status of the Chechen-Ingush Republic was not at all clear; practically speaking, it escaped from the control of the federal authorities.20 The Republic of Bashkortostan also had a special relation to the Treaty. The process of ‘republicanisation’ at this point began to transgress the limits of legality. On 27 October 1993 Sverdlovsk oblast adopted a Constitution of the Urals Republic. This republic was proclaimed unilaterally.21 On 9 November 1993 President Yeltsin issued his decree ‘On stopping the activities of the Sverdlovsk Oblast deputies’ and declared that the actions of the Soviet in adopting its decisions of 1 July 1993 on the status of Sverdlovsk oblast within the RF and of 27 October 1993 on the Constitution of the Urals Republic were null and void from the moment of their enactment (see also Easter22 as to the attempt to create a new Urals Republic).23

20 Umnova (1998), 72. 21 Yekaterinburgskiy Vedomosti, 30 October 1993. 22 See also Easter (1997). 23 Collection of the acts of the president and government of the RF 1993 No. 46, Art. 4447. We may note the very different trajectories of Chechnya and Tatarstan since then.24 The history of Chechnya is an exception that proves the rule. The Chechen Republic was and is one of the most ethnically homogenous of all the Russian republics, with Chechens, unusually, in an absolute majority. The Chechens defied the Russian empire until 1864. They suffered the brutal deportation by Stalin of their whole population in 1944. On 9 June 1991 the Chechen Republic, arbitrarily carved out of the Chechno-Ingush Republic, decided to secede from the USSR and RSFSR. This did not automatically mean the use of armed force by Russia, but there was no possibility of compromise. This was not least because of the gross injustices suffered by Chechens in this and past centuries. Conflict followed almost as a matter of course and lasted from 1994 to 1997.

24 Derlugyan (2002). As noted elsewhere in this book, Russia acceded to the Council of Europe in 1996 and ratified the European Convention on Human Rights in 1998. Russia has, on a large number of occasions, been condemned by the European Court of Human Rights for serious violations of the human rights of Chechens.25 On 23 March 2003 a new Constitution of the Chechen Republic was adopted by referendum.26 That constitution was drafted by Putin’s supporter Akhmad Kadyrov, who was assassinated on 9 May 2004.27 His son Ramzan Kadyrov then ruled Chechnya, with the assistance of the ‘Kadyrovtsy’, fighters who until very recently had been fighting the Russians. The journalist Anna Politkovskaya, a fierce critic of Kadyrov, was murdered in Moscow on 7 October 2006, while carrying out an investigation of his autocratic rule.28 On 5 April 2007 Ramzan Kadyrov was installed as president,29 having reached the necessary age of 30, under President Putin’s new rules, discussed later. Kadyrov was nominated by Putin and confirmed the following day by 56 of 58 possible votes in the Chechen legislature. He has now asserted a greater degree of autonomy even than was sought in 1991–1992, and has been described as the ‘Warrior King’ of Chechnya.30

25 In 2002 the author founded the European Human Rights Advocacy Centre (EHRAC), which has now taken some 50 cases to the European Court of Human Rights on behalf of Chechens against the Russian Federation; there have already been significant victories. 26 BBC News, 24 March 2003 ‘Chechnya backs new constitution’ at http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/europe/2879383.stm (accessed 29 October 2012). 27 Eurasia Daily Monitor1(6), Charles Gurin ‘Akhmad Kadyrov is assassinated’ 9 May 2004 at http://www.jamestown.org/single/?no_cache=1&tx_ttnews[tt_news]=26479 (accessed 29 October 2012). 28 Washington Post, 8 October 2006 ‘Editorial: murder in Moscow: the Putin era of brutality claims a victim of rare courage’ at http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2006/10/07/AR2006100700718.html (accessed 29 October 2012). 29 New York Times, 5 April 2007 ‘Ramzan Kadyrov, Chechnya strongman, installed as president’ at http://www.nytimes.com/2007/04/05/world/europe/05iht-web0405-chech.5161439.html (accessed 9 August 2009). 30 Osborn (2007). The trajectory of Tatarstan has been quite different. I have noted already Tatarstan’s Declaration of State Sovereignty, in which state sovereignty was declared as the ‘realisation of the inalienable right of the Tatar nation, of all people of the republic to self-determination’.31 Former President Shaimiev stresses the fact that the ‘people of Tatarstan’ was not divided into ethnic groups.32 In the referendum of that time, no less than 62 per cent of its population, Tatars and Russians, supported sovereignty. Tatarstan, like Chechnya, refused to sign the Federative Treaty in March 1992, but, unlike the Chechen leadership, Shaimiev entered into lengthy negotiations with the Russian government.

31 Tishkov (1997), 56. 32 Shaimiev (1996a), 5.





Second phase: the 1993 Constitution of the Russian Federation


During 1993, a so-called ‘budget and finance war’ developed between the federation and its subjects and turned into a full-fledged crisis in September 1993. The situation deteriorated sharply at the beginning of October 1993. On 3 October demonstrators who supported the Parliament removed police cordons placed around the White House, the seat of parliament (Supreme Soviet) by Yeltsin, took over the Mayor’s offices and tried to storm the Ostankino television centre. The army, which had initially declared its neutrality, was ordered by Yeltsin to storm the White House in the early morning of 4 October. The leaders of the opposition were arrested. This 10-day conflict was the first incident of street fighting in Moscow’s history since the 1917 Revolution. According to government estimates, 187 people were killed and 437 wounded, while sources close to Russian Communists put the death toll at as high as 2,000. Yeltsin tore up the existing Constitution and suspended the Constitutional Court.

This crisis was brought to an end only by the adoption of the new constitution, by dubious referendum, on 12 December 1993. The philosopher and constitutional expert Andrei Medushevskiy has described these events as ‘the constitutional revolution of 1993’. According to Medushevskiy, at the time of this revolution:

Russia passed through a constitutional cycle, the working out of which continues to the present day. The cycle contained three phases: the decisive rejection of nominal Soviet constitutionalism (the first phase), the transition to the real, connected with the revolutionary adoption of the 1993 Constitution (second phase), and the consequent establishment of an imprecise balance of the relation of norms and realities with the growing attraction of all systems towards authoritarianism (third phase).33



33 Medushevskiy (2007), 170. Indeed, the new constitution itself bore all the hallmarks of a compromise document. Article 76 provided that like the republics all subjects of the federation could enact their own laws and independently decide on their own executive system.

Moreover, in seven of the ethnic republics (Bashkortostan, Mordoviya, Chuvashiya, Adygeya, Dagestan, Karachaevo-Cherkessiya, Tyva) as well as in 10 oblasts the majority of voters voted against the new constitution. In two republics, Tatarstan and Chechnya, referendum did not take place at all. Unofficial sources suggest that 13 of the 21 republics voted against the new constitution, and altogether 31 subjects voted against. Thus the new constitution suffered a deficit of legitimacy from the outset.

The constitutions of a number of republics to the Russian Federation also varied sharply: three groups could be distinguished by Mikhailov in 2004.34 First, the constitutions of Tatarstan and Chechnya lacked any reference to the fact that they were part of the Russian Federation or to the supremacy of its Constitution and laws. Second, the constitutions of Bashkortostan, Ingushetiya, Tyva and Sakha (Yakutiya) recognised membership of the Federation but said nothing about the supremacy of the federal constitution or laws. Third, all the others recognised both membership and supremacy.

34 Mikhailov (2004), 409.





Third phase: post-1993 Russian federalism


Varlamova identified three stages in the evolution of Russian federalism from 1993.35 She termed the first stage, from 1994 to 1995, ‘subject treaty based’. During this period priority was given to individual regulation (at the level of the constitution or charter of the subject and bilateral agreements with the federal centre) and a growing tendency to asymmetry in the Federation and even movement toward confederation. Two leading commentators, Constitutional Court Judge B.S. Ebzeev and Professor L.M. Karapetyan took the view36 that Russia as a constitutional state could not be treaty based. Umnova strongly disagreed37 and she must be right. Thus, Article 70 of the Constitution of the Republic of Bashkortostan declares that its relation with the RF is based on treaties. By its Constitution, it retains the gamut of state power with the exception of those powers voluntarily ceded by it to the RF.

35 Varlamova (2001), 25. 36 Ebzeev and Karapetyan (1995). 37 Umnova (1998), 78. Many republics did not recognise any change to their status under the Federative Treaty as the result of the 1993 Constitution. Article 8 of the Constitution of Sakha (Yakutiya) declared that it is a subject of the RF on the basis of the Treaty. Article 1 of the Constitution of the Republic of Tyva recognised its inclusion in the RF on the basis of this and other treaties. Article 68 of the Constitution of the Republic of Buryatiya provided that it had the right to delegate voluntarily defined competences for defined periods to the RF.38

38 Umnova (1998), 79–80.





Bilateral treaties – between the federation and its subjects


What is, however, most extraordinary and probably unique to the RF was the number of bilateral treaties entered into between the federation and some 46 of its subjects. Very many of these treaties contradicted the express provisions of the 1993 Constitution. Thus, on 15 February 1994, Tatarstan signed the treaty ‘On the Demarcation of Competences between the Government of the Russian Federation and the Government of the Republic of Tatarstan’ and 12 agreements with Moscow, affirming its constitution and presidency, republican citizenship, a significant degree of sovereignty over oil and other natural resources, special provisions for military service and other rights and powers.39 Further examples are the treaties with Kabardino-Balkariya (June 1994), Bashkortostan (August 1994), Udmurtiya (October 1995) and Omsk Oblast (May 1996).40 As of June 1998, more than half the regions had concluded some 46 similar treaties.41 Frommeyer considered that this process ‘could lead to the disintegration of the Russian Federation’.42 Varlamova also points out that the Constitutional Court was rarely called on to decide issues arising between Federation and its subjects; these were for the most part resolved through informal and political means.43

39 Tishkov (1997), 243. 40 Shulzhenko (1998), 68; Lysenko (1998), 2. 41 Alekseyev et al. (1998a), 334. 42 Frommeyer (1992), 53. 43 Varlamova (2001), 26. In the second stage identified by Varlamova,44 the RF sought to unify the legal situation of subjects of the federation and guarantee the supremacy of the federal constitution. Thus, the presidential decree of 12 March 199645 made it clear that treaties must not change the constitutional status of a subject of the federation. However, treaties concluded between 1996 and 1998 continued to contain provisions violating the federal constitution, if not so obviously.46 For this reason, laws were enacted in June and October with detailed rules for the organisation subjects of the RF.47 The enactment of these laws gave rise to a series of judgments and decisions of the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation, at the request of regional bodies and private citizens, seeking authoritative adjudication of the powers and duties of subjects of the RF.48

44 Varlamova (2001), 29. 45 No. 370, Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 1996 No. 12, 1058. 46 Ivanov (1999). 47 Law of 24 June 1999, ‘On principles and procedure for delimitation of matters of powers and competences between organs of state power of the RF, and organs of state power of subjects of the RF’, No. 119-FZ Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 1999 No. 26, 3176. Law of 6 October 1999, ‘On general principles of the organisation of legislative (representative) and executive organs of state power of subjects of the RF’ No. 184-FZ Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 1999 No. 43, 5005; 2000 No. 31, 3205; 2001 No. 7, 608. 48 Varlamova (2001), 30. The year 1996 also saw the beginning of Russia’s experiment in ‘national-cultural autonomy,’ that is, non-territorial autonomy, which I discuss in the next chapter.





The Russian Federation according to the 1993 Constitution


According to the 1993 Constitution, there are two equally correct names for the territory under discussion: ‘Russia’ and ‘the Russian Federation’. It should be noted first that the phrase Rossiyskaya Federatsiya (Russian Federation) cannot be directly translated into English. The Russian language has two words that can be translated into English as ‘Russian’. The first is ‘russkiy’, which means ‘ethnic Russian’, while the second is ‘rossiiyskiy’, ‘civic Russian’. The Federation is the ‘Rossiiyskaya’ not the ‘Russkaya’ federation – it is the country not of ethnic but of civic Russians, that is, of bearers of citizenship under the Constitution.

The subjects are as follows. First, and most important for our purposes, Russia has 21 ethnic republics, the successors of the ‘autonomous republics’ of the USSR, named after their ‘titular’ people, with their own presidents, constitutions and, in many cases, constitutional courts. There has been no reduction in the number of ethnic republics, not least because of the potential strength of their resistance.

Next, there are nine enormous krai, a word often translated as ‘region’, (but literally ‘Edge’) with their own appointed governors. In 2000 there were six, with elected governors.

The most numerous subjects of the Federation are the 46 oblasts, territorial formations inhabited primarily by ethnic Russians, also with governors. There were 49 oblasts in 2000.

The four autonomous okrugs (AO) are also ethnic formations and reflect a relative concentration of the indigenous peoples who give them their name. There were 10 of these in 2000; the six that have disappeared have been ‘united’ with larger neighbours, often in very controversial circumstances. For all their formal constitutional equality under the 1993 Constitution, they were for the most part located within other formations (krai and oblast), with consequences that will be explored later in this chapter. There is a Jewish autonomous oblast, located in the Russian Far East. Finally, two ‘cities of federal significance’, Moscow and St Petersburg, are also subjects of the Federation.

It is important to remember that of at least 150 nationalities in the Russian Federation, only 32 had their own territorial units.49 This number has now shrunk.

49 Kempton (1996), 609. The diversity and respective powers of subjects of the Federation, as set out in the 1993 Constitution, are shown in Table 6.2. This extraordinary ramification reflects both the Soviet nationalities policy and the multiethnic character of the Tsarist Empire, themes which will be explored in the next chapter.
