The contradictory legal basis of Russian federalism


In her classic monograph, Irina Umnova observed that the legal foundations of the Russian Federation were to be found in a number of documents. These are: the Constitution of the Russian Federation of 1993, the Federal Treaty, the federal constitutional and federal laws, treaties between organs of state power of the Russian Federation and the organs of state power of the subjects of the federation, and, lastly, the constitutions (charters) of the subjects themselves.50

50 Umnova (1998), 44; Salikov (2004). Thus, there were two levels of federal relation. First, there were direct relations of state power between the Federation and its subjects as such. Second, there were relations between unmediated bearers of state power of the Federation and its subjects. These included the ‘people’ of the RF (its citizens, who by Article 3 of the Constitution make up its political community) and organs of state power. It follows that a series of complex relations were possible: the people of the RF and the people of a subject; the people of the RF and organs of state power of a subject; organs of federal state power and the people of a subject. However, Umnova pointed out that according to Article 66(2) of the RF Constitution, the constitution (charter) of a subject was adopted by its legislative organ; adoption by referendum was precluded. This, in her view, was an undemocratic exclusion of the unmediated bearer of state power (the people of the subject).51

51 Umnova (1998), 50. Varlamova also pointed out a number of competing, if not contradictory, principles at work in the Russian Constitution (Table 6.3).52 She added that there were no insurmountable contradictions between these principles, only that the future federal character of Russia depends on their interpretation: the broadening of the authority of central power or its decentralisation.53

Table 6.3 Contradictory principles of the Russian Federation Unity, integrity and equality of the Federation Principles tending to decentralisation and asymmetry

Reach of the sovereignty of the RF to all of its territory (Art. 4(1)); the integrity and inviolability of its territory (Art. 4(3)) Self-determination of the peoples of the RF (preamble, Art. 5(3)); plenitude of power of subjects of the RF outside the limits of competence of the RF for matters of joint competence of the RF and its subjects (Art. 73); recognition of (ethnic) republics within the RF and the existence of their rights to external state attributes: constitution, state language (Arts 5(2) and 68(2))

Supremacy of the RF Constitution and federal laws on the whole of its territory (Arts 4(2), 15); the unity of the legal and economic space (Arts 6(2), 8(1), 74, 75) Possibility of treaty-based delimitation of matters of competence and jurisdiction between organs of state power of the RF and those of its subjects (Art. 11(3)); right of subjects to establish their own legal regulation on matters outside the competence of the RF and outside joint competence (Art. 76(4), (6))

Unity of the system of state power (Arts 5(3), 77(2), 78) Right of subjects of the RF independently to establish their own system of organs of state power in accordance with the fundamental constitutional system of the RF and the general principles of the organisation of executive and legislative organs of state power provided by law (Art. 77(1))

Equal rights of subjects of the RF (Art. 5(1), (4)) Asymmetrical nature of the RF, and the differentiated constitutional and legal status of its subjects (Arts 5(1), 65(1), 66, and 68(2))



52 Varlamova (2001), 13. 53 Varlamova (2001), 14.





Asymmetric federations: a Russian view


Russia is frequently chosen by Western scholars as a case study in federalism. McGarry states that ‘Russia created an asymmetrical federation in the immediate years after the fall of the Soviet Union, although the Putin government has since sought to roll this back.’54 De Figueiredo and Weingast assert that ‘an appropriately structured federal system seems well suited for a country as large and diverse as Russia.’55 However, they consider that actual federalism in Russia displays a range of pathologies. First, they point to the ‘striking lack of cooperation between center and regions’.56 For Obydenkova, ‘asymmetry, as a result of “federal bargaining” and the flexibility of institutions, was unavoidable, especially in the process of regime change accompanied by miscalculation of multi-level reforms and mistakes’.57 Did it help to avoid conflict? She examined the Russian case, taking the republics of Bashkortostan, Tatarstan and Sakha-Yakutiya against three parameters: geopolitical, economic and ethnic. Her conclusion was that while these factors had an impact on centre–periphery relations, they did not determine the intensity of conflict or the scope of demands.58 While size, population, natural resources and the ethnic factor did stimulate a subject of the Federation to bargain for more independence, the intensity of conflict was also influenced by fiscal arrangements and by the nature and interpersonal relations of the elite.

54 McGarry (2005), 1. 55 De Figueiredo and Weingast (2002), 19. 56 De Figueiredo and Weingast (2002), 20. 57 Obydenkova (2004), 3. 58 Obydenkova (2004), 38. Umnova provided what remains, in my view, the strongest Russian analysis and prognosis of the genesis and fundamental features of Russia’s unique brand of federalism. She started from two types of federalism, delegated (where states join together in a union) and decentralised (where power is transferred from the center).59 The principle of a noncentralised state is subsidiarity,60 as in the United States. She argued that Russian political practice often mixes the principle of subsidiarity and the principle of delegation. For this she quoted the speaker (in 1993) of the Supreme Council of Tatarstan, F.K h. Mekhametshin, who insisted that a federation must be formed on the principle of subsidiarity, that is, from the bottom to the top on the basis of delegation.61

59 Umnova (1998), 29. 60 Umnova (1998), 31. 61 Umnova (1998), 33. Umnova described three types of federation: symmetrical, asymmetrical and symmetrical with aspects of asymmetry. The first, with subjects that are identical in nature and equal in status, was the ideal but exists nowhere. The second was the least successful and, as in the case of the Tanzanian Constitution of 1977, brought together two utterly dissimilar entities – Tanganika and Zanzibar. She distinguished three types of state in which the national factor has been brought into play. The first group were states that had undergone collapse and either disappeared or been reorganised – the USSR, Yugoslavia, Czechoslovakia, Nigeria. The second were states retaining their integrity but undergoing internal cataclysms in the form of local ethnic conflicts inspired by separatism – India, Canada, the Russian Federation. The third group consisted of stable and relatively peaceful federations – Belgium and Switzerland.62 In her view, the existence of this group showed that the combination of territorial and ethnic (national) factors in structuring a federation was possible. This would only succeed, however, if the following conditions were met: statehood was preserved; the rights and freedoms of the person and citizen were not violated, neither were the equal rights of nations and their right to self-determination; and the development of nationalism was not stimulated.

62 Umnova (1998)43.





Dismantling sovereignty after 2000: Putin’s policies


The third stage identified by Varlamova was the ‘strengthening of the “vertikal” of power’.63 This had three main objectives:

63 Varlamova (2001), 73. bringing the legislation of the subjects into line with the law of the RF;

weakening the influence (formal and informal) of leaders of the regions on the activities of federal organs of power;

strengthening responsibility toward federal power.



Following his election in 2000, President Putin on several occasions declared his strong opposition to the bilateral treaties and his determination to bring them to an end. According to Hyde, writing at the beginning of this process: ‘Putin’s reforms may represent only a brief departure from previous practices, only a temporary swing of the pendulum in favor of central power.’64 This was to underestimate Putin’s determination,65 exemplified by the Presidential Decree of May 2000,66 which, without amending the RF Constitution, created seven federal districts, each comprising six to 18 subjects of the RF, headed by pleni-potentiary representatives, drawn mostly from the military and FSB,67 and supported by senior representatives of the prosecutors, FSB, police and other federal organs. A federal register of all normative acts enacted by the subjects of the RF was created, to be held at the Ministry of Justice, and copies of all normative acts were required to be sent within seven days of their enactment in the regions.68 In February 2001 Deputy General Prosecutor Yu.S. Biryukov announced that in the previous six months 3,273 unlawful normative acts had been discovered, of which 2,544 had either been repealed or brought into line.69

64 Hyde (2001), 738. 65 SeeHahn (2001). 66 Decree of 13 May 2000 ‘On the plenipotentiary representatives of the President of the RF in the federal districts’ No. 849 Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 2000 No. 20, 2112. 67 Jane Henderson has reminded me that all but one were drawn from the ‘power ministries’. 68 Decree of 10 August 2000 ‘On supplementary measures for guaranteeing the unity of the legal space of the RF’ No. 1486 Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 2000 No. 33, 3356. 69 Yu.S. Biryukov, Prokuratura bystrovo reagirovaniya (Quick response prosecution service), Nezavisimaya Gazeta, 28 February 2001. In the view of V.E. Chirkin, the creation of the seven federal districts was a first step toward the strengthening and unifying of subjects of the RF with a view to a future federation, not, of course, of seven subjects, but of 25 to 30.70 However, Varlamova argued, in my view correctly, that given the historical roots of the present structure, a quick and artificial solution should be avoided in favor of a more cautious, step-by-step approach, even if this takes a number of years.71

70 Chirkin (2000), 48–9. 71 Varlamova (2001), 91. At last, the RF Constitutional Court issued a fundamental judgment on 7 June 2000,72 followed by a clarification on 27 June 2000,73 in response to a group of Duma deputies requesting investigation of a number of provisions of the constitutions of Altai in the first case and of the (ethnic) republics of Adygeya, Bashkortostan, Ingushetiya, Komi, Severnaya Osetiya-Alaniya (known as North Osetiya) and Tatarstan in the second. The Court held the following to be incompatible with the Constitution of the RF:

72 No.10-O. 73 No. 92-O. state sovereignty of the republics as subjects of the RF;

the assumption of the republics of supreme power within their territories;

recognising the people of the republic as the bearers of sovereignty and unique source of power;

asserting, following from the principle of sovereignty, the supremacy of the republic’s constitution and laws;

suspending the effect of legal acts of the RF in its territory;

asserting the treaty-based character of the status of the republic as a subject of the RF and the fact of its inclusion or remaining in the RF at all;

asserting the status of the republic as a subject of international law;

declaring the rules governing property relations with regard to natural resources.



Varlamova asked why it took seven years for the republics’ assertion of sovereignty to be challenged. The answer to that question is simple, she said: the deputies were instructed to do so by the new president, Vladimir Putin. She did not know, however, why only six of 21 republics were challenged.74

74 Varlamova (2001), 79. Varlamova also pointed out that the reaction of the republics could be characterised as ‘deaf dissatisfaction’.75 In Bashkortostan, there were serious problems with publication of the decision.76 Only Severnaya Osetiya-Alaniya (known as North Osetiya) made the necessary amendments to its Constitution. Adygeya, Altai, Ingushetiya and Tatarstan retained the provisions the Constitutional Court had found to be unconstitutional. There are very similar provisions in the Constitution of Bashkortostan adopted in November 2000.77

75 Varlamova (2001), 79. 76 Na net i publikatsiy net: Kto pryachet ot zhitelei Bashkortotstana resheiye Constitutsionnovo Suda Rossiy (No publication whatsoever: who is hiding the decision of the Constitutional Court of Russia from the inhabitants of Baskortostan), Rossiyskaya Gazeta, 2 September 2000. 77 Vedomosti gosudarstvennovo sobraniya, Presidenta i Kabineta Ministrov Respubliki Bashkortostan (Gazette of the State Council, President and Cabinet of Ministers of the Republic of Bashkortostan) 2000, No. 17, 1255. In the opinion of the General Prosecutor, the old Constitution had 44 articles that contradicted federal law, while the new one had 51.

Nevertheless, an amending law of July 2000 introduced strict mechanisms for responsibility, including individual criminal liability.78 In Varlamova’s view, the main problem of this law was that it contradicted the RF Constitution, since it purported to give to the parliament and president of the RF powers that they did not have.79

78 No. 106-FZ, Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 2000, No. 31, 3205. 79 Varlamova (2001), 96. Finally, the Federal Law of 5 August 2000 provided that the Council of Federation was to be composed not, as previously, of the presidents and governors of the subjects of the Federation, but by elected representatives of the legislatures of the subjects and appointed representatives of the president or governor.80 On the one hand, this could be said to increase the likelihood that members would focus on the business of the Council. On the other hand, however, in Varlamova’s view, the Council’s status as an independent and influential body, able to act as part of a system of checks and balances, was radically reduced.81

80 ‘On the procedure for composition of the Council of the Federation (Upper House) of the Federal Assembly (Parliament) of the RF’ No. 113-FZ, Sobraniye zakonodatelstvo RF 2000, No. 32, 3336. 81 Varlamova (2001), 103. At the same time, President Putin continued to take measures to eliminate treaty-based relations within the RF. By 2003 some 30 of the 42 treaties had been abrogated.82 And on 9 February 2007 the Russian State Duma ratified a new power-sharing agreement ‘giving Tatarstan a degree of economic and political autonomy that no other region enjoys’. This development directly contradicted Putin’s policy of centralisation and was seen as a serious defeat for the siloviki, the representatives of the ‘power ministries’, Ministry of the Interior, Ministry of Defence, FSB, etc., and for the ‘party of power’, United Russia.83 On 11 July 2007 the Federation Council, having first rejected the law in February, voted 122 to four in favour of the new treaty.84 President Shaimaiev said in an interview: ‘First of all, I want to say that although this agreement is shorter in length, it is very significant, particularly from a political perspective, because the 1994 agreement was prepared under very difficult conditions.’ He explained that the original power-sharing treaty between Tatarstan and Russia dealt mostly with property issues, while the new treaty bore a mostly political significance and gave Tatarstan more autonomy than other Russian Federation subjects. Most importantly, Shaimiev said, it marked a first in Russian history.85 The treaty inter alia obliged the Tatar president to speak the Tatar language in addition to Russian and allowed regional authorities to issue ‘internal passports’ with an insert in the Tatar language.

82 Sharlet (2003), 338. 83 Smirnov (2007). 84 Arnold (2007). 85 Gilfanov (2007).





Putin’s eradication of directly elected presidents and governors


From 2000 on Putin used administrative and judicial pressure to keep politically inconvenient governors and other leaders from seeking reelection. Hashim adds: ‘Only a few defiant regional leaders, like Republican presidents of donor and ethnic regions such as Tatarstan and Bashkortostan, have maintained sufficient autonomy in spite of increased federal intervention in order to strengthen the power vertical.’86 In the view of Yuri Filippov, the basic idea was to ‘remove excessive politicisation … and to replace politicians with managers and bureaucrats’.87

86 Hashim (2005), 36. 87 Filippov (2004). The 3 September 2004 massacre at the Beslan school provided Putin with the pretext for replacing direct election of presidents and governors.88 As Lemaître put it: ‘The Kremlin wants us to believe that Beslan was possible because the vertical of power was imperfect and there was still too little managed democracy in Russia. We disagree … It had become clear, well before September 2004, that the Kremlin was more concerned with consolidating its grip on power than with the rule of law.’89 On 13 September at a session of the Government of the Russian Federation with the participation of the heads of the subjects of the federation – the presidents and governors – Putin simply announced that henceforward, as part of the response to terrorism, the heads of the subjects would no longer be directly elected but that the president would nominate a candidate to be elected, formally, by the subject’s legislature.

88 On the massacre: The Guardian, ‘The Beslan school tragedy’ at http://www.guardian.co.uk/world/beslan (accessed 9 August 2009); on Putin’s action: Website of the President of Russia 4 September 2004 at http://president.kremlin.ru/appears/2004/09/04/1752_type63374type82634_76320.shtml (accessed 29 October 2012). 89 Lemaître(2006), 410. The opposition politician Irina Khakamada described this as nothing short of a coup d’état and unconstitutional.90 But most governors did not protest.91 The requisite federal law was signed by Putin on 12 December 2004 – ironically, on Constitution Day.92 Under the new law, if the president’s candidate were rejected twice by the subject, he could dissolve the legislature and impose his own choice as acting governor.93

90 Regnum.ru information agency, 14 September 2004, Inititsiativy po izmeneniyu sistemy gosudarstvennovo ustroistva – eto fakticheski perevorot – Irina Khakamada at http://www.regnum.ru/news/324418.html (accessed 29 October 2012). 91 See, e.g. Rossisskaya Gazeta, 15 September 2004, printing the responses of a number of heads, at http://www.rg.ru/2004/09/15/gubernatory.html (accessed 9 August 2009). 92 No. 160-FZ, at http://document.kremlin.ru/doc.asp?ID=025563 (accessed 9 August 2009); http://www.rg.ru/2004/12/15/gubernatory-dok.html (accessed 9 August 2009). 93 Bransten (2004); and McFaul and Stoner-Weiss (2008).





The asymmetric federation under threat? ‘Forced’ mergers


Has the Kremlin been looking to dissolve the ethnic republics altogether?94 Yasmann95 noted the series of mergers that were taking place as of 2006, which had led to the ‘shrinkage’ of the number of subjects of the Russian Federation with which I started this chapter. In his view, ‘there are many signs that political considerations are behind the merger efforts, and that the Kremlin’s real goal is effectively to dissolve the troublesome ethnic republics in the North Caucasus and Volga region.’96 However, research in 2008 showed that ‘the economic rationale of keeping the republic’s wealth motivates both Russians and titulars to support separatism’ in republics such as Tatarstan.97 Whatever the desire of the Kremlin for administrative logic, the desire of the ethnic republics for greater autonomy will persist and may grow stronger.

94 Yasmann (2006). 95 Yasmann (2006). 96 Yasmann (2006). 97 Hagendoorn, Poppe and Minescu (2008), 370; and see Badretdin (1997). As noted earlier, the regions concerned are the autonomous okrugs, precisely those ethnic autonomies created in the Soviet period. One reform plan published in 2006 envisaged 28, instead of 83, subjects of the RF, merging all ethnic republics with neighbouring Slav administrative areas to form new provinces, eliminating any reference to ethnicity in their names.98 The proposed mergers would be:

98 Yasmann (2006). the Republic of Tatarstan with Ulyanovsk Oblast to create Volgo-Kama Province;

the Republic of Bashkortostan with Orenburg Oblast to create Yuzno-Ural Province;

the Republic of Udmurtiya with Perm Krai to create Zapadno-Ural Province;

the Republics of Mariy El and Chuvashiya with Kirov Oblast to create Volgo-Vyatka Province;

the Republic of Kalmykiya with Astrakhan, Volgograd and Rostov oblast to create Volgo-Don Province;

the Republics of Chechnya, Ingushetiya, Dagestan, Kabardino-Balkariya, and Severnaya Osetiya with Stavropol Krai to create North Caucasus Province;

the Republics of Adygeya and Karachayevo-Cherkessiya with Krasnodar Krai to create Prichenornormskaya Province.



This process was named the ‘parade of mergers’ by Pimenov,99 echoing Yeltsin’s ‘parade of sovereignties’. Bazhenov argued100 that Russia should be composed ideally of 35–50 regions, which all should be named krai rather than the Tsarist guberniya, in keeping with Russian history. Table 6.4 lists the mergers that have taken place so far, starting at the end of 2003, just before Putin’s election to a second term. The process has, without doubt, been driven by the Kremlin.

99 Pimenov (2005). 100 Bazhenov (2007). As Dmitriyev observes,101 the apparently large votes in favour of each merger concealed considerable opposition to the process of merger. For example, protests by the Buryat intelligentsia, led by the historians Nikolai Tsyrempilov and Shirap Chimitdorzhiyev, have been ignored. They demanded the restoration of the borders of the Buryat-Mongol Autonomous Soviet Socialist Republic, which was abolished by Stalin in 1937 and divided into three parts, following allegations of ‘pan-Mongolism’ and irredentism – seeking to leave the USSR.

101 Dmitriyev (2007) In 2006 the Moscow Helsinki Group published a detailed report102 on gross irregularities committed during the 16 April 2006 referendum on the Irkutsk merger. They pointed out that the proportion of those voting in Irkutsk was barely 50 per cent in the March 2004 presidential elections, and the minimum turnout for local elections is only 20 per cent. More than 50 per cent was required for referendum. The ‘result’ was a vote in favour of 99.51 per cent in the Ust-Ordynski Buryat AO – reminiscent of the extraordinary ‘turnout’ in the 2007 and 2008 parliamentary and presidential elections – and 68.98 per cent in Irkutsk.

102 Moscow Helsinki Group (2006). I noted earlier that the Transbaikal (Zabaikalskiy) krai came into existence on 1 March 2008, eliminating another of the ‘fragments’ of the Buryat-Mongol autonomy. In an interview, the governor of Chita, Ravil Geniatulin, was quite clear as to the rationale behind merger: ‘the strengthening of the state as a whole’.103 He had nothing to say concerning the fate of the Buryat people.

103 Trunov (2007).





Research carried out on behalf of President Medvedev


In 2010 President Medvedev’s INSOR Research Institute, with a team of researchers headed by Yevgenij Gontmakher, published a thorough and extensive (175 page) report entitled ‘Merger of the subjects of the Russian Federation: for and against’.104

104 INSOR (2010). Their analysis led them to the following conclusions:

Mergers to the date of publications had had a null effect. It was quite possible that in the future the country would need a new ATD (administrative-territorial division), not excluding the merger of subjects, but this would need thought out reforms, not separate uncoordinated actions.

The campaign to strengthen subjects was supposed to demonstrate the power of the centre, but demonstrated its weak competence and insufficient professionalism.

Mergers did not raise the governability of the country.

Continuation of the policy will lead to all kinds of conflict, from ecological to ethnic.

The present campaign for mergers is practically exhausted: tasks are not implemented, the prestige of the centre in the regions, especially in the autonomous okrug has seriously suffered.

The only way out is to abandon directed democracy in favour of normal democracy and to return to a decentralised federative state.

The centre’s problems are connected not with the large number of subjects, but with the desire of the ‘federals’ to regulate everything and everyone. De jure the country is a federation (with independent local government), but de facto it is a unitary state (with a vertical of power that penetrates all territorial levels).

Merger is not so much harmful as senseless. Much money and political capital has been expended with zero effectiveness, even in the field of PR.

Merger is a mistake that is very hard to put right. There are not the conditions or the cadres for this. One has to feel sorry for the population.

Reform of ATD should be attempted only when political, economic and social stability has been achieved in the country as a whole.

The institutions of state power have practically ignored their own analytical departments, have hardly used independent consultants and have often considered scientific research as a waste of money and time.

The people taking the decisions have hardly known either Russian or foreign experience in the sphere of deciding territorial problems.



This is indeed a devastating critique of the policy driven by President Putin.





Conclusion


As I indicated at the start of the chapter, the complex, contradictory, but ultimately rather effective Russian form of asymmetric federalism has helped Russia to maintain its territorial integrity and, with the exception of Chechnya, to avoid ethnonational conflict. I have shown how recent ‘reforms’, ostensibly designed to strengthen the Russian state and its unity, have the potential for reopening past conflict and creating new ones. Writing in 2007, Oversloot observed: ‘That V.V. Putin has, thus far, not attempted to change the text of the 1993 Constitution, and that he has not tried to remove the limits which the 1993 Constitution also sets upon presidential power, is, nevertheless, considered to be of great importance.’105 However, two years later he concluded that: ‘A reduction in the number of subjects to merely a few dozen … may prove to be much more difficult, and even politically risky.’106

105 Oversloot (2007), 64. 106 Oversloot (2009).

Chapter 7

Russian autonomy




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-8





Introduction


In the last chapter, I described and analysed the turbulent events and constitutional crises surrounding the sudden and unexpected collapse of the Soviet Union. In this chapter, I turn to another aspect of Russia’s legal and constitutional system: the extraordinary popularity of forms of non-territorial autonomy.

First, it should be noted that Article 9(3) of the Federal Law of 11 July 2001 No. 95-FZ, ‘On political parties’,1 provides:

1 Website:http://base.garant.ru/183523/1/#100 (accessed 18 November 2012). I was reminded of this point by Jane Henderson. The formation of political parties on professional, racial, national (ethnic) or religious affiliation is forbidden.

By professional, racial, national or religious affiliation in the present Federal Law is meant the indication in the charter and programme of the political party of the purpose of protecting professional, racial, national or religious interests, and also the reflection of these purposes in the name of the political party.

A political party may not consist of members of one profession.2



2 Mytranslation. On 15 December 2004 the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation, on the application of the ‘Orthodox Party of Russia’, upheld the constitutionality of this provision.3 The Court declared: ‘Thus, the constitutional principle of a democratic and secular state in relation to the concrete historical realities in the Russian Federation as a multi-ethnic and multi-religious country, does not permit the establishment of political parties based on national or religious affiliation.’ In the Court’s view, the formation of such parties could exacerbate racial and ethnic tension. This judgment of the Constitutional Court has been adversely commented on by Tatars and others.4

3 Website:http://www.rg.ru/2004/12/24/partii-ks-dok.html (accessed 18 November 2012). 4 See Rakhmetov, Zapret tatarskoi/tyurskoi/musulmanskoi politicheskikh partii v RF (The ban on Tatar/Turkish/Muslim political parties in the RF) at http://forum.tatar.info/index.php?showtopic=5247 (accessed 18 November 2012). Since 2002 I have published two journal articles and a book chapter on the question of the Russian experiment in national–cultural autonomy (NCA).5 My particular focus has been the history of the hotly contested struggle in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries between the Austro-Marxist conception of non-territorial personal cultural autonomy, taken up with enthusiasm by the Jewish Bund and by Muslim activists; and the Social-Democratic and Bolshevik orthodoxy of territorial autonomy under Lenin’s banner of the right of nations to self-determination. This chapter takes a different direction, with, for me, completely new materials and sources.

5 Bowring (2002); Bowring (2005b); Bowring (2007). My initial interest was spurred by an invitation in 2000 from Antti Korkeakivi at the Secretariat of the Council of Europe (CoE) to comment on Russia’s first state report,6 dated 8 March 2000, following ratification of the CoE’s 1994 Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities (FCNM). Russia joined the CoE in 1996, signed the FCNM on 28 February 1996, ratified it on 21 August 1998 and it entered into force on 1 December 1998. This Report highlighted (p. 12 and elsewhere) the experiment in non-territorial, personal autonomy initiated by the Federal Law ‘On national – cultural autonomy’ of 19 June 1996. I had never come across this concept before, and my curiosity drove me to the work of Ephraim Nimni and others – and back to the Austro-Marxists.

6 At http://www.coe.int/t/dghl/monitoring/minorities/3_FCNMdocs/PDF_1st_SR_RussianFederation_en.pdf. My own estimation in 2002 was that this was an astonishing reversal of the Soviet hostility to non-territorial autonomy, dating back to the series of substantial polemics against this Austro-Marxist proposal written by Lenin, Stalin and Trotsky, to name but three. At the same time, it represented a significant victory on the part of Professor Valeriy Tishkov and his co-thinkers including the politician Gavriil Popov, in their contemporaneous campaign against the very concept ‘nation’.7

7 See Tishkov (1996); Tishkov (1997); Tishkov (1998). Dr Aleksandr Osipov has provided the most cogent and searching critique of the NKA experiment in Russia, which commenced in 1996.8 There is a paradox. The evident weaknesses of the NCA form notwithstanding, it is surprisingly popular. Osipov has noted:9

8 See, e.g. Osipov (2004); Osipov (2010). 9 Osipov (2010), 42–3. The concept of ‘autonomy’ still sounds attractive to ethnic activists. Despite legislative restrictions and bureaucratic burdens, the number of NCAs has grown over the years. By early 1999, 227 NCAs had been registered, of which 160 were local, 60 regional, and 7 federal. The respective figures for the beginning of 2005 were: 315, 173, and 16 (in total, 504). On 1 January 2009, there were 717 organizations, of which: 488 were local, 211 regional, and 18 federal.

NCAs represent more than 60 ethnicities – in particular, federal NCAs have been established on behalf of Armenians, Assyrians, Azeris, Belarusians, Chuvash, Germans, Jews, Karachais, Kazakhs, Koreans, Kurds, Lezghins, Lithuanians, Poles, Roma, Serbs, Tatars, and Ukrainians. Regional and local NCAs have been set up in 72 of 83 constituent units (subjects) of the Russian Federation. Several NCAs represent ethnic groups officially acknowledged as small indigenous peoples of Russia.



This chapter addresses this paradox – why does the concept sound attractive? My aim is to show that autonomy has rather deeper roots in Russia than might at first be supposed. I have been much assisted in this by the late Professor Oleg Kutafin’s10 monumental work.11

10 Oleg Emelyanovich Kutafin (1937–2008) Soviet then Russian constitutional legal scholar, President of the Moscow State Legal Academy, which is now named after him. 11 Kutafin (2006). I start with autonomy in the Russian Empire. This took a wide variety of forms and I give an overview of a number of cases: Ukraine, Georgia, Poland, Khiva and Bukhara, Finland, the Baltics, Russian Germans, Tatars and Inorodtsy. Second, I turn to religious diversity in Russia, after Catherine II’s reforms. Third, I look at the extensive scholarly literature on the constitutional role of autonomy in the later years of the Russian Empire. Fourth, I examine Bolshevik nationalities policy before and after the 1917 Revolution and the creation of the territorial autonomies in 1920–1922. Fifth, I have a number of criticisms of Terry Martin’s The affirmative action empire and turn to a Tatar case study. My conclusion emphasises the deep roots of autonomy in Russia.





Autonomy in Tsarist Russia


Robert Suny points out that: ‘With Ivan IV’s conquests of Kazan and Astrakhan in the mid-sixteenth century, the Muscovite state incorporated ethnically compact non-Russian territories, indeed and alien polity, and transformed a relatively homogenized Russia into a multinational empire … when the problem of security was settled, Moscow allowed local elites, though no longer sovereign, to rule and atraditional customs and laws to continue in force.’12 Kutafin makes the point that, as it expanded, the Russian Empire often preserved in the territories it incorporated their local laws and institutions, and accorded them more or less broad autonomy.13 He reminds his readers that the legal status of Russian regions changed continually over time.14

12 Suny (1997), 21–2. 13 Kutafin (2006), 41. 14 Kutafin (2006), 42.





Finland


The most famous example of autonomy in the Russian Empire was Finland, about which Russian scholars have conflicting views. Some consider Finland to have been an independent state in de facto union with Russia. Others see it as a province with a high degree of autonomy. A third camp see Finland as a non-sovereign state united with Russia on the basis of subordination. The late nineteenth-century scholar N.M. Korkunov15 considered these distinctions to have enormous practical significance.16 Thus, the Grand Duchy of Finland was considered to be a special independent state united with Russia only through the Romanov dynasty.

15 Nikolai Mikhailovich Korkunov (1853–1904) leading scholar at St Petersburg University. I referred to him in Chapter 2. 16 N.M. Korkunov Russkiye gosudarstvennoye pravo (Russian state law), Vol. 1, St Petersburg (1892), 134; Kutafin (2006), 46. Finland was incorporated into the Empire in three stages. With the Treaty of Nystad, on 30 August 1721, at the end of the Great Northern War, Estonia, Livonia, Ingria and southeast Finland (Kexholmslän and part of Karelia) were transferred to Russia. The Peace Treaty of 1743 transferred more territory; and on 5 September 1809 by the Treaty of Fredrikshamn, the remainder of Finland was annexed to Russia. During 1808–1809 Aleksandr I by degrees recognised Finnish autonomy and, on 16 March 1809, the Emperor opened the four-chamber Seim, or legislature, which had been elected according to Swedish electoral law. The chambers were: knighthood and nobility; spiritual leaders; town-dwellers; and peasants. Legislation was considered to have been adopted if it passed three out of four chambers. That is, the full Swedish model of that time.

The linguistic situation was extremely interesting.17 On 25 October 1858 Finnish was allowed to be used in legal disputes and dissertations; on 1 August 1863 the Finnish language received equal status with Swedish for all purposes; and on 9 February 1863 the Russian language ceased to be a compulsory subject in primary schools. On 20 February 1865 the Finnish language became the working language in judicial and administrative institutions along with Swedish and on 30 November 1871 the Finnish Senate was given the right to determine the language of secondary education.

17 Kutafin (2006), 55. Thus from 1863 it could be said that Finland was part of the Russian Empire with its own laws and judicial and administrative institutions. On 3 September 1863 Aleksandr II opened the Seim with the words that he preserved the basis of a constitutional monarchy reflecting the spirit of the Finnish people. From that date the Seim was elected every five years. Other parts of the Empire, although not autonomous in the sense that Finland enjoyed, nevertheless had administrative or legal status giving the impression that they were on the way to autonomous status.





The Baltics


Thus, the Baltic territories were incorporated into Russia in the eighteenth century. Ingeborg Fleischhauer18 points out that the Peace of Nystadt in 1721 reconfirmed the Baltic Germans’ freedom of religion and cultus, that is, Protestantism including a Protestant University at Dorpat, German administration in town and country and German law. The Baltic territories were divided into the Estlyandskiy, Liflyandskiy and Kurlyandskiy governorates, as part of one general-governorate with Ostzeiskiy Krai. The inhabitants of these krai maintained their own religious observance, as well as their own laws – the German, Swedish, Polish laws in operation before Russian rule.

18 Fleischhauer (1981). With the great judicial reforms of Aleksandr II in 1864, the Baltics obtained their own judicial organs. They had their own system of local government. The Baltic nobility provided the Empire with soldiers, diplomats, senior civil servants and members of the court.





Russian Germans


Not in fact mentioned by Kutafin were the Russian Germans as a whole. Fleishhauer points out that the Russian Empire’s attitude to non-Russians was ‘based on pragmatic and not on racial grounds’.19 National groups considered useful for imperial purposes were to be attracted and favoured by the state. The Russian Germans comprised three groups: the Baltic Germans, often called ostseizskiye nemtsy; the urban population; and the colonists.

19 Fleischhauer (1981), D1066. According to the 1897 census the three together numbered about 2 million, with 1.3 million colonists; half a million urban-dwellers (375,000 in industry, 100,000 in trade and commerce, 50,000 in academic professions and 35,000 in the civil service and army, as well as more than 50,00 belonging to the hereditary or personal nobility or honorary citizens).20 By the outbreak of World War I the German population was much greater and very wealthy.

20 Fleischhauer (1981), D1072–3. According to Fleischhauer, this success was in part the result of their legally privileged situation. I have already mentioned the privileges of the Baltic Germans. The urban Germans enjoyed 10 years’ exemption from taxes outside the cities and five years within them; free movement in Russia and the right to leave when they wishes; large state loans for new factories; tax-free imports and exports in the first years; use of local Russian labour including serfs; and membership of the Russian guilds with all their privileges.21

21 Fleischhauer (1981), D1078. German peasants settling in Russia had the following privileges. According to the ‘call’ of 1763, every male farmer was given 30 to 60 desyatiny of arable land, granted by the Crown as community property belonging to the colony in which the farmer settled; 30 years’ exemption from taxes; exemption from all forms of military service for ever; freedom of movement; the right to acquire goods, chattels and land in the whole Empire; freedom to leave and to export all legally acquired property; free transition to another social estate; state loans; etc.22

22 Fleischhauer (1981), D1082–3. The oldest German colonies were those on the Volga, with about 30,000 settlers after the first two decades of settlement. Colonies were established in other parts of Russia. And at the beginning of the twentieth-century colonies were established in Siberia. There were some 300 colonies in all.





Ukraine


Ukraine by degrees lost all its initial autonomy. Thus, the Pereyaslavskaya Rada of 8 January 1654 decided on the union of Ukraine and Russia (in 1954 Khrushchev celebrated its anniversary by ‘giving’ Crimea to Ukraine). The Martovskiy Statei of 1654 created the autonomous status of Ukraine within Russia, together with the rights and privileges of the Cossack elders and Ukrainian institutions and clerics. The territory of Ukraine was divided into three parts: Left Bank Ukraine, divided into polka; Slobodskiy Ukraine, also divided into five polki; and the Zaparozhskiy Sech, the most autonomous part of Ukraine. Polki and ‘hundreds’ (sotni) were the territorial administrative units of Ukraine. Power was concentrated in the hands of the hetman, who commanded military forces and made and administered the laws. Control over the activities of the Ukrainian authorities was instituted in 1663 by the Malorossiyskaya Prikaz, replaced in 1722 by the Malorossiyskaya Kollegiya.

In 1734 the power of the hetman was abolished and the Cossack forces came under Russian command. However, in 1747, the hetman institution was revived, with control over the Zaparozhskiy Sech.

In 1764 the hetman was again abolished and the Malorossiyskaya Kollegiya was again put in charge of Ukraine, with a presidency of four Russians and four Ukrainians. However, Ukrainians were not able to participate and the general-governorate of Malaya Rossiya was created. The laws effective in Ukraine were the second and third Lithuanian Statutes and Magdeburg (town) law. In 1728 a commission was established for the codification of Ukrainian law, which in 1743 completed preparation of the Svod ‘Law by which the Malaya Rossiyskaya people are to be judged’. It had three books: Lithuanian Statute, Saxon Zertsala and the book ‘Order’. Courts were established in 1763 on the basis of the Lithuanian statutes. In 1796 Left Bank Ukraine became the Malorossiyskaya Guberniya (governorate); and Slobodskaya Ukraine became the Slobodsko-Ukrainsko Guberbniya. Right Bank Ukraine was divided into the Kiev, Volynsk and Podolsk governorates, in 1832 renamed the Kiev general-governorate. In this way, Ukraine lost all its autonomy.





Georgia


Initially, Georgia was seen as a state under Russia’s protection. Closer ties were instituted by the Treaty of 1783, according to which Georgia’s Tsar Irakliy recognised no authority higher than himself save the Russian Empress, who, for her part, undertook to protect him from external foes. The final unification of Russia and Georgia took place in 1801 at the request in 1800 of Tsar Georgiy to Paul I. On 12 January 1801 Aleksandr I explained to the Georgian people in a manifesto that, although he desired to preserve the independence of Georgia, he was obliged by force of circumstances to preserve the Tsardom of Georgia for Russia. The Ulozheniye of Tsar Vakhtang was preserved as the law of Georgia. Much blood was shed subsequently for Georgia’s independence: Lenin’s last struggle was against Stalin, on the question of independence for Georgia, which Lenin supported.23

23 Lewin (2005).





Poland


In the same way as Ukraine and Georgia, Poland continuously lost its autonomy.

As a result of the three Partitions of the eighteenth century and, as a result of the decision of the Congress of Vienna in 1814, Russia gained part of the territory of Poland, on which the Polish Tsardom (kingdom) was organised. In 1815 Poland received a Constitutional Charter and the status of kingdom. The Russian Emperor became also the King of Poland. Its legislature was the Seim, with elections in 1820 and 1825. The Seim contained a Senate, composed of the Polish nobility, and the Posolskiy Izby, elected in the localities. The Seim had full legislative competence, including fiscal. The official state language was Polish.

However, after the Polish uprising of 1830, the organic status was published, amending the Polish constitution and making Poland an inseparable part of the Empire. The customs border between Russia and Poland was abolished in 1850.24

24 Kutafin (2006), 44–5. After the further uprisings in 1863–1864, the last traces of autonomy were extinguished. However, even after the end of autonomy the Code Civile of Napoleon and other French legislation were preserved, with obligatory force in the Duchy of Poland from 1 May 1808 and extended in 1810 to the governorates taken from Austria.





Khiva and Bukhara


However, Kutafin points out,25 far from all the states incorporated into Russia lost their autonomy. For example, Khiva, now in Uzbekistan, was to a large extent independent even though de facto fully subordinated to Russia. According to the Treaty of 12 August 1873 Said-Mukhamed Rakhim-Bogodur Khan maintained executive power.

25 Kutafin (2006), 45. Bukhara, also now in Uzbekistan, had even higher autonomy. Its status was defined in a Treaty of 28 September 1873. The Emir of Bukhara exercised power from Tashkent and from 1893 Russia maintained an ambassador. Of course, neither Khiva nor Bukhara was formally part of the Russian Empire. They were considered to be states under Russia’s protection.





Tatars


Kutafin says little about the Tatars, who have been the focus of some of my own work. Professor Uli Schamiloglu, born in New York of Kazan Tatar ancestry, now professor at Wisconsin-Madison,26 provides a more complete picture.27 His 1990 article concerns Sihabäddin Märcani (1818–1889), the father of modern Kazan Tatar historiography, who wrote in the nineteenth century, during the Tatar revival within the Russian Empire. Schamiloglu examines the process by which the ‘modern identity of the Kazan Tatars was created’. My own first response is that if the creation of Tatar national identity was crystallised in the nineteenth century, that is proof positive that the Tsarist Empire had not destroyed Tatar consciousness: on the contrary. Thus, Märcani was one of the leading Kazan Tatar intellectuals of the nineteenth century: following early education in a village madrassa in Taşkiçü, he received an Islamic education in Bukhara and Samarkand and returned to positions in a mosque and madrassa and was appointed teacher at the Russo-Tatar Teacher’s School.28

26 See his home page http://www.turko-tatar.com/uli/ (accessed 19 February 2013), and http://lca.wisc.edu/new_web/?q=node/50. 27 Schamiloglu (1990). 28 Schamiloglu (1990), 41–2. Schamiloglu points out that the name ‘Tatar’ was ‘originally not the self-appellation of the Muslim Turks of the Middle Volga region. Before the Mongol conquests they were known as ‘Bulğars’. The name ‘Tatar’ was introduced during the Mongol period and gained broad acceptance by the Kazan Tatars only in the nineteenth century. Märcani wanted, for nationalist reasons, to emphasise that under the ‘Tatar yoke’ (1223–1480) ‘the Russian dukes could not act without the permission of the Tatar Khans’ and emphasised the Russians’ role as tax collectors.29 It is no surprise that many Russian words – for money, treasury, etc. – are of Turkic origin.

29 Schamiloglu (1990), 45. But Schamiloglu points out that there is a dearth of historical sources for the period of the Khanate of Kazan from the 1430s to 1552, when Kazan was conquered by Ivan IV, not least because of Russian destruction of libraries and archives. He reminds us that:

Following the fall of the Khanate in 1552 as the first foreign conquest of the emerging Russian Empire, elements of the dynastic and tribal elite of the Khanate of Kazan either fled to other states, were incorporated into the developing Russian aristocracy, or were simply vanquished. The indigenous Muslim Turkic population in or around the city of Kazan was resettled beyond a perimeter extending many kilometres away from the city. The indigenous and newly-resettled population of the surrounding territories (again including the former core territories of the Volga Bulğarian state) would later form the core group of the modern Kazak Tatars. In the 19th century these Muslim Turks of the Middle Volga region began to acquire a shared identity under the name ‘Kazan Tatar’.30



30 Schamiloglu (1990), 40–41. Märcani’s work was not only an astonishing achievement in adverse circumstances, but more importantly a classic nineteenth-century ‘creation of an ideology of national identity’, as described by Eric Hobsbawm.31 It was also an essential ingredient for the creation of the Tatar ASSR in 1920–1921.

31 Hobsbawm (1983).





Inorodtsy


Russian legislation defined the situation of the so-called inorodtsy, the non-Slavic population of the Empire.32 These included the samoyedy of Arkhangelsk guberniya, the kocheviye inorodtsy of Stavropol guberniya, the Kalmyks of Astrakhan and Stavropol, the Kyrgyz of the Inner Horde, the inorodtsy of the Akmola and Semipalatinsk regions of present-day Kazakhstan and the Urals and Turgan regions, the inorodtsy of the Zakaspian regions and Jews.33 Inorodtsy enjoyed local government differing from the norm according to need. Inorodtsy in different localities used the Kitaiskoye Ulozheniye of 1725, the Mongolskoye Ulozheniye of 1798, the Ulozheniye of the Mongol and Kalmyk peoples of 1690.

32 Kutafin (2006), 77. 33 Svodof laws of the Russian Empire, v.9, Art.1. The Bashkirs were seminomadic tribes inhabiting the territory, forest and steppe, beyond the Volga. Robert Baumann pointed out that ‘despite its relative proximity to Moscow Bashkiria remain unassimilated by the late nineteenth century … an apt indication of Russia’s inability to impose civil administration on the vast realms to which it had laid claim.’34 Ivan IV penetrated the Bashkir lands following his conquest of Kazan and Astrakhan in the 1550s and a fort was established at Ufa in 1586. There were uprisings in 1662–1664, in 1676, from 1681–1683 and from 1705–1711. Following the creation of the fortified town of Orenburg on the Ural River in 1735, Empress Anna’s agent Ivan Kirillov mercilessly crushed the consequent uprising. Finally, after the influx of many Russians, bringing the population of Orenburg to 884,787, and the defeat of the Pugachev Rebellion (1773–1775), the Empire established a military administration for the Bashkirs similar to that of the Cossacks, named the Bashkirskoye voisko or Bashkir Host, with 11 cantons, each with a nachalnik.35 They fought splendidly in the war with Napoleon.

34 Baumann (1987), 490–91. 35 Baumann (1987), 493. In this way, paradoxically, the Bashkirs maintained their identity into the Soviet period and the creation of present-day Bashkortostan.

Suny also observes that ‘some elites like the Tatar and Ukrainian nobles, dissolved into the Russian dvoryanstvo, but others, like the German barons of the Baltic or the Swedish aristocrats of Finland, retained privileges and separate identities.’36

36 Suny (1997), 27.





Religious autonomy in Tsarist Russia


Crews37 points out that, as he was informed by a Tatar interlocutor, Islam arrived in Russia before Orthodox Christianity, so that Islam is the most ‘traditional’ of Russian religions. By the tenth century there were substantial Muslim communities along the River Volga, in Siberia, in the Caucasus and the oasis towns of Central Asia.38 By the reign of Catherine II Russia had ruled the Muslim peoples settled along the River Volga for more than two centuries, while she incorporated the Muslims of Crimea and the regions north of the Caucasus and Caspian Sea. By the late nineteenth century Muslims resided in 89 provinces and territories of the Empire, together with the protectorates of Khiva and Bukhara. The 1897 first Imperial census registered 14 million Muslims (out of a total population of 125,640,021),39 although the true number was probably 20 million. Three and a half million lived in European Russia and 12 million spoke languages of the ‘Turkic-Tatar’ group.40

37 Crews (2006). 38 Crews (2006), 11. 39 See http://www.demoscope.ru/weekly/ssp/census.php?cy=0 (accessed 18 November 2012). 40 Crews (2006), 12–14. While Peter the Great had oppressed the Muslim, especially Tatar, nobility, Catherine the Great, inspired by Enlightenment rationality, ordered in 1787 the printing of the Qur’an for distribution free of charge to Kazakhs on the frontier and in September 1788 ordered the establishment in Ufa of an ‘Ecclesiastical Assembly of the Muhammedan Creed’ (Dukhovnoye sobraniye Magometanskovo zakona).41 Mukhamedzhan Khusainov was given the title of mufti, with a salary, and was to be aided by ‘two or three Mullahs chosen from the Kazan Tatars’. By 1800 the mufti and three judges attached to the Assembly administered oral examinations for more than 1,900 clerics.42 The first mosque was built in Moscow in 1823, despite protests from the Orthodox Church.43 Kazan had Tatar schools in the time of Catherine II.44

41 Crews (2006), 52–3. 42 Crews (2006), 56. 43 Crews (2006), 93. 44 Crews (2006), 200.





Late Tsarist scholars on territorial and personal autonomy


Kutafin was not the first to analyse questions of territorial and non-territorial autonomy in Russia and his book uncovers a rich array of previous scholarship and opinion on the topic.

At the end of the nineteenth century leading Russian scholars argued at length as to the nature of autonomy in the Russian Empire. In 1892 N.M. Korkunov45 discussed the various forms of autonomy, preserving local laws and institutions, a greater of lesser extent.46 V.V. Sokolovskiy also sought to prove that the existence of autonomy did not contradict the integrity of the Russian Empire.47 In 1905 F.F. Kokoshkin48 argued that regional autonomy did not mean the creation of a state within a state.49 All these authors insisted that autonomy should not be confused with federation. For Kokoshkin, if there were to be local laws, as existed in Russia, there must be local legislatures. The state Duma could legislate for all-Russian matters and local legislatures on the local scale.50 The Empire retained its legislative supremacy.51 According to him, regional autonomy was the highest stage of the development of decentralisation. In 1908 he distinguished between autonomous krai and autonomous oblast.52 Kokoshkin returned to this question in 1917 and emphasised its close connection with the national (ethnic) question.53 He argued that decentralisation to a greater or lesser extent was a powerful means for satisfying the demands of ethnic groups to self-determination, especially cultural self-determination, and that this could take the form of territorial decentralisation, but also in other forms. He made the case that in Russia it would be impossible to build a federation on the basis of national territorial autonomy. For example, Ukraine with a population of 25–30 million, more than that of Spain, would far outweigh other regions of Russia. To give unequal competences to the component parts of Russia would also lead to a blind alley, he thought.54 Or, rather than a federation, to confederation.

45 Nikolai Mikhailovich Korkunov (1853–1904). Lectured at St Petersburg State University. 46 Korkunov (1892), 133; Kutafin (2006), 5. 47 Sokolovskiy (1890), cited in Kutafin (2006), 6. 48 Fyodor Fyodorovich Kokoshkin (1871–1918), jurist, leading member of the Constitutional Democratic (Cadet) party, member of the provisional government in 1917. 49 F.F. Kokoshkin Oblastnaya avtonomiy I yedinstvo Rossiy (Regional autonomy and the unity of Russia) Moscow (1905), 3. 50 Kokoshkin (1905), 9–10. 51 Kokoshkin (1905), 12. 52 F.F. Kokoshkin, Russkoye gosudarstvennor pravo (Russian state law), 2nd edn, Moscow (1908), 154–55; see also his Lektsiy po obsehmu gosudarstvennomu pravu (Lectures on general state law), 2nd edn, Moscow (1912), 305–6. 53 F.F. Kokoshkin Avtonomiya i federatsiya (Autonomy and federation), Petrograd (1917), 3–5. 54 Kokoshkin (1917), 9–10. In 1906 a collection Autonomy, federation and the national (ethnic) question 55 appeared edited by V.M. Gessen,56 arguing that national autonomy should precede the establishment of a parliamentary, rule of law state. Autonomy or other forms of political independence were the best way of resolving ethnic tensions. The authors pointed out57 that the centralised political government of Russia notwithstanding, it preserved within itself a series of local laws. In Privislenskiy Krai, the Code Civile of Napoleon I had been in force since 1808. In Ostzeiskiu Krai, a whole range of laws including Roman law were in force. In Finland, the Swedish laws on 1734 were in operation. Because of its Byzantine past, Bassarabiya had laws with Byzantine roots. In the governorates of Chernigov and Poltava, the Lithuanian statutes remained in force. In the Caucasus and many other places, special, particular laws were applied. Whole regions of the Russian Empire lived and had lived their own juridical life. The question of the unity of the state was also analysed by E. Pimenova,58 who argued that regional autonomy could be an efficient way of resolving the national (ethnic) problem in Russia.

55 Gessen (ed.) (1906) Avtonomiya, federatsiya i natsionalniy vopros (Autonomy, federation and the national (ethnic) question) St Petersburg. 56 Vladimir Matveevich Gessen (1868–1920), doctorate from Moscow State University, official of the Ministry of the Interior. 57 Gessen et al. (1906), 21–2, cited in Kutafin (2006), 19. 58 Pimenova (1906). G. Novotorzhskiy59 argued60 that the contemporary state has come into being through conquest and therefore consists of a whole range of regions, formed on a national as well as administrative basis. In Russia, these were Finland, Poland, Ukraine, Caucasus, the Pribaltiskiy Krai, Lithuania, Siberia and, finally, ‘Great Russia’. He wrote:61

59 Author of What is a rule of law state? (Paris 1914); The significance of the right to land for the working class; and The socialisation of land. 60 Novotorzhskiy (1906), 14–15. 61 Novotorzhskiy (1906), 21, cited in Kutafin (2006), 26. Finland at the present time has almost complete autonomy. The question of Polish autonomy demands resolution in the shortest possible time, as soon as the police state is abolished. The question of autonomy for the Caucasus is more complex, since many ethnicities are living there, mixed with each other. The Jewish question can be resolved by giving the Jews the full rights enjoyed by other ethnicities in Russia, and by guaranteeing the rights of the minorities which they will constitute in Poland, Ukraine, Great Russia and Byelorussia. As concerns the autonomies of Ukraine, Siberia autonomous regions in Great Russia, they can be created as required. As, so to say, autonomies of the second rank.



Other works of the same nature included A.T. Snarskiy’s 1907 Autonomy or federation? and the survey published in 1907 by the Pole, K. Kulchitskij (Mazovetskiy), Autonomy and federation in contemporary constitutional states.62

62 Kutafin (2006), 28. Kulchitskiy was one of a number of Russian authors who discussed ‘cultural–national autonomy’, although the Latvian Jewish scholar and politician M.Ya. Lazerson63 wrote in 1917 a book entitled Autonomy and federation,64 which argued forcefully for ‘personal autonomy’ – that is, non-territorial autonomy – drawing on Karl Renner’s writings. In his view, one should not, in a contemporary state, consider the population as tied to particular territories. Such a state, with many ethnicities cannot be divided into territories in each of which there is a single ethnicity.65 However, he argued strongly against the term ‘national–cultural autonomy’, which, in his view, confused the territorial principles and the personal principle.66

63 Maksim Yakovlevich Lazerson (1887–1952) sociologist and professor at St Petersburg then Petrograd State University, member of the Saime (Parliament) of inter-War independent Latvia, leader of the Jewish Ceire Cion party; from 1939 lectured at Columbia University, NewYork. 64 Lazerson (1917). 65 Lazerson (1917), 23–4. 66 Lazerson (1917), 25–6, cited in Kutafin (2006), 704.





Bolshevik policy on territorial autonomy


Kutafin makes the point that the Bolsheviks, as convinced centralists, approached the idea of national (ethnic) autonomy very cautiously.67 In my contributions referred to earlier, I have investigated in detail the way in which Lenin developed his position on the right of nations to self-determination in fierce argument with those like the Jewish Bund espoused non-territorial personal cultural autonomy on the Austro-Marxist model.

67 Kutafin (2006), 81. In October 1913 Lenin wrote the following, having stated that: ‘The right of nations to self-determination, i.e., the right to secede and form independent national states, will be dealt with elsewhere’:

Marxists are, of course, opposed to federation and decentralisation, for the simple reason that capitalism requires for its development the largest and most centralised possible states. Other conditions being equal, the class-conscious proletariat will always stand for the larger state. It will always fight against medieval particularism, and will always welcome the closest possible economic amalgamation of large territories in which the proletariat’s struggle against the bourgeoisie can develop on a broad basis.

Capitalism’s broad and rapid development of the productive forces calls for large, politically compact and united territories, since only here can the bourgeois class – together with its inevitable antipode, the proletarian class – unite and sweep away all the old, medieval, caste, parochial, petty-national, religious and other barriers.68



68 Lenin (1913). He emphasised that centralism must be understood only as democratic centralism: ‘Far from precluding local self-government, with autonomy for regions having special economic and social conditions, a distinct national composition of the population, and so forth, democratic centralism necessarily demands both.69

69 Lenin (1913). And on this basis he was in favour of local autonomy:

Obviously, one cannot conceive of a modern, truly democratic state that did not grant such autonomy to every region having any appreciably distinct economic and social features, populations of a specific national composition, etc. The principle of centralism, which is essential for the development of capitalism, is not violated by this (local and regional) autonomy, but on the contrary is applied by it democratically, not bureaucratically. The broad, free and rapid development of capitalism would be impossible, or at least greatly impeded, by the absence of such autonomy, which facilitates the concentration of capital, the development of the productive forces, the unity of the bourgeoisie and the unity of the proletariat on a country-wide scale; for bureaucratic interference in purely local (regional, national, and other) questions is one of the greatest obstacles to economic and political development in general, and an obstacle to centralism in serious, important and fundamental matters in particular.70



70 Lenin (1913). And further:

Why national areas with populations, not only of half a million, but even of 50,000, should not be able to enjoy autonomy; why such areas should not be able to unite in the most diverse ways with neighbouring areas of different dimensions into a single autonomous ‘territory’ if that is convenient or necessary for economic intercourse – these things remain the secret of the Bundist Medem.71



71 Lenin (1913). Lenin returned to this question in 1916, when he wrote:

But as everyone knows, in practice, a reform is often merely a step towards revolution. It is autonomy that enables a nation forcibly retained within the boundaries of a given state to crystallise into a nation, to gather, assess and organise its forces, and to select the most opportune moment for a declaration … in the ‘Norwegian’ spirit: We, the autonomous diet of such-and-such a nation, or of such-and-such a territory, declare that the Emperor of all the Russias has ceased to be King of Poland, etc.72



72 Lenin (1916/64). Thus it was clear that before the October Revolution, Lenin and the Bolsheviks were in favour of the idea of creating broad regional autonomies in Russia. This continued after the Revolution. In 1918 Lenin wrote:

We are for democratic centralism. And it must be clearly understood how vastly different democratic centralism is from bureaucratic centralism on the one hand and from anarchism on the other. The opponents of centralism continually put forward autonomy and federation as a means of struggle against the uncertainties of centralism. As a matter of fact, democratic centralism in no way excludes autonomy, on the contrary, it presupposes the necessity of it … The example of the Russian Soviet Republic shows us particularly clearly that federation, which we are introducing and will introduce, is now the surest step towards the most lasting union of the various nationalities of Russia into a single democratic centralised Soviet state.73



73 Lenin (1918/1965), 207. In his letter of 26 September 1922, at the time of increasing conflict with Stalin as to the right of Georgia to gain independence outside the USSR – to secede – he wrote: ‘[W]e consider ourselves, the Ukrainian S.S.R. and others, equal, and enter with them, on an equal basis, into a new union, a new federation, the Union of the Soviet Republics of Europe and Asia.’74

74 Lenin (1922). In his Last Testament, written in 1922–1923, Lenin wrote, warning against Stalin:

It is quite natural that in such circumstances the ‘freedom to secede from the union’ by which we justify ourselves will be a mere scrap of paper, unable to defend the non-Russians from the onslaught of that really Russian man, the Great-Russian chauvinist, in substance a rascal and a tyrant, such as the typical Russian bureaucrat is.75



75 Lenin (1922–23). Lenin by then regarded Stalin as just such a Great Russian chauvinist.

Thus, the 1918 Constitution of the RSFSR76 provided in Article 1, Chapter 4:

76 See English translation at http://www.marxists.org/history/ussr/government/constitution/1918/index.htm (accessed 28 March 2011). 8. In its effort to create a league – free and voluntary, and for that reason all the more complete and secure – of the working classes of all the peoples of Russia, the Third Congress of Soviets merely establishes the fundamental principles of the Federation of Russian Soviet Republics, leaving to the workers and peasants of every people to decide the following question at their plenary sessions of their soviets, namely, whether or not they desire to participate, and on what basis, in the Federal government and other Federal soviet institutions.



And in Article 2, Chapter 5:

11. The soviets of those regions which differentiate themselves by a special form of existence and national character may unite in autonomous regional unions, ruled by the local congress of the soviets and their executive organs.

These autonomous regional unions participate in the Russian Socialist Federated Soviet Republic upon a Federal basis.77



77 Website:http://www.marxists.org/history/ussr/government/constitution/1918/article2.htm (accessed 19 February 2013). On this basis, the Labouring Commune of Germans of the Volga was organised at the end of 1918, which in 1924 became an autonomous republic. The Bashkir ASSR was organised within the RSFSR in 1919, followed in 1920–1921 by the Kirgiz (Kazakh) ASSR and the Tatar, Dagestan and Gorkiy autonomous republics, the Karelian Labouring Commune and the Chuvash, Kalmyk, Mariy, Botskaya (Udmurtskaya) autonomous oblast (regions). In 1921–1922 the Yakutsk ASSR, the Karachaev-Cherkess, Kabardino-Balkar, Komi, Mongol-Buryat and a series of other autonomous oblast were created.78

78 Kutafin (2006), 133–34. The 1924 Constitution of the USSR79 contained the following:

79 English translation at http://www.answers.com/topic/1924-constitution-of-the-ussr (accessed 28 March 2011). CHAPTER II Sovereign Rights of the Member Republics

ARTICLE 3.

The sovereignty of the member Republics is limited only in the matters indicated in the present Constitution, as coming within the competence of the Union. Outside of those limits, each member Republic exerts its public powers independently; the USSR protects the rights of the member Republics.

ARTICLE 4.

Each one of the member Republics retains the right to freely withdraw from the Union.

ARTICLE 5.

The member Republics will make changes in their Constitutions to conform with the present Constitution.

ARTICLE 6.

The territory of the member Republics cannot be modified without their consent; also, any limitation or modification or suppression of Article 4 must have the approval of all the member Republics of the Union.

ARTICLE 7.

Just one federal nationality is established for the citizens of the member Republics.





The affirmative action Empire?


In his book of that title,80 Terry Martin starts with the question ‘Why did the Bolsheviks adopt this radical strategy?’. His answer starts: ‘When they seized power in October 1917, they did not yet possess a coherent nationalities policy.’81 As we have seen already, that is not quite right. He acknowledges that they had a ‘powerful slogan’ but states that it was shared with Woodrow Wilson, a surprising proposition. It seems that Martin is not sufficiently aware of the passionate debates of the pre-war years on the question of the right of nations to self-determination, as against personal cultural non-territorial autonomy.82

80 Martin (2001). 81 Martin (2001), 2. 82 Although he cites a number of Lenin’s key works at n. 10 on 3. According to Martin, the content of Soviet nationalities policy was ‘finally delineated’ at the 12th Party Congress in April 1923 and at a special Central Committee conference on nationalities policy in June 1923.83 That is, in the very last months of Lenin’s life and after he had written to Stalin, with whom as already noted he was in bitter conflict over Georgian independence, on 5 March 1923:

83 Martin (2001), 9. You have been so rude as to summon my wife to the telephone and use bad language. Although she had told you that she was prepared to forget this, the fact nevertheless became known through her to Zinoviev and Kamenev. I have no intention of forgetting so easily what has been done against me, and it goes without saying that what has been done against my wife I consider having been done against me as well. I ask you, therefore, to think it over whether you are prepared to withdraw what you have said and to make your apologies, or whether you prefer that relations between us should be broken off.84



84 At http://www.marxists.org/archive/lenin/works/1923/mar/05.htm (accessed 28 March 2011). This was Lenin’s last written text.

Martin, therefore, cannot explain how, well before April 1923, most of the territorial autonomies that have continued in existence until the present day had already been created.

Neither does Martin have anything to say about the rich history of autonomy in the Tsarist Empire. The obvious point is this: Soviet nationalities policy did not come from nothing. It had deep roots not only in Bolshevik ideology, but also in Tsarist history.

However, Martin does provide series of case studies showing in detail how the Bolsheviks responded to the rising tide of nationalism ‘by systematically promoting the national consciousness of its ethnic minorities and establishing for many of then the characteristic institutional forms of the nation-state.’85

85 Martin (2001), 1. Thus, he notes that:

The Tatar national movement was among the strongest in the Soviet Union, and certainly the strongest in the Soviet era. The Tatar nationalist elite lobbied aggressively for and vigorously supported to Soviet policy of korenizatsiya: the formation of a Tatar republic, the promotion of Tatars into leadership positions, the use of the Tatar language in government and education, and support for Tatar national culture. However, this movement also faced formidable resistance. Tatars formed a narrow plurality in their own republic (48.7% Tatar and 43.1% Russian).86



86 Martin (2001), 58. But he had not seen Schamiloglu’s work, apparently.





A Tatar case study


Peter Frank provided a fascinating study of the CPSU First Secretary, including details of the careers of the first and second secretaries of the Tatar Obkom of the CPSU, based on the Tatar ASSR.87 Terry Martin did not see this work either.

87 Frank (1971). As was the usual Soviet practice, Fikret Akhmedzhanovich Tabeev,88 the First Secretary, was a Tatar and Nikonov, his Second Secretary, was a Russian.89 Tabeev was born into a Tatar peasant family in 1928 and became a collective farmer, before attending Kazan University, one of the oldest in Russia. In 1951 he became a Party member and was appointed assistant lecturer at the university. In six years he rose to be reader and head of the department of political economy, having successfully defended his kandidat thesis. He moved to full-time Party work in 1957 and in 1960 at the very young age of 32 he was appointed First Secretary.

88 See biography and picture at http://gazetart.su/articles/43–44_24859/48306/ (accessed 28 March 2011). 89 Frank (1971), 181. I can add to Frank’s account that Tabeev served as First Secretary from 28 October 1960 to 2 November 1979 and from 1979 to 1986 was USSR Ambassador to Afghanistan. In 1989 he became a People’s Deputy of the USSR. In 2008 he was awarded the medal ‘For services to the Republic of Tatarstan’. As far as I know he is still alive. Tabeev’s successor as First Secretary was Rashid Musinovich Musin (1927–1982), who served until his death in 1982. His successor, Gumer Ismailovich Usmanov90 (b. 1932), who was Chairman of the Council of Ministers of the Tatar ASSR from 1966 to 1982, served as First Secretary of the Obkom from 1982 until 23 September 1989. In 1986 he became a Member of the Central Committee of the CPSU and in 1989 a Secretary of the CC. His successor was none other than Mintimer Sharipovich Shaimiev, the first President of the Republic of Tatarstan from 12 June 1991 to 25 March 2010.

90 Biography and picture at http://www.hrono.info/biograf/bio_u/usmanov_gi.html (accessed 28 March 2011). These details show how the ‘affirmative action’ policies of the USSR were no ‘Potyomkin village’. The ethnic first secretaries were substantial political and administrative figures and there was a strong continuity.





Conclusion


It should be no surprise at all that the word ‘autonomy’ resonates so strongly in contemporary Russia. I have shown that this was no Soviet trick or show, but that territorial and indeed the concepts of non-territorial autonomy have deep roots in Russia and its history.





Chapter 8

Human rights in the Yeltsin period




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-9

In this chapter, I delve into the rich and surprising history of Russia’s unexpected plunge into the new world of European human rightswithin one year and ratify within standards – ironically developed in Western Europe as an ideological instrument in the Cold War. In order to place this momentous event into its proper context, I first survey the USSR’s approach to human rights, before asking two questions: first, why did the Council of Europe member states in 1996 permit Russia’s accession in the midst of the First Chechen War (1994–1997). And, second, why did Russia decide to subject itself to unprecedented (in its history) interference in its internal affairs. After all, the two pillars of Soviet international law had been state sovereignty, and the prohibition of interference in a state’s internal affairs.

Writing in 1993, Viktor Zaslavsky declared:

Today’s Russian youth is thoroughly Westernised. Global Western culture, with its emphasis on democracy, human rights and individual freedoms, is become a viable substitution for the traditional political culture in several crucial groups.1



1 Zaslavsky (1993), 49–50. In an anxious response, Rittersporn asked:

Why is he discovering the heuristic merits of categories such a ‘Western values’, ‘Market’ or ‘democracy’ without trying to see what they denote in the context in which he uses them and without asking himself if they have a clear and unambiguous meaning?2



2 Rittersporn (1993), 59–60. What was the USSR’s attitude to contemporary human rights?3 A new Soviet Constitution was unanimously approved on 5 December 1933. In September 1934 the USSR joined the League of Nations and was elected a permanent member of the Council. According to Andrey Vyshinsky, notorious as prosecutor of the Moscow show trials, the 1936 (Stalin) Constitution reflected the following changes:

3 See Bowring (1995). The complete triumph of the socialist system in all branches of the national economy, the fundamental realisation of socialism, the liquidation of the exploiter classes, the annihilation of man’s exploitat ion by man … all these factors evoked the necessity of changing the Constitution of the USSR so that the new Constitution should reflect all the changes … since 1924.4



4 Vyshinsky (1948a), 120. The first Constitution of the USSR was adopted on 31 January 1924.

Nikolai Bukharin, one of the most sophisticated and intellectually attractive members of the Bolshevik leadership, was a member of the drafting Commission, and later boasted that he had written the document from the first word to the last. He was reported as saying that the new Constitution was a document ‘which would make it impossible for the people any longer to be pushed aside’.5

5 KochanandAbraham (1990), 377.





The 1936 Stalin Constitution


The Stalin Constitution6 contained Chapter X, ‘Fundamental rights and duties of citizens’. Naturally, the first right guaranteed was the right to work (Article 118): ‘Citizens of the U.S.S.R. have the right to work, that is, are guaranteed the right to employment and payment for their work in accordance with its quantity and quality.’ This was followed by other social and economic rights: rest and leisure (Article 119); old age pensions and social security (Article 120); free education (Article 121); equal rights for women (Article 122); non-discrimination (Article 123); and freedom of conscience and religious belief (Article 124).

6 English translation at http://www.departments.bucknell.edu/russian/const/1936toc.html (accessed 12 October 2012). Freedom of speech, freedom of the press, freedom of assembly and freedom of demonstrations were all guaranteed in the following highly qualified form: ‘In conformity with the interests of the working people, and in order to strengthen the socialist system’ (Article 125). And freedom of association and self-organisation were similarly qualified: ‘In conformity with the interests of the working people, and in order to develop the organisational initiative and political activity of the masses of the people’ (Article 126).
