It is a surprise, therefore, that the Constitution gave guarantees of freedom from arbitrary arrest (Article 127) and inviolability of the home and secrecy of correspondence (Article 128) without qualification.

That Bukharin believed sincerely that the Constitution would be respected shows that he imagined that a genuine relaxation was taking place.7 But as is now well known the last months of 1936 were a lull in the Great Purge of 1934 to 1938, in which at least 10 to 15 million people died. The rights enshrined in the new Constitution were unenforceable: there were no remedies. Vyshinsky was not at all embarrassed by this:

7 Conquest (1971), 134. Proletarian declarations of rights frankly manifest their class essence, reflecting nothing of the desire of bourgeois declarations to shade off and mask the class character of the rights they proclaim.8



8 Vyshinsky (1948a), 554. Bukharin was tried in the Trial of the Twenty One on 2–13 March 1938 and was executed on 15 March 1938.





The USSR and the Universal Declaration of Human Rights


By a supreme irony of history, Vyshinsky was, at Stalin’s death in March 1953, appointed the USSR’s permanent delegate to the United Nations, of which the USSR was a founder member. Vyshinsky became Foreign Minister of the USSR in March 1949,9 having attended the Third Session of the UN General Assembly at the end of 1948. That was the session which adopted the Universal Declaration of Human Rights, on 10 December 1948. Forty-eight states voted in favour of the Declaration, while eight, including the USSR, abstained.

9 Vaksberg (1990), 279. In the introduction to a collection of Russian translations of major human rights instruments, published in the last year of the USSR, Lev Shestakov10 asked why the USSR abstained. He gave three reasons,11 which were also Vyshinsky’s reasons. First, in the opinion of the Soviet delegation, the draft Declaration had limited itself only to fixing formal rights and did not contain measures for the material and legislative guaranteeing of those rights. Second, the draft failed to enshrine the right of peoples to self-determination, on which the USSR had insisted, as a principled question. Third, Articles 19 and 20 (freedom of expression and freedom of association) had been drafted in such a way as to permit fascist propaganda; the USSR wished to include in Article 19 the words ‘the inalienable right of every human being is … the fight against fascism in the field of ideology, politics, state and social life’. Despite this abstention, the USSR ratified every one of the UN human rights treaties thereafter.

10 .03.1937–31.05.2009, Head of the Department of International Law at Moscow State University, until his death. I met him in 1993, with Grigoriy Tunkin (30.09.1906–23.08.1993). 11 Shestakov (1990), 6–7. The Brezhnev Constitution of 1977 followed the same model in Chapter 7, entitled ‘The basic rights, freedoms and obligations of citizens of the USSR’. On 11 March 1985 the Politburo of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union elected Mikhail Gorbachev General Secretary. He was 54 years old. At the 27th Congress of the CPSU in February 1986 he announced new policies of glasnost (‘openness’), perestroika (‘restructuring’), demokratizatsiya (‘democratisation’) and uskoreniye (‘acceleration’ of economic development). In place of Marxism–Leninism, there was a turn to ‘common human values’.12 Watching TV in Krasnodar in the summer of 1986, I saw Gorbachev addressing a conference of party officials in Khabarovsk, in the far east of Russia, informing them ‘Comrades, there will be a revolution!’ They looked aghast. One of the innovations under his leadership was the law of the USSR of 30 June 1987 ‘On judicial review of administrative acts’.

12 Gorbachev (1987) Perestroika i novoye mishleniye dlya nashei strany i dlya vsevo mira(Perestrioka and new thinking for our country and the whole world) (Moscow: Politizdat).





The Committee for Constitutional Supervision


In December 1988 Gorbachev proposed the creation of a Committee for Constitutional Supervision (CCS) of the USSR, the forerunner of the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation. On 23 December 1988 the law ‘On constitutional supervision in the USSR’ was adopted. According to the 19th All-Union Conference of the CPSU in 1988 the CCS had ‘the goal of guaranteeing the strictest correspondence of laws and Government decrees with the Constitution of the USSR’. It started work in April 1990.

Decisive steps had already been taken towards a greater respect for international law. On 10 February 1989 the Presidium of the Supreme Soviet passed a Decree recognising the compulsory jurisdiction of the UN’s International Court of Justice (ICJ) with respect to six human rights treaties, including the 1948 Genocide Convention and the 1984 Convention against Torture.13 This Decree reversed the reservation entered by the USSR when ratifying the Genocide Convention, refusing the compulsory jurisdiction of the ICJ, which led to one of the most important early advisory opinions of the ICJ, the Reservations to the Convention on the Prevention and Punishment of the Crime of Genocide opinion of 28 May 1951.14 Schweisfurth commented that ‘this move marked a positive shift in the previously negative attitude of the Soviet Union towards the principle judicial organ of the United Nations.’15 He saw, rightly I think, these developments as exemplifying a farewell to the traditional strict positivistic approach to human rights.16

13 Reported in 1989 (4) Interights Bulletin; the other treaties were the 1949 Convention for the Suppression of Traffic in Persons; the 1952 Convention on Political Rights of Women; the 1965 Convention on the Elimination of Racial Discrimination; and the 1979 Convention on the Elimination of Discrimination against Women. 14 Website:http://www.icj-cij.org/docket/files/12/4283.pdf (accessed 13 October 2012). 15 Schweisfurth (1990), 111. 16 Bowring (2008). The USSR had always ratified UN human rights treaties, but with no intention that they should apply within the Soviet Union, much less that there should be the possibility of interference in internal affairs. During its short existence the CCS heard 29 cases. Some of these were of considerable significance, demonstrating the seriousness with which the CCS took human rights.

Here are a few examples. First, in the Unpublished Laws Case of 29 November 1990 the CCS ruled that all unpublished USSR regulations (there were many such ‘secret’ laws) violated international human rights standards and would lose their force unless published within three months.17 Second, in the Right to Defence Counsel Case, the CCS (petitioned by the Union of Advocates), decided that the USSR law of 10 April 1990 on reforms to the criminal law, which restricted the right to defence counsel, violated both the Constitution and international standards.

17 Vedomosti Syezda Narodnikh Deputatov SSSR i Verkhovnovo Sovyeta SSSR, 1990 No. 50, 1080. Third, in the Ratification of the Optional Protocol Case (4 April 1991), in a move which put the USSR ahead of the UK and the USA, the CCS requested the Supreme Soviet to secure ratification by the USSR of the First Optional Protocol to the UN International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR).18 The USSR had ratified the ICCPR– in 1973 – but not the Protocol, which enables individual complaint to the UN’s Human Rights Committee by a person complaining of a violation. There was a commendably prompt and positive response. On 5 July 1991 the Supreme Soviet adopted two Resolutions acceding to the Optional protocol and recognising the jurisdiction of the HRC.19 Fourth, in the Dismissal on Attainment of Pensionable Age Case, the CCS considered the USSR Presidium Decree amending labour law by adding as a ground for termination by the employer ‘attainment by the worker of pensionable age with entitlement to old age pension’. The CCS declared: ‘The rights and freedoms granted to citizens in carrying out any form of activity in the field of work and occupation not forbidden by law form a most important part of human rights, and indicate the level of social protection attained in a society. In a law-governed state every human must be guaranteed equality of opportunity in the possession and use of these rights … The principle of equality before the law is enshrined in the Constitution, in statutes, and in international legal acts on human rights and freedoms … and its main guarantee is the prohibition of discrimination.’20

18 VSND SSSR ibid, 1991 No. 17, 502; see also Sovyetskaya Iustitsiya I 23 December 1991, 17. 19 Vedomosti SSSR, 1991 No. 29, 842, 843. 20 Vedomosti SSSR, 1991 No. 17, 501. Finally, there was the Residence Permit Case of 11 October 1991, where the CCS decided that propiska, the Soviet system of registration and residence permit, plainly contradicted that rights freely to move around and to choose one’s place of residence, which is to be found in several international human rights treaties, but was not then part of the Soviet Constitution (although it was in Article 21 of the USSR Declaration of the Rights and Freedoms of Man adopted on 5 September 1991). This is an issue that has continued to exercise the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation.

Savitskiy believed that the role of the CCS ‘was a secondary one and amounted to the publication of a number of purely symbolic “conclusions” that were binding on no-one’.21

21 Savitskiy (1993). I disagree. Indeed, in the view of van den Berg, the work of the CCS ‘in the field of human rights on the whole was positive’; it was not an illogical institution; he considered that ‘[I]n fact it had much more power than most constitutional courts in the world ….22 The examples I gave earlier show that its work brought about substantial changes.

22 vandenBerg (1992), 106–7. Further significant steps taken before the collapse of the USSR included the publication of the Conception of Judicial Reform published on 24 October 1991, after several years work, by Sergei Pashin,23 Sergei Vitsin,24 Tamara Morshchakova25 and others and the enactment on 22 November 1991 of the Declaration of the Rights and Freedoms of the Person and Citizen by the Supreme Soviet of the RSFSR.

23 He was born in 1962 and was from 1990 to 1996 the leading expert on judicial reform at the legal department of the President’s Administration, and introduced the first experiment in jury trial in nine regions of Russia in 1993. Following his fall from grace and dismissal, was twice (being once dismissed and reinstated) dismissed and is now a leading scholar and critic of Russian constitutionalism and the Constitutional Court. 24 Born in 1929, for many years professor and general of police at the Moscow University of the Ministry of the Interior; one of the most respected reformers in Russia. 25 She was born on 28 March 1936. From 1958 to 1991 she was a senior legal researcher and professor. She was elected a justice of the Constitutional Court on 29 October 1991 and from February 1995 to April 2002 she was Deputy Chairman of the Court. An amendment to the Law on the Constitutional Court enacted in January 2001 forced her to retire by reason of age. Many consider that this was a deliberate ploy to remove her, in view of her independent and principled stance. The Law of the RSFSR on the Constitutional Court of the RSFSR, drafted by Sergei Pashin, was signed by President Yeltsin on 12 July 1991.26 Pashin was only 29 years of age. Thirteen justices were elected by the Congress of People’s Deputies of the RSFSR on 30 October 1991. The justices elected Valeriy Zorkin (born 1943) as their Chairman. Zorkin has proved to be a great survivor: on 10 February 2012, the then President Medvedev appointed Zorkin to a further six-year term.27 Zorkin was Chairman of the Court from 1991 to 1993 and again from 2003 to 2006, and 2006 to 2009. Justices Gadis Gadzhiev (b. 1953, from Dagestan) and Yuriy Rudkin (b. 1951) are also still members of the Court. Tamara Morshchakova (b. 1936) was Vice-Chairman of the Court from 1995 to 2002 and a leading proponent of human rights, but was forced to retire on reaching the age of 65 in 2001 (see n. 25). She remains one of the most trenchant commentators on the Russian Constitution and court and critics of the Putin regime.

26 See Trochev (2008) for a complete history; also Henderson (2011a). 27 Website:http://www.brookings.edu/opinions/2012/0213_russia_zorkin_partlett.aspx (accessed 29 September 2012). The Court started work in January 1992. Pashin commented that the law represented ‘an important guarantee of the right of the citizen to judicial protection’. He wrote that the provisions of the new law were based on Chapter 4, Article 93 of the German Constitution, which provides that the Federal Constitutional Court considers cases ‘by way of petition on constitutionality, which may be laid by any person considering that one of his basic rights has been infringed by the state power’.28 Article 32 of the Constitution of the RSFSR, as amended in April 1992, stated that universally accepted human rights norms have precedence over the laws of the RSFSR and have direct effect.

28 Pashin (1992). From 6 July 1992 to 30 November 1992 the Court was occupied by the Case of the Communist Party, which did not produce the hoped for (by the applicants) definitive condemnation of the Communist Party, a Russian Nuremberg, but instead in a compromise decision ruled that President Yeltsin rightly dissolved the highest bodies of the Party, but also ruled that the Party could continue to exist at the local level.29 The Communist Party of the Russian Federation is to this day the only significant opposition party organised in every region of Russia.

29 Henderson (2011a). However, prior to July it rendered significant decisions. In its very first case, decided on 14 January 1992, the Formation of the Ministry of Security and Internal Affairs case.30 it held that President Yeltsin’s Decree of 19 December 1991 merging the KGB and the militia (police) was unconstitutional. President Yeltsin backed down, albeit reluctantly. In its judgment in the Tatarstan Sovereignty case, on 13 March 1992,31 the Court ruled that the provisions of Article 5(3) and Article 6 of the Declaration of State Sovereignty of theRepublic of Tatarstan of 30 August 1990, limiting the jurisdiction of RSFSR laws on the territory of Tatarstan, violated the RSFSR Constitution. Furthermore, it ruled that the Order of the Supreme Soviet of Tatarstan ‘On holding a referendum on the question of the state status of Tatarstan’ was unconstitutional to the extent that the formulation of the question presupposed that Tatarstan was a subject of international law and built its relations with the RSFSR and other republics and states on the basis of equal treaties, signifying that Tatarstan was not part of the RSFSR.

30 1-P-U/1992; the text of all judgments decisions is to be found at http://www.ksrf.ru/ru/Decision/Pages/default.aspx (accessed 13 October 2012). 31 P-R3–1/1992. Its decisions on human rights concerned social and economic rights. In the Age Discrimination case (4 February 1992),32 it set aside the dismissal of two workers for having reached pensionable age and ordered an amendment to the Labour Code. It applied not only the Declaration of Rights and Freedoms (not by then incorporated into the Constitution), but also the UN’s 1966 International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights and a range of International Labour Organisation conventions ratified by the USSR. In the Time Limits for Challenging Unfair Dismissal, a number of workers filed petitions complaining of the unconstitutionality of amended legislation providing for two-year time limits. On 23 June 199233 the Court held that the amendments violated five norms of international law and 14 articles of the Constitution.

32 Vedomosti Verkhovnovo Soveta RSFSRNo. 13, Art. 669. 33 Vedomosti Verkhovnovo Soveta RSFSRNo. 30, Art. 1809. There were further progressive moves on 26 June 1992 with the enactment of the law ‘On the status of judges of the Russian Federation’, giving judges unlimited tenure until a fixed retirement age and on 27 April 1993 with the enactment of the law ‘On complaining to court about activities and decisions which violate the rights and freedoms of citizens’. This vitally important law on judicial review continues in force. On 16 July 1993 a great experiment, led by Sergei Pashin, began with enactment of the new Part X to the Criminal Procedural Code (UPK), which introduced jury trial, as an experiment, in nine Russian regions.34

34 For analysis of these developments, see F.J.M. Feldbrugge, Russian law: the end of the Soviet system and the role of law (1993); G. Smith, Reforming the Russian legal system (1996); Solomon, ‘Courts and their reform in Russian history’ in Reforming justice in Russia, 1864–1996. Power, culture, and the limits of legal order (1997), 3–20. The Court was suspended by Yeltsin on 6 October 1993, after he tore up the 1978 Constitution, disbanded parliament and finally shelled the White House, seat of the parliament. The Court had sat all night following Yeltsin’s decree of 21 September 1993 and held that his actions violated the Constitution. The Court did not sit again until February 1995. To Yeltsin’s credit Valeriy Zorkin and his colleagues continued to sit in the reconstituted court, but the number of judges was increased from 13 to 19.

An initial test of the renewed Constitutional Court was presented by the First Chechen War. On 31 July 1995 the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation delivered its judgment on the constitutionality of President Yeltsin’s decrees sending federal forces into Chechnya.35 The Court was obliged in particular to consider the consequences of Russia’s participation in the 1977 Additional Protocol II to the 1949 Geneva Conventions.36 As Gaeta pointed out:

35 An unofficial English translation of this judgment was published by the European Commission for Democracy through Law (Venice Commission) of the Council of Europe, CDL-INF (96), 1. 36 The RF is a party to the 1949 Geneva conventions. The Soviet Union ratified both the 1977 additional protocols on 29 September 1989 to become effective on 29 March1990. The Russian Federation deposited a notification of continuation on 13 January1992. The Court determined that at the international level the provisions of Protocol II were binding on both parties to the armed conflict and that the actions of the Russian armed forces in the conduct of the Chechen conflict violated Russia’s international obligations under Additional Protocol II to the 1949 Geneva Conventions. Nonetheless, the Court sought to excuse this non-compliance because Protocol II had not been incorporated into the Russian legal system.37



37 Gaeta (1996), 568. Despite the order of the Court, incorporation has still not taken place. And Chechnya was de facto independent for two years from 1997 to 1999, having defeated the federal forces, although it was not recognised by any state.





Accession to the Council of Europe38


38 See Bowring (1997). Russia applied to join the Council of Europe on 7 May 1992, at a time when, as noted already, there were a number of progressive developments, legislative and in the work of the Constitutional Court. Despite the fact that it was by then engaged in bitter internal armed conflict in Chechnya, Russia became a full member of the Council of Europe on 28 February 1996. The Council now has 47 member states, including nine of the former ‘Union Republics’ of the USSR.39 The process has been completed whereby the Council of Europe, which began life in 1949 as (in the words of Ian Brownlie and Guy Goodwin-Gill)40 ‘a sort of social and ideological counterpart to the military aspects of European co-operation represented in the North Atlantic Treaty Organisation … inspired partly by interests in promotion of European unity and partly by the political desire for solidarity in the face of the ideology of Communism’, now includes almost all the formerly ‘communist’ states. This is an extraordinary ‘irony of destiny’, to poach the title of the ever popular Soviet film Ironiya sudby, ili s lyogkim parom! (1975).

39 Armenia, Azerbaijan, Estonia, Georgia, Latvia, Lithuania, Moldova, Russia, Ukraine. 40 BrownlieandGoodwin-Gill (2010). Following the application, the Council’s executive body, the Committee of Ministers, by Resolution (92) 27 of 25 June 1992, asked the Parliamentary Assembly (PACE) to give an opinion, in accordance with Statutory Resolution (51) 30 A. Four experts visited Russia in May and June 1994, in order to report to the Parliamentary Assembly. Rudolf Bernhardt reported in general on the human rights situation; Felix Ermacora reported on national minorities; Albert Weitzel reported on those aspects of human rights of particular importance to democratic development; and Stefan Trechsel made findings as to Articles 3, 5 and 6 of the European Convention on Human Rights.41 It was their impression that Russia had ‘embarked upon the road to democracy’. However, the rule of law, as enshrined in the Constitution, particularly the provisions as to human rights and incorporation of international standards, seemed to them to be more a matter of theory than practice. Furthermore, conditions in pre-trial detention centres were degrading if not inhuman and the guarantee of individual liberty was far behind the requirements of Article 5 of the European Convention. They concluded that the Russian Federation ‘does not yet fulfil the condition of the enjoyment by all persons within its jurisdiction of human rights and fundamental freedoms’.42

41 Their reports are set out in full at (1994) Vol. 15 Human Rights Law Journal, 7:250–300. 42 Ibid, 287. In July 1994, the UN Special Rapporteur on the question of the human rights of all persons subjected to any form of detention or imprisonment and, in particular, torture and other cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment or punishment, Nigel S. Rodley (now Professor Sir Nigel Rodley) visited Russia at the request of the government. His Report to the 51st Session of the Human Rights Commission in 1995,43 which has been published and widely disseminated in Russia,44 was devastating. He commented as follows on what he found in one prison:

43 E/CN.4/1995/34/Add.1: Report of the Special Rapporteur on the question of the human rights of all persons subjected to any form of detention or imprisonment in particular: torture and other cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment or punishment: visit to the Russian Federation. 44 In Prava Cheloveka v. Rossiy – Mezhdunarodnoye Izmereniye (Human Rights in Russia –International Perspectives), Issue 1 (Human Rights Publishers, 1995), 281–308; and in (1995) 2 Pravo-Zashchitnik (Human Rights Defender), 37–55. The Special Rapporteur would need the poetic skills of a Dante or the artistic skills of a Bosch adequately to describe the infernal conditions he found in these cells. The senses of smell, touch, taste and sight are repulsively assailed. The conditions are cruel, inhuman and degrading; they are torturous. To the extent that suspects are confined there to facilitate the investigation by breaking their wills with a view to eliciting confessions and information, they can properly be described as being subjected to torture.



His conclusion was uncompromising:

The prohibition of torture or cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment is absolute under international law. Indeed, according to article 3 of the Declaration on the Protection of All Persons from being Subjected to Torture and Other Cruel, Inhuman or Degrading Treatment or Punishment,45 even ‘exceptional circumstances such as a state of war or a threat of war, internal political instability or any other public emergency may not be invoked as a justification of torture or other cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment or punishment’ (art. 3). It follows inexorably that the Government of the Russian Federation is obliged to put an immediate end to this situation.



45 General Assembly Resolution 3452 (XXX) of 9 December 1975, annex. Similar views were taken by the President’s own Human Rights Commission, at that time headed by the redoubtable human rights activist Sergei Kovalyov, who was also for a period Russia’s first Human Rights Ombudsman (his period of office as Human Rights Plenipotentiary lasted only from 17 January 1994 to 10 March 1995, when he was dismissed for his uncompromising stance on the Chechnya conflict).46

46 For accounts of Kovalyov’s activities, see Mariana Katzarova and Antti Korkeakivi, The price of independence: the Office of Ombudsman and human rights in the Russian federation (NewYork: Lawyers’ Committee for Human Rights) 1995; Antti Korkeakivi, ‘The Russian human rights commissioner’ (1995), Parker School Journal of East European Law: 236–38; and Bowring (1998). On 14 June 1994 the Commission published their report on the Observance of Human Rights in the Russian Federation in 1993.47 They pointed out very serious violations relating to refugees and forced migrants; to freedom of movement and the right to choice of place of residence; to human rights of detained persons; to human rights in the armed forces; in the observance of labour rights; and in particular very serious violations during the state of emergency in Moscow in autumn 1993 and its consequences. Their second and last report was dated 5 February 199648 and covered the period 1994–1995. Their conclusion49 was that the situation in relation to observance of human rights in 1994–1995 remained extremely unsatisfactory. The Russian Constitution, particularly as concerns human rights, remained essentially a declaratory document. The development of legislation proceeded slowly and with many contradictions. Social and economic rights, in particular, were under threat.

47 O Soblyudeniy Prav Cheloveka i Grazhdanina v. Rossiskoi Federatsiy za 1993 god (Moscow: Yuridicheskaya Literatura, 1994). 48 O Soblyudeniy Prav Cheloveka i Grazhdanina v Rossiskoi Federatsiy v. 1994–5 godakh (Moscow: Yuridicheskaya Literatura, 1996). 49 Ibid, 79.





Debate in the Council of Europe


All this critical expert comment notwithstanding, Russia was finally accepted into membership on 28 February 1996. This followed a controversial vote50 that took place on the evening of 25 January 1996 in the Parliamentary Assembly of the Council of Europe (PACE), when more than two-thirds of the votes cast supported a resolution point 10 of which read: ‘To recommend to the Cabinet of Ministers of the Council of Europe to invite the Russian Federation to become a member of the Council of Europe; and to allot to the Russian Federation 18 places in the Parliamentary Assembly.’ As many as 58 of the members took part in the debate, as well as three rapporteurs: Muehleman (Switzerland); Binding (Germany); and Atkinson (United Kingdom), and deputies of the Russian State Duma: V. Lukin (Yabloko); G. Zyuganov (KPRF); V. Zhirinovsky (LDPR); and A. Abdulatov (Russian Regions). Lukin is now the Ombudsman of the Russian Federation for Human Rights; and Zyuganov and Zhirinovsky still lead their respective parties.

50 This information, and much more, is to be found in Glotov (1996). Some 214 of the 263 members of the Assembly took part in the vote. Of them, 164 voted for the resolution, with 35 against and 15 abstentions. Significantly, at the request of more than 10 members, the voting was by roll call. The members who insisted on this procedure included those from the formerly Soviet Baltic states, as well as members from Austria and Norway. Russian membership was supported firmly by the United Left (Communist), Socialist and Liberal Reform groups, and less energetically by the Christian Democrats and Conservatives.

Speakers for Russian membership included former PACE Chairman M. Martinez (Spain) who said ‘With Russia’s participation the Council of Europe will be able to play a big role in the construction of Europe’; Shiller (Austria), who said ‘Russia is most of all a great power and without it a complete Europe cannot be’; Boldtzer (Switzerland) who said ‘Europe cannot be secure without Russia’; Karakas (Greece) who said ‘Russia has a vast population with a powerful culture and history, and they must be Europeans with full rights’; E. Laaksa of the United Left who said ‘Acceptance of Russia will help to develop the rule of law and will protect civil liberties’; Boris Oleinik (Ukraine) who said ‘If Russia is not accepted today, there will be a political Chernobyl’; and Paunescu (Romania) who said ‘Russia cannot be sent home from Strasbourg like a school-child who has not learnt her lesson.’ It is notable that most speakers in favour of Russian accession concentrated on European security and the future of the project of European unity, rather than on the protection of human rights in Russia.

This was not the case with speakers against, particularly those with recent experience of Soviet rule. They included Colomberg (Switzerland) who said: ‘There has been progress in Russia, but Russia is not a rule of law state, there are victims in Chechnya, and bad prison conditions’; Sinko (Latvia) who said ‘Peaceloving people in Chechnya must be free’; Kelam (Estonia) who said ‘Things are going very badly for human rights in Russia’; and Kalus (Czech Republic) who said ‘Russia must stop its imperialist policy and withdraw its occupation forces. Chechnya must be free.’





Debate in the Russian state Duma


Domestic political opinion was for the most part more straightforward, focusing largely on Russian national interest. On 21 February 1996 the Russian state Duma approved membership of the Council of Europe by 204 votes to 18, by adopting the federal law ‘On the Russian Federation joining the Council of Europe’.51 First Deputy Foreign Minister Igor Ivanov told the Duma that joining the Council was in Russia’s national interest. He added that membership would permit Russia better to defend the interests of ethnic Russians living abroad, particularly in the Baltic republics. The Chair of the Duma’s International Affairs Committee, Vladimir Lukin, assured deputies that the benefits of Council membership would more than justify the $25 million a year dues that Russia must pay.52 The upper house, the Federation Council, unanimously approved the Duma’s two bills on the subject. They were signed by the President on 23 February 1996.

51 Federal Law No. 19-F3. The text is set out in Glotov (1996), 118. 52 Scott Parish (1996) OMRI Daily Digest, 22 February 1996. The debate that had taken place in the State Duma a few days earlier, on 16 February 1996 was much more controversial and revealing.53 Vladimir Lukin, Head of the Russian delegation to the Parliamentary Assembly of the Council of Europe, introduced the debate on the question ‘On the entry of Russia into the Council of Europe’. He pointed out that Russia is ‘a great Euro-Asian country, two thirds of whose territory is in Asia, but four fifths of whose population lives in Europe’. Russia, he said, is a more European state than Turkey, which is already a member of the Council. In answer to questions from members of the Communist Party, he said that despite his own negative views about Stalin, under Stalin the USSR had joined the United Nations and signed [sic] the Charter on Human Rights of the UN and Stalin was in his own way not stupid in advancing Russia’s interests. Deputy A.G. Ghekhoyev specifically took up54 the issue of the obligations to ratify within a specified time, set out in Opinion No. 193 (1996). Was this not an instruction to the Russian parliament? Did Lukin not have the feeling that Russia was being spoken to as if it were Honduras?

53 The transcript of the debate is reproduced in Glotov (1996), 95–117. 54 See Glotov (1996), 103–4. Lukin replied that this was not the case. The essence of the opinion was that the Parliamentary Assembly expected of Russia that it would, within a specified time, conform to what the other 38 members of the Council had already accepted. This was an expectation; therefore the opinion had a recommendatory character. He added that a number of things had been placed in the opinion by Russia’s enemies, who wished that Russia would not join the Council of Europe. He insisted that it was necessary for Russia to join and then ‘to see what we shall do with each of these recommendations’. In answer to a specific question, from Deputy V.D. Rozhkov, about the death penalty, he replied that if Russia had a problem with regard to a moratorium and eventual abolition, so did a number of other member states, which had been members for a number of years without having abolished the death penalty (he probably had in mind states like the UK and Belgium).

In the debate on joining, the position of the KPRF (through its spokesman Anatoliy Lukyanov, who spent a year in detention following the unsuccessful putsch of August 1991) was, contrary to expectation, that Russia should join.55 The rights of workers would be better protected; political rights to meetings and demonstrations would be guaranteed; parliamentary control on the executive would be enhanced, as would the role of local government. It would, therefore, be useful for the Communist Party’s constituents.

55 Glotov (1996), 107. Vladimir Zhirinovsky, however, for the LDPR, went straight to the point. He disagreed with Lukin’s assessment of the provisions of the opinion; they were indeed obligations, not recommendations. In his view, Russian norms were in many respects quite different from those of the Council of Europe. S.A. Glotov himself told the Duma, on behalf of the ‘Narodovlastiye’ (People’s Power) faction, that they would vote in favour of entry; he focused mainly on the expected benefits to Russian national interests.

It is quite clear from this debate that most members of the state Duma did not understand Russia’s obligations to be obligations at all, but simply recommendations, a number of which were to be taken as provocative attempts to keep Russia out of the Council of Europe and which could be adopted or discarded at will. It is not surprising, then, that Sergei Kovalyov had already on 26 January 1996 expressed deep ambivalence about membership, saying: ‘I fear the Council does not realise the responsibility it bears.’56 On the same day Viktor Ilyushin, Chair of the Security Committee, said that Russia would be unable to end its use of the death penalty in the near future since its prisons do not have the capacity to keep large numbers of life prisoners. A senior Interior Ministry official said it would be ‘premature’ to ban the death penalty and that two-thirds of the population agreed.57

56 Scott Parish (1996) OMRI Daily Digest, 26 January 1996. 57 Penny Morvant (1996) OMRI Daily Digest, 29 January 1996. On 12 March 1996 President Boris Yeltsin addressed the 12 deputies who would represent Russia in the Parliamentary Assembly and insisted that the Council’s four basic Conventions should be ratified as soon as possible. He said Russia had ‘earned’ Council membership by making progress on democratisation and human rights; but added that Russia could not immediately implement the Council’s standards, including abolition of the death penalty. He urged the deputies to defend vigorously the rights of ethnic Russians in the Baltic and CIS states, and, referring to Chechnya, to ‘rebuff resolutely attempts to pressure Russia, interfere in its internal affairs, and apply double standards’. Leni Fischer, speaker of the Parliamentary Assembly, reacted quickly, saying that ‘human rights are never a domestic issue’.58

58 Scott Parish (1996) OMRI Daily Digest, 13 March 1996.





Why did the Council of Europe need Russia; why did Russia need the Council of Europe?


S.A. Glotov, himself a deputy of the state Duma and member of the ‘Narodovlastiye’ (People’s Power) faction59 gave the following reasons why the Council of Europe should wish to have Russia as a member, on the basis, of course, that Russia had fulfilled most of the demands of the Council of Europe – the holding of new elections to parliament, the abolition of the Congress of People’s Deputies of the RSFSR, the promulgation of an extensive report on the state of law and order and perspectives for improving it. First, the acceptance of Russia gave the Council of Europe a ‘second wind’, as well as the opportunity to encourage the spread of Western European values and norms in this country of millions of people. Second, European security was likely to be better safeguarded. Third, Russia’s accession significantly strengthened a more united Europe in its rivalry with the United States and Japan. Fourth, the Council of Europe was able to give further support to Russia’s ‘reformers’ – in Glotov’s view this serves the interests of major Western financial companies and political groups. Fifth, failure to accept Russia could tend to support the growth of terrorism in Russia.

59 Glotov (1996), 11. Why should Russia wish to join the Council of Europe? First, because Russia would have a sound international tribune from which to express its interests and help form public opinion. In particular, this tribune could be used for the protection of the Russian-speaking populations in the Baltic states (Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania) and for the purpose of expressing Russia’s negative attitude towards the expansion of NATO. Second, joining would help with the establishment of legal and social reform. Third, Russia’s joining the Council would permit the organisation of international humanitarian cooperation. Fourth, although this appears paradoxical, the Council of Europe would provide an arena for promoting integration between Russia and states of the former USSR. Fifth, membership of the Council of Europe would permit direct cooperation (within the Congress of local and regional authorities) between regions of other member states and the republics, krai and oblast of the CIS. Sixth, the long experience of European states in the political struggle of various parties and groups supplies the basis for norms that guarantee the functioning of the opposition and the handing over of power.





The CIS Convention on Human Rights


One of the most controversial problems surrounding Russian accession was Russia’s promotion of a separate regional instrument, the ‘CIS Convention on Human Rights’,60 which was signed at Minsk, Belarus’ on 26 May 1995 by seven of the 12 member states of the Commonwealth of Independent States.61 Jeremy McBride commented that ‘the apparent width of the Convention guarantee may actually be at the expense of its depth’. He raised a number of serious questions as to its effectiveness and its compatibility with European standards.62 By Article 38, it comes into force after it has been ratified by three states. Moldova was a signatory to the Convention and became a member of the Council of Europe on 13 July 1995. Russia was the only state to have ratified this Convention. Opinion No. 188 (1995) on Moldova’s application to join the Council of Europe stipulated that Moldova enter into a commitment ‘to withhold ratification of the CIS Convention until the implications of the co-existence of this Convention and the ECHR, especially as the control mechanisms are concerned, have been clarified’ and not to ratify without the consent of the Council. On 20 October 1995, the Russian Duma adopted a law on ratification of the CIS Convention and on 25 October this was approved by the Federation Council, with signature and promulgation by President Yeltsin following on 4 November 1995.63 However, nothing more was heard of the CIS Convention, for reasons that are explained later.

60 The text appears at Vol. 17 (1996) Human Rights Law Journal: 159–62. 61 SeeDrzemscewski (1996). 62 McBride (1995). 63 Rossiskaya Gazeta, 11 November 1995. In a letter dated 23 June 1995, the Council of Europe’s then Secretary General, Daniel Tarschys, asked two eminent human rights experts, Professor Antonio Augusto Cancado Trindade and Jochen A. Frowein, to carry out an analysis of the legal implications for states that intend to ratify both the ECHR and the CIS Convention, so as to know what practical solutions might be envisaged so as to ensure the highest possible degree of effective protection of human rights by states parties to both instruments. These two studies were dated 6 October and 10 August 1995, respectively.64 Professor Trindade concluded that ‘to try to erect the CIS Human Rights Convention into an alleged “regional remedy” to be exhausted before recourse can be made to the European Convention on Human Rights’ would be ‘highly objectionable … weakening the international protection of human rights’.65 Professor Frowein was concerned to avoid a situation where a degree of protection might be established that was in violation of the European Convention on Human Rights, but that would continue to exist in practice.66

64 They are reproduced at Vol. 17 (1996) Human Rights Law Journal: 164–84. 65 Ibid, 178. 66 Ibid, 184.





Russia’s obligations to the Council of Europe on accession


At a ceremony held in Strasbourg on 28 February 1996 to mark Russia’s entry into the Council of Europe, the Russian Foreign Minister, Yevgeniy Primakov, handed over the documents affirming Russia’s adherence to the Council’s Charter. He also signed the European Convention on Human Rights, the European Convention against Torture, the European Charter on Local Self-Government and the Framework Convention on Protection of National Minorities He did this pursuant to the obligations placed on Russia in Parliamentary Assembly Opinion 193 (1996) of 25 January 1996,67 confirmed in Committee of Ministers Resolution No. 96/2.

67 This is set out in Glotov (1996), 82–9. Point 7 of the opinion set out a number of respects in which Russia was already seeking to conform with the fundamental principles of the Council of Europe (more precisely, as set out in Article 3 – ‘Every member of the Council of Europe must accept the principles of the rule of law and the enjoyment by all persons within its jurisdiction of human rights and fundamental freedoms, and collaborate sincerely and effectively in the realisation of the aim of the Council’):

Since 1992 Russia had taken part in various activities of the Council of Europe, connected with the carrying out of the inter-governmental programme on ‘cooperation and assistance’ (in particular in the fields of legal reform and human rights) and had sent its own delegation, with specially invited status, to participate in the work of the Parliamentary Assembly and its commissions;

From May 7, 1992 ‘political dialogue’ was established between Russia and the Committee of Ministers;

Russia had acceded to a series of Council of Europe Conventions including the European Cultural Conventions;

With immediate effect, with the use of international expertise, the following statutes are being drafted: new Criminal and Criminal Procedural Codes, new Civil and Civil Procedural Codes and a Criminal Correctional Code. On the basis of these, the principles and standards of the Council of Europe will be implemented;

It is expected that new laws will be adopted, in accordance with Council of Europe standards, as follows: on the role, activities and organisation of the Procurator’s Office and the Office of the Commissioner for Human Rights; on the protection of national minorities; and on freedom of assembly and freedom of religion;

The status of the legal profession will be protected by law; a professional bar association will be set up;

Persons, found responsible for human rights violations, especially in connection with the events in Chechnya, will be brought to justice;

Effective exercise will be guaranteed of the rights enshrined in Article 27 of the Constitution and in the law on freedom of movement and choice of place of residence;

Conditions of detention will be improved in accordance with Recommendation A (87)3 on common European prison rules, in particular the practically inhuman conditions in many pre-trial detention centres will be ameliorated without delay;

The responsibility for the prison administration and the execution of judgments will be transferred to the Ministry of Justice as soon as possible;

The state and progress of law reform will permit the signature and ratification, within the delays indicated, of the Council of Europe conventions listed in paragraph 10;

The Russian Federation will assist persons formerly deported from the occupied Baltic states or those belonging to the descendants of deportees, to return home in accordance with special repatriation and compensation programmes which must be worked out.



Under paragraph 10, the Parliamentary Assembly noted that ‘the Russian Federation shares fully its understanding and interpretation of the commitments entered into as spelt out in paragraph 7, and intends’:

to sign the European Convention on Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms at the moment of accession; to ratify the Convention and Protocols Nos: 1, 2, 4, 7 and 11 within a year; to recognise, pending the entry into force of Protocol No. 11, the right of individual petition to the European Commission on Human Rights and the compulsory jurisdiction of the European Court of Human Rights (Articles 25 and 46 of the Convention);

to sign within one year and ratify within three years from the time of accession, Protocol No. 6 to the European Convention on Human Rights on the abolition of the death penalty in time of peace, and to put into place a moratorium on executions with effect from the day of accession;

to sign and ratify within a year from the time of accession the European Convention for the Prevention of Torture and Inhuman and Degrading Treatment or Punishment;

to sign and ratify within a year from the time of accession the European Framework Convention on the Protection of National Minorities, to conduct its policy towards minorities on the principles set out in Assembly Recommendation 1201 (1993), and to incorporate these principles into the legal and administrative system and practice of the country;

to sign and ratify within a year from the date of accession the European Charter of Local Self-Government and the Charter for Regional and Minority Languages; to study, with a view to ratification, the European Social Charter; and meanwhile to conduct its policy in accordance with the principles of these conventions;

to sign and ratify and meanwhile to apply the basic principles of other conventions of the Council of Europe – notably those on extradition; on mutual assistance in criminal matters; on transfer of sentenced persons; on laundering, search, seizure and confiscation of proceeds from crime;

to settle international as well as internal conflicts by peaceful means (an obligation incumbent on all member states of the Council of Europe), rejecting resolutely any forms of threat to use force against its neighbours;

– not strictly human rights issues;

to ensure that the application of the CIS Convention on Human Rights does not in any way interfere with the procedure and guarantees of the European Convention on Human Rights;

to revise the law on federal security services in order to bring it into line with Council of Europe principles and standards within one year from the time of accession, in particular, the right of the Federal Security Service (FSB) to possess and run pre-trial detention centres should be withdrawn;

to adopt a law on alternative military service, as foreseen by Article 59 of the Constitution;

to reduce, if not eliminate, incidents of ill-treatment and deaths in the armed forces outside military conflicts;

to pursue legal reform with a view to bringing all legislation in line with Council of Europe principles and standards; amongst other legislation, Presidential Decree No. 1226 should be revised without delay;

– not strictly human rights issues;

to co-operate fully in the implementation of Assembly Order No. 508 (1995) on the honouring of obligations and commitments by member states of the Council of Europe, as well as in monitoring processes established by virtue of the Committee of Ministers’ Declaration of 10 November 1994 (95th session);

to respect strictly the provisions of international humanitarian law, including the cases of armed conflicts on its territory;

to co-operate in good faith with international humanitarian organ-isations and to enable them to carry on their activities on its territory in conformity with their mandate.



In paragraph 11, these are further described as ‘commitments and understandings’ – certainly not as ‘recommendations’. The list is almost overwhelmingly extensive and it was immediately apparent that a number of Council of Europe member states had not ratified all of these – in Britain’s case Protocols 4 and 6 to the ECHR and the Charter on Local Self-Government. It is not difficult to understand the sense of many Russians that unreasonable and humiliating demands were being made.

A number of speeches were made at the ceremony. Leni Fischer, Chairman of PACE, stressed that with Russia’s admission, the Council of Europe’s relations with the European Union and the OSCE would be transformed.68 Daniel Tarschys, the General Secretary, recalled Russia’s role, led by President Gorbachev, in bringing about democratic change in eastern Europe and Gorbachev’s speech at Strasbourg in 1994, when he spoke of a ‘common European home’. In answer to the question whether Russia was a European country, he said that it was now beyond doubt that Russia was part of Europe and that the wealth of Russia’s culture was an inalienable part of Europe’s common heritage. However, Russia’s parliament and government had undertaken important obligations in relation to the Council of Europe.

68 Transcriptions of the speeches made at the ceremony are to be found in Glotov (1996), 120–29. Finally, Russian Foreign Minister Yevgeniy M. Primakov outlined the importance of entry into membership both for Russia and for the Council of Europe. For Russia, this confirmed the depth and integrity of the democratic changes taking place in Russia, as well as showing that the whole European community believed that it would continue its democratic reforms. For the Council of Europe, this meant that the organisation now had a truly universal character. Russia did not join with empty hands; its enormous cultural, scientific and intellectual potential would enrich the Council’s activities. He said nothing about Russia’s obligations, other than acceptance of the right of individual petition to the Commission on Human Rights and of the Court’s compulsory jurisdiction.

But in his comments that day, Sergei Kovalyov urged the Council to monitor Russian compliance with its obligations very carefully, especially in Chechnya. Otherwise, he said, ‘nothing good will come’ of Russian membership.69

69 Scott Parish, (1996) OMRI Daily Digest, 29 February 1996.





Russia’s compliance with its obligations in the first year


Russia’s compliance in the first year after accession was far from complete. The death penalty is a prime example. On 24 September 1996 at a press conference in Moscow, Anatoly Pristavkin, Chairman of the Presidential Clemency Commission and Valery Borshchev, representative of the Human Rights Commission, reported that 53 executions had been carried out in 1996. They also said that judicial errors had been made in 30 per cent of death penalty cases. Despite the fact that the Clemency Commission regularly recommends clemency, Amnesty International received information that President Yeltsin had granted clemency in only five cases in 1995. Seven hundred prisoners remained on death row. On 3 October 1996 Amnesty International issued a statement saying that Russia was treating its Council of Europe commitments with contempt.70 This was followed by a resolution of the Parliamentary Assembly on 29 January 1997, calling on Russia to honour the obligations it undertook to end executions and threatening to exclude Russian delegates unless a moratorium was quickly imposed.71

70 Amnesty International, AI Index: E.U.R. 46/43/96, 3 October 1996. 71 Penny Morvant (1997) OMRI Daily Digest, 30 January 1997. It was no surprise, therefore, when on 3 March 1997 the General Secretary of the Council of Europe, Daniel Tarschys, visited Moscow with a view to reviewing Russia’s progress in fulfilling its obligations.72 According to research carried out by journalist Svetlana Sukhova, Russia had done a great deal in the space of a year. The list is impressive, and worth setting out in full:

The European Convention on Human Rights was signed on 29 February 1996. A draft law entrenching the right of individual petition was to be presented to the state Duma in spring 1997.

The new Criminal Code entered into force on 1 January 1997.73 A draft law has been prepared on the introduction of a moratorium on the death penalty. On 28 February 1997 President Yeltsin instructed the Russian Foreign Ministry to sign Protocol 6 of the ECHR and the Ministry of Justice to prepare a scheme for its implementation.

The following instruments had been signed, but not yet ratified:

the European Convention on Prevention of Torture and Inhuman and Degrading Treatment

the Framework Convention on the Protection of National Minorities

the European Charter on Local Self-Government

the Charter on Regional or Minority Languages.





Two parts of the Civil Code had been enacted74 and the third – concerned with rights of inheritance – is in the process of drafting.

The working out of the Criminal Procedural Code had not yet started.

The Law on Advocates received its first reading in November 1996.

The Law on the Human Rights Ombudsman had been enacted and signed by the President,75 although, at time of writing, a new ombudsman to replace Sergei Kovalyov had not yet been appointed.

The Law on Freedom of Association had received readings in the state Duma and the Federation Council, but had been returned by the President.

The Law on National–Cultural Autonomy was being drafted.

On 7 November 1996 the European Conventions on Reciprocal Assistance in the Criminal Justice Field and on the Transfer of Prisoners had been signed.

In connection with the Russian government’s review of the question ‘On the state and measures for securing the conditions of persons in custody in the investigating isolators and prisons of the Russian Interior Ministry’, a proposal has been made for amendments to the law ‘On the organs of the federal security service’, as well as an analysis of measures previously taken for their adoption in a series of investigative isolators (SIZOs – remand prisons).

The Law on Alternative Military Service had received its first reading in the state Duma on 14 December 1994.



72 Svetlana Sukhova, Rossiya stala na god blizhe k Evrope (In a year Russia has become closer to Europe), Sevodnya, 7 March 1997. 73 Adopted by the State Duma on 24 May 1996, approved by the Council of the Federation on 5 June 1996 and signed by President Yeltsin on 13 June 1996. A commentary was published by the journal Yuridicheskiy Biulletien Predprinimatelya (Moscow, 1996). 74 Part 1 came into force on 1 January 1995, while Part 2 came into force on 1 March 1996. 75 The Bill was based by the Duma on 25 December 1996 and by the Federation Council on 12 February 1997. It was signed by President Yeltsin on 26 February 1997 – see Penny Morvant (1997) OMRI Daily Digest, 27 February 1997. Tarschys carried out an exhaustive inquiry. He met the speakers of both houses of parliament, the heads of the Ministries of Foreign Affairs and the Interior, the Presidential Administration, the Constitutional Court and the General Procurator. The Russian side was frank in its admission of shortcomings. Yelena Mizulina, Deputy Chairman of the state Duma’s Committee on Legislation, told Svetlana Sukhova that while a number of draft laws – for example on freedom of assembly, on national minorities, the criminal procedural code – were the subject of active work, it had to be said that the draft law on the introduction of a moratorium on the death penalty had many opponents in the state Duma and its acceptance was doubtful. Similarly, the draft law on alternative military service had already been heard three times and had now been ‘buried’ in the depths of the Committee on Defence.

There were even greater problems with implementation of the Council of Europe’s recommendation on normalisation of conditions of detained persons. There were some successes, for example the adoption of a programme of reforms, and Yuriy Shcherbanenko, head of the Prokuratura’s Administration for Supervision of Detention, said that in the last year there had been a reduction in the growth of numbers of prisoners. The numbers held in SIZOs fell by 7,000. This was partly due to pressure exerted on the courts to speed up the trial procedure. But Shcherbanenko noted that so far there is no law limiting the period of investigation.

As the President’s Commission on Human Rights pointed out on 25 February 1997, and the Coordination Conference of the Prokuratura confirmed on 3 March 1997, problems in the prison system continued as before. Within the SIZOs there was gross overcrowding, lack of medicines (in 1996 some 12,000–13,000 detainees were released, ill with active tuberculosis), lack of food (only 3,700 roubles a day was spent feeding each detainee three meals – at that time, there were 5,700 roubles to the dollar). In these conditions, which were ‘inhuman’ in the words of the Prokuratura, alcoholism and drug addiction flourished. In 1996 alone, 19,000 litres of vodka and 125 kilograms of narcotics had been seized. There was insufficient money for reform: the prison system received only 66 per cent of the budget planned for 1996.

It should only, perhaps, be added that throughout her article Svetlana Sukhova persisted in referring to Russia’s obligations as Council of Europe ‘recommendations’. As has already been pointed out, the use of this terminology was not restricted to journalists and posed considerable problems for continued Russian participation in the Council of Europe.





Ratification of the ECHR


On 28 February 1998 the state Duma of the Russian Federation voted to ratify the ECHR. A total of 294 (65.3 per cent) deputies voted for, with only 11 (2.4 per cent) against and two (0.4 per cent) abstentions. Thus, 307 of the deputies voted, but many members failed to vote at all. The upper chamber of the Russian parliament, the Federation Council, approved this decision on 13 March 1998, and the federal law of the Russian Federation ‘On ratification of the Convention for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms and the Protocols to it’ entered into force on 30 March 1998. The ECHR itself entered into force for Russia on 1 November 1998.76 In this way, Russia fulfilled one of the most important commitments that it made on accession to the Council of Europe.

76 It should be noted that on the same day the state Duma voted, by an even larger majority, to ratify the European Convention for the Prevention of Torture and Inhuman and Degrading Treatment (CPT). Questions and speeches from the deputies, in general, addressed three themes. First, some deputies asked why ratification was necessary at all. For example: how would ratification help to pay wages and pensions? How would it help those deputies who were prevented from travelling when they wished to go to Iraq or Libya? How would it prevent nationalist and far-right deputies like Govorukhin, Kobzon and Zhirinovsky from being refused visas for Germany? How would it prevent Iraqi children from starving as a result of the US embargo? How would it prevent NATO’s eastward expansion? Second, questions were raised as to the real cost of ratification. Third, deputies were anxious as to the extent that the ECHR’s demands were consistent with the Russian legislation in force and how and why the legislation should be changed.

All these questions, and more, were answered to the satisfaction of most of the deputies, by I.S. Ivanov, the Foreign Minister of the Russian Federation, and by V.P. Lukin, Chairman of the Duma’s Committee on International Affairs. Lukin had to remind deputies that the USA is not a member of the Council of Europe, which would not be an appropriate forum for addressing their concerns. Ivanov pointed out that Russia was already bound to pay the Council’s subscription of $24 million a year and that ratification of the ECHR would not incur additional indirect costs – until and if Russia begins to lose cases at the Court.

Thus, in the Duma debate on 20 February 1998,77 Yu.P. Ivanov, speaking on behalf of the Agrarian Party, repeated that his party did not recognise the argument that Russia signed the ECHR under Opinion No. 193 as an obligation. In his party’s view, the opinion had only a recommendatory character. But he wanted his colleagues to recognise the reality that Russia’s integration into the world legal process was essential. Moreover, he said, the more than 200 conventions adopted by the world community contained an enormous quantity of positive norms, many of which were for the amelioration of the social position of working people and the protection of human rights. V.D. Filomonov, of Zhirinovsky’s Liberal Democratic Party of Russia, was deeply convinced that ratification of the Convention would fulfil the interests both of the Russian state and of its citizens.78 First, it would strengthen the provisions of Russian legislation protecting the rights and freedoms of citizens regardless of their political persuasion. Second, it would guarantee their protection not only in Russia but in the whole of Europe, especially in the Baltic states. S.A. Glotov of the ‘Narodovlastiye (People’s Power)’ group,79 added that Russia would gain a very serious and authoritative tribunal for expressing the national state interests of Russia in the European space. L.P. Andreyev, of ‘Nash Dom – Rossiya’ (Our Home – Russia)80 made the other, essential point, that, ‘to speak openly, the Convention will stimulate above all the judicial and legal reforms taking place in our country and will create the essential conditions for bringing our legislation into accordance with international norms, which also, in our view, positively reflects the interests of the inhabitants of Russia.’

77 This is recorded in Yevropeiskye konventsiy i Rossiskiye zakonodateli(European Convention and Russian Lawmakers), Rossiskiy Byulleten po Pravam Cheloveka, 1998, 10: 76–81. 78 Ibid, 79. 79 Ibid, 81. 80 Ibid, 80.





Russia’s reservations


Russia made two reservations when depositing its instrument of ratification of the ECHR on 5 May 1998,81 both relating to the then existing Russian Criminal Procedural Code. Before turning to these in detail, I explore the legal basis for such reservations.

81 See http://sim.law.uu.nl/SIM/Library/RATIF.nsf/be2c697381d495be41256bfb003493b1/1647bf9d4202c07b41256bfe00489a28? Open Document (accessed 13 October 2012). Article 57 (formerly Article 64) of earrested or detained in accordancet ECHR provides that a state may, when signing the Convention or when depositing its instrument of ratification, make a reservation in respect of any particular provision of the Convention to the extent that any law then in force in its territory is not in conformity with the provision. Reservations of a general character are not permitted under this article. This is a controversial provision. P. van Dijk and G. van Hoof82 commented at the time that the possibility for states to make reservations would seem to be at odds with the presumption of universality of human rights. They added:83

82 Van Dijk and van Hoof (1998), 773. 83 Ibid, 783. If one of the purposes of the Convention, according to its Preamble, is to pursue the achievement of greater unity between the Member States of the Council of Europe through the maintenance and further realisation of human rights, then the unity aimed at may have reached a certain stage where there is no longer room for differences on important issues between those Member States in its obligations which they have accepted concerning the protection of human rights.



Nevertheless, a reservation is permitted, subject to review by the European Court of Human Rights and a number of cases have been decided.84 Russia’s reservations relate to Article 5(3) and (4), of the ECHR. These provide:

84 Belilos v. Switzerland (Judgment of 29 April 1988, Series A, No. 132), Temeltasch (1983) D. & R. 31 (1983), 120 (144–45), Loizidou v. Turkey (Judgment of 23 March 1995, Series A, No. 310). 3. Everyone arrested or detained in accordance with the provisions of para graph 1(c) of this article shall be brought promptly before a judge or other officer authorised by law to exercise judicial power and shall be entitled to trial within a reasonable time or to release pending trial. Release may be conditioned by guarantees to appear for trial.

4. Everyone who is deprived of his liberty by arrest or detention shall be entitled to bring proceedings by which the lawfulness of his detention shall be decided speedily by a court and his release ordered if the detention is not lawful.



Russia’s reservation was as follows:

In accordance with Article 64 of the Convention, the Russian Federation declares that the provisions of Article 5(3) and (4), shall not prevent the application of the following provisions of the legislation of the Russian Federation:

The temporary application, sanctioned by the second paragraph of point 6 of Section Two of the 1993 Constitution of the Russian Federation, of the procedure for the arrest, holding in custody and detention of persons suspected of having committed a criminal offence, established by Article 11, paragraph 1, Article 89, paragraph 1, Articles 90, 92, 96–1, 96–2, 97, 101 and 122 of the RSFSR Code of Criminal Procedure of October 27, 1960, with subsequent amendments and additions;

Articles 51–53 and 62 of the Disciplinary Regulations of the Armed Forces of the Russian Federation, approved by Decree No. 2140 of the President of the Russian Federation of December 14, 1993 – based on Article 26, paragraph 2, of the Law of the Russian Federation ‘On the status of servicemen’ of January 22, 1993 – instituting arrest and detention in the guard-house as a disciplinary measure imposed under extra-judicial procedure on servicemen – private soldiers, seamen, conscripted non-commissioned officers, non-commissioned officers and officers;

The period of validity of these reservations shall be the period required to introduce amendments to the Russian federal legislation which will completely eliminate the incompatibilities between the said provisions and the provisions of the Convention.





These reservations had been anticipated in Section Two of the 1993 Constitution (adopted by referendum of 12 December 1993), which contained concluding and interim provisions. The second paragraph of Point 6 provides: ‘Until the criminal procedural legislation of the Russian Federation has been brought into line with the provisions of this Constitution, the previous procedure for the arrest, detention and keeping in custody of persons suspected of committing a crime shall apply.’85

85 The reference is to Art. 22 of the 1993 Constitution, which provides: ‘1. Everyone shall have the right to freedom and personal inviolability. 2. Arrest, detention and keeping in custody shall be permissible only under a court order. A person may not be detained for more than 48 hours without a court order.’ It should be noted that the reservation as to Article 5(4) applied only to the case of military detention, since in all other cases of arrest and detention in custody an application to a court is possible. This was implemented by the 1992 amendments to the Russian Criminal Procedural Code, contained in Article 220–1 (complaint to a court of arrest or the continuation of the period of detention in custody) and 220–2 (judicial review of the lawfulness and grounds for arrest or the continuation of the period of detention in custody).86 Thus, the provisions in Part Two of the Constitution made no provision for military detention.

86 However, the Russian Code itself contained a serious flaw. The authoritative commentary to the Russian CPC noted later, stated (390) that on receiving a complaint against detention, the court had power only to verify the legality and grounds for detention, arrest or the prolongation of a period of detention in custody, but not the substance of the accusation itself. In particular, the court could not make a judgment as to the guilt of the detained person or the crime of which he is accused. This had been established in Orders of the Supreme Court of 1993 and 1995. The main problem for Russia lay in the fact that it had not yet succeeded in enacting a new Criminal Procedural Code (CPC) and criminal proceedings were still governed by the CPC of the Russian Socialist Federation of Soviet Republics (RSFSR) of 27 October 1960, a Soviet code, albeit many times amended.87 This Code enshrined the decisive role of the prokuratura in the criminal justice system. Thus, Article 11 of the CPC, on ‘Inviolability of the person’, provided that arrest was possible not only on the basis of a judicial decision, but by order of a prokuror. The prokuror, with the investigator and court, could take decisions as to mery presecheniye, ‘preventive measures’ or ‘measures of restraint’ (bail or custody), under Article 89 and usually does. Article 90 provided that it was the prokuror who gave the sanction for arrest and, under Article 97, decided on the period of detention in custody pending trial and its extension, for up to one and a half years. Changes in the ‘measures of prevention’, where these had been ordered by the prokuror, could only be changed by the prokuror, according to Article 101. Under Article 122, where a suspect had been detained by the militia (police), it was the prokuror who sanctioned detention. Finally, the prokuror was also responsible for reviewing the lawfulness of opening a criminal case (Article 116) and, generally, reviewing the lawfulness of the activities of the police and investigators (Articles 211–217), as well as conditions in places of detention.

87 For an excellent contemporaneous commentary, which took full account of the ECHR, see V. Radchenko (ed.), Kommentariy k Ugolovnovo-protsessualnomu Kodeksu RSFSR (Commentary on the CPC of the Russian Socialist Federation of Soviet Republics) (Moscow), 1999. Radchenko was then the First Deputy Chairman of the Supreme Court of the Russian Federation. He worked on the new Criminal Procedural Code. The fact that the prokuror, rather than a court or judicial officer, decided on the lawfulness of arrest and also whether the accused should receive bail or detention in custody awaiting trial was not only an undiluted inheritance from the Soviet period. It also contradicted a clearly established principle of European human rights law. The jurisprudence of the ECHR did not recognise a prokuror as an ‘other officer authorised by law to exercise judicial power’.15 Thus, in the case of Brincat v. Italy,88 the Commission held that: ‘[O]nly the objective appearances at the time of the decision are material: if it then appears that the “officer authorised by law to exercise judicial power” may later intervene, in the subsequent proceedings, as a representative of the prosecuting authority, there is a risk that his impartiality may arouse doubts which are to be held objectively justified.’

88 Judgment of 26 November 1992, Series A, No. 249-A, 12. This was precisely the situation with Russia, where the prokuratura was directly involved at all stages. Indeed, there was a longstanding problem. The USSR ratified the United Nations’ 1966 International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR) as long ago as 1973 and ratified its Optional Protocol, permitting individual complaints to the UN Human Rights Committee, in September 1991, shortly before the collapse of the USSR itself. Article 9(3) of the ICCPR provides that anyone arrested or detained on a criminal charge ‘shall be brought promptly before a judge or other officer authorised by the law to exercise judicial power’.

Of course, the continued role of the prokuratura was not the only problem faced by Russia. There were too few judges and these are underpaid.89

89 See Bowring (2000). There are two further respects, according to Professor Igor Petrukhin,90 in which the CPC contradicted the ECHR. First, Article 89, ‘Adoption of (preventive measures)’, contains, as a basis for choosing preventive measures the ‘presence of sufficient facts as to the fact … that the accused person may obstruct the establishment of the truth in a criminal case’. This ground for detention was not to be found in the list in Article 5(1) of the ECHR, which includes at (c) the ‘lawful arrest or detention … on reasonable suspicion of having committed an offence’. Professor Petrukhin also believed91 that the reservations, so far as they concerned Articles 96–1 and 96–2 (the procedure for detaining persons in custody), sought to take account of the fact that the law of the Russian Federation ‘On detaining in custody persons suspected and accused of committing criminal offences’92 did not sufficiently secure the conditions of detained persons so as to comply with the provisions of Article 3 of the ECHR relating to the prevention of torture and inhuman and degrading treatment.

90 Petrukhin (2000). 91 Ibid, 67. 92 Federal Law of 15 July 1995, No. 103-FZ. Moreover, Article 97 of the CPC, ‘Periods of detention in custody’, violated the requirement of the ECHR that the period in custody before trial must be ‘reasonable’. The problem was that, in Russia, a person might be detained for a period of two years before trial, whereas the period in Germany was one year, in Scotland, 110 days and in the USA (not, of course, a member of the Council of Europe), 120 days. In all these countries, the period of pre-trial detention was under the control of the court.

The Information Report of 2 June 199893 to the Committee of the Parliamentary Assembly of the Council of Europe (PACE) noted in paragraph 14 that despite the fact that these reservations to some extent limited the protection of persons under arrest, as concerns the necessity of the written permission of the court, they did not belittle the significance of Russia’s recognition of all the other provisions of the ECHR. However, it was recommended that the PACE must follow this issue up so that the reservations were withdrawn as soon as possible and in any event within a period not exceeding one year.

93 Honouring of obligations and commitments by the Russian Federation, Information Report, Doc. 8127, 2 June 1998, Committee on the Honouring of Obligations and Commitments by Member States of the Council of Europe. Co-Rapporteurs: Mr Rudolf Bindig, Germany, Socialist Group and Mr Ernst Muehlmann, Switzerland, Liberal Democrat and Reformers’ Group. Since the report, there was a further positive development. According to Articles 218 and 220 of the CPC complaints about the activities of organs of investigation or investigators may be considered only by the prokuror and complaints about the prokuror to the more senior prokuror. On 23 March 199994 the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation heard the complaints of four citizens and firms whose complaints to courts of general jurisdiction had been refused on this ground. Their complaints concerned the suspension of investigation of the case, the extension of the length of preliminary examination, the execution of a search, imposition of arrest on property and the instigating of a criminal case. The Court held that these articles of the CPC violated Articles 46 and 52 of the Constitution, which guarantee legal protection of rights and freedoms and the right of access to justice and compensation for damage caused by abuse of office. Thus, all the activities of the prokuror in relation to a criminal case must be complained about to the court.

94 Decision of 23 March 1999, No. 5. There remained the most important problem for Russia: that a person in custody would not be brought automatically before a court, as the ECHR and the ICCPR require, and the fact that he or she had a right to appeal to a court did not satisfy the requirement. This was a continuing violation that could, in principle, easily be remedied by amending the Code.





Putin in power


From 2000 to 2003 President Putin expressly referred to himself as following in the footsteps of the great reforming Tsar, Aleksandr II, and his law reforms of 1864 (see Chapter 3 in this book). Putin too presided over creation of a system of justices of the peace; installation of jury trial throughout Russia with the exception of Chechnya; enhanced judicial status; a much reduced role for the prosecutor in criminal and civil trials. I have analysed Putin’s speeches and the events of this period.95 The reforms of 2001 to 2003 were driven through the Russian parliament by Dmitriy Kozak of the President’s Administration.96 These included the three new procedural codes enacted from 2001 to 2003, Criminal,97 Arbitrazh (Commercial)98 and Civil,99 as well as the radical improvements to Yeltsin’s Criminal Code of 1996, 257 amendments in all, which were enacted on 8 December 2003.100 The Council of Europe had substantial expert input into all of this new legislation. However, the initial phase of legal reform from 2000, which included enactment of the new procedural codes referred to in the following, came to a definitive end in late 2003, simultaneously with the arrest of Khodorkovsky and the destruction of YUKOS.

95 Bowring (2005). 96 I worked as an expert for the Council of Europe with Mr Kozak on the draft of the new Criminal Procedural Code; in my view, Kozak is the most effective public servant in Russia. 97 Into force on 1 July 2002. 98 Into force in September 2002. 99 Into force in January 2003. 100 Federal Law No.162-FZ ‘On amendments and modifications of the criminal code of the Russian federation’. However, simultaneously with the speeches just reported, Putin was prosecuting with unprecedented ruthlessness a new conflict in Chechnya.

Following from the decision of the CCS referred to earlier, and in accordance with the 1993 Constitution, generally recognised principles and norms of international law and international treaties of the Russian Federation shall be an integral part of its legal system. If other rules have been established by an international treaty of the Russian federation than provided for by law, the rules of the international treaty shall apply. The apotheosis of this new relationship seemed to have truly arrived with the Resolution of the Plenum of the Supreme Court of the Russian Federation of 10 October 2003. The Resolution was entitled On application by courts of general jurisdiction of the commonly recognised principles and norms of the international law and the international treaties of the Russian Federation.101 The Supreme Court consulted widely in composing this Resolution: participants in discussion included justices of the RCC, Justice Kovler and other experts.

101 In English at http://www.supcourt.ru/catalog.php?c1=English&c2=Documents&c3=&id=6801 (accessed 13 October 2012).
