However, Russia began to lose a number of high-profile cases in the Strasbourg Court. In May 2004, in Gusinskiy v. Russia,102 the Court held that Russia had acted in bad faith in using the criminal justice system to force a commercial deal, by arresting the TV magnate. In July 2004, in Ilaşcu and Others v. Moldova and Russia,103 the majority of the Grand Chamber of the Court found that Russia rendered support to Transdniestria, which broke away from Moldova, amounting to ‘effective control’. The first six Chechen applicants against Russia won their applications to Strasbourg in February 2005.104 In April 2005, in Shamayev and 12 others v. Russia and Georgia,105 the Court condemned Russia for deliberately refusing to cooperate with the Court despite diplomatic assurances; and in October 2002 the Court had given ‘interim measures’ indicating to Georgia that Chechens who had fled to Georgia should not extradited to Russia pending the Court’s consideration.

102 Gusinskiy v. Russia, ECHR, Application no. 70276/01, decision of 19 May 2004. 103 Ilaşcu and Others v. Moldova and Russia, ECHR, Application no. 48787/99, decision of 8 July 2004. 104 These applicants were represented, from 2000, by me and my colleagues from the European Human Rights Advocacy Centre, which I founded, in partnership with the Russian human rights NGO ‘Memorial’, with €1 million EU funding, in 2002. 105 Shamayev and 12 others v. Russia and Georgia, ECHR, Application no. 36378/02, decision of 12 April 2005. Under Putin, Medvedev and now Putin again the human rights situation in Russia has continued to deteriorate. Russia has found itself subject to the new ‘pilot judgment’ procedure of the European Court of Human Rights.

On 10 January 2012 the Court delivered a pilot judgment against Russia in the case of Ananyev and Others v. Russia.106 Ananyev was held in the Smolensk remand prison IZ-67/1 from 20 January to 23 March 2007. During his two months’ stay he was accommodated in a cell that measured 15 square metres and featured 13 sleeping places. He had under 1.25 square metres of personal space and the number of detainees significantly exceeded the number of sleeping places available. This was inhuman and degrading treatment, violating Article 3 of the Convention.

106 Applications Nos 42525/07 and 60800/08. The Court further noted that inadequate conditions of detention were a recurrent problem in Russia that had led the Court to find violations of Articles 3 and 13 of the Convention in more than 80 judgments since the first such finding in the Kalashnikov case in 2002. There were also approximately 250 prima facie meritorious applications against Russia awaiting first examination that featured, as their primary grievance, a complaint about inadequate conditions of detention. These violations was substantially similar: detainees suffered inhuman and degrading treatment on account of an acute lack of personal space in their cells, a shortage of sleeping places, unjustified restrictions on access to natural light and air and non-existent privacy when using the sanitary facilities. It appeared, therefore, that the violations were neither prompted by an isolated incident nor attributable to a particular turn of events in those cases, but originated in a widespread problem resulting from a malfunctioning of the Russian penitentiary system and insufficient legal and administrative safeguards against the proscribed kind of treatment. Russia was given six months to work out, with the Committee of Ministers, a comprehensive programme for improvement.

The Council of Europe has continued to monitor Russia’s progress or lack of it in complying with the undertakings it gave on accession in 1996. On 14 September 2012 the PACE published the latest report of its rapporteurs, Mr György Frunda (Romanian) and Mr Andreas Gross (Swiss), on ‘The honouring of obligations and commitments by the Russian Federation’,107 covering the previous seven years from 2005. The rapporteurs considered that lack of independence and the interconnected question of the lack of confidence of the public in the judiciary were the main problems in the Russian judicial system. These problems have deep roots in the legal and political culture, as in Soviet times judges were often seen not as arbiters, but rather as defenders of the interests of the state. They reported:

107 Doc. 13018, at http://assembly.coe.int/ASP/Doc/XrefViewHTML.asp?FileID=18998&Language=EN (accessed 19 February 2013). 317. The poor state of judicial independence is clearly facilitated by a legislative and administrative framework that fails to protect judges from undue influence by State or private interests. The way the judiciary operates puts pressure on judges through a complex system which is not always apparent or visible and includes not only external pressure, but also internal mechanisms and bureaucracy.

318. These internal mechanisms have become more significant as a result of the government’s drive to strengthen the powers of the executive, known in Russia as ‘strengthening the vertical power’. Political interference has increased under laws brought into force in the wake of the Beslan siege, allegedly for ‘counter-terrorism’ purposes.



The rapporteurs were particularly struck by events at the Constitutional Court:

327. In a 2009 interview with the Spanish newspaper, El País, Constitutional Court Judge Vladimir Yaroslavtsev claimed that the presidential executive office and security services had undermined judicial independence in Russia. In October 2009, the Constitutional Court, in an unprecedented motion, accused Mr Yaroslavtsev of ‘undermining the authority of the judiciary’ in violation of the judicial code and forced him to resign from the Council of Judges.

328. Judge Anatoly Kononov, who has frequently dissented from decisions taken by the majority of the court, in his interview to Sobesednik, supported Mr Yaroslavtsev, claiming that there was no independent judiciary in Russia. Mr Kononov was forced to step down from the Constitutional Court on 1 January 2010, seven years ahead of schedule.



These were two of the most hard-working, intelligent and independent judges on the Constitutional Court.

On 2 October 2012 PACE adopted a Resolution108 based on the report. The European Parliament of the European Union has also expressed grave concern. On 13 September 2012 a plenary sitting of the European Parliament passed a Resolution on ‘The political use of justice in Russia’.109 The preamble to the Resolution noted as follows:

108 Resolution 1896 (2012), Assembly debate on 2 October 2012 (30th and 31st sittings) (see Doc. 13018 and addendum, report of the Committee on the Honouring of Obligations and Commitments by member states of the Council of Europe (Monitoring Committee), co-rapporteurs: Frunda and Gross). Text adopted by the Assembly on 2 October 2012 (31st Sitting), at http://assembly.coe.int/ASP/Doc/XrefViewHTML.asp?FileID=19116&Language=EN (accessed 13 October 2012). 109 See http://www.europarl.europa.eu/document/activities/cont/201209/20120924ATT52181/20120924ATT52181EN.pdf (accessed 29 September 2012). C. [T]he human rights situation in Russia has deteriorated drastically in the last few months and the Russian authorities have recently adopted a series of laws which contain ambiguous provisions and could be used to further restrict opposition and civil society actors and hinder freedom of expression and assembly.



This was followed by a list of burning individual cases:

D. whereas the deaths of Anna Politkovskaya, Natalia Estemirova, Anastasia Barburova, Stanislav Markelov and Sergei Magnitsky remain unaccounted for;

E. whereas Mikhail Khordorkovsky and his business associate Platon Lebedev were given a guilty verdict for embezzlement by Moscow’s Khamovnichesky district court on 30 December 2010; whereas the prosecution, the trial and the verdict were internationally portrayed as being politically motivated;

F. whereas the case of Sergei Magnitsky is only one of several cases of abuse of power by the Russian law enforcement authorities, strongly violating the rule of law and leaving those guilty of causing his death still unpunished; whereas there are a multitude of other judicial cases where politically constructed reasons are being used to eliminate political competition and threaten civil society;

G. whereas the sentencing of the members of the Russian punk group Pussy Riot to two years’ imprisonment for a protest performance against President Vladimir Putin in a Moscow Orthodox cathedral is disproportionate;

H. whereas the Duma is scheduled to vote on 12 September 2012 to lift the mandate of Gennady Gudkov for business activities during his mandate without following the necessary democratic procedures; whereas, for the sake of the rule of law, parliamentary rules should apply equally and impartially to all members of the Duma; whereas other members of the Fair Russia faction such as Dimitri Gudkov and Ilya Ponomarev face similar accusations;

I. whereas the new NGO legislation and the legislation on the right to freedom of assembly could be used to suppress civil society, stifle opposing political views and harass NGOs, democratic opposition and the media; whereas the Russian Parliament adopted a bill in July 2012 granting the status of ‘foreign agent’ to Russian non-commercial organisations engaged in political activities and financed from abroad.



The Resolution declared as follows:

2. Takes the view that Russia, as a member of the Council of Europe and the Organisation for Security and Cooperation in Europe, should meet the obligations it has signed up to; points out that recent developments have moved in the opposite direction to the reforms necessary to improve democratic standards, the rule of law and the independence of the judiciary in Russia.



As at the time of writing this book, it is plain that, on the one hand, many significant changes have been made in Russia since its accession to the Council of Europe nearly 20 years ago and it will be impossible to put the clock back. Russia now regularly accepts a level of interference that would have been quite unthinkable in Soviet times. The fact that so many of the reforms were in effect restorations of Aleksandr II’s Great Reforms has made the process more complex but also more lasting. On the other hand, the current regime in Russia, while paying respect to Aleksandr’s reforms, is also strongly influenced by anti-Western and anti-liberal thinking, as I show in the final chapter of this book. Continued monitoring by the Council of Europe is a sign that in a number of respects, especially conditions of detention, and independence of the judiciary, especially the latter, Russia has a long way to go to catch up even with the 1860s.





Chapter 9

Russia and the death penalty




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-10





Introduction


Following the last chapter, which reviewed and explored Russia’s accession to the Council of Europe, this chapter goes back to the beginning in order to set the scene for one of the most burning questions troubling Moscow’s relations with Strasbourg: the death penalty.

As a result of the 2005 judgment in Öcalan v. Turkey,1 the use of the death penalty has effectively been prohibited in the whole of the territory of the Council of Europe, with its 47 states and over 811 million inhabitants. When Russia joined the Council of Europe in 1996 it entered into an obligation to ratify Protocol No. 6 to the European Convention on Human Rights (ECHR), prohibiting the death penalty, within three years. President Yeltsin moved swiftly to impose a moratorium on executions – in Russia, by shooting. Russia signed the Protocol on 16 April 1997.2 Yet Russia is the only remaining Council of Europe member state to have failed, 13 years on, to ratify Protocol No. 6 so far.

1 Öcalan v. Turkey, Application no. 46221/99, 12 May 2005. 2 See the Council of Europe’s chart of signatures and ratifications at http://conventions.coe.int/Treaty/Commun/ChercheSig.asp?NT=114&CM=8&DF=18/09/2009&CL=ENG (accessed 19 February 2013). In the Öcalan v. Turkey case, the Grand Chamber agreed with the Chamber’s view:

[E]qually the Court observes that the legal position as regards the death penalty has undergone a considerable evolution since Soering was decided. The de facto abolition noted in that case in respect of twenty-two Contracting States in 1989 has developed into a de jure abolition in forty-three of the forty-four Contracting States and a moratorium in the remaining State that has not yet abolished the penalty, namely Russia. This almost complete abandonment of the death penalty in times of peace in Europe is reflected in the fact that all the Contracting States have signed Protocol No. 6 and forty-one States have ratified it, that is to say, all except Turkey, Armenia and Russia. It is further reflected in the policy of the Council of Europe, which requires that new member States undertake to abolish capital punishment as a condition of their admission into the organisation. As a result of these developments the territories encompassed by the member States of the Council of Europe have become a zone free of capital punishment.3



3 Öcalan v. Turkey, para. 163. Since then, Armenia ratified the Protocol on 29 September 2003 and Turkey ratified on 12 November 2003. Russia now stands alone.

The Russian Constitution of 1993 does not prohibit the death penalty, but provides, in Article 20, that every citizen threatened with the death penalty has the right to a jury trial. From the nine regions in which an experiment in jury trial began in 2003 (see the previous chapter), all 83 regions of the Russia Federation now have trial by jury, since the coming into force of the Criminal Procedural Code of 2002, including, from 1 January 2010, the Republic of Chechnya. On 26 April 2010 the first jury trial took place in Chechnya. The jury list comprised more than 6,000 persons, with 1,000 spares.4

4 See V Chechnye vperviye costoitsya sud s uchastiem prisyazhnikh(In Chechnya the first jury trial is taking place), 26 April 2010 at http://grani.ru/Politics/Russia/m.177451.html (accessed on 28 October 2012). In 1999 the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation ruled that the death penalty cannot be imposed in Russia until jury trial is guaranteed in every part of Russia. Jury trial should have come into force for Chechnya on 1 January 2007, but at the last moment the state Duma intervened to delay jury trial until 1 January 2010, when, in fact, Chechnya introduced jury trial.

The issue has never ceased to be prominent in the Russian media and public discussion.5 As in many countries of the world, the death penalty is enormously popular with the general public. And if Russia were to restore the death penalty, it would join an illustrious group of states that between them execute some 1,500 persons a year. In September 2009 Russian Newsweek provided a list of 15 states for its readers, in order of executions carried out (see Table 9.1).

Iran 346 No data Firing squad, hanging, stoning Yes

Saudi Arabia 102 No data Beheading, firing squad, stoning Yes

USA 37 111 Lethal injection, electric chair, firing squad, gas, hanging No

Pakistan 36 236 Hanging, stoning Yes

Iraq 34 285 Hanging No

Vietnam 19 59 Firing squad No

Afghanistan 17 131 Firing squad, hanging No

North Korea 15 No data Firing squad, hanging Yes

Japan 15 27 Hanging No

Yemen 13 No data Firing squad Yes

Indonesia 10 10 Firing squad No

Libya 8 No data Firing squad, hanging No

Bangladesh 5 185 Hanging No

Belarus 4 1 Firing squad No



5 Taratuta and Zygar (2009). In this chapter, I first explore the religious and philosophical arguments for and against the death penalty in Russia. Second, I explain the curious trajectory of the death penalty in Tsarist Russia, followed, third, by the USSR. Fourth, I chart the halting progress that has been made in post-Communist Russia. Fifth, I examine the current tensions between Russia and the Council of Europe. Finally, I conclude by bringing matters up to date and offering a tentative diagnosis and – possible hopeful – prognosis.





Religious, political and philosophical issues


The Christian religion (indeed, any religion) ought, one would think, to be incompatible with the death penalty and Communism would seem in theory to be the type of society able to dispense with it.6 Although the great rational-ist Spinoza, whose philosophy I revere, had no problem with the death penalty – see Ethics Part IV (Of human bondage) proposition 63, scholium: ‘a judge who condemns a guilty man to death – not from hate and anger and the like, but only from a love of the general welfare – is guided only by reason.’ And the debate concerning the Terror in revolutionary France has recently been re-opened.7 But the debate concerning abolition or retention has taken some surprising turns in Russia. Will Adams has described8 a tradition of Russian hostility to the death penalty, which was exemplified by a passage from the Testament of the Grand Prince of Kiev, Vladimir Monomakh, in 1125: ‘[D]o not kill anyone, either guilty or not, nor do you order to kill. Do not destroy a Christian soul, even in case death is well deserved.’ Adams points out that capital punishment did not reappear in Russian codes until the end of the fourteenth century, although the Mongol Tatars brought the death penalty as part of their rule in the meantime.9

6 Spinoza (1996), 150. 7 Wahnich (2012). 8 Adams (1970). 9 Adams (1970), citing Vernadsky (1947).





The death penalty in the Russian Enlightenment


Donald Barry and Eric Williams have pointed out10 that capital punishment has been part of Russian law throughout most of the country’s history. Mikhail Gernet11 explained that in seventeenth-century Russia about 60 articles of the Ulozheniye (Code) of 1649 provided for various forms of the death penalty. But in practice alternative penalties were often imposed. In the sixteenth to seventeenth centuries the death penalty was prescribed for unlawful picking of crops, sale of prohibited goods, purchase of fur without payment of excise, inaccurate weighing of salt when affixing excise duty, helping to hide deserting soldiers and many religious and state crimes.

10 Barry and Williams (1997), 232. 11 Gernet (1913). The Empress Elizabeth abolished the death penalty in 1735, but abolition was short lived.12 Gernet explained13 that an example of the total abolition of the death penalty in legislation was given to Russians when in 1744 the Empress Elizabeth decreed that all death sentences should be submitted to her for reconsideration. On 30 September 1754 the Senate decreed that the death penalty be abolished and replaced by a life sentence of imprisonment with hard labour and branding the offender on the forehead with the word ‘thief’ and cutting off the tip of the nose. This decree was based on the Royal Instruction of 29 March 1753, replacing the death sentence by hard labour. According to Adams,14 Elizabeth exiled rather than executing her opponents and ‘[o]n this basis, Russia could claim to be the first European country in the modern era to abolish capital punishment, although abolition was never complete de facto.’ However, Gernet pointed out,15 later judicial practice interpreted this decree as not abolishing the death penalty for all crimes and therefore death sentences were handed down and carried out, for example, in 1764, 1771, 1775 and on others occasions.

12 Mikhlin (1997). 13 Gernet (1913). 14 Adams (1970), 576. 15 Gernet (1913). As Barry and Williams observe,16 several other Russian rulers instituted moratoriums or near moratoriums on executions for parts of their reign, while leaving the legal provisions permitting the death penalty untouched.

16 Barry and Williams (1997), 232. The eighteenth century was a formative period for the development of Russian law. The great work of Cesare Beccaria, On crimes and punishments, first published in 1764, had a powerful impact in the Tsarist Russia of that period and later, as T. Cizova shows in an extraordinary work of scholarship.17 Beccaria focused on torture, mitigation of penalties and capital punishment. His argument against the death penalty was that it was unnecessary for the protection of society. In defence of his argument, Beccaria cited the fact that, in Russia, the death penalty had been abolished and that this had not led to an increase in crime18 and almost one-third of Catherine II’s Nakaz of 1766 came directly from Beccaria’s On crimes and punishments. Catherine had received, through d’Alembert, a copy of the French translation of Beccaria’s book,19 a Russian translation did not appear until 1803. Beccaria’s ideas had decisive influence on the leaders of the Decembrist uprising of 1825. According to Cizova, ‘they were the first intellectual group who fearlessly brought Beccaria’s arguments to their logical conclusion in the framework of Russian reality’.20

17 Cizova (1962). 18 Cizova (1962), 388. 19 Cizova (1962), 392. 20 Cizova (1962), 404. Adams also points out that the death penalty did not reappear in Russia’s ordinary criminal legislation until the Speransky codification of 1833, although landowners could and did execute their serfs. Offences such as murder and rape were not capital.21 Beccaria also strongly influenced those who drafted the laws that made up the Great Reforms of Aleksandr II in 1864. Cizova states:

21 Adams (1970), 576. [O]n 20 November 1864, law reform was introduced and, reading the Sudebnyye Ustavy, one realises that their creators adhered to the rules laid down in On Crimes and Punishments. It was not a coincidence that the man who contributed most to the compilation of the Ustavy, S. I. Zarudniy, published in 1879 a new translation of Beccaria and compared it to Chapter X of Catherine’s Nakaz.22



22 Cizova (1962), 407. It is an ineluctable feature of Russian history that the achievements of the liberals and ‘Westernisers’ are balanced if not overwhelmed not only by ‘Slavophile’ intellectuals, but especially by the Russian Orthodox Church. According to Mikhail Gernet:23 ‘[M]any facts bear witness to the welcoming attitude of the higher echelons of the Russian Orthodox clergy.’ This was especially the case in the aftermath of the 1905 Revolution. Reasonably reliable contemporary sources show that between 2,000 and 3,000 persons were executed in the period 1905 to 1908.24 Stephen Wheatcroft has argued25 in an analysis of the crackdown following 1905, with an upsurge in mass executions, that ‘it is here, in the reversal of this liberal trend in the post-1905 period, that we can see the roots of the monstrous developments that were to come during the First World War, Civil War, collectivisation and the Ezhovshchina.’ The liberal trend in question was that of Aleksandr II, who, in the aftermath of Russian defeat in the Crimean War, abolished serf-dom in 1861 and in 1864, in his great legal reforms, instituted an independent judiciary as well as jury trials, as I explored in Chapter 3 of this book.26

23 Gernet (1913). 24 See http://www.law.edu.ru/script/cntSource.asp?cntID=100066404 (accessed 19 February 2013). 25 Wheat croft (2002); see also http://www.h-net.org/reviews/showrev.php?id=9524 (accessed 19 February 2013). 26 See Bowring (2009). Gernet27 cites, among others, the fact that at the session of the State Council on 27 June 1906 the Protodeacon (protoierei) T.I. Butkevich made a long speech in favour of the death penalty, although he held himself out as a dedicated opponent of spilling even one drop of blood. But, he said, there were special political moments when the use of the ‘exclusive measure’ was necessary. Turning his attention to the opinions against the death penalty, he said ‘those commentaries on the teachings of the New Testament which say the Evangelists had a direct condemnation of the death penalty are too bold … I find no such condemnation.’ He was inclined to think that the death penalty was not the most severe punishment. He had seen one of the cells in the old Shlisselburg Fortress and ‘was horrified at the human cruelty … I do not have the spirit to ask you, dear statesmen, what is worse for the human being: the momentary death penalty or many years of confinement in the Shlisselburg Fortress.’

27 Gernet (1913). Another Protodeacon, E. A-ov, wrote in the journal Christian Teachings at great length, discussing various texts of holy writers and hotly defending the death penalty. What was interesting for Gernet was the tone, quite unbecoming to a spiritual person, in which the article was written. Thus, ‘in the name of love of humanity’ this holy man was ready to advise the authorities to seize the noose and ‘use it for the preparation of a forgotten but none the less in some cases effective means for making some of those who have been led astray understand’. He found that it was not popular conscience in the state Duma that uttered the decisive sentence of execution, but the ‘sad inability to think things out of those Duma neveglasov (heathens – an archaic word) who after what the unforgettable Lyapkin-Tyapkins28 thought up for themselves, with their own wisdom get to the creation of the world’. In fact, Gernet observed that there were relatively few protests against the death penalty by the clergy. Some of those who protested were held responsible by the higher spiritual leadership. Thus, five priests from Kharkov and one from Tiflis (Tbilisi) were condemned for protesting against the death penalty.

28 Lyapkin-Tyapkin – a vindictive judge, one of Nikolai Gogol’s satirical inventions in Dead souls (Gogol 2004). And the many executions following 1905 were not based on the criminal code, but were the product of military courts and codes and extraordinary decrees. During the late Tsarist period leading scholars, on the basis of analysis of international research and practice, began to argue against the death penalty.29 They were joined by literary giants such as Radishchev, Dostoevsky and Tolstoy.30

29 Kistyakovsky (1896). 30 Adams (1970), 577. The Russian Communists were equally contradictory in their approach. In his essential study of ‘The Soviet Union and the death penalty’, Ger van den Berg31 was anxious to show that the foundation for the bloodthirsty excesses of the Soviet period was provided by Karl Marx himself. The youthful Marx is cited by him as saying in 1849: ‘We are merciless and we do not ask for mercy for us. When it is our turn, we will not conceal the terror behind the hypocritical phrases.’32 The English translation of Marx’s article in the final issue of the Neue Rheinische Zeitung (Marx 1849) is rather different:

31 van den Berg (1983). 32 van den Berg (1983), 154. [D]id we therefore have to advance our social republican tendency only in the ‘last pieces’ of the Neue Rheinische Zeitung? Did you not read our articles about the June revolution, and was not the essence of the June revolution the essence of our paper?

Why then your hypocritical phrases, your attempt to find an impossible pretext? We have no compassion and we ask no compassion from you. When our turn comes, we shall not make excuses for the terror. But the royal terrorists, the terrorists by the grace of God and the law, are in practice brutal, disdainful, and mean, in theory cowardly, secretive, and deceitful, and in both respects disreputable.



Will Adams cites33 a rather different passage, from an article written by Marx in 1853 for the New-York Daily Tribune: ‘[I]t would be very difficult, if not altogether impossible, to establish any principle upon which the justice or expediency of capital punishment could be founded, in a society glorying in its civilization.’ Robert Bohm has provided34 a detailed analysis of Marx’s article, drawing out its implications for the debate concerning the death penalty.

33 Adams (1970), 578. 34 Bohm (2008).





The Bolsheviks and the death penalty


Lenin opposed the death penalty prior to the Bolshevik revolution for reasons broadly similar to those expressed by Marx. In July 1917 he wrote: ‘[I]nstead of liberty, the old tyranny is coming back. The death penalty is being introduced for the soldiers at the front.’35 However, in the conditions of intervention and civil war after 1917, Lenin wrote36 differently: ‘[S]o long as martial law introduced on the railways by the Council of Defence continues, the district transport departments of the Cheka are to be given the right to apply capital punishment to persons found guilty of bribery, theft of freights and railway material, and systematic abuse on the part of officials.’ And in 1920 he explained that the death penalty had been reintroduced strictly for reasons of expediency and that it was his firm intention to renounce it again as soon as possible.37 He argued that his sincerity had been proved by the facts:

35 Lenin (1917/1977). 36 Lenin (1919/1971). 37 Lenin (1922/1964). [W]e were forced to use terror in response to the terror employed by the Entente, when the mighty powers of the world flung their hordes against us, stopping at nothing. We could not have lasted two days had we not replied to these attempts of officers and whiteguards in a merciless fashion. This meant the use of terror, but this was forced on us by the terrorist methods of the Entente. But as soon as we had gained a decisive victory, even before the end of the war, immediately after the capture of Rostov, we renounced capital punishment, and have therefore proved that we intend to carry out our own programme as we had promised. We say that the use of violence arises from the need to crush the exploiters, the landowners and capitalists. When this is accomplished we shall renounce all extraordinary measures. We have proved this in practice. And I think, I hope, and I am confident that the All-Russia Central Executive Committee will unanimously endorse this measure of the Council of People’s Commissars and will implement it in such a way that it will be impossible to apply the death penalty in Russia.



Of course, he died in 1924 and then the real terror began.

Officially, the death penalty was restored by a decree of 5 September 1918, ‘On Red Terror’,38 although the first death sentence by a Soviet court had already been pronounced on 21 June 1918 by the Supreme Tribunal of the Russian Socialist Federation of Soviet Republics (RSFSR) in the case of Aleksei Shchastny, the Admiral of the Baltic Fleet.39 However, on 17 January 1920, in keeping with Lenin’s arguments mentioned earlier, the Soviet state once again declared the death penalty abolished.40 And abolition applied only to the ordinary courts, since military and revolutionary tribunal could still sentence to death. However, with the Polish advance on Ukraine, capital punishment was again restored.

38 SU RSFSR1918, No. 65, item 710. 39 van den Berg (1983), 155. 40 SU RSFSR1920 No. 4–5, item 22. The RSFSR Criminal Code of 24 May 192241 provided for the death penalty for political, military and some economic crimes. The new Code enacted in 192642 also included the death penalty as ‘an exceptional measure for the protection of the worker’s state … until its abolition’.43 Stalin consolidated his power following Lenin’s death in 1924 and in 1927 began his campaign of collectivisation, which, combined with his policy of ‘liquidation of the kulaks (richer peasants)’ led inexorably to the great famine of 1932–1933 in which many millions died, not executed, but from starvation. From 7 August 1932 the collectivisation campaign extended the use of the death penalty to cases of theft of property from trains, ships and collective farms (kolzhozy).44

41 SU RSFSR1922, No. 15, item 153. 42 SU RSFSR1926, No. 80, item 600. 43 Art. 21 of the 1926 Code. 44 SZ SSSR1932, No. 62, item 360. Van den Berg identifies45 a third period in the history of the death penalty in the USSR, the period from 1934 until Stalin’s death. In 1934 the penalty was imposed in peacetime on theft of weapons, murder committed by servicemen under aggravating circumstances, and treason. The death penalty could not be imposed on people under 18. Precise information as to the numbers of people killed in the purges of 1937–1938 is not available, but as many as one million may have been executed. In 1947 the death penalty was abolished in peacetime,46 but it was revived again in 1950, but only for certain crimes against the state.47 It would seem that at Stalin’s death very few executions were taking place.48

45 van den Berg (1983), 158. 46 Ved. SSSR 1947, No.17. 47 Ved. SSSR 1950, No. 3. 48 van den Berg (1983), 159. Ironically, the last edition of Beccaria’s work in Russia appeared at an extraordinarily unpropitious time. It was done:

[B]y the well-known Russian jurist, Professor M. M. Isayev in I939. It was published in Moscow by the then people’s commissariat of justice. It is prefaced by an essay; terse in style, scholarly and comprehensive in its content … One cannot help wondering at the fact that the new translation and edition of the work which passionately defended the dignity and the rights of the individual was being prepared at the very time when the people of the Soviet Union experienced injustice and arbitrariness on a scale unparalleled in the whole of Russian history.49



49 Cizova (1962), 408.





The death penalty after Stalin


There was a dramatic change in 1954, when a decree of 30 April 1954 imposed the death penalty for intentional homicide under aggravating circumstances.50 According to van den Berg, the reason for this dramatic change was its demand by public opinion. The reason was the closing by Beria of a number of prison camps, releasing large numbers of hardened criminals, followed by a steep rise in the rate of serious crime. This has been dramatised in the film Cold Summer of 1953 (Aleksandr Proshkin). However, the Principles for Criminal Legislation of the USSR and the Union Republics promulgated on 25 December 195851 retained the death penalty as an ‘exceptional measure of punishment until its complete abolition’ for certain crimes against the state, some military crimes and aggravated intentional murder. In 1961–1962 economic crimes and some other specified crimes attracted the death penalty and in 1973 hijacking became a capital crime.52

50 Ved. SSSR 1954, No. 11, item 221. 51 Ved. SSSR 1959, No.1, item 12. 52 van den Berg (1983), 159. W. Cary Quillin wrote a few years later53 in terms indicating that there were many similarities between the USA and USSR in the attitude to the death penalty, with an equally small prospect of abolition. By the time van den Berg composed his article, in 1983 (the year this author first visited the USSR), the death penalty in peace time was possible for 17 crimes (see Table 9.2).54

Table 9.2 Seventeen crimes for which the death penalty was possible, 1983 Political crimes Criminal code

Terrorist acts against a public official or representative of a foreign state Articles 66, 67

Sabotage Article 68

The crimes above, committed by an organised group Article 72

The crimes above, committed against another workers’ state Article 73

Banditry Article 77

Action disrupting the work of prison camps Article 77–1

Economic crimes

Counterfeiting Article 87

Speculation in currency, speculation as a business Article 88

Stealing of state property on an especially large scale Article 93–1
