53 Quillin (1977). 54 van den Berg (1983), 160. According to van den Berg,55 at a conservative estimate, about 2,000 death sentences were pronounced each year during the 1960s and 1970s, although only one-third were actually carried out. Andrei Sakharov estimated in 197556 that between 700 and 1,000 criminals were shot each year. In one well-known case, Yuri Sokolov, the manager of the most prestigious food store in Moscow, Gastronom No.1 in Gorky Street, was arrested in April 1983 on charges of corruption and shot in July 1984.57

55 van den Berg (1983), 162. 56 Sakharov (1975), 43. 57 Website:http://www.time.com/time/magazine/article/0,9171,926717,00.html (accessed 21 October 2012).





The post-Soviet period


When Barry and Williams published their study in 1997,58 attitudes had substantially changed. In 1987 in the context of Gorbachov’s glasnost and perestroika, a substantial public debate began in the mass media. By 1989 it was possible for the leading official law journal, Soviet State and Law to publish a translation of a chapter from the Amnesty International book When the state kills: the death penalty and the human rights issue.59

58 Barry and Williams (1997). 59 Kelina (1989); see also Barry and Williams (1997), 234, n. 11. A new draft of the USSR Principles of Criminal Legislation, which appeared in 1988, had six rather than 17 capital crimes and when the Principles were finally adopted in July 1991 (to come into force one year later, by which time the USSR had collapsed), just a few months before the USSR ceased to exist, there were five capital crimes: treason, premeditated murder under aggravating circumstances, rape of a minor under aggravating circumstances, child kidnapping resulting in particularly grave circumstances and especially grave crimes against the peace and security of mankind.60 However, the RSFSR chose not to follow the USSR and in December 1991 legislated61 that three of the economic crimes (illegal currency operations, theft of state property and bribery) should cease to be capital offences, but leaving counterfeiting as a capital crime until its removal as a offence carrying the death penalty in 1994.62

60 Ved. SSSR 1991, No. 30, item 82, art. 40. 61 Rossiyskaya Gazeta25 December 1991, 6. 62 Rossiyskaya Gazeta7 July 1994, 3; see also Barry and Williams (1997), 235.





The Yeltsin period


Until 1992 pardons and commutation of sentences was within the jurisdiction of the Presidium of the RSFSR Supreme Soviet and then by a Supreme Soviet Clemency Commission. When in 1991, for the first time in its history, the RSFSR elected a president, Boris Yeltsin, he was given authority over pardons.63 On 12 January 1992 very shortly after the collapse of the USSR, President Yeltsin decreed the creation of a Clemency (pomilovaniye)Commission within the President’s Administration. This body, composed of 15 members and led by the well-known writer Anatoly Pristavkin, met once a week and reviewed between 100 and 150 cases.64 Of these some 7 to 10 were capital cases. The Commission then made a recommendation to the President, who would write ‘agree’ or ‘don’t agree’ on the draft.

63 RSFSR Law of 24 April 1991, Sovetskaya Rossiya 20 April 1991, 1. 64 Barry and Williams (1997), 237. However, as Robert Kushen points out,65 the fractured and dynamic political situation of the time, when President Yeltsin’s future was in doubt, made any substantive reform or abolition highly unlikely. Nevertheless, abolition remained a longer term goal of the reformers. They succeeded in cutting out over half the population by an amendment to the Code of Criminal Procedure on 29 April 1993 making the death penalty applicable only to men aged between 18 and 65.66 The new Constitution of the Russian Federation, adopted by referendum in December 1993 following Yeltsin’s ‘constitutional coup d’état’, contained the following Article 20:

Each person shall have the right to life.

The death penalty, until its abolition, may be prescribed by federal law as an exceptional penalty for particularly grave crimes against life with the granting to the accused of the right to have the case heard by a court with the participation of jurors.



65 Kushen (1993). 66 I am indebted for this point to Jane Henderson. A new Criminal Code of the Russian Federation was not enacted until May 1996 and came into force on 1 January 1997.67 This contained the following capital crimes: premeditated murder under aggravating circumstances; murder or attempted murder of a state or pubic official; murder or attempted murder of a law enforcement officer; and genocide. The death penalty for offences against minors had gone, as well as crimes against peace and security of mankind. Article 59 provides:

The death penalty as an exceptional measure of punishment may be established only for exceptionally grave crimes which are life threatening.

The death penalty is not imposed upon women, nor upon persons who committed the crime prior to 18 years of age, nor upon men who had reached the age of 65 at the time of sentencing.

When clemency is granted, the death penalty may be replaced by life imprisonment or imprisonment for a term of 25 years.



67 Naumov (1997); English translation at http://www.legislationline.org/documents/section/criminal-codes/country/7 (accessed 19 February 2013). Barry and Williams provided68 an analysis of the numbers of executions carried out from 1985, when death penalty data, albeit containing discrepancies, began to be released. There was a significant drop in the numbers executed in the USSR, from 770 in 1985, to 526 in 1986, 344 in 1987, 271 in 1988, 276 in 1989 and 195 in 1990. Numbers granted clemency varied from 20 in 1985 to 72 in 1988. The Russian Federation is, of course, smaller than the USSR, but from 1989 the statistics show that 93 persons were executed in 1989, 76 in 1990, 59 (or 15 according to the Clemency Commission)69 in 1991, 18 (or one) in 1993, four in 1993, 19 in 1994, 86 in 1995 and 56 in 1996.

68 Barry and Williams (1997), 239–42. 69 See Pristavkin (1997). Amnesty International70 estimated that 140 persons were executed in Russia in 1996, the year Russia joined the Council of Europe. Anatoly Pristavkin wrote71 that the Council of Europe considered the total of 16 executions reported to its officials unacceptably high. When Pristavkin explained that the true number was 86, he was told that, had this been known, Russia would never have been admitted.

70 Amnesty International (1997), 5. 71 Pristavkin (1997), 5; Barry and Williams (1997), 241. The sudden increase in the number of executions in 1995–1996 is striking. In the same period there are practically no instances of clemency. According to Barry and Williams72 Yeltsin was responsible for a harsher policy following the murder of the famous journalist Vladislav Listyev and was influenced by senior law enforcement officials for whom ‘an act of clemency is equivalent to weakening the struggle against crime’.73 And a study published in early 1995 based on reputable polling showed that 25 per cent of respondents wanted an expansion of the death penalty; 37 per cent preferred retaining the death penalty as contained in the USSR Criminal Code; and only 15 per cent advocated moving gradually to its abolition.74 Of course, Russia is not the only country in which a large majority of the population is in favour of the death penalty.

72 Barry and Williams (1997), 242. 73 Pristavkin (1997), 5. 74 Levada (1995); see also Barry and Williams (1997), 251, n. 62.





Obligations undertaken on joining the Council of Europe


As already noted, on accession to the Council of Europe in 1996, Russia entered into an obligation: ‘[T]o sign within one year and ratify within three years from the time of accession, Protocol No. 6 to the European Convention on Human Rights on the abolition of the death penalty in time of peace, and to put into place a moratorium on executions with effect from the day of accession.’

Accordingly, and as noted already, Russia signed Protocol No. 6 on 16 April 1997. It should be noted that as a matter of international law, enshrined in the 1969 UN Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties,75 a state which has signed a treaty is prohibited, pending ratification, from acting in such a way as to violate the substance of the treaty it has signed.

75 At http://untreaty.un.org/ilc/texts/instruments/english/conventions/1_1_1969.pdf (accessed 19 February 2013). On 16 May 1996 President Yeltsin issued a Decree76 ‘On the gradual curtailing of the application of the death penalty in connection with the admission of Russia to the Council of Europe’.77 Barry and Williams observe78 that the decree had four substantive provisions. It:

charged the Russian government within one month to prepare the draft of a law ratifying Protocol No. 6;

recommended that both houses of parliament speed up the process for amending the criminal legislation;

instructed the Ministry of Internal Affairs to submit within three months proposals for the facilities for life imprisonment, in accordance with UN standards;

recommended that the Prosecutor General increase supervision over the implementation of laws on conditions under which capital prisoners are held.



76 Decree No. 724 (16 May 1996). 77 Barry and Williams (1997), 244. 78 Barry and Williams (1997), 245. It was unfortunate that the very first sentence of the Decree referred to Council of Europe ‘recommendations’, when it was perfectly clear that Russia had entered into an obligation on joining. Furthermore, the parliament was not called on to eliminate capital punishment, but only to curtail it. According to Pristavkin,79 a paragraph instituting a moratorium was dropped from the draft Decree following ‘intense pressure from officials of the Office of the Prosecutor General, the Ministry of the Interior and the Russian Supreme Court.’ The Decree, moreover, was promulgated only a few weeks before the presidential elections, in which he was seeking a second term, against a very strong challenge from the Communist Party.

79 Pristavkin (1996), 10; Barry and Williams (1997), 245, n. 45. A further indication of the discrepancy between Russia’s expressions of good faith towards the Council of Europe and reality was the enactment in 1996 of the new Criminal Code, noted earlier, that came into force on 1 January 1997, containing five offences attracting the death penalty, several of them new. On 2 August 1996 Yeltsin announced an unofficial moratorium on executions. One typical response was that of Vladimir Guliev:

[L]iberals, in our opinion, completely ignore Russian sociocultural ‘background’. Among us there has always existed an extremely significant number of people who are not deterred from committing murder either by prison or in particular by labor colonies… And the succession of wars and cruel repressions [that we have experienced] has lowered catastrophically the value of the human personality, of life itself … And this applies even more so to present Russia.80



80 Guliev (1996). Catherine Merridale supported this view:

Russian attitudes to death and to the value of life have probably been affected, in the medium term, by repeated exposure to unmourned, unvalued death. What others have described as the brutalization of society may have some applicability to Russia, though distinctions need to be made between those directly exposed to violence and those who experienced it at second hand or as children.81



81 Merridale (1996), 16. In March 1997 the Duma refused to ratify Protocol No. 6 and also refused to enact a law on moratorium. However, at the same time the Clemency Commission simply stopped sending cases to the President, so executions ceased for that reason if no other.82 In August 1999 the Russian Government once more submitted Protocol No. 6 to the Duma for ratification. This met a similar fate. However, the matter was resolved indirectly when, in February 1999, the Federal Constitutional Court held83 that in order for the death penalty to be applied in Russia, the accused must in have the right to a trial by jury every part of Russia: then trial by jury existed in only 9 of 89 regions of Russia. Igor Petrukhin, a leading expert on criminal law and procedure, was one of many scholars and experts calling for full abolition.84 Following a detailed legal analysis, he concluded: ‘[T]he death penalty breeds cruelty in society and nourishes the illusion that crime can only be resolved through measures of horror. It is impossible to fight cruelty with cruelty.’

82 Barry and Williams (1997), 247. 83 Decision of 2 February 1999, No. 3-P, Rossiskaya Gazeta, 10 February 1999, English summary in Venice Commission, Bulletin on Constitutional Case-Law, Edition 1999–1: 96–8. 84 Petrukhin (1999). And Sergei Pashin, who was the driving force behind the reintroduction of trial by jury in Russia, described the desire to restore the death penalty as ‘disgraceful atavism’.85 His close colleague Sergei Vitsin, a member of the Clemency Commission, wrote:

85 Pashin (2001). [T]here has now appeared in Russia a great historical chance once and for all to rid ourselves of the punishment which brings disgrace to our country. This is necessary for our own benefit, and not that of other countries or the Council of Europe. The impermissible retention of the death penalty in a society’ as Karl Marx once wrote ‘which plumes itself on its own civilisation’.86



86 Vitsin (2009).





Will the death penalty return to Russia?


Russia has not executed an accused since 1999. But the Criminal Procedural Code of 2001 extended jury trial to the whole of Russia except Chechnya, where it was to have been introduced not later than 1 January 2007. This would then, of course, have triggered the automatic restoration of the death penalty, following the judgment of the Constitutional Court referred to earlier.

However, on 15 November 2006 the state Duma adopted at first reading a draft law that changes the date for introduction of jury trials in Chechnya from 1 January 2007 to 1 January 2010. The (good) reason they gave was that lists of potential jurors must be compiled by municipalities, which did not yet exist in Chechnya. On 27 December 2006 the draft law was signed by the President; and it was published in the Russian Gazette and came into force on 31 December 2006, in the nick of time. So Russia had only until 1 January 2010 before the death penalty would automatically become available once more.

By the same token, as Roger Hood pointed out,87 on 18 December 2007 at the United Nations General Assembly Russia voted with 103 other states for a resolution calling for a moratorium on the use of the death penalty with a view to its abolition. All the other former Soviet states, except Belarus, which as Table 9.1 shows, retains the death penalty, were in support; and Belarus abstained rather than opposing. There is now renewed speculation that the death penalty will indeed return to Russia. The Beslan school siege tragedy led to renewed calls for restoration. On 9 September 2004 the influential Governor of Kemerovo Oblast, Aman Tuleev, demanded that parliamentarians reintroduce capital punishment for terrorist offences. He was joined by the then Deputy Speaker of the state Duma, the leader of the nationalist Liberal Democrat party, Vladimir Zhirinovsky.88

87 Hood (2008). 88 Website:http://www.newsru.com/arch/russia/09sep2004/tuleev.html (accessed 21 October 2012). Further, Nikolai Petrov of the Moscow Carnegie Centre has been quoted as saying:

[T]he time to revise Russia’s foreign policy concept may not have come, but there have been lots of signals suggesting the need to improve relations with European structures. Reintroducing the death penalty would directly contradict all the signals President Dmitry Medvedev has been sending.89



89 Oliphant (2009). And on Wednesday 9 September 2009 President Medvedev sent a strong signal, when his spokeswoman, Natalia Timakova, denied that reintroducing the death penalty was even under discussion (ibid), apparently in direct response to the Newsweek article referred to earlier.90

90 Taratuta and Zygar (2009).





Conclusion – and a happy ending?


Sociologists joke that the current opinion poll rating of capital punishment in Russia is, at 80 per cent, even higher than that of Vladimir Putin. The former Deputy Chairman of the Constitutional Court and renowned law reformer, Tamara Morshchakova, said that ‘[O]ur parliamentary deputies suffer from dependence on populism. They say that society does not want to abolish the death penalty. So what? In no country in the world does society want to abolish the death penalty. Even in England, in that ethical old democracy. But there society is not asked.’91 At the root of the continuing debate is a perplexing paradox. Mikhail Margelov wrote:

91 Taratuta and Zygar (2009). [T]he will of the Russian people is bewildering. They have no trust in punitive bodies yet two-thirds of the population want the death penalty, that is, they are ready to entrust to these bodies the right to decide who must live and who must die. The average Russian seems to be more inclined to act on his conviction rather than on the rational assessment of possible consequences.92



92 Margelov (2006). Russia is often portrayed as a primitive and bloodthirsty society, ruled by the authoritarian governments it deserves. Yet, as has been shown in this chapter, Russia was the first European state to abolish the death penalty in the eighteenth century, if not permanently, and throughout its contemporary history there has been an engaged and informed debate on the question of abolition. There have now been no executions for more than a decade and the present political leadership has shown no desire for the restoration of capital punishment.

There appears to be a happy ending to this tale. The Russian Constitutional Court has once again appeared as a deus ex machine and may well have secured an almost final abolition of the death penalty in Russia. Following a request from the Supreme Court of the Russian Federation, and with unprecedented speed, on 19 November 2009 the Constitutional Court handed down its Decision (Opredeleniye), clarifying its Resolution (Postanovleniye) of 2 February 1999 referred to already.93

93 In Rossiyskaya Gazetaon 27 November 2009, at http://www.rg.ru/2009/11/27/postanovlenie-ks-dok.html (accessed 12 January 2010). The Decision ruled that the death penalty may not be reintroduced even when trial by jury becomes available in the whole of the Federation, including Chechnya. The basis for the decision is very significant. The Court noted that Russia signed Protocol 6 to the ECHR on 16 April 1997 and is therefore obliged, by virtue of Article 18 of the Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties 196994 ‘to refrain from acts which would defeat the object and purpose’ of the Protocol. Thus, the moratorium presently in place continues until ratification by Russia of the Protocol. As the commentator Anna Zakatnova pointed out,95 three judges (out of 19), Sergei Kazantsev, Mikhail Kleandrov and Yuriy Rudkin, voted against the Decision and the Court’s Chairman, Valeriy Zorkin, could not predict what the Russian parliament would do in the future. But Judge Kononov, one of the majority of judges who voted in favour, insisted that the Constitutional Court had not exceeded its jurisdiction and that the effective abolition of the death penalty in Russia is irreversible.

94 Website:http://untreaty.un.org/ilc/texts/instruments/english/conventions/1_1_1969.pdf (accessed 12 January 2010). 95 In Rossiyskaya Gazeta20 November 2009, at http://www.rg.ru/2009/11/20/kazn.html (accessed 12 January 2010). Celebration of the Constitutional Court’s decision was swiftly tempered by disturbing news. On 2 December 2009 Judge Kononov and his colleague Judge Yaroslavtsev, both of them leading advocates of human rights on the Court, were forced to resign their positions following public criticism by them of political control of the Russia judiciary.96 Yaroslavtsev was criticised and recommended to stand down as the CCRF representative on the RF Council of Judges. This he did on 1 December 2009, but remained a judge. At the same time, Anatoliy Kononov, a criminal law expert and one of the longest serving CCRF judges, resigned his tenure.97

96 In Russian in the daily Kommersant, at http://www.kommersant.ru/doc.aspx?DocsID=1284828 (accessed 12 January 2010); see also Nikolaus von Twickel, ‘2 Senior Judges Quit after Criticism’, Moscow Times, 3 December 2009. 97 Henderson (2011b). Ten years have passed since Russia signed Protocol 6 and there is no sign that the state Duma will enact legislation for ratification. But in order to restore the death penalty Russia would be obliged to leave the Council of Europe, a step it is presently unlikely to take. But, as always, the future of the rule of law in Russia is clouded by uncertainty.





Chapter 10

Sovereign democracy




DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-11





Introduction


My final chapter brings the wheel full circle and shows how ideas which have their roots in the mindset of the Slavophiles, with a significant dose of Messianism, and language borrowed from Eurasianism, have a strong influence on the ideology and policy of the current Russian regime and on its attitude to its European human rights commitments.

Both Britain and Russia are showing signs of rebellion against the European Court of Human Rights. This is not a chapter on British radical conservatism, but my readers may well be familiar with the refusal to obey the Strasbourg Court’s judgment in Hirst v. UK (No. 2) (2005) on prisoners’ voting rights. In November 2010 the Court ruled that the UK had failed to comply with the 2005 judgment and the Grand Chamber refused an appeal by the UK. On 10 February 2011 Conservative members of Parliament were allowed a free vote and defied the Court. In April 2011 Kenneth Clarke said: ‘[A]t times, the court has been rather too ready to substitute its own judgment for that of national courts, without giving enough weight to the strength of the domestic legal system or allowing for genuine differences of national approach.’1 At the time of writing, Kenneth Clarke’s successor as Minister of Justice, Chris Grayling, insisted, on 28 October 2012, that Parliament has a ‘clear’ right not to accept the European Court of Human Rights’ ruling prisoners must get the vote. But he warned there would be ‘consequences’ for the UK’s position in Europe if MPs chose to defy the judgment. The government has insisted it will not support the granting of voting rights to prisoners and most MPs agree. Mr Grayling said he was thinking ‘very carefully’ about how to act.2

1 Website:http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/justice-secretary-warning-over-echr-judgments-2274962.html (accessed 22 October 2012). 2 Grayling, ‘MPs to decide on prisoner votes’, 28 October 2012 at http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-20115675 (accessed 28 October 2012). Russia is not immune from such difficulties. On 8 June 2011 the Grand Chamber of the European Court of Human Rights held an oral hearing on Russia’s appeal against the Chamber judgment of 7 October 2010 in Markin v. Russia, on the Russian authorities’ refusal to grant the male applicant parental leave, which represented a difference in treatment compared to female military personnel and civilians. This was an appeal from the judgment of the Chamber, which for the first time directly criticised the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation.3 I analyse the Russian reaction later in the chapter.

3 Application No. 30078/06, Judgment of 15 January 2009. The focus of this chapter is the concept of ‘sovereign democracy’, the brain-child of Vladislav Yuryevich Surkov (b. 1964 – one year older than Dmitry Medvedev), previously First Deputy Head of the President’s Administration and also previously the acknowledged ideologist of the regime.4 Surkov, although no longer in the President’s Administration, is now a Deputy Prime Minister, with a crucially important role in government.

4 Interestingly, his father’s name was Dudaev and he may have been born in Chechnya, although his claimed birthplace is in Lipetsk Oblast. In 2006 the collection Suverenitet (Sovereignty) appeared, edited by the ‘young conservative’ Nikita Garadzha. This book contained articles by Putin, Medevedev and Surkov himself. It has been said that the doctrine of ‘sovereign democracy’ has no content. But I argue in this chapter that there is indeed a determinate content to the doctrine – and a controversial one at that. Policymakers and even scholars in the West are, I think, insufficiently aware of this content.

What I show is that this doctrine has a direct impact on law and on human rights. That is where I start in this chapter. I continue with an article in the journal Radical Philosophy, which initially sparked my interest in these developments in Russia. At the centre of this chapter is the startling (for me) influence of Carl Schmitt, the ‘crown jurist’ of the Third Reich, on contemporary Russian debates. The next two sections look more closely at the Sovereignty question and one of its authors; and then at a leading figure among the ‘young conservatives’, Mikhail Remizov. My conclusion places these debates in their Russian historical context.





The Russian rebellion in late 2010


The contemporary significance of the word ‘sovereignty’ in Russia was demonstrated on 29 October 2010 when Valeriy Zorkin, Chairman of the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation, published a long article in the Rossiyskaya Gazeta entitled ‘Limits of compromise’, expressing defiance of the European Court of Human Rights.5 Zorkin was especially agitated by the Markin case and argued:

5 Zorkin (2010). The principles of state sovereignty and the supremacy of the Constitution in the legal system of Russia lie at the foundation of its constitutional system. The Convention as an international treaty of Russia is a component part of its legal system, but it is not higher than the Constitution … Each decision of the European Court is not only a legal but a political act. When such as decision is taken in the interests of the protection of the rights and freedoms of the citizen and the development of our country, Russia will always precisely obey it. But when it or another decision of the Strasbourg court is doubtful from the point of view of the goal of the European Convention on Human Rights and moreover in a direct fashion concerns national sovereignty, and fundamental constitutional principles, Russia has the right to work out a defence mechanism against such decisions. Precisely through the prism of the Constitution the problem of the relationship between orders of the CC and the ECtHR must also be worked out … Like any other European state, Russia must fight as much for the preservation of its sovereignty, as for the careful relationship with the European Convention, and defence of its sovereignty against inadequate, doubtful decisions. [Emphasis added.]



On Thursday 18 November 20106 the Strasbourg Court communicated to Russia the United Opposition case, in which complaints were made about the 2003 elections. Zorkin complained that the Court’s decision was ‘connected with political reasons’ and said: ‘Not all decisions of the ECtHR are obligatory for execution, in particular, those concerning sovereignty’ (emphasis added). This was followed by heated exchanges with Jean-Paul Costa, the ECtHR’s then President and others in St Petersburg in November 2010.

6 Website:http://www.newsru.com/arch/russia/18nov2010/zorkin.html (accessed 21 October 2012). On 11 December 2010, at a meeting at former President Medvedev’s residence with Constitutional Court judges, Judge Zorkin referred to the St Petersburg discussions of the previous month. The President replied: ‘As it seems to me, we all the same never transferred such a part of our sovereignty, the sovereignty of Russia, as would permit any international court or foreign court to take a decision, changing our national legislation.’7

7 Website:http://kremlin.ru/news/9792 (accessed 21 October 2012). On 12 April 2011 Zorkin and his fellow thinkers were further outraged by the Strasbourg Court’s judgment in Republican Party v. Russia, holding that dissolution of the party in 2007 violated its rights under Article 11.

On 16 May 2011 Zorkin continued his challenge, at a conference dedicated to the anniversary of the Nuremberg trials.8 Zorkin presented a paper entitled ‘Legal results of the Nuremburg trials and their contemporary significance’ and once more spoke out against the ‘liberal-globalist’ principle of ‘the priority of international law over national law’, which he defined as ‘the attempt at senseless extension’ of the unprecedented situation of the Nuremburg trials in relation to ‘private and dubious international collisions’. At the same conference, the Minister of Justice, Aleksandr Konovalov, delivered a paper ‘Legal traditions in the contemporary global world’, giving some ‘nuances’ to Zorkin’s presentation. In his view, the general tendency of public law was ‘an inadequate legal reaction to new threats – from terrorism to illegal migration, which leads to an absence of balance between the rule of law and police and administrative aspects of expediency.’

8 Website:http://www.kommersant.ru/doc/1642018 (accessed 21 October 2012) Zorkin is not the only senior Russian judge to have expounded on the nature of sovereignty. On 17 July 2012 the commentator and political scientist Nikolai Zlobin published an article entitled ‘Anton Ivanov as the last stronghold of Russian sovereignty’.9 Anton Ivanov (b. 1965), a fellow student of Dmitry Medevedev’s at the law department of Leningrad State University, is the Chairman of Russia’s Supreme Commercial Court. Zlobin started by commenting on the recent Berezovsky v. Abramovich case at the London Commercial Court. He and colleagues were impressed by the general atmosphere of objectivity and supremacy of law; for him, that is what is now needed in Russia. But this is not the case in the view of the Russian judges. Ivanov and other senior judges propose ‘defending sovereignty’ from the London and other foreign courts:

9 Nikolai Zlobin Anton Ivanov kak poslednij oplot rossiyskovo suverenitetaon his blog at http://nzlobin.livejournal.com/73343.html, and on Radio Ekho Moskvy at http://echo.msk.ru/blog/nzlobin/909838-echo/ (accessed 1 October 2012). Ivanov wants not simply to overrule in Russia their decisions, but even to confiscate the property of companies and persons who run to foreign courts, and to refuse the foreign judges entry to Russia. The judicial claims of foreigners against Russians, accusing them of corruption, Ivanov considers to be an ‘abuse of law’ and he demands that Russian courts be given the possibility of overruling such decisions. And this is in a situation in which foreign investors are not standing in line at the Kremlin, and the flight of capital grows, and Russian entrepreneurs hold all their interests offshore.

D. Afanasiev of the leading lawyers firm ‘Yegorov, Puginskiy, Afanasiev and Partners’ not long ago asserted that not more than 10% of significant deals are concluded in the Russian jurisdiction, and that businessmen prefer English law as the proper law of the contract. The Chairman of the Duma Committee for Economic Policy Ye. Fyodorov recognises that 95% of the largest Russian firms are to be found in foreign jurisdictions.





Zorkin as an early participant in the sovereignty discussion


Zorkin is no newcomer to debates about ‘sovereignty’. On 17 November 2006 he participated in and led a discussion on ‘The sovereign state in conditions of globalisation: democracy and national identity’, together with leading intellectual and scholarly figures such as Vladislav Fronin, Sergey Karaganov, Ivan Melnikov, Andrei Isaev, Gleb Pavlovskiy, Igor Bunin, Dmitriy Orlov, Aleksandr Babakov, Sergey Ivanenko, Nikolai Levichev, Vyacheslav Volodin, Andryanik Migranyan, Leonid Polyakov, Yegor Solomatin and Vladislav Surkov.10

10 Surkov (2008). The roundtable discussion followed publication on 22 August 2004 of Zorkin’s article ‘Apology for the Westphalian system’ in Rossiyskaya Gazeta.11 Zorkin declared at the discussion’s conclusion that ‘From this point of view Russian democracy – is sovereign, and the sovereignty of the Russian state – is democratic … Precisely for this reason in the globalising world the defence of the interests of the state demands the uniting and not the breaking up of sovereignties.’

11 Zorkin (2004).





The Radical Philosophy article


My curiosity was originally aroused by the article in Radical Philosophy by Julia Svetlichnaja with James Heartfield12 ‘Sovereign democracy. Dictatorship over capitalism in contemporary Russia’,13 which appeared in January 2010. Svetlichnaja, whose intervention with Heartfield in the Litvinenko murder case stirred up something of a storm,14 is a PhD student with Chantal Mouffe at the University of Westminster. Mouffe is well known as a serious student of Carl Schmitt (see her 1993 and 1999 books). Garadja’s 2006 collection Suverenitet is also the centre of their attention.15 They correctly point out that the collection is ‘rooted, theoretically in François Guizot’s political rationalism and Carl Schmitt’s “decisionism” … [and] endorses their contempt towards key concepts of the liberal “democratic” age … the new Russian theorists of sovereign democracy prefer instead to understand democracy as an ‘identity of the governors and the governed’.

12 James Heartfield was prior to 1997, when it disbanded, a theoretician of the Revolutionary Communist Party and is now involved in the RCP’s successors the Institute of Ideas and Spiked Online. 13 Svetlichnaya with Heartfield (2010). 14 Website:http://larussophobe.wordpress.com/2006/12/12/the-kremlins-litvinenko-disinformation-campaign-exposed-2/ (accessed 21 October 2012). 15 Garadja (2006). This was also noted by Andrey Makarychev, who wrote in 2005 that:

Putin’s reforms in this sense rely on the logic of Carl Schmitt: both understand the core of the problems to lie in the fact that the state is turning into one among many institutions (groups, associations) and as a result is in a relation of dependency on other institutions. A ‘relativisation’ of the state takes place: it has to take part in discussions, and conclude formal or informal agreements with other instances which claim political status. As a result the state becomes derivative on the interrelations of forces between conflicting group interests, and the central power becomes in the end only an ‘arbiter’ or ‘peacemaker’.16



16 Makarychev (2005). Makarychev went on:

The paradoxical nature of Putin’s answer to the ‘Schmittean problem’ lies in the fact that with the help of depoliticised methods he tries to resolve the political – in their foundations – problems of the restoration of the subjectivity of the state. But then again, this means, to speak the language of C. Schmitt, the question of norm or exception – this is the question which is still completely open for evaluation.



He cited Mouffe, Žižek, Agamben, Schmitt and others. The translation by Svetlichnaja and Heartfield, by the way, is incorrect and misleading in my view.

My curiosity was aroused by the fact that – especially in a journal of radical philosophy – Schmitt made such an unproblematised appearance. The authors concluded:

Unlike the governing classes in Western Europe and North America, the Russian elite is engaged in a political struggle for authority. Authority in the West is evasive, difficult to pin down, diffuse. No so in Russia, where the elite are forcefully politicising events, for good or ill. That might hold dangers, but, as Makarichev suggests, it may at least open the possibility for an engagement, a contest for a different interpretation of events.



Thus, they are, one might think, attempting a Schmittean justification for the Putin regime.





Fascination with a fascist


This fascination with Schmitt and his disciple Agamben is shared by other – erstwhile leftist – intellectuals in USA and British academe. For reasons why this might be misplaced, see Jan Werner Muller’s A dangerous mind: Carl Schmitt in post-war European thought,17 on Schmitt’s post-war trajectory. See also Empire and human rights by my Birkbeck colleague Costas Douzinas,18 a thoroughly Schmittean book, in which Schmitt is frequently called on as an authority, without context or critique, and drawing on William Rasch’s 2004 Sovereignty and its discontents: on the primacy of conflict and the structure of the political. This last is a text crying out to be translated into Russian, as its title suggests.

17 Muller (2003). 18 Douzinas (2007). Schmitt has his own trajectory in Russia. In 1992 Aleksandr Dugin, who has made a remarkable transition from ideologue for Limonov and his National Bolshevik Party to the leading exponent of Eurasianism and now Professor of Sociology at Moscow State University, published in Nash Sovremennik (Our Contemporary) a seminal article entitled: ‘Conservative revolution. Carl Schmitt, 5 lessons for Russia’. Dugin displayed a detailed knowledge of Schmitt’s writings and rehearsed the familiar Schmittean themes: friend/ enemy, political theology, the nomos of the earth, etc. He concluded:

Russia – this is the Great Space and Great Thought which its people bear in their gigantic continental Eurasian spirit. If the German Genius will serve our Awakening, then those very Teutons will deserve for themselves a privileged place among the ‘friends of Great Russia’, will become ‘ours’, will become ‘our own’, will become ‘Asians’, Huns’, ‘Scythians’, like us, the autochtonous people of the Great Forest and the Great Steppe.



In 2008 Aleksandr Mikhailovskiy (b. 1977), a dotsent in philosophy at the Higher School of Economics and expert on the ‘conservative revolutionary’ Ernst Jünger (1895–1998), published in the prestigious journal Questions of Philosophy an article entitled ‘The struggle for Carl Schmitt: on the reception and actuality of the concept of the political’.19 He started:

19 Mikhailovsky (2008). The German theoretician of law Carl Schmitt is one of the most powerful conservative intellects of the last century, whose ideas not only have not lost their actuality, but on the contrary, arouse more and more interest among those who try to comprehend the political situation of the contemporary world.



He recognised that Schmitt became the ‘court jurist’ of the Third Reich. He cited Agamben, Negri, Žižek and Mouffe as those who have found resources in Schmitt, from the left.





The Sovereignty collection of 2006 – and Carl Schmitt


Which brings me back to the collection to which Putin and Medvedev contributed. A key chapter in Garadzha’s Suverenitet collection was the Schmitt-infused ‘Sovereignty as a political choice’20 by Aleksandr Filippov (b. 1958), for six years Dean of Sociology at the Moscow Higher School of Social and Economic Science and now head of the Kafedra (Chair) of Practical Philosophy at the prestigious National Research University – Higher School of Economics and Head of the Centre for Fundamental Sociology. He defended his kandidat of science (PhD) thesis on the sociology of the German systems theorist Niklas Luhmann, but is now a leading adept of Carl Schmitt. In 2009 he published in the journal Sociological Observer a translation into Russian of Schmitt’s 1923 Crisis of parliamentary democracy, together with a lengthy analysis of Schmitt’s ‘political and legal philosophy of space’.21 Filippov’s chapter in the Sovereignty collection starts with the question: ‘Do we live in a sovereign state?’ He states in conclusion:

20 Filippov (2006), 92. 21 Filippov (2009c), 92. Therefore both the recognition of sovereignty and its denial are not simply rhetorical figures. The question is of political communication, which proposes that the consolidation of political agents (whether citizens, official institutions or representatives of self-organisation, or other significant figures of politics) proposes the consolidation of sovereignty. This means that any really consolidated ‘we’ as a political or politically relevant unity, in the final analysis, without exception, which could appear as the subject source of the decision, must in defined circumstances in general speak of the decision (and not the depersonalised force of circumstances and events).



In 2008, in the philosophical journal Siniy Divan (Blue Divan), Filippov published his translation of Schmitt’s 1954 dialogue ‘Discussion on power and access to the power-holder’ (Gespräch über die Macht und den Zugang zum Machthaber, not yet translated into English).22 This is plainly his chosen academic focus and one that fits well with the ideology of the regime. In the same year, he published an article in the Russkiy Zhurnal entitled ‘The Leviathan grown fat: do they read Carl Schmitt in the Kremlin?’ It is plainly his ambition that they should.23

22 Filippov (2008a). 23 Filippov (2008b).





The Russian young conservatives – and Mr Remizov


One of the leading ‘young conservatives’ along with Garadzha is the prolific Mikhail Vitalyevich Remizov (b. 1978), now President of the Institute of National Strategy founded by Stanislas Belkovskiy.

His writings also have a clear direction. On 2 February 2011 he published an article ‘A collection of superstitions. The nationalisms of the ruling class’,24 in which he accused the ruling elite of having no ‘joined-up conception of national (ethnic) policy’, but only ‘a collection of superstitions, handed down from the Soviet time, which make the approach of the state to the national (ethnic) sphere completely contradictory’.

24 Remizov (2011). The first contradiction he identifies is that between the idea of a united political nation and the principle of a multinational (ethnic) state. The second contradiction is that between the status in the state of national (ethnic) minorities and the absence of an analogical status for the national (ethnic) majority. He adds that in the Russian Federation there is no form of self-determination for Russians, as opposed to Chechnya for the Chechens, Tatarstan for the Tatars, etc. He adds:

When power has a transcendental source of legitimacy, it can permit itself to play with multi-nationalism, and its point of support in general is above the nation. The problem of Russian power consists in the fact that it lost its highest point of support, but kept multi-nationalism as a principle. And precisely that principle blocks the democratic evolution of Russian power.



He referred to the 1789 French Declaration of Rights of Man and of the Citizen, to the effect that the source of sovereign power can only be the nation. In this context, the idea of the nation reflects the fact that power cannot issue from the people, if that people does not express the collectivity of self-knowledge and cultural singularity (odnorostnost), necessary, as a minimum, for mutual understanding and interconnected trust between people and as a maximum, for the development of the effects of ‘common destiny’ and ‘general will’. He cited President Medvedev who, after the recent race riots on the Manyezh Square in central Moscow, said that if we do not form a Russian national identity, then ‘the destiny of our country will be very sad’.

And in 2008 Remizov wrote an article ‘Because we are conservatives’, for the website APN (Agency for Political News), which he headed before becoming President of the Institute for National Strategy.25 He defined his position as follows: ‘Marxism was sharpened against another enemy – against liberalism, that is, in some way, against the market, elevated to the rank of ideology. But conservatism is the alternative – to Marxism – ideology of limiting the market.’

25 Remizov (2008). In his view, the Marxist critique of conservatism was precisely vulgar rather than scientific. He admitted that fascism has nothing in common with conservatism, but if ‘one has in mind classical, Italian fascism, and not Hitler’s “racial experiment”, then, by and large, it represented nothing other than the unification of conservative thought with futuristic ethics and the methods of street terror.’

For Remizov, the fundamental concept is ‘inheritance’. Thus, he says, each of the three great ideologies, liberal, Marxist and conservative, has its own basis for demanding power. In the liberal model, taxes are the basis: ‘We pay taxes, consequently, we can make demands on those who spend them.’ For the Marxists, this role is played by surplus value: the state as agent for capital lives off the surplus product created by our labour. According to conservative logic, demand on the state is based on the fact that it disposes of a shared inheritance, directly or by defining the rules for its use. For Remizov, each of these foundations has a right to existence. But in contemporary Russia the first two are absolutely fictitious: ‘We present our bill to the contemporary state not because we are tax-payers. And not because we are labourers. But because we are inheritors, therefore we are conservatives.’

The origins of present-day ‘young conservatism’, which without shame bases itself on Nazi ideology and ‘classical fascism’, as Parland also points out,26 the Messianism identified by Peter Duncan,27 with its roots in 1511, can be traced through the Soviet period; and the Eurasianism which, with its origins in the White emigration after 1917, has become one of the main sources of vocabulary and outlook for Russia’s conservative elite.28 Its main exponent, Aleksandr Dugin, has moved from overt neo-fascism and occultism to authoring key textbooks on international relations, or geopolitics, and is now a professor at Moscow State University. From a slightly different direction the late Vadim Tsymburskiy, author of the hugely influential The island of Russia,29 drew heavily from the geopolitics of Klaus Haushofer (1869–1946) and Halford Mackinder (1861–1947), both of them inspirations for Nazi ideology. All these sources feed the current Russian debates on ‘national identity’.

26 Parland (2005). 27 Duncan (2000). 28 Shlapentokh (2007). 29 Tsymbursky (2007). And, as I showed at the start with my reference to Valeriy Zorkin, who is known to have the support of the great majority of justices of the Russian Constitutional Court, this ideological brew exercises a strong influence not only on political discourse, but on the ideology of law and relations between Russia and the European Court of Human Rights.





Zorkin and sovereignty again


Indeed, on 16 May 2012, Zorkin published again in the Rossiyskaya Gazeta – a long (more than 7,000 words) essay entitled ‘The supremacy of law and the imperative of security’.30 Zorkin started by presenting his account of the supremacy of law, which was the supremacy of human rights, as set out in Article 2 of the 1993 Constitution of the Russian Federation:

30 Zorkin (2012). Article 2

Man, his rights and freedoms are the supreme value. The recognition, observance and protection of the rights and freedoms of man and citizen shall be the obligation of the State.31



31 At http://www.constitution.ru/en/10003000-02.htm (accessed 29 October 2012). However, he was at pains to point out that he meant human rights in the classical liberal sense, that is, starting from the equality of human rights and freedoms, in which it is understood that people are different and have different hierarchies of values, ideals and preferences. Thus, the rights and freedoms of one person finish where the rights and freedoms of another person begin. Zorkin distinguished this ‘classical liberalism’ from what he described as ‘aggressive “liberalisation”‘, whose apologists confuse freedom with ‘everything is permitted’. In practice, this meant social individualism and state egoism. This led Zorkin to a discussion of multiculturalism:

[W]ithin the one-sided concept of ‘liberalisation of democracy’ there is more and more to be found a priority of the minority over the majority. This tendency is especially apparent in the USA and a number of countries of Western Europe, where for some time now they argue that the protection of minority rights must become the fundamental task of key legal institutions which regulate the life of society. And they say this in such a way that in fact signifies the right of the minority to aggressive propaganda of its values, norms and way of life.



This tendency was exemplified for Zorkin by such human rights organisat ions as Amnesty International and Freedom House which propagate the assertion that to the extent that the self-expression of the person is one of the highest freedoms, this freedom is acceptable even when it shocks, discredits, and defames other people. Zorkin also saw dangers in the process of formation of a unified legal space within the Council of Europe, in which the key player is the European Court of Human Rights: ‘In their desire to take on themselves the function of “norm control” on the quality of national legislation, they turn, in this way, into a general European legislator.’ He further pointed out that if the Strasbourg court is now a legislator, there is no general European system of checks and balances.

This led Zorkin into a discussion – of sovereignty:

I consider very dangerous the assertion, popular nowadays, that the withdrawal from or even rejection of the principle of state sovereignty is a movement to the side of integration of world society. On the contrary, we must try to prove that it is the only means of guaranteeing security and the protection of human rights in conditions of globalisation, when the national state only is capable of resolving these tasks.



In Zorkin’s view, the UN itself exists only thanks to the will of sovereign states that decided to create the UN in the aftermath of World War II. He insisted that the concept of state sovereignty, starting with Westphalia, lies at the foundation of the supremacy of national constitutions in the legal system. This is the only basis for a person-centred system of international law.

According to Zorkin, the most active idea for ‘wiping out’ state sovereignty is developed by lawyers who support a relation between international and national law a concept that can be called ‘radical legal monism’. They consider that it is essential to create a global legal system in which international law will have absolute priority over national legal norms and over the national legal system as a whole, including the constitution. Zorkin considered this either to be very short sighted or a provocation. His conclusion was that the most important step in improving the system of international law would be:

[T]o make the formulation of the basic documents of the UN more precise, defining legal boundaries and the relation and hierarchy of basic concepts such as sovereignty, non-interference in the internal affairs of sovereign states, territorial integrity, the right of nations to self-determination, the protection of human rights and so on.



This is not so far at all from the Soviet conception of international law.





Russia’s continuing conflict with Strasbourg


Nothing has been resolved in Russia either as far as the European Court of Human Rights is concerned. On 22 March 2012 in the case of Markin the Grand Chamber held by 16 votes to 1 that Russia had indeed violated Article 14 of the ECHR (non-discrimination) taken in conjunction with Article 8 (right to respect for family and private life). The Grand Chamber considered that:

[T]he exclusion of servicemen from the entitlement to parental leave, while servicewomen are entitled to such leave, cannot be said to be reasonably or objectively justified. The Court concludes that this difference in treatment, of which the applicant was a victim, amounted to discrimination on grounds of sex. (para. 151)



The Grand Chamber avoided the criticism of the Constitutional Court of the Russian Federation to which the Chamber had devoted some strong language. My colleague Kirill Koroteev commented on the day of the judgment:

It has to be said that the Grand Chamber judgment, although identical in argumentation and conclusion, is written rather more diplomatically [than the Chamber]. All comments on the reasoning of the Constitutional Court have been replaced by reference to the Government’s arguments. If that suits V. D. Zorkin, so be it (although it is regrettable that the ECHR succumbed to the pressure of one person).32



32 Kirill Koroteev, 22 March 2012, at http://zakon.ru/Blogs/OneBlog/2525 (accessed 28 October 2012). Koroteev further commented in the June–August issue of the journal Sravnitelnoye Konstitutsinnoye Obozreniye (Comparative Constitutional Observer), under the heading ‘The conflict which never was’.33

33 Summarised at http://www.ilpru/page_pid_610_abc_26_lang_1.aspx (accessed 28 October 2012). On 30 August 2012 it was reported that the Military Court of St Petersburg had rendered a further judgment in the case of Markin, again refusing his claim.34 Russia had decided to ignore the judgment of the European Court of Human Rights and not to amend the relevant law. Such an amendment was the logical consequence of the Grand Chamber’s judgment. On 31 August 2012 Dmitriy Marakulin and Anna Pushkarskaya of Kommersant daily business paper reported, under the headline ‘The Military Court of St Petersburg did not subordinate itself to the European Court’, that Markin was determined to appeal and in the event of losing again would take his case back to Strasbourg on the basis of Russia’s failure to implement the Grand Chamber’s judgment.35

34 Website:http://www.gazeta.ru/social/2012/08/30/4745729.shtml (accessed 28 October 2012). 35 Website:http://www.kommersant.ru/doc/2011935 (accessed 28 October 2012).





Conclusion


DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-12

This brings me to some concluding remarks.

First, as to ‘law’. It is a fact that the Russian Empire, the Soviet Union and the contemporary Russian Federation, far from ever being a space of ‘no law’, have all been suffused, even saturated, with law. The great debates have, even in the midst of the Civil War that followed the Bolshevik revolution, almost all been conducted in terms of and in the name of law. I have tried to show that at crucial moments, my landmarks, the debate has not only been richly varied but has been fully informed by developments elsewhere, especially in the West. The foundation of law as an academic discipline in Russia and Catherine’s own direction drew directly from the most recent intellectual achievements in England and in Scotland. Academic discourse in Russia at that time can be seen as a struggle between German abstraction and the new thought emanating from the British Isles.

However, it is in my view a tragedy that so little Russian legal scholarship is translated into English, given the very rich and strong traditions established in Russia since the eighteenth century. I hope that an ‘added value’ of this book is the extent to which I have drawn from Russian legal scholarship on all the landmark events I have investigated.

There is another aspect to this, which is the close relationship that developed between Russia and England, since the sixteenth century, when Ivan IV (the Terrible) proposed marriage to Elizabeth I.1 Subsequently England (later Britain) and Russia were on the same side in every major international conflict except the Crimean War. This was in large part because Moscow became the centre of a great continental empire, whereas ‘Britannia ruled the waves’. Even the Great Game for Central Asia never went beyond pushing and shoving.

1 See John Vincent ‘Ivan the terribly rude’, Daily Telegraph, 1 February 2003, at http://www.freerepublic.com/focus/f-news/1051442/posts (accessed 31 October 2012). However, Russian law since the twentieth century is German law; the Russian Constitutional Court is modelled on the German Bundesverfassungsgericht 2 and the 1993 Russian Constitution bears strong traces of the German Constitution, especially the Sozialstaatsprinzip, the constitutional commitment to a social state.3

2 Website:http://www.bverfg.de/en/index.html (accessed 31 October 2012). 3 For this central principle of the German legal and political order, see http://www.bpb.de/politik/grundfragen/deutsche-demokratie/39302/sozialstaat?p=all (accessed 31 October 2012). Turning to ‘rights’, it is my contention that the inhabitants of the Russian Empire had in some respects more rights than those of countries such as Britain, France and the United States. Serfdom was abolished in Russia several years before slavery was abolished in the USA. And, although it is not the subject matter of this book, there is increasing evidence that the Russian Empire was considerably kinder to its subjects than was the British Empire to its.4 At the time of writing a court in London ruled that three elderly Kenyans who were tortured under British rule in the 1950s could pursue their claim for damages from Britain, in the words of the Financial Times: ‘a judgment likely to encourage other claims from victims of colonial-era brutality’.5 I wrote ‘Russian Empire’ deliberately: I have already referred to the atrocities carried out by Russian Federal forces in Chechnya in the First (1994–1997) and Second (1999–) Chechen Wars.

4 See, e.g. Newsinger (2000); see also Cobain (2012). 5 Financial Times, 5 October 2012, ‘Kenya torture victims win UK court ruling’, http://www.ft.com/cms/s/0/88d457a6–0ee9–11e2–9343–00144feabdc0.html#axzz2AtUyD5Wi (accessed 31 October 2012). I have also traced in my final chapter the sinister commonality between the UK and the Russian Federation as concerns compliance with the European Court of Human Rights (although I have more sympathy for Justice Zorkin’s arguments than those of David Cameron).

Finally, ‘ideology’. Both Britain and Russia have found it very hard to cope with the loss of empire in very recent years and demotion from superpower, even hegemonic, status. But while Britain is now an average-sized member of the European Union, and having to live with its new ranking as best it can, Russia remains a very large and important country indeed, which has suffered a demographic nightmare that is now stabilising. I have great sympathy with those who argue – and this is maybe my only point of agreement with Vladimir Putin – that it is essential for the future of global politics that Russia remains a great power and realises the potential of its location in Europe and in Asia, as a great Christian and Muslim country. I have shown how the current ideology of ‘sovereignty’ does not provide the answer, especially not when informed by the ruminations of Carl Schmitt.

An autobiographical note follows; but I am obliged to state that I fell in love with Russia, its language and people(s) on my first visit in 1983 and that passion had already been latent for many years.





Autobiographical note


DOI: 10.4324/9780203490211-13

This note explains how and why I became so enthusiastically interested in Russia and its history.

My parents were both born in the USA. My father was born to an English family in 1910 near what is now San José in California; his father had moved to California in 1890. He was educated in England after the age of 13 and fought in the British Army in World War II. My mother was born in 1924 in Clinton, Missouri, of a Dutch-American family – her maiden name was Schuyler – and grew up in St Louis, where she made her undergraduate studies. She won her masters’ degree in biochemistry at Boulder, Colorado. She came to work in the US Embassy in London after World War II and my parents were introduced through mutual relatives in California and settled in England.

My mother was a passionate Russophile – that fact is at the origin of this book.

My mother became a Russophile in an unusual way. Her lifelong friend, Tilghman B. (Skip) Koons, also Dutch-American, was born in 1926, entered Princeton in 1943 and majored in 1946 in the Woodrow Wilson School of Public and International Affairs. He was then taught Russian at the language school at the university in Boulder, Colorado, which is where he met my mother. They were very close friends for the rest of her life. Skip was in government service in Washington and then in China – Shanghai, Peking and Manchuria – for two and a half years. 1 The notice of his death on the Princeton alumni page states that he ‘did a tour in China’. 2 In fact, he was an officer in the Office of Strategic Services (OSS), the predecessor of the Central Intelligence Agency (CIA), and served with White Russian officers fighting with the Kuomintang. His Russian was perfect. Skip was awarded a Fulbright Scholarship in 1952 and his PhD from the Sorbonne in Paris traced the political ideas and activities of the Russian émigrés from the Russian Revolution to the outbreak of World War II. He was a senior CIA officer until 1959. He visited Russia on several occasions before and after the collapse of the USSR in 1991, especially to attend the Kirov Opera and ballet in St Petersburg.

1 Website:http://ucblibraries.colorado.edu/archives/collections/jlsp/interpreter127.doc (accessed 17 September 2011). 2 Website:http://paw.princeton.edu/memorials/13/71/index.xml (accessed 17 September 2011). While my mother was at Boulder with Skip she attended lectures given by Lenin’s chief opponent in 1917, Aleksandr Kerensky (1881–1970) and made friends in the White Russian émigré community. She learned a great deal of Russian culture, especially art and music. Through Skip my mother developed a fascination with Russia and love of its culture, and her Russophilia was passed on to me.

I studied philosophy at university and, not uncommonly for my generation, was a very active Trotskyist from 1969 to 1974, thereby receiving an excellent grounding in the works of Marx, Engels, Lenin and Trotsky and became rather well read, even expert, in Russian history, especially the 1905 and 1917 revolutions, and the ‘Revolution betrayed’ under Stalin.

Having found that work as a philosopher is hard to come by, I took the quick two-year route to the bar and started to practise as a barrister in 1975; in 1990 I started to teach at East London Polytechnic and have since taught at Essex University, the University of North London (later London Metropolitan University) and, since 2006, at Birkbeck College, University of London.

I first visited Russia and learned the Russian language in the following way. From 1978 to 1986 I was a borough councillor in the London borough of Lambeth, elected twice for the Labour Party. Since World War II Lambeth had been twinned with Moskvoretskiy Rayon, a district of Moscow on the south bank of the Moscow River opposite the Kremlin, just as Lambeth is situated on the south bank of the Thames opposite Westminster. I travelled for the first time to Moscow in 1983 with a Lambeth delegation and I fell in love with Russia and the Russian language. The Executive Committee (Ispolkom) of the rayon was located in Bakhrushina Ulitsa, near Paveletskiy Station, near the Bakhrushina Theatre Museum, the Tretiakov Gallery and Pyatnitskaya Ulitsa (Good Friday Street).

I began to study Russian in 1985 with a brilliant teacher, Lara Bushell (Putsello). Her father was Bashkir and her mother Jewish; she grew up in Ufa, the capital of Bashkortotstan. She is tall with Asiatic features, a permanent leather cap and a stormy temperament. She studied at Moscow State University in the 1980s, but avoided raspredeleniye, the system by which graduates were sent to employment anywhere in the USSR, becaming instead a limitchitsa, living illegally in Moscow. She was a fanatic for the acting and singing of the Soviet semi-underground bard, Vladimir Visotskiy. This was the time when Visotskiy performed at the Taganka Theatre, under Lyubimov. My two years learning Russian with Lara, two hours a week one to one, and homework including memorising poetry each week, meant that my head is full of Vysotskiy, Yesenin, Akhmatova as well as Pushkin, Lermontov and many others. I benefited from a classically Soviet education in Russian.

In October 1987 my translation of Boris Vasiliev’s Zaftra byla voina (Tomorrow was war) provided the simultaneous translation into English when the Mayakovsky Theatre under Andrei Goncharov performed for a week at the National Theatre in London, to enthusiastic audiences and reviews. 3 I also translated Yevgeniya Ginsburg’s Krutoi marshrut (known in English as Into the whirlwind). In 1988 the Russian journal Soviet Literature published my translation of a story in a special issue on science fiction. 4 I am an enthusiastic theatre goer (teatralnik) in Russia and read Russian texts voraciously; but I have pursued an academic rather than a literary career.

3 Michael Billington ‘Intimations of mortality. A moving tribute to the Class of “40” and “A tale of two survivors”. Michael Golfarb talks to a writer and director about telling it like it was’, The Guardian, 30 October 1987, 29 – interview with Boris Vassiliev and Andrei Goncharov. 4 Vyacheslav Bukur, ‘He Repaired Me’ translated by Bill Bowring, no. 12 (1988) Soviet Literature 6–45. Since 1983 I have visited Russia several times a year, have worked as an expert in projects of the UN, EU, the Council of Europe, OSCE as well as for DFID and have taught at a large number of universities in Russia from Kaliningrad to Khabarovsk, including St Petersburg and Moscow State Universities and MGIMO. I have published scholarly articles in Russian in several academic journals. I was deported from Russia in 2005 (on my way to observe the trial of Stas Dmitrievsky – for ‘incitement to racial hatred’ as a result of his activities in the Russia-Chechnya Friendship Society – in Nizhny Novgorod) and in 2007 (arrested by the Federal Migration Service while teaching senior judges in Astrakhan). I took judicial review proceedings in the Khimki City Court in 2006 and was granted another visa; in 2007 I was saved by General (retd) Saltykov, the Human Rights Ombudsman of Astrakhan Oblast.

Skip died on 7 July 2005, a few years after my mother, in 2001; I got to know him well, as did my wife Olga, who was born in Ulyanovsk and attended the same gymnasium at which Lenin and Kerensky were both pupils; Kerensky’s father was the headmaster, Lenin’s father was the schools’ inspector. We married in 1989 in Moscow – she was a young member of the circle of Sergei Adamovich Kovalyov, the first Russian Human Rights Ombudsman, and to this day one of the two (the other is Liudmila Alekseeva) senior figures in the Russian human rights movement. In 1996 I assisted Rachel Walker in securing an honorary doctorate from Essex University, where I then taught, for Sergei Kovalyov.

In the 1990s I worked with the Kurdish Human Rights Project, representing Turkish Kurds against Turkey at the European Court of Human Rights. Russia joined the Council of Europe in 1996 and ratified the European Convention on Human Rights in 1998. In late 1999 Prime Minister Putin started the Second Chechen War and in 2000 I started working with Memorial, of which Sergei Kovalyov is President, assisting Chechen victims of gross human rights violations – my association with Memorial began in the late 1980s. In 2002 I was awarded €1 million from the EC and founded the European Human Rights Advocacy Centre (EHRAC), with 12 lawyers in Russia and an office in London, in partnership with Memorial. In 2005 the first six Chechen applicants won their cases at Strasbourg and since then we have taken hundreds of Chechen, environmental and other cases.

I visit Russia almost every month, for EHRAC, to teach and to take part in seminars, roundtables and conferences. My wife, Olga, and our children, Vanya and Masha, have all shown extraordinary patience while this book was being written.
